## Classification of Medicinal Plants

Sedatives Heat, or the Excited Tissue State Relaxants Wind, or the Constrictive Tissue State Nutritive Tonics Dry, or the Atrophic Tissue State Astringents Damp Flowing, or the Relaxed Tissue State Alteratives or Blood Purifiers Damp Stagnation, or the Torpid Tissue State Stimulants Cold, or the Depressed Tissue State Bibliography

# W

<!-- chunk -->

### The life so short, the craft so long to learn,

<!-- chunk -->

### experience difficult to obtain,

<!-- chunk -->

### experiment dangerous, judgment hard.

<!-- chunk -->

### —T

HE

<!-- chunk -->

### F

IRST

<!-- chunk -->

### A

PHORISM OF

<!-- chunk -->

### H

IPPOCRATES

<!-- chunk -->

### estern herbalism was originally a part of Western medicine. In

<!-- chunk -->

### order to understand much of the herbal tradition we have

<!-- chunk -->

### inherited, we need to comprehend some of the concepts of old

<!-- chunk -->

### time Western practice. The old doctors did not possess the modern

<!-- chunk -->

### scientific technology that allows researchers to gaze upon the exact

<!-- chunk -->

### molecular structure of tissues, drugs, disease lesions, microorganisms,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the human genome itself. Instead, they had to define diseases and

<!-- chunk -->

### medicines in terms of broad physiological patterns, processes,

<!-- chunk -->

### characteristic symptoms, and symptom-pictures observable with the

<!-- chunk -->

### naked eye, or a few simple instruments.

<!-- chunk -->

### Until 1930, the Western pharmacopoeia consisted largely of plant and

<!-- chunk -->

### mineral substances which were defined in terms of constituents which

<!-- chunk -->

### are sensible to the hand, eye, or tongue—mucilage, astringent, bitters,

<!-- chunk -->

### and so forth. These substances act on general physiological conditions,

<!-- chunk -->

### rather than molecular lesions. Within this milieu, plant medicines made

<!-- chunk -->

### sense. They provided sources of such ingredients in different

<!-- chunk -->

### combinations suitable to different problems and structures of the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### With the change to modern biomedicine, drugs came to be defined

<!-- chunk -->

### according to a specific molecular structure. Herbs, with their less refined

<!-- chunk -->

### ingredients composed of masses of different constituents, became

<!-- chunk -->

### increasingly irrelevant. If they had value, it was because they might

<!-- chunk -->

### contain an ingredient that could be separated and refined to become a

<!-- chunk -->

### drug. The whole herb was no longer valuable in and of itself.

<!-- chunk -->

### In a similar manner, diseases are now defined in terms of molecular

<!-- chunk -->

### structure and change rather than as general processes, functions, or

<!-- chunk -->

### symptom-pictures. Thus, not only are herbs ignored, but the entire

<!-- chunk -->

### system of medicine of which they formed a part has also been forgotten.

<!-- chunk -->

### This has made it very hard to understand the old herbal literature,

<!-- chunk -->

### interwoven as it is with an old-time Western medicine which has become

<!-- chunk -->

### irrelevant and been forgotten.

<!-- chunk -->

### Recently there has been a revival of interest in Western herbalism,

<!-- chunk -->

### along with other systems of natural, traditional, and holistic medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most of these approaches—acupuncture, homeopathy, and Ayurveda, for

<!-- chunk -->

### example—possess a long-standing medical philosophy and system upon

<!-- chunk -->

### which they may be practiced. On the other hand, Western herbalism,

<!-- chunk -->

### bereft from old-time Western medicine, possesses very little doctrine or

<!-- chunk -->

### guidance by which herbs can be related to diseases or parts of the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism. As a consequence, those who attempt to practice Western

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalism are forced to adopt a system from one of these other

<!-- chunk -->

### disciplines, or from biomedicine itself. Unfortunately, this causes the

<!-- chunk -->

### medicinal plants of the West to become, as it were, homeopathic,

<!-- chunk -->

### Chinese, Ayurvedic, or biomedical agents. They have lost their unique

<!-- chunk -->

### identity and history, associated with thousands of years of popular and

<!-- chunk -->

### professional use.

<!-- chunk -->

### To compound this problem, Western science dismisses folk medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### as unscientific or even fraudulent. This has caused the demise of

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional healers, if not by outright persecution, then by economic and

<!-- chunk -->

### legal exclusion. By comparison, in China the folk medical applications of

<!-- chunk -->

### a plant are taken as the basis from which research may be conducted

<!-- chunk -->

### and traditional healers have been encouraged within government

<!-- chunk -->

### regulations.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another side effect of this situation has been that herbs are now

<!-- chunk -->

### described and sold according to advertising based on “science.” In many

<!-- chunk -->

### cases the science is incomplete or misrepresentative, but only when a

<!-- chunk -->

### product sounds scientific will people flock to it. Thus, much of the herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### and nutritional marketplace apes conventional biomedicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### If we are to use Western herbs in a holistic fashion we need to return to

<!-- chunk -->

### the physiological medicine with which they were associated. This is the

<!-- chunk -->

### system within which Western herbalism developed and its limited

<!-- chunk -->

### restoration allows us to recognize nuances in the Western herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### tradition which are lost when we adopt foreign concepts. We do not

<!-- chunk -->

### need to resurrect the old system entirely, only the most fundamental

<!-- chunk -->

### elements. We also need to bring it up to date.

<!-- chunk -->

### Not all physiological processes were understood in the old days—they

<!-- chunk -->

### are not, indeed, understood today—but, by using the physiological

<!-- chunk -->

### approach of old-time medicine, we can generate a system of medical

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding which is physiologically accurate and conducive to

<!-- chunk -->

### holistic care, i.e., cure. Plant medicines, constructed as they are from a

<!-- chunk -->

### broad slurry of chemical components, are naturally suited to the

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment of broad physiological patterns in the body, rather than

<!-- chunk -->

### specific molecular lesions. They also cure, that is to say, they stimulate

<!-- chunk -->

### or sedate or nourish or cleanse, as required by the case, to bring the

<!-- chunk -->

### physiological function back to health and restore self-maintenance and

<!-- chunk -->

### regulation within the organism, i.e., homeostasis.

<!-- chunk -->

### The radical concept of traditional folk medicine is that disease is

<!-- chunk -->

### curable, i.e., the organism can be returned to self-regulation and health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most modern people still go to their doctors nursing this quaint folk

<!-- chunk -->

### concept, but are instead treated to the modern theory: the organism is

<!-- chunk -->

### broken, nature is not able to take care of herself, hence science will take

<!-- chunk -->

### care of it through “health maintenance.” Modern biomedicine does not

<!-- chunk -->

### attempt to cure, but to maintain the organism through artificial means.

<!-- chunk -->

### In The Practice of Traditional Western Herbalism we will focus on the

<!-- chunk -->

### use of humors, qualities, or tissue states to describe basic, simple

<!-- chunk -->

### categories of pathology. We offer this as a contrast to the conventional

<!-- chunk -->

### view of molecular lesions. Due to the complexity of the subject, there is

<!-- chunk -->

### not enough space to include more than introductory material on the

<!-- chunk -->

### physiological patterns seen in the behavior of the major organs and

<!-- chunk -->

### systems of the body. This will have to await further publication. I am

<!-- chunk -->

### also reserving, for now, discussion of the regulatory systems, which

<!-- chunk -->

### oversee the general physiology of the organism. All these topics can be

<!-- chunk -->

### learned from the standpoint of holism, rather than conventional

<!-- chunk -->

### materialistic science.

<!-- chunk -->

### Anyone who masters the terminology in the second chapter (qualities

<!-- chunk -->

### and degrees) will be able to understand the basics of Greek herbalism

<!-- chunk -->

### down to the Renaissance. The whole tradition will lie like an open book

<!-- chunk -->

### before the eye. Texts, which before seemed baffling or incomplete, will

<!-- chunk -->

### reveal depths not guessed. Likewise, anyone who masters the

<!-- chunk -->

### terminology in the third chapter (tissue states and a few herbal actions)

<!-- chunk -->

### will find the whole of nineteenth-and early twentieth-century herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### literature lying before the mind as an open book. There will then be no

<!-- chunk -->

### question what the old authors meant, or whether or not Western

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalism can be practiced upon a holistic foundation.

<!-- chunk -->

### The tools we use in complementary and alternative medicine are weak

<!-- chunk -->

### compared to those used by biomedicine. However, they can perform

<!-- chunk -->

### miracles which biomedicine cannot achieve. All of us in the field have

<!-- chunk -->

### seen this. How is this possible? It is because our tools work with one of

<!-- chunk -->

### the most powerful forces available, the energy of life or Nature herself.

<!-- chunk -->

### When we look at an animal or person we see an enormous amount of

<!-- chunk -->

### energy. When thwarted by sickness, this power is blocked or even

<!-- chunk -->

### empowers the illness. Supporting the self-healing power with simple

<!-- chunk -->

### methods like food, herbs, homeopathic medicines, acupuncture,

<!-- chunk -->

### therapeutic touch, mental attitude, and prayer can do wonders that can

<!-- chunk -->

### never be achieved when Nature is ignored or suppressed.

<!-- chunk -->

### Matthew Wood

<!-- chunk -->

### Sunnyfield Herb Farm

<!-- chunk -->

### January,

# I

<!-- chunk -->

### In vain hath He made Nature if we dwell on our own conceptions

<!-- chunk -->

### and make no use of her principles. It were a happy necessity if our

<!-- chunk -->

### thoughts could not vary from her ways.

<!-- chunk -->

### —T

HOMAS

<!-- chunk -->

### V

AUGHN

<!-- chunk -->

### t has often been said that Western herbalism lacks a “system of

<!-- chunk -->

### energetics.” What that means, in short, is a vocabulary for describing

<!-- chunk -->

### “energy patterns” or physiological and psychological patterns in the

<!-- chunk -->

### body. The point is to express the activities and configurations of energy,

<!-- chunk -->

### qi, or the vital force—call it what you will. Examples of energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### systems include the yin/yang theory of traditional Chinese medicine, the

<!-- chunk -->

### five elements of China and India, or the four humors of Greek medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### The use of an energetic system is virtually a prerequisite in holistic

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. Biomedicine is constructed upon a materialistic interpretation

<!-- chunk -->

### of nature, which looks to molecular structure as a guide. Holistic

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine is founded on the concept and experience that the organism is

<!-- chunk -->

### a functional unit or whole under the directing hand of an intelligent,

<!-- chunk -->

### self-regulating, and self-correcting guiding life force or energy. Since

<!-- chunk -->

### Nature in the organism is self-maintaining and self-healing, holistic

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine is further based upon the assumption that the organism can be

<!-- chunk -->

### cured, that is, returned from an unbalanced state to one of balance or

<!-- chunk -->

### homeostasis. As mentioned in the introduction, biomedicine does not

<!-- chunk -->

### seek to cure or return the organism to self-regulation, but removes and

<!-- chunk -->

### replaces broken parts through drugs or surgery. Thus, modern medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### speaks of “health management” rather than cure, while holistic or

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional medicine speaks of cure, vitalism, and energy patterns. The

<!-- chunk -->

### language is intrinsically different, and in order to practice holistic

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine, or to practice Western herbalism on a holistic basis, we need

<!-- chunk -->

### to learn a new vocabulary.

<!-- chunk -->

### It should be understood that herbs can be used either way: to

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulate the self-healing powers of the organism to return to health, or

<!-- chunk -->

### to artificially manipulate the organism to fit an artificial goal. For

<!-- chunk -->

### example, goldenseal can be used in small doses as a bitter tonic to

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulate the digestion and mucosa, removing tendencies to bacterial

<!-- chunk -->

### infection and mucus production, or it can be applied in massive doses as

<!-- chunk -->

### a “natural antibiotic” to kill bacteria. It contains a potent antibacterial,

<!-- chunk -->

### berberine. The first use is holistic because it increases the health of the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism while the second is an example of “health management”

<!-- chunk -->

### because it assumes that the organism cannot get rid of the bacteria on its

<!-- chunk -->

### own and the knowledge of the practitioner is superior. The use of herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### is not necessarily holistic.

<!-- chunk -->

### Whatever qi or life force is, most of us see it indirectly, in its effects. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is the fact that this energy forms patterns that interests us. There are

<!-- chunk -->

### particular patterns to health, and to disease. Thus, Hippocrates bids us

<!-- chunk -->

### learn the medical art by looking at a man in the bloom of health, and

<!-- chunk -->

### comparing him with a man on the edge of death. In order to describe

<!-- chunk -->

### and influence the patterns of disease formed by unbalanced energy or qi,

<!-- chunk -->

### we need a “language of energy patterns.”

<!-- chunk -->

### A survey of various cultures around the world shows that a simple

<!-- chunk -->

### system of two, three, four, or five elements, qualities, or humors forms

<!-- chunk -->

### the basis of diagnosis and treatment in traditional holistic medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Traditional Chinese medicine uses the yin/yang system and the five

<!-- chunk -->

### elements. Ayurvedic medicine uses the three doshas and the five

<!-- chunk -->

### elements. Greek medicine used the four elements, qualities, humors, and

<!-- chunk -->

### temperaments. American Indian medicine acknowledges four to seven

<!-- chunk -->

### directions.

<!-- chunk -->

### These different terms are translated fairly easily from one system to

<!-- chunk -->

### another. Yin and yang resemble fire and water in another system, the

<!-- chunk -->

### five elements of China and India are similar to the four elements of

<!-- chunk -->

### Greece, and the magical fifth element of alchemy. Local culture, weather

<!-- chunk -->

### patterns, and epidemiology determine the arrangement and number of

<!-- chunk -->

### elements or humors but they bear considerable resemblance to one

<!-- chunk -->

### another.

<!-- chunk -->

### One further point should be made. The arrangement of elements,

<!-- chunk -->

### directions, or humors into a fourfold mandala (cross or square) serves an

<!-- chunk -->

### important psychological and spiritual function. The psychiatrist C. G.

<!-- chunk -->

### Jung showed that the mandala was a symbol of wholeness for the

<!-- chunk -->

### psyche. Many of us, when we read about or look at pictures of the four

<!-- chunk -->

### or five elements, feel this deep sense of meaning and fulfillment.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although this symbol is mythological and spiritual in nature that does

<!-- chunk -->

### not mean it should be excluded from medicine. Rather medicine, to be

<!-- chunk -->

### holistic and curative, must include mythic elements to satisfy the soul

<!-- chunk -->

### and spirit.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Forgotten Energetics of Western Herbalism

<!-- chunk -->

### At the beginning of the chapter, it was said that Western herbalism lacks

<!-- chunk -->

### a system of energy patterns. Actually, this is not true—it is just that this

<!-- chunk -->

### special vocabulary has been forgotten.

<!-- chunk -->

### These energetics still exist. They are inferred in the list of herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### “actions” which are found in the front or back of just about every herb

<!-- chunk -->

### book. These include terms like diuretic, diaphoretic, stimulant,

<!-- chunk -->

### emmenagogue, aromatic, and astringent. When I first began the study of

<!-- chunk -->

### Western herbalism, I couldn’t get very far because these terms seemed so

<!-- chunk -->

### wooden and mechanical. I thought the terms were unrelated to a deeper

<!-- chunk -->

### energetic context connected to the life force, and which could stimulate

<!-- chunk -->

### my intuitive understanding and problem solving. After many years, I

<!-- chunk -->

### came to appreciate that they were the remains of an energetic system

<!-- chunk -->

### that had been forgotten.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are two basic subdivisions of herbal actions, one referring to the

<!-- chunk -->

### “tissue state” or pathological condition, and the other to the organ or

<!-- chunk -->

### seat on which the agent operates. The first category is composed of

<!-- chunk -->

### terms like astringent, emollient, mucilage, stimulant, aromatic,

<!-- chunk -->

### refrigerant, antispasmodic, antiseptics, and normalizer. The second refers

<!-- chunk -->

### to specific local organs or functions: diuretic (kidneys), nephritic

<!-- chunk -->

### (kidneys), expectorant (lungs), pectoral (lungs), diaphoretic (skin and

<!-- chunk -->

### fever), febrifuge (fever), nervine, emmenagogue (menses promoting),

<!-- chunk -->

### cholagogue (gall bladder), etc. There was originally a third category,

<!-- chunk -->

### which described whether the herb raised or lowered, internalized or

<!-- chunk -->

### externalized, thinned or thickened, but this was dropped by the end of

<!-- chunk -->

### the eighteenth century. A classical reference for these three categories

<!-- chunk -->

### can be found in A Key to Galen’s Method of Physick, (c. 1650) by Nicholas

<!-- chunk -->

### Culpeper.

<!-- chunk -->

### It may not be readily apparent how the word astringent is an energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### term. However, a short examination shows that it does refer us to an

<!-- chunk -->

### energetic pattern. The condition which an astringent acts upon is not a

<!-- chunk -->

### specific molecular lesion or disease in a modern biomedical sense, but

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue which is prolapsed and leaking fluids. Astringents contain tannic

<!-- chunk -->

### acids that contract tissue and prevent prolapse and fluid loss. Thus, the

<!-- chunk -->

### term astringent refers to an energetic category—what the Chinese,

<!-- chunk -->

### Indian, Greek, or Arabic doctors would have called “dampness.”

<!-- chunk -->

### During the evolution of Western medicine the old energetic terms hot,

<!-- chunk -->

### cold, damp, and dry were dropped, and in their place, terms like

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulant (warming), refrigerant (cooling), mucilage (moistening), and

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent (drying) were adopted. This represented a movement away

<!-- chunk -->

### from a mythological frame of mind towards one that was increasingly

<!-- chunk -->

### scientific. Yet, such terms still refer to general physiological patterns in

<!-- chunk -->

### the organism rather than specific molecular lesions, as is the practice in

<!-- chunk -->

### modern diagnosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### The organ-and function-specific terms are also energetic in that they

<!-- chunk -->

### refer to broad physiological functions in the organism rather than

<!-- chunk -->

### specific lesions. They refer us back to the period when even conventional

<!-- chunk -->

### doctors treated conditions in this manner. At that time, drugs and herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### were more closely related because both were defined in terms of general

<!-- chunk -->

### physiological effects or local affinities. Now drugs are applied to

<!-- chunk -->

### molecular changes and people want to ape modern biomedicine by

<!-- chunk -->

### applying herbs in the same fashion. Unfortunately, herbs generally do

<!-- chunk -->

### not work this way. Sometimes they contain enough berberine, for

<!-- chunk -->

### instance, to kill bacterium, but usually their positive effect is based upon

<!-- chunk -->

### a slurry of different chemicals found together in the original plant.

<!-- chunk -->

### The reason herbs are suited to the old-fashioned approach, i.e., to the

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment of broad physiological patterns in the body rather than

<!-- chunk -->

### specific molecular lesions, is that they are in fact complexes of many

<!-- chunk -->

### different chemicals drawn together. These combinations come together

<!-- chunk -->

### in the plant, not randomly, but to solve a certain environmental problem

<!-- chunk -->

### or imbalance which assaulted the plant from outside. The chemicals

<!-- chunk -->

### found in goldenseal undoubtedly help it to live in the rich, shady, well-

<!-- chunk -->

### watered woodlands of Eastern North America where it is native. It is not

<!-- chunk -->

### apparent exactly how that environment resembles the damp, nutrient-

<!-- chunk -->

### coated mucosa of the digestive tract. One can see a sort of metaphorical

<!-- chunk -->

### similarity, but the peculiar slurry of chemistry found in goldenseal

<!-- chunk -->

### makes it an ideal tonic for sub-functional mucosa. The very least that

<!-- chunk -->

### can be said is that the taste, a mixture of bitter and sweet, stimulates the

<!-- chunk -->

### secretions of the mouth and thus triggers secretions all the way down

<!-- chunk -->

### the digestive canal. Goldenseal is thus defined, according to the list of

<!-- chunk -->

### actions, as a “bitter tonic” for the digestive mucosa in particular.

<!-- chunk -->

### Modern medicine does not think in broad, physiological terms. For

<!-- chunk -->

### instance, stomach ulcer would be treated by the use of an antibacterial

<!-- chunk -->

### that kills the bacteria associated with gastric ulceration. It does not

<!-- chunk -->

### attempt to change the tissue imbalance that led to the proliferation of

<!-- chunk -->

### bacteria at that location—it is not even interested in the fact that there

<!-- chunk -->

### might be a changed tissue condition that encouraged the proliferation in

<!-- chunk -->

### the first place. Consequently, when the person is exposed to the bacteria

<!-- chunk -->

### again it will again multiply and settle in. In holistic herbal medicine, on

<!-- chunk -->

### the other hand, we would want to change the tissue state that supports

<!-- chunk -->

### the invasive agent. Here we could hardly do better than to use

<!-- chunk -->

### goldenseal, which is almost infallible for the treatment and long-term

<!-- chunk -->

### cure of stomach ulcers. Goldenseal is successful, not just because it kills

<!-- chunk -->

### bacteria, but because it stimulates increased mucosal activity and

<!-- chunk -->

### secretions, making the stomach tissue more active and less susceptible to

<!-- chunk -->

### bacterial infection.

<!-- chunk -->

### For several hundred years there has been a bias in Western education

<!-- chunk -->

### against the intuitive approach in medicine and science. Intuition,

<!-- chunk -->

### imagination, and instinct are relegated to art or religion. There it is

<!-- chunk -->

### permissible to use such faculties. While one can appreciate the need for

<!-- chunk -->

### objective observation and analytical study, the use of these methods

<!-- chunk -->

### alone, as if they were the only true measures of reality, in and of itself, is

<!-- chunk -->

### an inherently sick and flawed condition. It skews the human organism

<!-- chunk -->

### by rendering obsolete skills and faculties it has always relied upon to

<!-- chunk -->

### contend with environmental and internal stress. As a consequence, we in

<!-- chunk -->

### the West have lost much in the way of our traditional energetic systems.

<!-- chunk -->

### We pride ourselves on education and sophistication, but we have lost the

<!-- chunk -->

### ability to speak about the world as an intuitive experience.

<!-- chunk -->

### Whatever people say they believe in terms of spirituality and religion,

<!-- chunk -->

### what they do when they are sick and in need reflects the true basis of

<!-- chunk -->

### their belief system. For the average person, science and medicine is the

<!-- chunk -->

### answer. Religion is reserved for miracles, not solving everyday problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### In other words, science and medicine visualize and teach us that we are

<!-- chunk -->

### innately separated from our spiritual and, to a large extent, our

<!-- chunk -->

### psychological resources; that we cannot solve our everyday problems

<!-- chunk -->

### through these means but must turn away from spirit and put our faith in

<!-- chunk -->

### the material world. From a spiritual perspective, this view is not neutral

<!-- chunk -->

### but actively anti-spiritual. It reinforces a completely materialistic view of

<!-- chunk -->

### the nature of life. Miracles—if they exist—and spiritual life—if it exists

<!-- chunk -->

### —are far removed from everyday life.

<!-- chunk -->

### In holistic medicine we hold up a different view. We are not isolated

<!-- chunk -->

### from life, we are an expression of life. We are part of this unseen force

<!-- chunk -->

### that propels and patterns us in our daily lives. If we can believe in the

<!-- chunk -->

### unseen power of the life force we are much closer to a real appreciation

<!-- chunk -->

### of the existence and power of the soul and spirit, i.e., the psychological

<!-- chunk -->

### apparatus and the spirit which guides us towards a meaningful life.

<!-- chunk -->

### Those who kill the life of the body also, in some measure, kill the life of

<!-- chunk -->

### the soul and spirit.

<!-- chunk -->

### A culture that has lost its folk medicine, or its ability to heal and cure

<!-- chunk -->

### everyday problems with everyday materials, has lost a part of its soul.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is something deeply sick about Western culture. If we are to

<!-- chunk -->

### restore it to health we need to restore its lost folk medical soul. This

<!-- chunk -->

### means that people are going to have to trust their personal resources and

<!-- chunk -->

### make decisions for themselves, rather than being controlled by statistical

<!-- chunk -->

### studies, worse case scenarios, or the consensus opinion of experts and

<!-- chunk -->

### counter-intuitive advice. It also means that we are going to have to

<!-- chunk -->

### restore folk medicine to Western culture.

<!-- chunk -->

### It also has to be said that it is not possible to understand the

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional uses of Western herbs unless they are viewed against the

<!-- chunk -->

### backdrop of the folk medical or pre-modern system in which they

<!-- chunk -->

### developed. Using Western herbs according to an Eastern energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### system does not bring forth their cultural and medical richness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Furthermore, it is not enough to borrow concepts from Eastern or

<!-- chunk -->

### American Indian sources because this only feeds the illusion that some

<!-- chunk -->

### expert outside ourselves has the answer. Thus, in order to restore the

<!-- chunk -->

### folk medical soul of our white Western culture, we need to use terms

<!-- chunk -->

### that are already in use in the culture and supply them with their lost

<!-- chunk -->

### energetic meanings.

<!-- chunk -->

### It would seem like an almost hopeless job to restore Western folk

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine, but in fact the terms used to describe energetics in the late

<!-- chunk -->

### nineteenth and early twentieth century are still in use today. Thus, we

<!-- chunk -->

### can use terms that are both energetic and familiar. These are the “tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### states” described in the third chapter: irritation or excitation;

<!-- chunk -->

### constriction or tension; atrophy or wasting; relaxation; torpor or

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnation; and depression or lack of excitation. In addition, we can

<!-- chunk -->

### restore the organ-and function-based medicine of the old doctors which

<!-- chunk -->

### common people intuitively understand—my “stomach hurts, or,” I’m

<!-- chunk -->

### going to the “heart specialist.” At the same time, these relatively modern

<!-- chunk -->

### terms relate exactly to the elements, humors, or qualities of ancient

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. Thus, we can make references between different systems and

<!-- chunk -->

### adjust terminology to fit our needs.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is for these reasons that this book will describe the ancient energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### system of Western medicine and herbalism, and the energetic method of

<!-- chunk -->

### the immediate past, in order to build a background for a profound

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding of herbs and their actions on the human organism. Our

<!-- chunk -->

### approach will be to define the “tissue states,” their relationship to

<!-- chunk -->

### ancient elemental or humoral energetics, and apply them to the organs

<!-- chunk -->

### and functions of the body as understood in broad physiological patterns,

<!-- chunk -->

### not as molecular structures and functions.

<!-- chunk -->

## Energetic Classification

<!-- chunk -->

### In case our meandering in the history of medicine is too difficult for our

<!-- chunk -->

### readers, let us sum up the above argument by introducing some of the

<!-- chunk -->

### basic energetic terminology used in traditional medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### The most simple and elegant breakdown of matter and energy into

<!-- chunk -->

### patterns begins with the dualism, so well expressed in traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### Chinese philosophy and medicine as yin and yang. Philosophically, the

<!-- chunk -->

### former represents the passive, feminine side of the universe, while the

<!-- chunk -->

### latter represents the active, masculine side. Traditional medicine is more

<!-- chunk -->

### pragmatic, with yin standing for water, cooling, nourishing, and rest,

<!-- chunk -->

### and yang standing for fire, warming, and action. The four qualities of

<!-- chunk -->

### hot, cold, damp, and dry can easily be deduced from the primal two:

<!-- chunk -->

### yang excesshot

<!-- chunk -->

### yang deficiencycold

<!-- chunk -->

### yin excessdamp

<!-- chunk -->

### yin deficiencydry

<!-- chunk -->

### This gives us the four qualities of Greek/Arabic medicine derived from

<!-- chunk -->

### an initial dyad. There is a system of six environmental influences in

<!-- chunk -->

### Chinese practice analogous to the four qualities: hot, cold, wind, damp,

<!-- chunk -->

### dry, and summer heat (a kind of humid heat). The four qualities also

<!-- chunk -->

### correspond to the four elements of Greek philosophy. Interestingly, they

<!-- chunk -->

### correlate with the states of matter recognized in chemistry:

<!-- chunk -->

### Firehotcombustion

<!-- chunk -->

### Airdrygas

<!-- chunk -->

### Waterdampliquid

<!-- chunk -->

### Earthcoldsolid

<!-- chunk -->

### The ancient Israelites and Egyptians only recognized three elements—

<!-- chunk -->

### air, water, and earth—thinking of fire as a different kind of agent that

<!-- chunk -->

### changed the other three. In Greek and Indian philosophy, a fifth element

<!-- chunk -->

### was added which acts on the other four: the quintessence or ether. This

<!-- chunk -->

### was associated with wind. And thus, in Chinese medicine, we have five

<!-- chunk -->

### elements including wind. The whole round of associations would look

<!-- chunk -->

### like this:

<!-- chunk -->

## GreeceIndiaChina

<!-- chunk -->

### Etherether (wind)wind

<!-- chunk -->

### Firefirefire

<!-- chunk -->

### Airairmetal

<!-- chunk -->

### Waterwaterwater

<!-- chunk -->

### Earthearthearth

<!-- chunk -->

### After the fall of Greek medicine, the Western doctors adopted the

<!-- chunk -->

### theory of irritation, based on the observation that nerve tissue when

<!-- chunk -->

### removed from the body, was irritable or reactive to certain influences

<!-- chunk -->

### such as, for example, heat and cold. This led to the organizing of tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### states, or energetic patterns, based on irritability and relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Eventually, J.M. Thurston developed a model comprised of six basic

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue states:

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue StateQuality

<!-- chunk -->

### irritation, excitationheat

<!-- chunk -->

### constriction, tensionwind

<!-- chunk -->

### atrophydry

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxationdamp flowing

<!-- chunk -->

### torpordamp stagnation

<!-- chunk -->

### depressioncold

<!-- chunk -->

### In Greek medicine, dampness was divided into damp that flowed and

<!-- chunk -->

### damp that stayed stuck in the body. In Ayurvedic medicine, the water

<!-- chunk -->

### element also is used twice as the three doshas (water, fire, and air) and

<!-- chunk -->

### the five elements are placed in relationship:

<!-- chunk -->

## DoshaElementTissue State

<!-- chunk -->

### vataairatrophy

<!-- chunk -->

### windconstriction

<!-- chunk -->

### pittafireirritation, excitation

<!-- chunk -->

### waterrelaxation (water flowing)

<!-- chunk -->

### kaphawater earthtorpor (water stagnant) depression

<!-- chunk -->

### We will visit the theme of the three doshas and the corresponding

<!-- chunk -->

### elements and tissue states in the chapter on constitution.

<!-- chunk -->

### A final set of relationships might be useful:

<!-- chunk -->

## ExcessDeficiency

<!-- chunk -->

## RateExcitationDepression

<!-- chunk -->

## TensionConstrictionRelaxation

<!-- chunk -->

## DensityAtrophyTorpor

<!-- chunk -->

### Atrophy represents a state where the system is too dry or solid, while

<!-- chunk -->

### torpor represents a condition where it is too wet, lacking in solidity and

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnant.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Energetic Organism

<!-- chunk -->

### The organism does not remain fixed permanently in a constitutional or

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state, but is subject to perpetual change. This is already implicated

<!-- chunk -->

### in the energetic classification scheme mentioned above, where wind

<!-- chunk -->

### serves to represent the element, or factor of change. Thus, in addition to

<!-- chunk -->

### providing a classification of energy types, patterns, and constitutions,

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional and holistic medicine also provide a view of the organism as

<!-- chunk -->

### a dynamic biological entity.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the traditional view, which we find in virtually every area of the

<!-- chunk -->

### world, the organism is seen as a dynamic entity consuming and breaking

<!-- chunk -->

### down food to build an inner fire, which, in turn, radiates outward to

<!-- chunk -->

### keep the organism cleansing and eliminating. The fire of life is visualized

<!-- chunk -->

### in Ayurvedic medicine as a digestive/metabolic flame, situated at the

<!-- chunk -->

### pyloric sphincter, consuming, transforming, and breaking down food and

<!-- chunk -->

### toxins. As one feeds the organism, the other is eliminated. If toxins

<!-- chunk -->

### (called amma in Ayurveda) are retained, the internal flame will be

<!-- chunk -->

### “gummed up” and the digestion and metabolism will be less successful.

<!-- chunk -->

### This leads to the generation of disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### Very much the same view exists in the traditional American herbalism

<!-- chunk -->

### descending down to us from Samuel Thomson (1767–1842), a self-

<!-- chunk -->

### taught New Hampshire herbalist. He used both herbs and a simple sort

<!-- chunk -->

### of sweat lodge to “raise the fire” in the interior (in the stomach, as he

<!-- chunk -->

### visualized it), break down “canker” (mucus) obstructing the fire, and

<!-- chunk -->

### open the skin, so that the fire could radiate outwards, from center to

<!-- chunk -->

### circumference. In this way, the body was nourished and warmed, while

<!-- chunk -->

### the canker or obstructions were eliminated.

<!-- chunk -->

### There can be little doubt that Thomson’s model came from the

<!-- chunk -->

### American Indian people. He used a simple sweat lodge, as much as his

<!-- chunk -->

### “course of medicine” or herbs to raise the fire, cut canker, and open the

<!-- chunk -->

### periphery. His early disciples were known as “steaming doctors.” This

<!-- chunk -->

### method was unknown in central Europe but is well attested on the

<!-- chunk -->

### American frontier. Lewis and Clark describe how one of their men was

<!-- chunk -->

### cured of arthritic stiffness in a sweat lodge erected by friendly Indians

<!-- chunk -->

### during their journey. The sweat lodge itself, with a fire in the middle

<!-- chunk -->

### generating steam from water poured on it, spreading outward towards

<!-- chunk -->

### the perimeter, is almost the perfect picture of the dynamic, energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### organism.

<!-- chunk -->

### In these simple models from throughout the world, water is actually of

<!-- chunk -->

### equal importance with fire. It controls and modulates the activity of fire.

<!-- chunk -->

### In Chinese and European alchemical tradition, the organism is pictured

<!-- chunk -->

### as a three chambered vessel sitting on a fire. In the lower chamber,

<!-- chunk -->

### water is boiling; in the middle chamber it is running down the sides of

<!-- chunk -->

### the vessel in little rivulets; while in the upper chamber, it is steam. This

<!-- chunk -->

### reflects the interaction of fire and water in the body. In traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### Chinese medicine, the thoracic cavity is considered to be the “upper

<!-- chunk -->

### burner” where water is found in the steamy phase. Heart and lungs

<!-- chunk -->

### disperse the aerated blood like a mist through the body. In the “middle

<!-- chunk -->

### burner,” the digestive organs run like rivers full of water and nutriment,

<!-- chunk -->

### past and through the fires of transformation, down to the “lower

<!-- chunk -->

### burner,” where water is held in check and periodically removed.

<!-- chunk -->

### One can see the origins of the “humoral model” of medicine in this

<!-- chunk -->

### picture of the behavior of the body. When the organism is in balance,

<!-- chunk -->

### the “humors” leave on a regular, healthy basis through the channels of

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination, the kidneys, colon, skin, and lungs. When the organism is

<!-- chunk -->

### not healthy, the humors spill over or are retained. The job of the

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional physician or nurse, therefore, is to regulate the activity of the

<!-- chunk -->

### humors.

<!-- chunk -->

### What modern practitioners of holistic medicine call “energetics” is

<!-- chunk -->

### called “humoralism” by historians of medicine. I have, however, chosen

<!-- chunk -->

### the terminology used by practitioners rather than by outsiders.

<!-- chunk -->

### One more aspect of this model requires elucidation. In all of these

<!-- chunk -->

### models there is some concept of a waste product which obstructs the fire

<!-- chunk -->

### within and thickens the internal waters. In Ayurveda this is called amma,

<!-- chunk -->

### a term which incorporates the idea of both thickened phlegm and toxins.

<!-- chunk -->

### In Thomson’s model it was called canker, a colonial American folk

<!-- chunk -->

### medical rendering of what the old English practitioners would have

<!-- chunk -->

### called humor, or humor in the blood. When the fluids of the body are

<!-- chunk -->

### thickened up, they block the internal fire, whether we visualize it in the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach, as Thomson did, or more internally.

<!-- chunk -->

### The terms humor and canker came to be replaced later in the

<!-- chunk -->

### nineteenth century by the concept of “impure blood” or “bad blood.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The former term is a translation from German while the latter is the

<!-- chunk -->

### English phrase typically used by American Indian practitioners,

<!-- chunk -->

### including many I know at the present time. Today this folk medical

<!-- chunk -->

### substance would be called a “toxin.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The concept of toxin is a very important one in Western herbalism. It

<!-- chunk -->

### has been updated with improvements in the understanding of the

<!-- chunk -->

### function of the organism. In the early twentieth century, toxins were

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with the liver, because this was the seat of the preparatory

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism (the preparation of food/toxins coming out of the intestine),

<!-- chunk -->

### and the thyroid, which sets the rate of cellular metabolism. Dr. Broda

<!-- chunk -->

### Barnes (1976) actually equates “bad blood” with hypothyroidism and

<!-- chunk -->

### also mentions that one of the most characteristic symptoms of low

<!-- chunk -->

### thyroid found in autopsies is a thickened, phlegmy substance in the

<!-- chunk -->

### internal fluids composed of mucopolysaccharides—an amma, canker, or

<!-- chunk -->

### humor.

<!-- chunk -->

### The concept of toxins and detoxification has, in the current revival of

<!-- chunk -->

### holistic practice, been almost detached from any kind of diagnostic study

<!-- chunk -->

### of the organism, so that many people simply detoxify themselves,

<!-- chunk -->

### whether there is evidence of toxins in the system or not.

<!-- chunk -->

## Energetic Laws

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to a system of classification for energy patterns and a view of

<!-- chunk -->

### the organism as an energetic biological entity, holistic and traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine also has come to recognize certain laws governing the behavior

<!-- chunk -->

### of energy, qi, or vital force. Thus, in Chinese philosophy the concept of

<!-- chunk -->

### qi is intimately associated with li, or law. In the West, these laws or

<!-- chunk -->

### principles have been particularly developed. From Galen to the present,

<!-- chunk -->

### much has been made of therapeutic laws by which the medicinal agent

<!-- chunk -->

### is applied to the sick organism. Thus, Galen supported the “law of

<!-- chunk -->

### contraries” while Paracelsus and Hahnemann rebelled against him and

<!-- chunk -->

### advocated the use of “similars.” Dr. Harold Lindlahr, the “philosopher of

<!-- chunk -->

### naturopathy,” saw the two laws as coming under one heading: the “law

<!-- chunk -->

### of action and reaction.” A similar unified doctrine, the Arndt/Schulz law,

<!-- chunk -->

### states that every biological action creates an equal and opposite reaction;

<!-- chunk -->

### therefore, both contraries and similar restore the organism to its original

<!-- chunk -->

### state.

<!-- chunk -->

### The law of contraries teaches that if the organism has gotten too cold,

<!-- chunk -->

### it will be restored to balance by warming agents. Thus, from the time of

<!-- chunk -->

### Galen to the present, “carminatives” or warming, aromatic herbs and

<!-- chunk -->

### spices are used to “warm up” a “cold stomach,” i.e., a digestive tract in

<!-- chunk -->

### which the secretions are deficient. Carminatives are pungent and bitter,

<!-- chunk -->

### so they stimulate, warm, and excite secretions, from the mouth on down

<!-- chunk -->

### the gastrointestinal canal.

<!-- chunk -->

### The law of similars was originally based on the “doctrine of

<!-- chunk -->

### signatures,” i.e., the idea that the plant looks like or resembles in some

<!-- chunk -->

### way the disease, organ, or symptom. This is considered by modern

<!-- chunk -->

### science, as it was by Galen himself, to be a fallacious concept based on

<!-- chunk -->

### “magical thinking,” or thinking by analogy. Rudolf Steiner, on the other

<!-- chunk -->

### hand, defended the use of signatures, stating that the same power that

<!-- chunk -->

### made the color, shape, or habit, in a plant created a disease imbalance in

<!-- chunk -->

### the human organism, and thus the one would treat the other. There is

<!-- chunk -->

### power in analogy, when the two similars are related by essential,

<!-- chunk -->

### internal characteristics, rather than simple, external resemblance.

<!-- chunk -->

### Emmanuel Swedenborg differentiated between these two as

<!-- chunk -->

### “correspondents” and “representations.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Samuel Hahnemann, the founder of homeopathy, introduced the use

<!-- chunk -->

### of the law of similars on a different basis. He noted that the symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### caused by a poison were those which would be cured by the same agent

<!-- chunk -->

### used in a smaller dose. His thinking originated at the same time as the

<!-- chunk -->

### smallpox vaccination was being developed by Edward Jenner and

<!-- chunk -->

### represents a vitalistic interpretation of the fact that the body is

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulated by a similar to fight against the original contagion.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hahnemann’s followers have also used the “doctrine of signatures.”

<!-- chunk -->

### According to homeopathic doctrine, foods work upon the system by

<!-- chunk -->

### contrary while poisons cure by similarity. Thus, if the organism is short

<!-- chunk -->

### on iron it needs to consume iron in some friendly food. However, if there

<!-- chunk -->

### is a sickness in the organism that causes it to reject, or eliminate, or not

<!-- chunk -->

### pick up iron, then a remedy is needed which operates by similar, one

<!-- chunk -->

### which creates the same imbalance in the healthy.

<!-- chunk -->

### In reality, as Lindlahr, Arndt, and Schulz pointed out, the organism is

<!-- chunk -->

### cured both by contraries and by similars. The important point is that

<!-- chunk -->

### sickness develops along an axis—too hot, too cold—and is fixed along

<!-- chunk -->

### the same axis. If the cooling agent is applied to fever in a way that helps

<!-- chunk -->

### the struggling organism, it will strengthen the organism to remove fever.

<!-- chunk -->

### On the other hand, if the similar causing the fever is administered in a

<!-- chunk -->

### small dose, it will stimulate the body to renew and reinvigorate the fight

<!-- chunk -->

### against the pathogenic factor, strengthening the powers of resistance and

<!-- chunk -->

### leading to cure. What is most important is that the cure is in the same

<!-- chunk -->

### axis as the disease. Suppression occurs when the organism is forced to

<!-- chunk -->

### act in a certain way, according to artificial, external agents, which hide

<!-- chunk -->

### the disease process and symptoms. This, however, does not cure, but sets

<!-- chunk -->

### up a new disease. As mentioned previously, this is the difference

<!-- chunk -->

### between holistic healing and conventional biomedicine: the one sees the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism as able to return to self-governance while the other does not

<!-- chunk -->

### believe in this self-cure and therefore seeks to “manage” health for the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are three primal laws of energetic healing: the law of action and

<!-- chunk -->

### reaction (or contraries and similars), the law of direction of cure

<!-- chunk -->

### (Hering’s law), and the law of the healing crisis. These themes will be

<!-- chunk -->

### more fully developed throughout our work in The Practice of Traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### Western Herbalism.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Medicine Wheel

<!-- chunk -->

### Before closing, let us return to the theme of the four or five elements.

<!-- chunk -->

### Throughout the world, the four directions or quarters are a potent

<!-- chunk -->

### symbol. C.G. Jung observed that the mandala, a cross or square picturing

<!-- chunk -->

### the four directions, represents for the human soul a feeling of wholeness,

<!-- chunk -->

### completeness, and well-being. This gives a different dimension to the

<!-- chunk -->

### four qualities and elements. Not only do they provide an analytical

<!-- chunk -->

### method for classifying patterns in nature, but also they represent

<!-- chunk -->

### wholeness and well-being. They are, in and of themselves, healing.

<!-- chunk -->

### Jung taught that the four directions represented, among other things,

<!-- chunk -->

### the four functions of the psyche: intuition (sensing the whole picture),

<!-- chunk -->

### thinking (mentation), feeling (emotionality), and sensation (body

<!-- chunk -->

### knowledge). Each person favors one of these functions, but together they

<!-- chunk -->

### represent wholeness.

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to the four functions, Jung taught the existence of a fifth

<!-- chunk -->

### or “transcendent” function, which came into operation when a person

<!-- chunk -->

### was stuck and their ordinary inventory of knowledge was insufficient to

<!-- chunk -->

### solve their problems. The transcendent function first appears as the

<!-- chunk -->

### helpful dream or daydream. With practice, the imagination can be

<!-- chunk -->

### accessed for solutions more easily. Ultimately, the transcendent function

<!-- chunk -->

### is essentially the active imagination. Jung saw this function as

<!-- chunk -->

### corresponding to the “fifth element” or quintessence that both moves and

<!-- chunk -->

### stands outside the four directions or elements in mytho-poetic culture. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is often referred to as the wind, breath, or ether in traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### symbolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rudolf Steiner used a fourfold classification system similar to the

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional Greek method. He associated the “I-organization” or self-

<!-- chunk -->

### consciousness with the fire element; the “astral body” or animal

<!-- chunk -->

### instincts/autonomic nervous system with air; the “etheric body” or

<!-- chunk -->

### vegetative/cellular functions with water; and the physical body or

<!-- chunk -->

### mineral world with earth. For Steiner, warming plants tend to increase

<!-- chunk -->

### sensation and consciousness by stimulating circulation and nerve

<!-- chunk -->

### sensation. (Compare Robert Schmidt’s description of heat in the four

<!-- chunk -->

### qualities model as individualizing or separating).

<!-- chunk -->

### We see this in the mint family, which is largely warming and

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulating. Rosemary was perhaps the archetypal fiery mint used by

<!-- chunk -->

### Steiner to increase circulation, nervous sensation, and consciousness. He

<!-- chunk -->

### would express this by saying that the “I-organization” exerted its

<!-- chunk -->

### influence from the mind down through the bodily pole, though it would

<!-- chunk -->

### be simpler to say that the warmth or fire element is extended into bodily

<!-- chunk -->

### functions through innervation and circulation. By comparison, the

<!-- chunk -->

### cooling plants, of which the rose family is a prime example, tend to

<!-- chunk -->

### refine and articulate perception and experience.

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to his occult training and his life-long study of the

<!-- chunk -->

### alternative science of J. W. Goethe, Steiner studied under an herbalist.

<!-- chunk -->

### They met one day on a crowded train. Steiner was impressed with the

<!-- chunk -->

### wisdom of the older man and thereafter often traveled to study with

<!-- chunk -->

### him. It is therefore not surprising that Steiner’s work is filled with a

<!-- chunk -->

### considerable knowledge of the medicinal properties of plants.

<!-- chunk -->

### In many Native American cultures, the circle with four directions

<!-- chunk -->

### represents healing. This is called the “sacred hoop” or “medicine wheel.”

<!-- chunk -->

### It is thus used to organize medical knowledge. J.T. Garrett in The

<!-- chunk -->

### Cherokee Herbal (2003) gives an excellent description of the classification

<!-- chunk -->

### of medicinal plants by the four directions. The east relates to the heart

<!-- chunk -->

### and family life, heart remedies, and blood remedies. The south relates to

<!-- chunk -->

### acute injuries and diseases brought on by exposure to the dangers of the

<!-- chunk -->

### world. The west relates to internal diseases. The north relates to stored

<!-- chunk -->

### knowledge, wisdom, teaching, peace, and being in harmony with the

<!-- chunk -->

### world.

<!-- chunk -->

### This way of defining the directions corresponds closely with the

<!-- chunk -->

### directions as described independently by my friend Paul Red Elk, who

<!-- chunk -->

### received traditional herbal training from his Lakota grandfather in

<!-- chunk -->

### western South Dakota.

<!-- chunk -->

### The point should be made that the four directions can be defined in

<!-- chunk -->

### many different ways and the medicine wheel itself can be arranged in

<!-- chunk -->

### even more variety. Even the correlations between the values of the four

<!-- chunk -->

### elements or directions are variable to a certain extent. For instance,

<!-- chunk -->

### Aristotle correlated the four qualities to the four elements as: fire (hot

<!-- chunk -->

### and dry), air (hot and damp), water (cold and damp), earth (cold and

<!-- chunk -->

### dry). They have sometimes been arranged in a different fashion that I

<!-- chunk -->

### personally find more accurate: fire (hot), air (dry), water (damp), and

<!-- chunk -->

### earth (cold).

<!-- chunk -->

### Karyn Sanders, a Native American practitioner living in California,

<!-- chunk -->

### first introduced me to the herbal medicine wheel. She emphasized heat

<!-- chunk -->

### and cold rather than the different segments of life. I have followed her

<!-- chunk -->

### example, modifying it according to my experience.

<!-- chunk -->

### It should be remembered that the medicine wheel is based on a

<!-- chunk -->

### personal and subjective experience of the world and that there is no true

<!-- chunk -->

### configuration.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Green Tongue

<!-- chunk -->

### A good background in energetic terminology is excellent for developing

<!-- chunk -->

### the intuition as well as an expressive vocabulary. When I was a young

<!-- chunk -->

### man, I was barely able to speak beyond the rudiments of ordinary

<!-- chunk -->

### conversation. I thought in what I would now call “energetic patterns,”

<!-- chunk -->

### and since this was not addressed in conventional education, I had no

<!-- chunk -->

### terminology in which to express my observations. After learning

<!-- chunk -->

### energetic systems or symbols, I was able to talk to other people versed in

<!-- chunk -->

### the same language. This was an improvement, but I couldn’t speak with

<!-- chunk -->

### anyone else. Eventually I was able to translate my thoughts into regular

<!-- chunk -->

### speech and became known as an excellent speaker.

<!-- chunk -->

### The development of speech is related to one’s ability to perceive, and

<!-- chunk -->

### therefore one’s “take on the world” and spiritual life. Consequently,

<!-- chunk -->

### there are stories in folk tradition and literature about changes in spirit

<!-- chunk -->

### and speech. The ballad of Thomas the Rhymer describes how a

<!-- chunk -->

### sympathetic relationship with Nature results in a gift called the “tongue-

<!-- chunk -->

### that-cannot-lie.” The implications of this relationship and gift are more

<!-- chunk -->

### fully developed in commentaries on this ballad by R. J. Stewart in The

<!-- chunk -->

### Underworld Initiation (1985). Another book on gifted speech as a

<!-- chunk -->

### reflection of understanding natural, social, and spiritual processes is

<!-- chunk -->

### found in Linda Sussman’s The Speech of the Grail (1995). This is a

<!-- chunk -->

### commentary on the epic romance Parzival, by Wolfram von Eschenbach.

<!-- chunk -->

### Perhaps the most beautiful name for this gift can be found in David

<!-- chunk -->

### Ovason’s The Secrets of Nostradamus (2001). This name also serves as a

<!-- chunk -->

### description of the special vocabulary used by the old alchemists and

<!-- chunk -->

### philosophers. Through word play, this language came to be known as

<!-- chunk -->

### the langua vert, or “green tongue,” in contrast to the langua ouvert, or

<!-- chunk -->

### “mundane language” of everyday speech.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the green tongue, the old philosophers could represent discoveries

<!-- chunk -->

### about life, the life force, and the ever-changing lessons provided by

<!-- chunk -->

### Mother Nature. It is said that the alchemists used this tongue to couch

<!-- chunk -->

### their wisdom in obscurity so that profane people could not abuse their

<!-- chunk -->

### knowledge, or, in a time of Inquisition, their persons. However, a more

<!-- chunk -->

### realistic interpretation would stress the fact that they had to develop a

<!-- chunk -->

### poetic, metaphorical, and suggestive/elusive language in which to

<!-- chunk -->

### convey their discoveries.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mystics or scientists have never claimed that Nature is an open book.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the world of material science, it is a object of intense and long study.

<!-- chunk -->

### Likewise, it is the same when this grand subject of inquiry is examined

<!-- chunk -->

### through the inner eye, intuition, and instinct. The green tongue is not a

<!-- chunk -->

### language to hide, but one that gives expression to that which is hidden

<!-- chunk -->

### and which should, when properly used, evoke understanding from the

<!-- chunk -->

### listening. In bringing understanding from one level to another, speech

<!-- chunk -->

### becomes spiritual, and magical.

<!-- chunk -->

### The great medieval seer, Hildegard von Bingen, called the power of

<!-- chunk -->

### Nature the viriditas or “green power.” She found it in all living things, in

<!-- chunk -->

### the seed ready to burst forth; in youth and sexuality; in the expression of

<!-- chunk -->

### art and creativity; and in the healing power of the green world. Her life

<!-- chunk -->

### as a leader, visionary, musician, artist, and herbal healer was a

<!-- chunk -->

### manifestation of viriditas. Her speech was not obscure, but rather

<!-- chunk -->

### evocative.

<!-- chunk -->

### Originally, the green tongue did not belong to the alchemists or

<!-- chunk -->

### philosophers of Nature, but to the bards and poets. It is thus that

<!-- chunk -->

### Thomas the Rhymer, after having fallen asleep under a hawthorn tree,

<!-- chunk -->

### met with the green clad Queen of Elfland. She bound him to her service,

<!-- chunk -->

### and finding him obedient, rewarded True Thomas with green slippers

<!-- chunk -->

### and “the tongue-that-cannot-lie.”

<!-- chunk -->

### I used to think that the tongue-that-cannot-lie referred to a speaker

<!-- chunk -->

### who could never tell a lie, like the young George Washington, when he

<!-- chunk -->

### chopped down his father’s tree. Later I came to realize that Thomas the

<!-- chunk -->

### Rhymer was a poet and a bard, and that his gift gave him the ability to

<!-- chunk -->

### describe the Green World, the Elfland of the Elven Queen, the viriditas. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is therefore the tongue of internal truth and clear seeing, not the tongue

<!-- chunk -->

### of outward truth or legal exactitude. It is the tongue of art and it belongs

<!-- chunk -->

### to all artists, no matter what trade they may ply. It belongs to those who

<!-- chunk -->

### have served their apprenticeship in obedience to Dame Nature. Art does

<!-- chunk -->

### not come from commandments, or follow logic or the consensus opinion

<!-- chunk -->

### of scientific experts, but springs fresh every time from stone and wood

<!-- chunk -->

### and green sprig of earth. It is the stuff that makes good gardeners,

<!-- chunk -->

### hunters, farmers, builders, carpenters, poets, bards, sculptors, painters,

<!-- chunk -->

### drawers, healers, physicians, and herbalists.


<!-- chunk -->

### They would suppose that there is some principle harmful to man;

<!-- chunk -->

### heat or cold, wetness or dryness, and that the right way to bring

<!-- chunk -->

### about cures is to correct cold with warmth, or dryness with

<!-- chunk -->

### moisture and so on.… These are the causes of disease, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy lies in the application of the opposite principle according to

<!-- chunk -->

### the hypothesis.

<!-- chunk -->

### —H

IPPOCRATES

<!-- chunk -->

### he origins of the four elements lie far back in the mists of time,

<!-- chunk -->

### but it was Empedocles who introduced them into Greek

<!-- chunk -->

### philosophy and argued for them as the basis of the world. Plato

<!-- chunk -->

### lent his weight to this view but Aristotle felt that the four elements were

<!-- chunk -->

### derivative. He postulated the four qualities from which they were

<!-- chunk -->

### derived. Thus, fire is hot and dry, air is hot and moist, water is moist and

<!-- chunk -->

### cold, and earth is dry and cold. The four elements subsequently became

<!-- chunk -->

### more of a philosophical or mythological system, while the four qualities

<!-- chunk -->

### were used in the science and medicine of antiquity to describe the

<!-- chunk -->

### attributes of stones, plants, animals, and people.

<!-- chunk -->

### The Hippocratic authors were developing their philosophy at the same

<!-- chunk -->

### time this theory appeared (see quotation at the head of the chapter).

<!-- chunk -->

### However, they disapproved of the four qualities and instead taught a

<!-- chunk -->

### system which depended upon a fasting diet composed of bland foods. By

<!-- chunk -->

### shielding the organism from food which was stressful (too hot, too cold)

<!-- chunk -->

### they hoped to cleanse the body and release toxins during a healing

<!-- chunk -->

### “crisis.” Eventually, Greek medicine adopted both the detoxification fast

<!-- chunk -->

### of the Hippocratic doctors and the four qualities of Aristotle.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Four Qualities

<!-- chunk -->

### To the modern ear, the four qualities appear to be fairly clear-cut.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, the passage of time has altered the way we think about these

<!-- chunk -->

### terms compared to how the ancients defined them. Lacking a

<!-- chunk -->

### thermometer, they had no way of actually measuring hot or cold.

<!-- chunk -->

### Therefore, they did not define temperature as we would today. Under

<!-- chunk -->

### the circumstances, it was more objective to define the four qualities by

<!-- chunk -->

### their effects.

<!-- chunk -->

### Robert Schmidt, a Greek scholar and translator, of Project Hindsight,

<!-- chunk -->

### in Cumberland, Maryland, helped me define the four qualities according

<!-- chunk -->

### to the ancient concept. The quotations are from Schmidt.

<!-- chunk -->

### Heat is the “active power that takes away, destroys, or eliminates

<!-- chunk -->

### what has a foreign or alien nature, so that the residue is more rarefied or

<!-- chunk -->

### porous, thereby allowing things that are of the same class to become

<!-- chunk -->

### more associated.” At the same time, things that are dissimilar are

<!-- chunk -->

### removed. Heat possesses the power of purification and refinement that

<!-- chunk -->

### intensifies the native identity.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cold is the “active power that aggregates, condenses, or packs things

<!-- chunk -->

### more closely together, whether they are of the same or different class; it

<!-- chunk -->

### is a compressive and cohesive power.” It can bind together properties

<!-- chunk -->

### that are opposite and incompatible. Even if similar in nature they are

<!-- chunk -->

### brought together in a way which is not comfortable for them.

<!-- chunk -->

### Damp is defined as the “passive capacity” which allows for pliancy,

<!-- chunk -->

### bendability, and coercibility. Because it is completely coercible, it can

<!-- chunk -->

### easily be “contained or confined by external limits or boundaries.”

<!-- chunk -->

### However, by itself it will not remain within these boundaries. It requires

<!-- chunk -->

### another power to keep it there. Spreading in all directions available to it,

<!-- chunk -->

### damp does not stop until it meets a boundary, where it collects. If no

<!-- chunk -->

### boundary appears, it flows away. Damp lacks the capacity for the

<!-- chunk -->

### “creation of pores or empty spaces” so it collects substances dissimilar to

<!-- chunk -->

### its nature and to each other.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dry is the “passive capacity” that is not pliable but incoercible. It sets

<!-- chunk -->

### boundaries and limitations. “Through its capacity for self-limitation or

<!-- chunk -->

### self-articulation, it admits of the creation of pores or empty spaces.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Thus, entities similar or dissimilar can pass through it without changing

<!-- chunk -->

### its nature. Because it is incoercible, dryness allows for the creation of

<!-- chunk -->

### structure and function.

<!-- chunk -->

### These definitions are quite practical and mechanical. They are not

<!-- chunk -->

### mythological or esoteric. All natural substances are rarified or dense,

<!-- chunk -->

### pliable or rigid. There is nothing inherently unscientific about this

<!-- chunk -->

### analytical method—it is just not how we are encouraged to think at the

<!-- chunk -->

### present time. The modern mind looks for ultimate particles and thinks

<!-- chunk -->

### that it has isolated something more true and useful than a mere pattern.

<!-- chunk -->

### The four qualities are further subdivided into the four degrees. These

<!-- chunk -->

### do not refer to degrees of temperature as on a thermometer the way we

<!-- chunk -->

### would think about it today. They represent distinct categories of action,

<!-- chunk -->

### increasing in intensity from mild to strong. Classification of an herb by

<!-- chunk -->

### temperature and degree does not necessary reflect the actual taste or

<!-- chunk -->

### sensation produced on the tongue by this herb. A plant that actually

<!-- chunk -->

### tastes hot may not have a hot medical value. It is the action or property

<!-- chunk -->

### of the plant, not the taste or sensation, which determines whether or not

<!-- chunk -->

### it is hot. Ultimately, the qualities are actually abstractions of the

<!-- chunk -->

### everyday experience of hot, cold, damp, and dry. This discrepancy must

<!-- chunk -->

### have led to confusion and contributed to the downfall of Greek

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Four Degrees

<!-- chunk -->

### The following are the chief actions of herbal remedies classified by

<!-- chunk -->

### quality and degree (Nicholas Culpeper, A Key to Galen’s Method of

<!-- chunk -->

### Physick, reprint 1990, 376).

<!-- chunk -->

## Hot

<!-- chunk -->

## • First degree. Opens pores to expel moisture by perspiration or other

<!-- chunk -->

### channels.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Second degree. Thins fluids so that they can better pass through

<!-- chunk -->

### pores and channels.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Third degree. Increases the internal heat of digestion and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism, so that the body stays warm and the perspiration is

<!-- chunk -->

### driven outward toward the surface. (Note the relationship to

<!-- chunk -->

### Aristotle’s idea of heat being rarifying and making things more

<!-- chunk -->

### porous).

<!-- chunk -->

## • Fourth degree. Burns the skin. These agents are used externally to

<!-- chunk -->

### awaken organs and functions that are blocked or inactive (cold,

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed) and to burn away tumors.

<!-- chunk -->

## Cold

<!-- chunk -->

## • First degree. Lessen heat from exposure to the sun. Salad greens are

<!-- chunk -->

### considered cold in the first degree.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Second degree. Thickens fluids and reduces internal heat.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Third degree. Restrains the outward flow of fluids and matters.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Fourth degree. Prevents “vapors” from rising upwards. Vapors are

<!-- chunk -->

### analogous to “rebellious qi” in traditional Chinese medicine. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### energy that is rising upward against the flow of nature. They include

<!-- chunk -->

### excessive coughing, vomiting, and menstrual cramps. Dizziness,

<!-- chunk -->

### fainting, swooning, hysteria, and PMS can be caused by vapors.

<!-- chunk -->

### Agents cold in the fourth degree stupefy the senses to ease violent

<!-- chunk -->

### pain and reduce mania, nervousness, restlessness, and insomnia.

<!-- chunk -->

## Damp

<!-- chunk -->

## • First degree. Causes substances to move through internal passages.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Second degree. Moistens and lubricates surfaces.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Third degree. Reduces hardness.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Fourth degree. Nourishes the flesh.

<!-- chunk -->

## Dry

<!-- chunk -->

## • First degree. Restrains the outflow of fluid when it is too extreme.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Second degree. Removes excess fluids when there is too much

<!-- chunk -->

### dampness.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Third degree. “Comforts and strengthens nature.” This phrase means

<!-- chunk -->

### that they protect the surface against wind and cold by closing the

<!-- chunk -->

### pores. Open pores cause sweating and chilling.

<!-- chunk -->

## • Fourth degree. Stops continuous, life threatening discharges, as in

<!-- chunk -->

### tuberculosis and dysentery.

<!-- chunk -->

## Temperate

<!-- chunk -->

### Remedies that are temperate (equal with regard to one of the two

<!-- chunk -->

### polarities: hot/cold or damp/dry) help to maintain the body in a

<!-- chunk -->

### condition where there is no apparent excess. They are not graded

<!-- chunk -->

### by degree. Remember that the Hippocratic physicians sought out

<!-- chunk -->

### bland substances. The temperate degree is virtually the opposite

<!-- chunk -->

### of wind.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here are some examples of the classification of plants by the four

<!-- chunk -->

### qualities taken from the pages of William Salmon’s English Herbal (1709).

<!-- chunk -->

### (I have eliminated the excessive use of capitalization which otherwise

<!-- chunk -->

### makes Salmon difficult to read, but have otherwise presented the

<!-- chunk -->

### material in the idiom of its time).

<!-- chunk -->

## Hot and Dry

<!-- chunk -->

## Nettle (Urtica dioeca). Hot and dry in the first degree. “It does not burn

<!-- chunk -->

### and sting, because of its extream heat, but because its downy or small

<!-- chunk -->

### prickles are hard and stiff.” This illustrates that “heat” is not the same

<!-- chunk -->

### thing as heat, as we would experience it, but is rather a category of

<!-- chunk -->

### action. Here it means “stimulating.” Nettle juice “taken inwardly to three

<!-- chunk -->

### or four ounces or more, according to age, it will make the belly soluble;

<!-- chunk -->

### it opens obstructions of the stomach and lungs; helps wheezing and

<!-- chunk -->

### shortness of breath; it provokes the term, allays vapors, and prevents

<!-- chunk -->

### hysterick fits; it provokes urine and expels sand, gravel, and tartarous

<!-- chunk -->

### matter out of the reins and bladder. It kills worms in children, eases and

<!-- chunk -->

### takes away stitches in the side, removes the oppilation of the spleen

<!-- chunk -->

### expelling the windiness therof; and is said also to provoke to venery;

<!-- chunk -->

### being taken for two or three days together it stops spitting or vomiting

<!-- chunk -->

### blood, coming from the lungs or stomach; being put into green wounds

<!-- chunk -->

### or old sores, it cleanses them and induces their healing.” The ancients

<!-- chunk -->

### seem not to have valued the nutritive qualities of nettle, which would

<!-- chunk -->

### make it moist rather than dry.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mugwort (Artemisia vulgaris). Hot and dry in the second degree. “It is

<!-- chunk -->

### prevalent against vapors and fits of the mother [uterus]: it powerfully

<!-- chunk -->

### provokes the terms, brings away both birth and afterbirth, and expels

<!-- chunk -->

### the dead child; it is good against the palsie, as also cramps and

<!-- chunk -->

### convulsions, it cures the strangury, as also the poyson of the black

<!-- chunk -->

### poppy.” The properties mentioned are predominately heating.

<!-- chunk -->

## Catnip (Nepeta cataria). Hot and dry in the third degree. “It provokes

<!-- chunk -->

### the terms in women, hastens and facilitates the labor, stops catarrhs and

<!-- chunk -->

### cold thin rheums, expels wind, gives ease in the colick, cures asthmas of

<!-- chunk -->

### difficulty breathing, as also cramps, convulsions, and all cold and moist

<!-- chunk -->

### disaffections of the nerves.” Most of the mints are classified as hot and

<!-- chunk -->

### dry in the third degree.

<!-- chunk -->

## Rosemary (Rosmarinus officinalis). Hot and dry in the third degree. “It

<!-- chunk -->

### prevails against the head-ach and megrim, from a cold cause; the

<!-- chunk -->

### vertigo, lethargy, epilepsy, apoplexy, palsy, convulsions, catarrhs,

<!-- chunk -->

### drowsiness, dullness of the senses, loss of memory, coldness of the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach, stinking breath, wind in the stomach and bowels, dimness of

<!-- chunk -->

### the sight, jaundice, obstructions of the terms, gout from a cold cause, all

<!-- chunk -->

### external pains and aches from a cold and moist cause, lameness,

<!-- chunk -->

### numbedness, weakness of the joints; and in contagious times, prevails

<!-- chunk -->

### even against the plague it self and all contagious and infectious

<!-- chunk -->

### diseases.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Mustard (Sinapis alba). The seed is hot and dry in the fourth degree—

<!-- chunk -->

### i.e., it will burn the skin. “It is found by experience to be of especial use,

<!-- chunk -->

### to provoke the terms in women, provoke urine, and give ease in the

<!-- chunk -->

### strangury, stone and gravel: it cures hysterick fits, pains of the sides and

<!-- chunk -->

### spleen, tooth-ach, kings evil, gout, cramp, and convulsions of the bowels;

<!-- chunk -->

### and prevails also against agues, epilepsy, lethargy, carus, vertigo, expels

<!-- chunk -->

### poison of all sorts, is good against impotency in men, and removes

<!-- chunk -->

### scabbiness, and other defedations of the skin.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Hot and Moist

<!-- chunk -->

## Madonna Lily (Lilium candidum). “ ’Tis the roots of Lillies which are

<!-- chunk -->

### chiefly used; but both roots and flowers are hot and moist in the first

<!-- chunk -->

### degree.” The juice of the root, “stamped and strained out with wine (for

<!-- chunk -->

### it is of too slimy a substance of itself) and given to drink for some few

<!-- chunk -->

### days together, it expels the poyson and malignity of the plague or

<!-- chunk -->

### pestilence, and causes it to break forth into blisters on the outward parts

<!-- chunk -->

### of the skin, as has been found by Experience.” The flowers extracted in

<!-- chunk -->

### olive oil in the sunlight, or gently warmed, make an oil which is

<!-- chunk -->

### “emollient, and softens the hardness of the nerves, and extends sinews

<!-- chunk -->

### shrunk; it also softens the hardness of the womb.” The roots are applied

<!-- chunk -->

### to “tumors and apostems of the privy parts.” I have used a tincture of

<!-- chunk -->

### Easter lily flowers to cure cysts of the ovaries and breasts dozens of

<!-- chunk -->

### times when I have been unable to obtain Madonna lily. One would think

<!-- chunk -->

### this plant cooling and moist, but its action on putrid heat shows that it is

<!-- chunk -->

### more warming or stimulating.

<!-- chunk -->

## Cold and Dry

<!-- chunk -->

## Raspberry (Rubus strigosus). “The roots, stalks, leaves, flowers, and

<!-- chunk -->

### unripe fruit are cold and drying in the first degree.… They stop fluxes of

<!-- chunk -->

### the belly, and bleeding in the inward parts; are good against the

<!-- chunk -->

### overflowing of the courses in women; they cure sores and ulcers of the

<!-- chunk -->

### throat, mouth, and other parts, are profitable against the bitings of

<!-- chunk -->

### serpents, and cure the stomacace, or scurvy, being used according to art,

<!-- chunk -->

### for some considerable time.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Plantain (Plantago spp.) The leaves are cold and dry in the end of the

<!-- chunk -->

### first degree. “It is dry without biting, and cold without benumming.” It

<!-- chunk -->

### “heals the excoriations of the stomach and other bowels, stops catarrhs,

<!-- chunk -->

### and all sorts of fluxes in man or woman. It stops also all sorts of

<!-- chunk -->
