### haemorrhages whether internal or external, the overflowing of the

<!-- chunk -->

### courses in women, spitting and pissing of blood, and therefore is of

<!-- chunk -->

### excellent use in the curing of a phthisis [tuberculosis] or ulceration of

<!-- chunk -->

### the lungs.” Today plantain is classified as a mucilage, thus moistening

<!-- chunk -->

### rather than drying.

<!-- chunk -->

## Rose (Rosa gallica). “Both the white and red are cooling and drying, but

<!-- chunk -->

### the white is taken to exceed the red in both these properties.” They are

<!-- chunk -->

### cold in the first degree and drying in the second. “The white and red,

<!-- chunk -->

### stop fluxes of the bowels, and catarrhs, and vulnerary, drying and

<!-- chunk -->

### healing, good against consumptions and ulcers of the lungs, as also

<!-- chunk -->

### internal ulcers of the other viscera, as of the stomach, gutts, reins,

<!-- chunk -->

### bladder, and womb, comfort the head, brain, and nerves, and are good

<!-- chunk -->

### against diseases afflicting those parts, proceeding from cold and

<!-- chunk -->

### moisture.” The wild roses (Rosa canina) “are much the same” in virtue.

<!-- chunk -->

## Cold and Moist

<!-- chunk -->

## Blue violet (Viola odorata). Cool and moist in the first degree. “Violets

<!-- chunk -->

### cool the heat of fevers, allay inflammations in the throat and lungs, are

<!-- chunk -->

### profitable in the beginning of a quinsie and pleurisie, and are said to

<!-- chunk -->

### help the falling-sickness in children.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Wild Lettuce (Lactuca virosa). “Wild Lettice is cold in the end of the

<!-- chunk -->

### second degree, and moist in the end of the first. It has nearly the same

<!-- chunk -->

### Properties of Cooling which the Garden Kind has; but the bitterness of

<!-- chunk -->

### this makes it the more aperitive; and yet it is more somniferous than the

<!-- chunk -->

### manured sorts, and comes up, as it is thought by some, almost to the

<!-- chunk -->

### degree of Opium; but this Opinion I can never assent to, my Experience

<!-- chunk -->

### having proved the contrary.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Watermelon (Cucurbita citrullus). The fruit and the seed are cold and

<!-- chunk -->

### moist in the second degree, “the seeds being reckoned one of the four

<!-- chunk -->

### cold seeds.” The fruit is “usually eaten raw, being pared from the rind

<!-- chunk -->

### and cut in slices or pieces. It cools and refrigerates admirably, quenches

<!-- chunk -->

### thirst, refreshes the spirits, abates the heat of the most burning and

<!-- chunk -->

### malign fevers, cleanses the bowels, provokes urines, takes away the heat

<!-- chunk -->

### and scalding thereof, and gives ease in the strangury.” The seed “takes

<!-- chunk -->

### away the febritick distemper of the blood; it gives ease in the strangury,

<!-- chunk -->

### abates the heart and scalding of the urine, and cures pissing of blood,

<!-- chunk -->

### and represses lust (being used for some time) in an admirable manner.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Dry

<!-- chunk -->

## Meadowsweet (Filipendula ulmaria). Dry in the first degree, but

<!-- chunk -->

### temperate with respect to hot or cold. “It stops bleeding, and cures fluxes

<!-- chunk -->

### of the bowels of all sorts, stops the whites and terms in women, and

<!-- chunk -->

### gives ease in the colick and gripings of the guts. And heals wounds and

<!-- chunk -->

### old ulcers.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Yellow Dock (Rumex crispus). Dry in the second degree and temperate

<!-- chunk -->

### with regard to hot or cold. “The whole plant is very astringent and

<!-- chunk -->

### abstersive, with a kind of opening faculty; and is traumatick or vulnerary

<!-- chunk -->

### and hepatick.” As a result, “it is peculiar for curing all fluxes of the

<!-- chunk -->

### bowels, chiefly the bloody-flux,” i.e., dysentery. The juice of the root

<!-- chunk -->

### “opens obstructions of the liver and spleen, cleanses and strengthens the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach and other viscera, stops a diarrhea and lienteria, and heals the

<!-- chunk -->

### corrosion of the guts in a dysenteria, strengthening them admirably, and

<!-- chunk -->

### prevails against the jaundice after a singular manner.” The old authors

<!-- chunk -->

### emphasize the use of yellow dock root in diarrhea, while the new

<!-- chunk -->

### emphasize it as a mild laxative because it contains some anthroquinones.

<!-- chunk -->

### I agree with the old authors here; I also think yellow dock is cooling.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tormentil (Potentilla tormentilla). Dry in the third degree and slightly

<!-- chunk -->

### warm, not quite approaching the first degree. The other cinquefoils are

<!-- chunk -->

### classified as dry in the third degree and temperate with regard to hot

<!-- chunk -->

### and cold. “It stops all fluxes of blood or humors in man or woman,

<!-- chunk -->

### whether at nose, mouth, belly, or any wound in any part; resists all

<!-- chunk -->

### poison, and the plague or pestilence it self, and all pestilential diseases,

<!-- chunk -->

### and such as are infectious, as the measles, smallpox, purples, spotted

<!-- chunk -->

### fever, by expelling thro’ a gentle diaphoresis, the malignity from the

<!-- chunk -->

### heart.” The primary property is astringent, or drying, but the actions

<!-- chunk -->

### cited would suggest it is also warming.

<!-- chunk -->

## Moist

<!-- chunk -->

## Marshmallow (Althea officinalis). Moist in the first degree, temperate

<!-- chunk -->

### with respect to hot or cold. “They expel tartarous mucilage out of the

<!-- chunk -->

### breast and lungs, as also from the guts, reins, and womb; are profitable

<!-- chunk -->

### against the bloody flux; and prevails against the stone and gravel,

<!-- chunk -->

### whether in the reins, ureters, or bladder.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Temperate

<!-- chunk -->

## Self Heal (Prunella vulgaris). “Temperate in respect to heat or coldness,

<!-- chunk -->

### dryness or moisture, yet rather enclining to dryness.” The ointment “is

<!-- chunk -->

### good for all sorts of simple contusions, hurts, sores, wounds, and ulcers,

<!-- chunk -->

### in any parts of the body. It also helps to consolidate broken bones; and

<!-- chunk -->

### strengthens and confirm such as have been lately out of Joint, and newly

<!-- chunk -->

### reduced. It heals also sores and ulcers, in the secret parts of man or

<!-- chunk -->

### woman.” The leaves worked up with a knife quickly form a mucilage, so

<!-- chunk -->

### it is surprising that this remedy is classified as dry. The idea is probably

<!-- chunk -->

### that it is drying because it stops bleeding.

<!-- chunk -->

## Sweet Woodruff (Asperula spp.) “Woodroof is temperate in respect of

<!-- chunk -->

### heat or cold, dryness or moisture; and is very like in Qualities to our

<!-- chunk -->

### Ladies-Bedstraw.” It will “chear the heart, resist melancholy, make a

<!-- chunk -->

### man lightsom and pleasant, and induce the curing of wounds.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Remember that temperate remedies bring things back to the norm.

<!-- chunk -->

### The classification of the plants according to the four qualities often

<!-- chunk -->

### agrees with modern ideas about their use, but there are sometimes areas

<!-- chunk -->

### of disagreement. For instance, mucilages, which we would consider

<!-- chunk -->

### moistening, are classified as drying. This is because they glue together

<!-- chunk -->

### the lips of a wound and prevent bleeding or fluid loss. Thus, comfrey

<!-- chunk -->

### and plantain are considered to be drying when we would consider them

<!-- chunk -->

### to be mucilaginous and moistening. Oily plants are not valued for their

<!-- chunk -->

### moistening or nourishing effects at all. Thus, fenugreek and anise are

<!-- chunk -->

### considered hot and dry.

<!-- chunk -->

### The tendency to view remedies which stop bleeding as drying was

<!-- chunk -->

### carried over into nineteenth-century herbalism. Antihemor-rhagics were

<!-- chunk -->

### characteristically classed as astringents. However, there are other ways

<!-- chunk -->

### in which herbs can stop bleeding. Thus, we find fleabane classified as an

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent when it is about the most peppery hot plant native to the

<!-- chunk -->

### fields of North America. Stimulants stop bleeding quickly by opening up

<!-- chunk -->

### the peripheral circulation and taking the blood away from the area of

<!-- chunk -->

### the wound. This is certainly the mode of action for herbs like fleabane,

<!-- chunk -->

### yarrow, cayenne, and shepherd’s purse.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Four Qualities as Pathology

<!-- chunk -->

### The four qualities are used to classify medicinal activity. Herbs are then

<!-- chunk -->

### applied by contrary, or hot to cold. The following definitions of the

<!-- chunk -->

### pathological states to which medicines are applied are somewhat more

<!-- chunk -->

### pragmatic than the abstract approach described above.

<!-- chunk -->

## Hot. Red, swollen, tender, hot tissues; excess of blood to an area,

<!-- chunk -->

### producing redness, heat, and fullness; excessive function, accelerated

<!-- chunk -->

### function, from richness of blood, hence excessive pain from over-

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulation and swelling; pulse rapid and superficial; tongue red,

<!-- chunk -->

### elongated, flame-like. It should be observed that since heat is rarifying

<!-- chunk -->

### and purifying, as a pathological condition it is producing an intense

<!-- chunk -->

### expression of self-identity and is similar to what we would today call

<!-- chunk -->

### auto-immune over-activity.

<!-- chunk -->

## Cold. Tissues cool, flaccid, pale or darkish, inactive, insensate;

<!-- chunk -->

### diminished circulation resulting in pallor or dark complexion from

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnation, cold, and lack of sensation from lack of stimulation;

<!-- chunk -->

### depression of senses and functions; pulse deep, slow; tongue dark red,

<!-- chunk -->

### blue, purple, gray, black or pale. Cold weakens the innate type or

<!-- chunk -->

### identity of the organism, bringing it into contact with forces and

<!-- chunk -->

### substances which are in opposition or unfriendly.

<!-- chunk -->

## Damp. In Greek medicine there are two kinds of dampness. The first

<!-- chunk -->

### corresponds to a state where the tissues are saggy, inelastic, pores open,

<!-- chunk -->

### with active discharge of fluids; pulse relaxed; tongue damp. In the

<!-- chunk -->

### second state the dampness is stuck so that tissues are soggy, flabby,

<!-- chunk -->

### damp, and the fluids are thickened and phlegmy; pulse obscure (from

<!-- chunk -->

### dampness obscuring the beat); tongue coated.

<!-- chunk -->

## Dry. The condition is one of dryness, withering, weakness, and

<!-- chunk -->

### (frequently) pallor from lack of fluids to moisten and bring in food;

<!-- chunk -->

### functions are reduced, energy deficient, tissues dried, withered, and

<!-- chunk -->

### finally hardened; pulse weak, slightly tense; tongue dry. The dry quality

<!-- chunk -->

### produces rigidity and brittleness. In health, dryness creates the

<!-- chunk -->

### opportunity for different functions to occur, since it sets boundaries and

<!-- chunk -->

### limitations, but in disease these functions become weak and ineffective.

<!-- chunk -->

### Practitioners abandoned Greek medicine in the late seventeenth

<!-- chunk -->

### century. It was replaced by the “theory of irritation,” which led to the

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state model of the nineteenth century, described in the following

<!-- chunk -->

### chapter.

# A

<!-- chunk -->

### If the student can be made to understand the principles of scientific

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment of tissue-states … his judgment as to prescribing comes

<!-- chunk -->

### to him as if by intuition, and can scarcely be wrong.

<!-- chunk -->

### —J

OSEPH

<!-- chunk -->

### M. T

HURSTON

<!-- chunk -->

### fter the demise of Greek medicine, Western physicians adopted

<!-- chunk -->

### the “irritation theory.” Founded on the observation that nerves

<!-- chunk -->

### react to stimulation, even after dissection and removal, treatment

<!-- chunk -->

### was based on stimulating or sedating the irritable nature of the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism. This led to the creation of two classes of medicines similar to

<!-- chunk -->

### the classes of heating and cooling in Greek medicine. Thus, practice

<!-- chunk -->

### continued in a similar vein, but based on a different theory. In folk

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine, these two categories were thought of as hot and cold.

<!-- chunk -->

### Samuel Thomson taught that there were three types of disease: hot,

<!-- chunk -->

### cold, and alternating hot and cold. This reflected the fact that many

<!-- chunk -->

### Americans at that time suffered from malaria, which is characterized by

<!-- chunk -->

### alternating chills and fever. Dr. Alvah Curtis, the founder of

<!-- chunk -->

### physiomedicalism or reformed Thomsonianism, translated these three

<!-- chunk -->

### basic imbalances into scientific lingo. He taught that the three basic

<!-- chunk -->

### types of disease were based on the reaction of biological tissue to

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulation. There are three phases: irritation, contraction, and

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the nineteenth century, doctors of all schools used such terms to

<!-- chunk -->

### define basic physiological imbalances. Since the emerging scientific

<!-- chunk -->

### model was not based on mythic elements or humors, no attempt was

<!-- chunk -->

### made to put these terms into an overall classification scheme. Definitions

<!-- chunk -->

### were personal and vague, but adequate for professional communication.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 1900, Dr. Joseph M. Thurston published an ambitious tome called

<!-- chunk -->

### The Philosophy of Physiomedicalism in which he defined six primary

<!-- chunk -->

### physiological imbalances or “tissue states,” as he called them. The terms

<!-- chunk -->

### which he chose to represent these states are excessively complex, so we

<!-- chunk -->

### will bypass them here and use ones which were more characteristic of

<!-- chunk -->

### the medical terminology of all schools at that time: irritation or

<!-- chunk -->

### excitation, constriction, relaxation, atrophy, torpor, and depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### Thurston’s theory survived in a modified form in the United Kingdom.

<!-- chunk -->

### A.W. Priest and L.R. Priest (1982, 31) refer to the “balance of function”

<!-- chunk -->

### between “contraction and relaxation, stimulation and sedation.” They

<!-- chunk -->

### also use the term “atrophy.” This theory, based as it is on actual

<!-- chunk -->

### physiology, is more compatible with the modern perspective, yet it is not

<!-- chunk -->

### incompatible with the ancient. It can serve as a language of energetics

<!-- chunk -->

### and a conceptual bridge. This is the basic system that will be used in The

<!-- chunk -->

### Practice of Traditional Western Herbalism.

<!-- chunk -->

### In order to elaborate the six tissue states, I have drawn from the

<!-- chunk -->

### literature of the great, nineteenth-century botanical doctors, including

<!-- chunk -->

### Samuel Thomson, Wooster Beach, Alvah Curtis, William Cook, Joseph M.

<!-- chunk -->

### Thurston, John M. Scudder, John William Fyfe, and others. I have

<!-- chunk -->

### quoted from them liberally to illustrate some concepts, while at other

<!-- chunk -->

### times I have brought them up to date by referring to more contemporary

<!-- chunk -->

### ideas.

<!-- chunk -->

## Heat • Excitation

<!-- chunk -->

### This tissue state represents an “exaggeration of the normal function-rate”

<!-- chunk -->

### of the tissues (Thurston, 1900, 264). “The condition of the part is one of

<!-- chunk -->

### excitation. The symptoms are very clear—there is swelling, heat,

<!-- chunk -->

### increased sensitiveness, and redness” (Scudder, 1874, 320). These are

<!-- chunk -->

### the classic symptoms of inflammation, but we do not use that term

<!-- chunk -->

### because inflammation can arise from several different tissue states. The

<!-- chunk -->

### term irritation is also used in the old literature but this has been avoided

<!-- chunk -->

### because many things other than heat can irritate tissues. Excitation

<!-- chunk -->

### represents an excess in the innate heat of the tissues. They are red, full,

<!-- chunk -->

### and tender because blood increases cellular activity.

<!-- chunk -->

### Characteristic symptoms of excitation include: temperature rise;

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous excitement; restlessness; wakefulness; excessive movement;

<!-- chunk -->

### susceptibility to stimulation; bright eyes; increased allergic response;

<!-- chunk -->

### heightened sensitivity to pain; sharp pain; fever; redness; swelling of the

<!-- chunk -->

### tissues; engorgement of blood; pink-red tissue color; an elongated, flame-

<!-- chunk -->

### shaped, pink-red or carmine tongue; excited vascular movements;

<!-- chunk -->

### capillary fragility; bleeding; tendencies to increased blood pressure; and

<!-- chunk -->

### a superficial, bounding, or rapid pulse. This tissue state especially

<!-- chunk -->

### damages the heart and circulatory system.

<!-- chunk -->

## Wind • Constriction

<!-- chunk -->

### When nerves are stimulated they contract. If they remain too long in this

<!-- chunk -->

### condition there will be constriction or tension in the organism. This

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state is the result of “abnormal contraction (clonic spasm) of the

<!-- chunk -->

### neuromuscular system” (Thurston, 1900, 267).

<!-- chunk -->

### Constriction may result from psychological or physical tension.

<!-- chunk -->

### Inability to relax and switch from sympathetic (awake, alert) to

<!-- chunk -->

### parasympathetic (rest, digest) is a common cause. However, tension can

<!-- chunk -->

### just as easily arise from physiological stress. When the body is chilled,

<!-- chunk -->

### the hypothalamus signals the sympathetic to start the shivering

<!-- chunk -->

### mechanism in the extremities to warm up the body. It also stands the

<!-- chunk -->

### hairs on end. If these mechanisms get stuck (which is often the case after

<!-- chunk -->

### influenza or malaria), the body remains too much on the sympathetic

<!-- chunk -->

### side or alternates back and forth between tension and relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alternating chills and fever are a characteristic symptom of the tensive

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state. These were called “intermittent chills” in the old literature.

<!-- chunk -->

### Characteristic symptoms of constriction are neuromuscular tension;

<!-- chunk -->

### spasm; rigidity or convulsion; symptom changes which are sudden,

<!-- chunk -->

### intermittent, irregular, or alternating; chills alternating with fever;

<!-- chunk -->

### diarrhea with constipation; pain with freedom from pain; and a pulse

<!-- chunk -->

### wiry, tense, resistant, hard, blocked, or obstructed. Usually, there is

<!-- chunk -->

### aggravation from getting chilled, drinking cold water, or a draft of cold

<!-- chunk -->

### air. Tension especially affects the digestion, gall bladder, and liver.

<!-- chunk -->

## Dry • Atrophy

<!-- chunk -->

### Thurston used this word to indicate a lack of trophism or function. It

<!-- chunk -->

### also applies to an underfed, withered, weak condition. Both usages are

<!-- chunk -->

### correct, since undernourished tissues have less energy to function.

<!-- chunk -->

### Atrophy should also be associated with dryness because fluids carry

<!-- chunk -->

### food. Without them, there is no nutrition. This includes both water and

<!-- chunk -->

### oil, both of which move foodstuffs to the cells and waste products out of

<!-- chunk -->

### them. Well-nourished tissue holds more water and oil.

<!-- chunk -->

### Water is the great medium for transportation within the body. Many

<!-- chunk -->

### substances, however, are not water-soluble but fat-soluble, so the body

<!-- chunk -->

### also requires lipids to allow complete movement and feeding. Several

<!-- chunk -->

### important hormones (thyroxine, aldosterone, cortisone, and sex

<!-- chunk -->

### hormones) are composed of lipids and require lipid carrier cells to travel.

<!-- chunk -->

### Without water and oil, cells dry, harden, and lose their function. Tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### become sclerotic or scarred due to lack of nutrition. Hormonal functions

<!-- chunk -->

### are reduced.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dryness and malnutrition affects the nerves as well as the endocrine

<!-- chunk -->

### system. A common symptom of atrophy is nervous exhaustion.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nineteenth-and early twentieth-century literature would frequently use

<!-- chunk -->

### the words asthenic (thin, weak) or neurasthenic (weak nervous system) to

<!-- chunk -->

### describe this condition. In neurasthenia the person is exhausted from

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulation. They do not have the reserves to recover easily from mental

<!-- chunk -->

### or physical exertion. While atrophy tends to produce a thin, dried out,

<!-- chunk -->

### emaciated frame, there are numerous instances where we find heavy,

<!-- chunk -->

### overweight people whose tissues are dried out and malnourished.

<!-- chunk -->

### The characteristic symptoms of atrophy include a dry tongue. In

<!-- chunk -->

### advanced cases, it may be narrow, thin, withered, or cracked, but it will

<!-- chunk -->

### always be dry. It can also be red from heat caused by a lack of fluids, or

<!-- chunk -->

### pale from lack of nutrition. The pulse is usually weak, enfeebled, low,

<!-- chunk -->

### uneven (scrapes along), and sometimes weakly tense on the patient’s left

<!-- chunk -->

### wrist. The skin is dry and rough, lacking oil, or water, or both. Skin

<!-- chunk -->

### conditions (acne, eczema, and psoriasis) are very common. There may be

<!-- chunk -->

### oozing from the skin as it cracks and opens up, but the general condition

<!-- chunk -->

### is one of dryness. The sensation given by dry, atrophic skin is like that

<!-- chunk -->

### given by running a finger across the end of a book.

<!-- chunk -->

### When tissues are dehydrated the lack of fluids can give rise to heat.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is called “hectic fever” in the old Western literature. It is described

<!-- chunk -->

### in traditional Chinese medicine as “phantom,” or “false heat,” or “yin

<!-- chunk -->

### deficiency.” The characteristic symptoms are a rapid, nonresistant pulse

<!-- chunk -->

### and red flushing across the checks—the hectic or malarial flush. Tension

<!-- chunk -->

### can also arise from lack of fluids. This is another symptom of “uprushing

<!-- chunk -->

### yang” from “lack of yin,” or “yin not holding down the yang.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Cold • Depression

<!-- chunk -->

### This tissue state is the opposite of irritation. Tissues are under-stimulated

<!-- chunk -->

### or incapable of responding to stimulation. There is a lack of oxidation in

<!-- chunk -->

### the cells. Cellular life is diminished so there is hypo-function, tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### deterioration, and retention of waste products and even unused

<!-- chunk -->

### foodstuffs. This represents a golden opportunity for bacteria to enter the

<!-- chunk -->

### body. They are not so much invading as helping to rid the body of

<!-- chunk -->

### superfluous material. Conversely, invasion by foreign bacteria, parasites,

<!-- chunk -->

### venom, poisons, heavy metals, or toxic chemicals depresses cellular life

<!-- chunk -->

### and induces tissue depression. Bacteria and parasites secrete exotoxins

<!-- chunk -->

### that suppress the cell life of the host, so that they can create an

<!-- chunk -->

### environment where they are healthy. Thus, there is a cumulative or

<!-- chunk -->

### ongoing effect.

<!-- chunk -->

### The body attempts to deal with the bacteria by increasing white cell

<!-- chunk -->

### production (hence, the high white cell counts). This results in the

<!-- chunk -->

### production of pus (a mixture of dead bacteria and white cells). In a

<!-- chunk -->

### healthier body, these find a vent, a boil or fistula, through which to

<!-- chunk -->

### discharge. In a sicker body, they discharge into the bloodstream, causing

<!-- chunk -->

### systemic poisoning or septicemia. There are low-grade fevers and

<!-- chunk -->

### infections with putrefaction, venting of pus, fatigue, exhaustion, and

<!-- chunk -->

### easy infection of wounds. The bone marrow, stem cells, and lymphatic

<!-- chunk -->

### system are eventually worn out trying to keep up with the bacteria and

<!-- chunk -->

### toxins, resulting in low immunity. The defenses against aberrant cell

<!-- chunk -->

### growths are down and cancer is more likely.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although the oxidation rate in the cells is low, in the spaces between

<!-- chunk -->

### the cells it is higher, because of the scavenging by bacteria. This creates

<!-- chunk -->

### a condition of “heat from cold,” or heat from cellular inactivity. This is

<!-- chunk -->

### the opposite of “heat from heat,” or the heat of cellular over-activity in

<!-- chunk -->

### the irritated tissue state. As a consequence, antioxidants are used for

<!-- chunk -->

### both conditions, even though completely non-holistic modern

<!-- chunk -->

### practitioners are incapable of differentiating the conditions where one

<!-- chunk -->

### antioxidant would be useful instead of another.

<!-- chunk -->

### Antibiotics kill one kind of bacteria, but unbalance the microflora of

<!-- chunk -->

### the colon, creating other types of bacterial imbalance and generation of

<!-- chunk -->

### toxins. They also do not remove the toxins that remain behind in the

<!-- chunk -->

### body after the bacteria are killed. This is why people who have had

<!-- chunk -->

### parasites or amoebae in the colon often experience a feeling as if they

<!-- chunk -->

### were still present, even after they have been killed by drugs The dragon

<!-- chunk -->

### tracks are still present. There is a further, very serious side effect of

<!-- chunk -->

### antibiotic treatment. When bacteria are killed they release endotoxins

<!-- chunk -->

### from inside themselves which otherwise would never enter the

<!-- chunk -->

### bloodstream. These cause additional poisoning of the body. In fact, if the

<!-- chunk -->

### bacterial die-off is high enough, there may be a “septic shock” resulting

<!-- chunk -->

### in sudden death. The appropriate way to kill bacteria is to assist the

<!-- chunk -->

### body in removing the toxins that support bacterial or parasitical life.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is generally the way herbs work. The attempt to kill bacteria with

<!-- chunk -->

### “natural antibiotic” herbs is an entirely misguided imitation of a deeply

<!-- chunk -->

### flawed biomedical model.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the depressed tissue state, circulation is usually retarded so that

<!-- chunk -->

### there is a lack of circulation to the extremities, resulting in cold hands

<!-- chunk -->

### and feet. As a result, the old doctors gave this symptom the highest

<!-- chunk -->

### importance, not necessarily as a cardiovascular symptom (which it

<!-- chunk -->

### usually affects eventually), but as an indication of the general tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### condition. The extremities include the entire capillary bed, where the

<!-- chunk -->

### force of blood pressure pushes the contents of the blood out into the

<!-- chunk -->

### interstitial fluids surrounding the cells. Blood pressure may be low,

<!-- chunk -->

### though often blockages in the portal vein or elsewhere lead to high

<!-- chunk -->

### blood pressure.

<!-- chunk -->

### If circulation is weak, oxygen and blood sugar, and even water, will

<!-- chunk -->

### have a hard time getting to the capillaries, and from there to the cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### This causes the depression of cell life. In the spaces around the cells

<!-- chunk -->

### there is retention of carbon dioxide, since return circulation is also weak.

<!-- chunk -->

### This results in a generally acid condition in the internal tissues. Acids,

<!-- chunk -->

### the treatment for excess heat, are detrimental in depression. (That is

<!-- chunk -->

### why, in Ayurvedic medicine, acids are considered to be heat generating;

<!-- chunk -->

### this is only true in “heat from cold”). This includes acid-generating foods

<!-- chunk -->

### such as meat and protein.

<!-- chunk -->

### When there is a lack of strong peripheral circulation—or a lack of the

<!-- chunk -->

### “determining powers,” in the phraseology of the old doctors—rashes

<!-- chunk -->

### recede to the interior, inflicting themselves on the nerves and viscera

<!-- chunk -->

### rather than coming out through the skin. Retrocession of the rash in

<!-- chunk -->

### smallpox was a very serious problem for the old doctors. Even today it

<!-- chunk -->

### still occurs in chicken pox and can be fatal. (It also occurs from

<!-- chunk -->

### vaccination, causing terrible havoc in sensitive children). The old

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment was to give stimulants (saffron and safflower were favorites)

<!-- chunk -->

### to bring out the rash. In homeopathy, Thuja (cedar) is used to bring out

<!-- chunk -->

### the vaccination.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another result of poor peripheral circulation and low oxygenation of

<!-- chunk -->

### the cells is heavy, thick, coagulated blood. This results in the typical

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of “congealed blood” recognized in traditional Chinese

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. (Congealed blood can also be due to relaxation causing local

<!-- chunk -->

### collection and coagulation of blood. Sometimes it is also due to

<!-- chunk -->

### constriction). There is often a blue/red, or blue/yellow, or blue/black

<!-- chunk -->

### complexion around the veins, which are more visible. Blood from

<!-- chunk -->

### hemorrhaging or menstruation is thick, clotted, dark, oozing.

<!-- chunk -->

### We have, seemingly, jumped over the simplest expression of

<!-- chunk -->

### depression. Cold from the environment will reduce cellular activity. The

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothalamus reacts with shivering and fever to return the organism to

<!-- chunk -->

### health. If the organism does not adequately respond, there will be

<!-- chunk -->

### “internal cold” or depression. If it gets stuck in the shivering phase there

<!-- chunk -->

### will be tissue constriction. If it gets stuck in the fever stage there will be

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue irritation. Accordingly, a chill or cold from the environment needs

<!-- chunk -->

### to be treated according to the stage it is moving through or the tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### state where it gets stuck.

<!-- chunk -->

### The characteristic symptoms of depression include a feeling of internal

<!-- chunk -->

### cold, as if one cannot get it out of the body; hands and feet cold; skin

<!-- chunk -->

### cold, dry and inactive, due to poor peripheral circulation; tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### deterioration and bacterial feeding; tendency to secondary, septic or

<!-- chunk -->

### putrefactive heat with discharges that are yellow, green, brown or black;

<!-- chunk -->

### skin color darkish—dusky red, blue, purple, brown, yellow or black;

<!-- chunk -->

### pulse deep, weak, or slow until putrefactive fever sets in, in which case

<!-- chunk -->

### it may become rapid, low, feeble, weak, and nonresistant; tongue usually

<!-- chunk -->

### blue, purple, or darkish in some (not usually all) areas; coating (if any)

<!-- chunk -->

### heavy, congealed, yellow, or yellow/brown.

<!-- chunk -->

### The major organs affected by the depressed tissue state are the colon,

<!-- chunk -->

### liver, pancreas, lymphatic/immune system, blood and cardiovascular

<!-- chunk -->

### system. In extreme cases the nervous system is damaged. The large

<!-- chunk -->

### intestine is subject to toxic secretion from unhealthy bacteria. The liver

<!-- chunk -->

### is subject to poisoning from toxins brought to it from the rest of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body. Excess bacteria and toxins weaken the lymphatic/immune system

<!-- chunk -->

### (which has to break down pathological cell growths). The great scourges

<!-- chunk -->

### of health—cancer, heart disease, insulin resistance, and type II diabetes

<!-- chunk -->

### —are more often found in association with this tissue state. Aberrant

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue growth and cancer are more likely when the immune system is

<!-- chunk -->

### depressed. Eventually, there is blood poisoning, with destruction of the

<!-- chunk -->

### blood and nerves.

<!-- chunk -->

## Damp • Relaxation

<!-- chunk -->

### The term “relaxation” goes all the way back to the Hippocratic writers,

<!-- chunk -->

### who called diarrhea a “relaxed” condition of the bowels. Thus, from the

<!-- chunk -->

### beginning, relaxation was associated with tissue lacking in tone and

<!-- chunk -->

### giving off excessive fluids.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Alvah Curtis and Dr. William Cook (1869, 45) associated

<!-- chunk -->

### “relaxation” with the need for astringents. The latter considered white

<!-- chunk -->

### oak bark (Quercus alba) to be the archetypal representative for this

<!-- chunk -->

### category. However, Thurston changed the name to “dilation” and

<!-- chunk -->

### associated it with “congestive chills” caused by swollen tissue deep

<!-- chunk -->

### inside the organism. The nauseant bitters that correlate with the tense or

<!-- chunk -->

### constricted tissue state, however, treat such chills. Thus, I have followed

<!-- chunk -->

### Curtis and Cook.

<!-- chunk -->

### Relaxation describes tissue that is atonic, flaccid, saggy, or collapsed.

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to sagging and losing its tone, it does not hold fluids, so there

<!-- chunk -->

### is a continuous loss of fluid, called “free secretion” in the old authors.

<!-- chunk -->

### The tendency to discharge is so strong that running ulcers may form.

<!-- chunk -->

### Discharges of saliva, mucus, urine, sweat, and diarrhea are thin, clear,

<!-- chunk -->

### watery, and copious. The mucus tends to be dilute, clear, or white, while

<!-- chunk -->

### that of stagnation is heavy, thick, yellow, green, or catarrhal. Astringents

<!-- chunk -->

### are called for in such conditions because they coat the membranes with

<!-- chunk -->

### proteins that stop free secretion. They also improve the tone and stop the

<!-- chunk -->

### sagging and prolapse.

<!-- chunk -->

### Flaccid tissue not only loses fluids but minerals. When we are at rest,

<!-- chunk -->

### calcium is moved from the bones to the serum, but when we are active it

<!-- chunk -->

### is moved in the other direction—hence the importance of exercise in the

<!-- chunk -->

### prevention of osteoporosis. Calcium helps magnesium sedate the muscles

<!-- chunk -->

### so it is needed in extra dosage when the muscles are in a relaxed state.

<!-- chunk -->

### As a consequence, the relaxed tissue state is associated with a generally

<!-- chunk -->

### alkaline condition of the blood, fluids, and urine, yet with

<!-- chunk -->

### demineralization, bone loss, and weakness of the bones. The alkalis may

<!-- chunk -->

### deposit out of the fluids, into the tissues, forming bony growths,

<!-- chunk -->

### swellings, arthritis, gouty deposits, indurated and nodulated glands,

<!-- chunk -->

### impediments to hearing, and cataracts. The bone marrow may be

<!-- chunk -->

### weakened, leading to poor production of white cells and red cells, low

<!-- chunk -->

### immunity, poor bone growth, anemia, and developmental problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### Keratin may leak out of the skin, causing hardness and roughness of the

<!-- chunk -->

### palms.

<!-- chunk -->

### One would think that relaxation of the tissues would cause a

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxation of the organism in general. However, the imbalances

<!-- chunk -->

### precipitated by fluid loss, including the loss of potassium and the

<!-- chunk -->

### accumulation of excess sodium, cause high blood pressure and

<!-- chunk -->

### psychological tension to occur. Thus, the relaxed tissue state can, though

<!-- chunk -->

### initially relaxed, become exceedingly tense.

<!-- chunk -->

### The relaxed tissue state also includes a condition called “passive

<!-- chunk -->

### venous congestion” by the old doctors. The arteries and veins are

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxed, leading to passive congestion of blood in the more easily dilated

<!-- chunk -->

### veins. There is congestion in the liver, lungs, and right side of the heart.

<!-- chunk -->

### High blood pressure accompanies this condition; there is excess sodium

<!-- chunk -->

### and not enough potassium. The complexion is blue from excess venous

<!-- chunk -->

### blood, or the veins are prominent or swollen. In addition to a tendency

<!-- chunk -->

### to blue complexion, the relaxed tissue state has a tendency to a pale

<!-- chunk -->

### complexion.

<!-- chunk -->

### When relaxation affects the kidneys, there is poor signaling to the

<!-- chunk -->

### bone marrow to produce replacement red blood cells. This results in

<!-- chunk -->

### “kidney anemia,” or what the old doctors called “poor blood.” The blue

<!-- chunk -->

### veins are usually prominent through the pale skin and the skin and

<!-- chunk -->

### mucosa are moister than in iron deficiency anemia. Poor maturation of

<!-- chunk -->

### white blood cells also seems to occur in the bone marrow, so that there

<!-- chunk -->

### are symptoms of what we would today call “low immunity”: recurrent

<!-- chunk -->

### upper respiratory infections, chronic swollen glands, and a tendency to

<!-- chunk -->

### tuberculosis. The “consumptive taint” or tubercular tendency was

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with thin, poorly nourished, pale persons with low calcium

<!-- chunk -->

### levels. Relaxation has low available calcium levels and it is calcium the

<!-- chunk -->

### body uses to wall off the tubercle.

<!-- chunk -->

### The characteristic symptoms of relaxation are pale, cool, and moist

<!-- chunk -->

### skin, with a tendency toward visible, prominent veins. Free secretion of

<!-- chunk -->

### clear, thin mucus, sweat, urine, and diarrhea. Glands, tonsils, and

<!-- chunk -->

### adenoids are swollen, what the old authors often refer to as “relaxed

<!-- chunk -->

### tonsils.” Chronic swollen glands eventually become hard, indurated, and

<!-- chunk -->

### calcified. Calcifications cause cataract and hearing problems, but there

<!-- chunk -->

### are weak, decalcified bones, teeth and tendon weaknesses, poor bone

<!-- chunk -->

### formation, and unhealthy bone marrow. The last causes low white and

<!-- chunk -->

### red cell counts and low immunity. Renal anemia from poor kidney

<!-- chunk -->

### signaling to the marrow. Muscles are at first relaxed, but, due to loss of

<!-- chunk -->

### calcium to assist the magnesium, they can become twitchy. The tongue

<!-- chunk -->

### is moist, often clear, with foamy streamers down the sides. Pulse is

<!-- chunk -->

### languid, nonresistant, or tense from venous obstruction.

<!-- chunk -->

## Damp • Torpor • Stagnation

<!-- chunk -->

### The Greeks recognized two types of dampness. Fluids can actively run

<!-- chunk -->

### out of the body (relaxation), or they can collect inside the tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### (stagnation, torpor). In the second case, when the water is stuck, tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### are swollen, weak, infiltrated with water and ill nourished, since excess

<!-- chunk -->

### water acts as a barrier to nutrition. The water itself is inactive. It collects

<!-- chunk -->

### materials and can thicken and stagnate. The old English herbalists

<!-- chunk -->

### referred to this tissue state as “thickening of the humors.” Fluids thicken

<!-- chunk -->

### to produce humors, catarrh, or canker. Membranes associated with such

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnation and catarrh do not secrete or excrete as they should. This

<!-- chunk -->

### causes impeded nutrition, metabolism, and elimination. These thickened

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids are associated with the accumulation of toxins. It is also possible

<!-- chunk -->

### that the reverse is true: weakened nutrition, metabolism, and elimination

<!-- chunk -->

### cause stagnant fluids.

<!-- chunk -->

### Toxins are substances that burden the body because they need further

<!-- chunk -->

### elaboration or breakdown. The accumulation of toxins was called “bad

<!-- chunk -->

### blood,” “impure blood,” “toxic blood” or “toxemia.” The word “bad

<!-- chunk -->

### blood” comes from American Indian practice, while “impure blood”

<!-- chunk -->

### translates a German term. Dr. J. Tilden introduced the term “toxemia” in

<!-- chunk -->

### 1890. The standard remedies for this condition are the “blood purifiers”

<!-- chunk -->

### or alteratives.

<!-- chunk -->

### Medical theories about “bad blood” have changed and evolved over

<!-- chunk -->

### the centuries. In the early nineteenth century, it was recognized that

<!-- chunk -->

### scurvy was one source of the condition, and remedies for humors were

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes called antiscorbutics. In the middle of the nineteenth century,

<!-- chunk -->

### “bad blood” was associated with incomplete nutrition, metabolism, and

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination. Scudder (1874, 313) associated “bad blood” with lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion and excretion. Thurston (1900, 270) concluded that torpor (or

<!-- chunk -->

### “atonicity” as he called it), was due to lack of secretion and excretion. In

<!-- chunk -->

### the twentieth century, the condition is sometimes called “toxic liver”

<!-- chunk -->

### since the liver is the center for non-cellular detoxification or catabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Broda Barnes (1976, 101) noted that the symptoms associated with

<!-- chunk -->

### “bad blood” are identical to those caused by low metabolism from

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothyroidism. The poor peripheral circulation and oxidation

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with this endocrine imbalance cause the skin lesions so

<!-- chunk -->

### characteristic of “bad blood.” They also cause thickening of the fluids

<!-- chunk -->

### with stagnant water mixed with a mucoid substance. The question is,

<!-- chunk -->

### though, which came first, the toxins encumbering the metabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### causing hypothyroidism, or low thyroxine causing low metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is the key to treating “bad blood.” Most of the blood purifiers or

<!-- chunk -->

### alteratives are bitters that increase secretion in the digestive tract, liver,

<!-- chunk -->

### and eliminative channels. This promotes tissue activity and nutrition.

<!-- chunk -->

### Excretion from the colon is also improved. Most of the old laxatives and

<!-- chunk -->

### cathartics are also bitter alteratives. Several thyroid active remedies are

<!-- chunk -->

### included among the old list of alteratives (kelp, bladderwrack, black

<!-- chunk -->

### walnut, iris, poke root), showing that the old doctors made this

<!-- chunk -->

### connection intuitively.

<!-- chunk -->

### Characteristic symptoms of stagnation or torpor include swollen,

<!-- chunk -->

### flabby, apathetic, weak tissues. The tissues show a “want of expression”

<!-- chunk -->

### as Scudder remarks. This extends to the face. “The patient is dull and

<!-- chunk -->

### apathetic, the eye dull, the face expressionless, the tongue somewhat

<!-- chunk -->

### full, and the pulse lacks sharpness in the wave—oppressed” (Scudder,

<!-- chunk -->

### 1874, 303, 314). Lack of expression is one of the most characteristic

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of hypothyroidism. The leading symptom of bad blood is a

<!-- chunk -->

### tendency to hangover conditions and feelings, because the liver and

<!-- chunk -->

### lymph system cannot handle heavy or toxic foods and excesses of

<!-- chunk -->

### various kinds, including exercise. A leading symptom of hypothyroidism

<!-- chunk -->

### is fatigue. With low metabolism, water builds up and thickens. There are

<!-- chunk -->

### often swollen lymphatics with low immunity or resistance to disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### Heavy, thick, adhesive mucus forms and clings to surfaces. Peripheral

<!-- chunk -->

### circulation is weak, with poor oxidation, leading to skin lesions and poor

<!-- chunk -->

### wound healing. Arthritic or rheumatic pains are common. The tongue is

<!-- chunk -->

### usually flabby, apathetic, or atonic with a thick, turbid, and adhesive

<!-- chunk -->

### coating.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most people will have a hard time differentiating the six tissue states

<!-- chunk -->

### at the start. The student can only understand one or two tissue states at

<!-- chunk -->

### first, incorporate that into practice, and then expand on this foundation,

<!-- chunk -->

### as experience deepens. The experienced clinician, on the other hand, will

<!-- chunk -->

### have trouble limiting tissue states to six. He or she will be able to

<!-- chunk -->

### imagine all sorts of tissue changes that could be given their own class.

<!-- chunk -->

### In practice, most people will exhibit a mix of tissue states—usually

<!-- chunk -->

### two, and sometimes three. Diseases often go through a progression of

<!-- chunk -->

### states. Opposites are often active: irritation/depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### constriction/relaxation, and atrophy/stagnation. In chronic cases, the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism seems to reduce to the least active: atrophy, torpor, and

<!-- chunk -->

### depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### The six tissue states comprise a model that is compatible both with

<!-- chunk -->

### modern science and traditional energetic medicine. It is related to the

<!-- chunk -->

### way that modern Western people think about their psychological and

<!-- chunk -->

### physical sensations and conditions. It thus forms a bridge between the

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional and the modern, the practitioner and the patient. Most

<!-- chunk -->

### important of all, it is an eminently practical method for understanding

<!-- chunk -->

### and treating illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Whenever we apply a system to nature we must be aware that there

<!-- chunk -->

### will be exceptions and anomalies. The rule works beautifully most of the

<!-- chunk -->

### time, but we should not expect it to work all the time. Thus, one of our

<!-- chunk -->

### best cooling sedatives, yarrow, is a stimulant!

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue States and Medicinal Actions

<!-- chunk -->

### In traditional Western medicine the actions of medicinal plants are

<!-- chunk -->

### described according to three basic classes: physiological imbalance

<!-- chunk -->

### (tissue state), physiological function, and organ-affinity. The first

<!-- chunk -->

### category includes astringents, stimulants, sedatives, antispasmodics, and

<!-- chunk -->

### aromatics. The second includes diaphoretics, antifebriles, diuretics,

<!-- chunk -->

### emmenagogues, and anodynes. The third includes stomachics,

<!-- chunk -->

### nephretics, nervines, and pectorals.

<!-- chunk -->

### The relationship between tissue states and medicinal properties is

<!-- chunk -->

### fairly exact. In fact, we can speak of specific tastes and temperatures as

<!-- chunk -->

### relating to specific tissue states. In fact, there is a tradition linking

<!-- chunk -->

### elements, humors, or tissue states with specific tastes in quite a few

<!-- chunk -->

### traditions. The following table represents the basic therapeutic

<!-- chunk -->

### correlations used in this book. It is not based on a metaphysical premise,

<!-- chunk -->

### but on physiological patterns. However, it can be correlated with the

<!-- chunk -->

### older systems, as well as with modern pharmacognosy.

<!-- chunk -->

### TISSUE STATES, TASTES, AND ACTIONS

<!-- chunk -->
