<!-- chunk -->

### In developing the preceding table I have been much influenced by the

<!-- chunk -->

### pioneering work of David Winston, registered herbalist (AHG). He has

<!-- chunk -->

### worked out a good system of correlations between traditional systems of

<!-- chunk -->

### energetics and the new science of pharmacognosy, or plant chemistry. I

<!-- chunk -->

### have not brought the present system up to the modern level, but

<!-- chunk -->

### retained the old nomenclature, which is based on a less refined organic

<!-- chunk -->

### chemistry, but which is just as accurate and also in line with a generally

<!-- chunk -->

### energetic view.

<!-- chunk -->

### When herbs are used in an overly intense, concentrated form they can

<!-- chunk -->

### engender reactions opposite to the above. Acidity (sour) turns down

<!-- chunk -->

### oxidation (heat) in the cells, but in large amounts it causes oxidation or

<!-- chunk -->

### burning. Acridity relaxes, but in large doses it agitates (valerian).

<!-- chunk -->

### Astringents are contracting, but large doses can bind so thoroughly that

<!-- chunk -->

### they cause dryness and tension. Sweets nourish and sedate in

<!-- chunk -->

### moderation, but prolonged overuse causes the cells to reject insulin and

<!-- chunk -->

### blood sugar, resulting in a depressed tissue state, i.e., type II diabetes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most plants act on a combination of two or three states. Thus, rose

<!-- chunk -->

### (sour astringent) is suited to irritation with relaxation, or hot conditions

<!-- chunk -->

### with uncontrollable discharges. It was a traditional remedy for

<!-- chunk -->

### tuberculosis. A good indication nowadays is a red/pink tongue with

<!-- chunk -->

### moist streamers down the sides. Peach, a cousin of the rose, is sour,

<!-- chunk -->

### bitter, and mucilaginous, so it is suited to conditions where there is heat

<!-- chunk -->

### with some dryness. Here the tongue is red/pink and dry. Another cousin,

<!-- chunk -->

### almond, is highly oily, so it is suited to dry/atrophic conditions with

<!-- chunk -->

### some irritation. Marshmallow (salty, moist, cool) will almost always act

<!-- chunk -->

### where dryness is combined with irritation. Tongue red, dry, cracked.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because it is salty it is further indicated where there is hardened tissue.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fenugreek (moist, oily, salty, pungent, warm) will act where dryness

<!-- chunk -->

### combines with cold. White pine (aromatic, oily) will act where there is

<!-- chunk -->

### depression with atrophy.

# A

<!-- chunk -->

### If we properly study our cases, so as to determine a definite

<!-- chunk -->

### condition of disease, and know the direct action of remedies in

<!-- chunk -->

### such conditions, we will have a certain and rational practice of

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### —J

OHN

<!-- chunk -->

### M. S

CUDDER

<!-- chunk -->

### s we have seen, the medicinal substance itself can be looked

<!-- chunk -->

### upon as an energetic entity. Herbs can be used for either material

<!-- chunk -->

### or energetic purposes, to suppress or cure. In order to use them

<!-- chunk -->

### holistically, we need to use them energetically, that is to say, according

<!-- chunk -->

### to the impression they make upon the life force of the whole organism,

<!-- chunk -->

### rather than the purely physical body. In other words, the energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### configuration of the disease must be related to the energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### configuration of the medicine. Down through the centuries, various

<!-- chunk -->

### principles have been applied to match remedy and sickness from this

<!-- chunk -->

### standpoint.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is an art and a science to using the medicinal substance in a

<!-- chunk -->

### holistic fashion. That is the subject of this chapter. In this book we will

<!-- chunk -->

### only consider the single remedy rather than the formula. The latter also

<!-- chunk -->

### is subject to energetic laws, but the subject is too complex for our

<!-- chunk -->

### immediate discussion. My good friend, the late William LeSassier,

<!-- chunk -->

### utilized an energetic system of herbal compounding that came to him in

<!-- chunk -->

### a vision. William once said to a small class, “all of us could take nettles

<!-- chunk -->

### and we’d all react in different ways, but once I stick nettles in a formula

<!-- chunk -->

### I know exactly what it does.” There is an art to using the single

<!-- chunk -->

### substance and the compound.

<!-- chunk -->

## Simpling

<!-- chunk -->

### When an herb is used by itself to heal it is called a simple. When several

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs are combined together they are called a compound. Simpling is a

<!-- chunk -->

### characteristic of the folk tradition while compounding was associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with the scholastic tradition of Galenic medicine and educated doctors.

<!-- chunk -->

### Compounds were, in fact, sometimes called Galenicals. The use of

<!-- chunk -->

### simples or single remedies was an important aspect of Samuel

<!-- chunk -->

### Hahnemann’s homeopathy. Dr. John M. Scudder (1827–93), the great

<!-- chunk -->

### eclectic physician, introduced it in botanical medicine in the nineteenth

<!-- chunk -->

### century. He termed it “specific medicine.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Galen (c.

C.E

<!-- chunk -->

### .) was the founder of the rational doctrine in

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. He taught the old Aristotelian idea that the properties of a

<!-- chunk -->

### plant were found in its qualities (hot, cold, damp, dry). If a condition

<!-- chunk -->

### was hot and dry, one combined cold and damp medicines to match the

<!-- chunk -->

### problem. Galenic doctors tended to use compounds established by

<!-- chunk -->

### professional tradition rather than single herbs known from experience.

<!-- chunk -->

### The simpler, by comparison, tended to view the plant as possessing

<!-- chunk -->

### unique virtues suited to a specific problem. He or she was usually looked

<!-- chunk -->

### down upon as an unsophisticated enthusiastic. There was little respect

<!-- chunk -->

### for the value of hands-on experience then (as now). Experience was

<!-- chunk -->

### considered to be inferior to rational doctrine, just as today experiential

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine is dismissed as “anecdotal.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Galen divided medicine into three schools: rational, methodist, and

<!-- chunk -->

### empirical. The first practiced according to a rational system, the second

<!-- chunk -->

### according to a single rational idea (“everyone needs to be purged”), and

<!-- chunk -->

### the third according to empiricism, or personal experience. The simpler

<!-- chunk -->

### or “empiric” was placed in the last category. Samuel Thomson was

<!-- chunk -->

### derided as a “botanic empiric.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Experience was the guiding light of empirical medicine, but to the

<!-- chunk -->

### extent that there was any system in traditional folk practice it was often

<!-- chunk -->

### based on the law of similars (“like treats like”) and the “doctrine of

<!-- chunk -->

### signatures.” The basic idea here is that if a plant resembles a disease,

<!-- chunk -->

### organ, or person it will help cure. Chelidonium, with its yellow sap, was

<!-- chunk -->

### considered a specific for the bile. Chelidonium was official in the U.S.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pharmacopoeia until after 1950 for hepatic disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes specifics were learned from observation of animal uses of

<!-- chunk -->

### plants. There was an old European peasant tradition that Chelidonium

<!-- chunk -->

### was used by swallows to treat the eyes of young chicks when they were

<!-- chunk -->

### stuck together. It was therefore adopted into peasant medicine as a

<!-- chunk -->

### specific for the eyes. Herbalist Maurice Messegue was taken by his

<!-- chunk -->

### father, a peasant herbalist in Gascony in the early twentieth century, to

<!-- chunk -->

### observe swallows rubbing celandine on the eyes of their young.

<!-- chunk -->

### Empirical medicine is largely uncharted territory, but it fascinated a

<!-- chunk -->

### certain number of off beat scholars down through the centuries. As a

<!-- chunk -->

### consequence, a number of simples, formulas, and recipes for specific uses

<!-- chunk -->

### were gathered and recorded. This resulted in the generation of a type of

<!-- chunk -->

### literature called the “books of secrets.” These tomes often consisted of

<!-- chunk -->

### long lists of simples and recipes for all sorts of industrial, medical, and

<!-- chunk -->

### occult purposes. They were often attributing to famous “scientists.” For

<!-- chunk -->

### instance, Albertus Magnus did not actually write the famous Book of

<!-- chunk -->

### Secrets of Albertus Magnus. Often the information was garbled or

<!-- chunk -->

### incredible, but the doctrine of specific knowledge was perpetuated as an

<!-- chunk -->

### ideal. With the advent of the printing press this knowledge was edited,

<!-- chunk -->

### sorted, and published. It proved to be a great stimulus, both in industry

<!-- chunk -->

### and medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### The word “secret” (arcana) was used in this literature for a number of

<!-- chunk -->

### reasons. It was thought that simples were known to animals and

<!-- chunk -->

### untutored rustics untainted by conventional science. It was felt that

<!-- chunk -->

### animals and people of Nature had a more direct insight into the

<!-- chunk -->

### workings of Nature. This information was secret in the sense that it lay

<!-- chunk -->

### outside the established tradition. In other cases it was secret because the

<!-- chunk -->

### directions were known only to some family, guild, or individual. We still

<!-- chunk -->

### do not know, for instance, how to make many of the colors known to the

<!-- chunk -->

### medieval glaziers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Paracelsus (1493–1541) was the first widely known physician who

<!-- chunk -->

### turned against rational medicine and embraced the empirical tradition.

<!-- chunk -->

### He was the first to work out an articulate medical system opposed to

<!-- chunk -->

### Galen. For Paracelsus, the basis of empiricism was the experience that

<!-- chunk -->

### Nature is alive and intelligent. Since this knowledge is subjective, it is

<!-- chunk -->

### hidden. Thus, the individual mineral, plant, or disease has an innate

<!-- chunk -->

### arcanum, intelligence, virtue, power, or energy. This inner arcana is

<!-- chunk -->

### outwardly reflected in its “signatures” or physical expression and

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents. The medicine contains the correct expression of healthy

<!-- chunk -->

### energy blocked in the disease. It communicates this virtue, power, or

<!-- chunk -->

### essence and returns the sick person to health. Treatment is thus by

<!-- chunk -->

### similars or signatures.

<!-- chunk -->

### Paracelsus represented a long line of thinkers who valued the internal

<!-- chunk -->

### or archetypal nature of the substance. This way of thinking traces back

<!-- chunk -->

### to Plato, the early Alexandrian philosophers who have come down to us

<!-- chunk -->

### under the name Hermes Trismegistos, the subsequent Hermetic tradition,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the Neo-Platonists, moving forward in time to Jacob Boehme,

<!-- chunk -->

### Emmanuel Swedenborg, J. W. Goethe, and Rudolf Steiner. During the

<!-- chunk -->

### Renaissance, the Paracelsian impulse was keenly felt and contributed

<!-- chunk -->

### much to the development of early chemistry, surgery, and medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Ultimately, however, this way of thinking, along with the folk medical

<!-- chunk -->

### empiricism upon which it drew, was expurgated by emerging science.

<!-- chunk -->

### Paracelsus used the term specific to indicate a single substance (or

<!-- chunk -->

### compound) with a definite relationship to a specific set of symptoms,

<!-- chunk -->

### condition, part, or process in the body. From this time forward, the

<!-- chunk -->

### alchemical physicians increasingly favored this term over the older term

<!-- chunk -->

### simple.

<!-- chunk -->

### After a period of conflict, the Galenic and Paracelsian approaches

<!-- chunk -->

### continued to be used along side each other. William Salmon, the last of

<!-- chunk -->

### the great Renaissance herbal physicians, included both the qualities and

<!-- chunk -->

### a “specification” for almost every plant in his English Herbal. For

<!-- chunk -->

### instance, under arrowhead (Sagittaria spp.) he writes (1709, 42):

<!-- chunk -->

### The Qualities. They are cold in the first degree, and temperate in

<!-- chunk -->

### respect to dryness or moisture. They are also alteratives, astringents,

<!-- chunk -->

### abstersives, incarnatives, glutinatives, and traumaticks or vulneraries;

<!-- chunk -->

### and appropriated to the spleen, reins, and joynts.

<!-- chunk -->

### The Specification. I have had great experience of the virtues and

<!-- chunk -->

### use of this herb, and have found it to be a peculiar wound herb,

<!-- chunk -->

### whether inwardly taken or outwardly apply’d; besides its signature

<!-- chunk -->

### bespeaks the very same thing.

<!-- chunk -->

### The first paragraph makes reference to the three classes used to

<!-- chunk -->

### designate the properties of herbs in Galenic medicines: qualities (hot or

<!-- chunk -->

### cold), appropriations (organ affinities), and actions (glutinative). The

<!-- chunk -->

### second paragraph refers to both experience and the doctrine of

<!-- chunk -->

### signatures—arrowhead looks like a wound-inflicting instrument. In the

<!-- chunk -->

### introduction to his Herbal, Salmon notes that the specifications were

<!-- chunk -->

### largely known either by experience or signature. This reflects empirical

<!-- chunk -->

### folk tradition and Paracelsian teaching.

<!-- chunk -->

## Specific Medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### Specificity continued to exert an influence after the passage of the

<!-- chunk -->

### Renaissance. Samuel Hahnemann originally called his homeopathic

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies specifics. However, as a careful student of language, he later

<!-- chunk -->

### defined them more correctly as similars. This is because they are

<!-- chunk -->

### administered on the basis of the law of similars, and not on the basis of

<!-- chunk -->

### specificity. As many homeopaths can testify from experience,

<!-- chunk -->

### homeopathic remedies are often specific when applied according to the

<!-- chunk -->

### law of similars, but this is not their distinguishing characteristic.

<!-- chunk -->

### These philological fine points left the term specific open for use by Dr.

<!-- chunk -->

### John Milton Scudder. He developed a system of therapy based entirely

<!-- chunk -->

### the use of specifics. In practice, many of these specifics were used

<!-- chunk -->

### several at a time, or in formulations mixed for a specific situation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder also included room for the use of well-established compounds

<!-- chunk -->

### alongside specifics.

<!-- chunk -->

### For Scudder, the specific was a medicine that could be reliably used

<!-- chunk -->

### for a certain condition, not because it was specific to an accepted

<!-- chunk -->

### pathological concept or disease name, but because it was suited to an

<!-- chunk -->

### exact energetic imbalance, or what he called a “wrong of life.” The

<!-- chunk -->

### knowledge of specifics was gained from the experience of what worked,

<!-- chunk -->

### and not merely theory. His work was, therefore, very much a

<!-- chunk -->

### continuation of the tradition of simpling and empiricism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder’s work not only reflected European tradition, but also the

<!-- chunk -->

### knowledge of specific medicines picked up from Native American

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. Specifics more readily crossed the language barrier than

<!-- chunk -->

### formulations. One frontier doctor, Peter Smith (1812) testifies, “I call

<!-- chunk -->

### myself an Indian Doctor, because I have incidentally obtained a

<!-- chunk -->

### knowledge of many of the simples used by the Indians; but chiefly

<!-- chunk -->

### because I have obtained my knowledge generally in the like manner that

<!-- chunk -->

### the Indians do …,” i.e., by experience rather than book knowledge.

<!-- chunk -->

### In contrast to the simplers and homeopaths, Scudder worked out a

<!-- chunk -->

### doctrine of medicine by which specifics were applied to particular

<!-- chunk -->

### “wrongs of life” or energetic imbalances. He called this system “specific

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine.” In a sense, he organized the empirical method, based as it

<!-- chunk -->

### was upon raw experience, into a system of medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### For Scudder, life is sensed by the life of the observer. Scudder taught

<!-- chunk -->

### his students to look at the complexion, tongue, pulse, and most obvious

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. These would communicate the “wrong of life.” He taught that

<!-- chunk -->

### the vital force of the physician would sense the wrong in the vital force

<!-- chunk -->

### of the sick person. Life communicates to life.

<!-- chunk -->

### In order to describe “wrongs of life,” Scudder (1874, 55–67) utilized

<!-- chunk -->

### the simple medical terms expressing the appearance of disease, such as:

<!-- chunk -->

### excitation, contraction, enfeeblement, depression, want of expression,

<!-- chunk -->

### determination of blood, congestion, hyperemia, inflammation, effusion,

<!-- chunk -->

### atrophic irritation, activity, want of activity, irritation. This is a

<!-- chunk -->

### sampling only from a dozen pages of one of his writings. Most of these

<!-- chunk -->

### are medical terms known to all nineteenth-century physicians. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### they were not used as the basis of prescription, but to describe aspects of

<!-- chunk -->

### disease. Scudder made these the categories by which disease and remedy

<!-- chunk -->

### were classified.

<!-- chunk -->

### For Scudder, that which lives senses life. Like Hippocrates, he taught

<!-- chunk -->

### that the study of medicine begins by training the senses to experience

<!-- chunk -->

### life. The human senses are the foundation of medicinal knowledge and

<!-- chunk -->

### they are trained by exposure to life in all its forms. “The education that

<!-- chunk -->

### gives the best results, and makes the successful practitioner, is of the

<!-- chunk -->

### senses, and of the brain to receive impressions, and make deductions.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Yet, this is ignored in medical education (1874, 12): “Men live a

<!-- chunk -->

### lifetime, and know nothing of the manifestations of life. Students

<!-- chunk -->

### become conversant with books, attend their lectures, pass their

<!-- chunk -->

### examinations, and yet have no practical knowledge of human life. And

<!-- chunk -->

### physicians will practice medicine a lifetime, and yet fail to know what

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy life is.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The intelligence and vital force of the medical aspirant can be trained

<!-- chunk -->

### to this deeper perception, says Scudder. Unfortunately, he also says,

<!-- chunk -->

### “men dislike work.” It is easier to study books and “objective facts” than

<!-- chunk -->

### to develop one’s subjective life, senses, perceptive abilities, and method

<!-- chunk -->

### of thinking. Modern medical education is predicated on the idea that

<!-- chunk -->

### subjective life is innately unreliable and untrustworthy. There is

<!-- chunk -->

### something wrong with the inner life of the medical student that cannot

<!-- chunk -->

### be trusted. Only objective facts can be trusted. Likewise, Nature herself

<!-- chunk -->

### is untrustworthy. She cannot repair herself. She is broken; the physician

<!-- chunk -->

### must fix her.

<!-- chunk -->

### In contrast, vitalism looks upon the body as a living being which can

<!-- chunk -->

### correct and cure itself. The physician, as a living being, is in touch with

<!-- chunk -->

### his or her own life force and can sense what is wrong. Nature has

<!-- chunk -->

### unlimited intelligence based on the survival of all living species down

<!-- chunk -->

### through the ages, even extinct species, for that matter.

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder taught the use of the pulse, tongue, complexion, and salient

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms as the principal methods for uncovering these “wrongs of

<!-- chunk -->

### life.” The pulse does not just represent the condition of the circulation,

<!-- chunk -->

### but of the nervous system, blood, and tissues as a whole. The tongue

<!-- chunk -->

### shows a graphic picture of the mucous membranes, as well as tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### nutrition, innervation, blood, and fluids. The complexion and expression

<!-- chunk -->

### indicate the condition of the entire organism, and the psychological

<!-- chunk -->

### state. By careful observation, the physician sees the “wrong of life”

<!-- chunk -->

### shining through, from its core into its peripheral expressions.

<!-- chunk -->

## Three Primal Therapeutic Laws

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder was a pragmatist. He was not interested in laws of therapy. If a

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy was found through experience to work for a certain condition or

<!-- chunk -->

### symptom, that was all he needed to know. However, we can find such

<!-- chunk -->

### universal laws in the behavior of medicinal substances. These help us to

<!-- chunk -->

### understand the way medicinal substances work in an energetic fashion.

<!-- chunk -->

### In homeopathy the “universal law of healing” is the “law of similars,”

<!-- chunk -->

### or “like treats like.” This is based on the fact that the symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### produced by a substance acting in excess upon the organism (as a

<!-- chunk -->

### poison) are the symptoms that it will cure when these appear in the sick

<!-- chunk -->

### person in the course of disease. In order to eliminate side effects, the

<!-- chunk -->

### dose is diluted. In Galenic medicine, on the other hand, the guiding light

<!-- chunk -->

### of therapy is the “law of contraries,” or “hot to cold.” If a person is

<!-- chunk -->

### chilled, he or she is given warm wine or herb teas to reestablish

<!-- chunk -->

### peripheral warmth and circulation. We can already observe that this law

<!-- chunk -->

### governs most of the remedies used to cure according to the tissue state

<!-- chunk -->

### model.

<!-- chunk -->

### How are we too make sense out of these conflicting “laws?” Some

<!-- chunk -->

### herbs work according to several approaches. Chelidonium (celandine) is

<!-- chunk -->

### bitter and heating. Used according to the law of contraries, it will warm

<!-- chunk -->

### up and dissipate congestion, and that is what it does in the liver and gall

<!-- chunk -->

### bladder. However, the location of its work is only specified through the

<!-- chunk -->

### doctrine of signatures: it is useful for problems with the bile because it

<!-- chunk -->

### has a unique yellow, bile-like sap. It has received a homeopathic proving

<!-- chunk -->

### (celandine is fairly toxic), producing symptoms of congestion in the liver

<!-- chunk -->

### and gall bladder so it works on the same basic conditions whether it is

<!-- chunk -->

### used as a contrary (hot to cold), a similar or by signature.

<!-- chunk -->

### In my first book, Seven Herbs: Plants as Teachers (1987) I proposed that

<!-- chunk -->

### there were three primary laws of healing: the law of similars, the law of

<!-- chunk -->

### direction of cure, and the law of the healing crisis. Since that time I have

<!-- chunk -->

### come to see that the law of contraries, the law of similars, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### doctrine of signatures are all valid and in fact derived from another,

<!-- chunk -->

### more unifying law. As a consequence, I have folded them together under

<!-- chunk -->

### one heading: the law of action and reaction.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Law of Action and Reaction

<!-- chunk -->

### The living organism responds to stress by taking countermeasures. When

<!-- chunk -->

### the cell gets cold, it contracts or shrinks to save energy. When it returns

<!-- chunk -->

### to health, it expands to take in potential energy. What our body feels is

<!-- chunk -->

### stiffening and shivering followed by fever. If, on the other hand, the cell

<!-- chunk -->

### becomes overheated, it expands to give off heat. As it contracts back to

<!-- chunk -->

### normal size, it gives off water. What we feel is a fever followed by sweat,

<!-- chunk -->

### urination, purging, or expectoration.

<!-- chunk -->

### The self-healing mechanism of the organism counteracts

<!-- chunk -->

### environmental stress. It takes an action in one direction and then a

<!-- chunk -->

### reaction in the opposite direction to contend with stress and return to

<!-- chunk -->

### the healthy mean or homeostasis. Dr. Harold Lindlahr, the “philosopher

<!-- chunk -->

### of naturopathy,” named this the “law of action and reaction.” It is also

<!-- chunk -->

### known as the Arndt/Schulz law in European phytotherapy: for every

<!-- chunk -->

### biological reaction there is an equal and opposite reaction.

<!-- chunk -->

### As long as the organism is able to act and react it will be able to

<!-- chunk -->

### recover from disease. Problems arise when it gets stuck in either

<!-- chunk -->

### polarity. Too strong an emphasis in one direction will soon make it

<!-- chunk -->

### difficult to move in the opposite direction. Action and reaction generates

<!-- chunk -->

### the six tissue states. Basic energetic imbalances in the body represent

<!-- chunk -->

### simple excesses in opposite directions: excitation/depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### constriction/relaxation, atrophy/torpor.

<!-- chunk -->

### When the organism becomes stuck in one of these conditions, the

<!-- chunk -->

### conventional biomedical response is to suppress the condition. For

<!-- chunk -->

### instance, aspirin or some other drug is given to suppress the febrile

<!-- chunk -->

### reaction. With more complicated diseases, more complex pathways are

<!-- chunk -->

### forced into action or inaction to suppress symptoms. This causes the

<!-- chunk -->

### original imbalance to be replaced by a drug-induced imbalance,

<!-- chunk -->

### ultimately causing a new, iatrogenic (drug-caused) disease. In a

<!-- chunk -->

### completely unconscious and unobservant practice, these symptoms are

<!-- chunk -->

### then suppressed by a new drug and so on, until the patient dies of

<!-- chunk -->

### “natural causes.”

<!-- chunk -->

### In holistic medicine, on the other hand, we want to return the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism back to the original condition where it was able to maintain

<!-- chunk -->

### and cure itself. We believe in the self-curative ability of Nature and its

<!-- chunk -->

### little microcosm, the organism. We want to work with, and restore,

<!-- chunk -->

### Nature. Our approach, therefore, is the opposite of the modern

<!-- chunk -->

### biomedical method.

<!-- chunk -->

### The most obvious action we can take is to counteract the initial

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalance. If the organism is too cold we can warm it up, starting with

<!-- chunk -->

### warm food, clothing, and proper environment. We can go further and

<!-- chunk -->

### use warming herbs (ginger, cayenne, pepper, thyme, rosemary, and

<!-- chunk -->

### others).

<!-- chunk -->

### At this point, the controversy between contraries and similars rears its

<!-- chunk -->

### ugly head. In treating the cold condition with hot herbs are we not

<!-- chunk -->

### suppressing symptoms? That would be the homeopathic position.

<!-- chunk -->

### Lindlahr pointed out the solution to this problem (Lindlahr, 1917, 59).

<!-- chunk -->

### When a poison acts on the organism it causes an initial or primary

<!-- chunk -->

### action, which represents the destructive effects on the body. Then the

<!-- chunk -->

### body responds to remove the symptoms. This is the secondary reaction.

<!-- chunk -->

### The primary action of poisons is thus destructive, the secondary action is

<!-- chunk -->

### curative. Thus, in homeopathy, poisons are diluted and succussed

<!-- chunk -->

### (shaken) to eliminate the primary, poisonous action.

<!-- chunk -->

### Foods, herbs, and natural substances like hot and cold water work the

<!-- chunk -->

### opposite way. They cure in their primary action. If the body is weak,

<!-- chunk -->

### foods nourish. If cold, warming herbs or hot water warms. Only if herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### or methods are used too often or for too long do they cause toxic side

<!-- chunk -->

### effects. No one dies from a spoonful of sugar, but they can die from a

<!-- chunk -->

### lifetime of over-consumption of refined carbohydrates. The secondary

<!-- chunk -->

### action of foods is toxic. Thus, foods and poisons act in opposite fashions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Non-toxic herbs generally act like foods. That is to say, they cure by

<!-- chunk -->

### contrary or hot to cold in their primary exposure to the body. In addition

<!-- chunk -->

### to nourishing, they warm or cool, relax or constrict, cleanse or lubricate.

<!-- chunk -->

### Taken too much or too long, they overly warm, cool, or relax in their

<!-- chunk -->

### secondary action. Thus, non-toxic herbs produce symptoms in their

<!-- chunk -->

### secondary action. The body reacts against this with a third curative

<!-- chunk -->

### phase.

<!-- chunk -->

## Primary Action

## Secondary Reaction

## HerbsCurativeDestructive

<!-- chunk -->

## PoisonsDestructiveCurative

<!-- chunk -->

### When a substance cures by hot to cold, the relationship is called the

<!-- chunk -->

### “law of contraries.” Like to like is called the “law of similars.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Homeopathic remedies work by the law of similars because they are

<!-- chunk -->

### largely poisons that cure what they cause. Herbs, on the other hand,

<!-- chunk -->

### work by both laws. They treat “hot to cold” in their primary phase, they

<!-- chunk -->

### poison in their secondary phase, and they treat as “like to like” in the

<!-- chunk -->

### third phase.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are two different foundations for the law of similars: signatures

<!-- chunk -->

### and pathogenesis. The doctrine of signatures has been much maligned,

<!-- chunk -->

### but it proves to be a useful law of cure itself. The shape, color, or habit

<!-- chunk -->

### of a plant represents the stress that has molded its existence and these

<!-- chunk -->

### contours are related to the same stress acting on a human being and

<!-- chunk -->

### causing disease. Rudolf Steiner argued that the same power that causes

<!-- chunk -->

### “distortions” in plants is the power that causes diseases in human beings.

<!-- chunk -->

### Where the plant responds by changing form, the human being gets sick,

<!-- chunk -->

### rallies, learns physical and spiritual lessons, and continues to grow as a

<!-- chunk -->

### human being. The “distortion” provides the mark or signature

<!-- chunk -->

### corresponding to the disease process in humanity (William Pelikan,

<!-- chunk -->

### 1997, 13–14).

<!-- chunk -->

### Food plants swell up with carbohydrates, lipids, and proteins. We see

<!-- chunk -->

### this in the swollen seed of wheat, the tuber of potato, the stem of

<!-- chunk -->

### kohlrabi, and the flower of cauliflower. Medicinal plants, on the other

<!-- chunk -->

### hand, are swollen, angled, enlarged, or reduced in size by natural

<!-- chunk -->

### stresses exerted by the environment. Yarrow is cut back to the vein and

<!-- chunk -->

### rib in order to contend with dramatic changes in cold and heat. Poplar is

<!-- chunk -->

### softened and moistened to take up water. Oak is hardened and dried to

<!-- chunk -->

### provide structure. Pulsatilla is furred to retain warmth in the cold of

<!-- chunk -->

### spring. These “swellings” are the marks or signatures on the plants,

<!-- chunk -->

### indicating the stresses they have overcome for themselves and the

<!-- chunk -->

### stresses they can help us endure. By the “doctrine of signatures,” the

<!-- chunk -->

### plant looks like what it is for—generally like the organ or the disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### The homeopathic principle of similarity has a different basis. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### founded upon pathogenesis, or the principle that the remedy cures the

<!-- chunk -->

### same symptoms that it causes. In order to develop the homeopathic

<!-- chunk -->

### materia medica, Samuel Hahnemann first collected symptoms from

<!-- chunk -->

### poisonings. Later he “proved” the medicinal substances, giving them to a

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy person in a small dose, until they produced symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is no reason why we should have trouble discriminating

<!-- chunk -->

### between cure and suppression if we understand these simple

<!-- chunk -->

### observations or laws. It is only when we use strong, powerful substances

<!-- chunk -->

### to suppress the organism, or to artificially over-stimulate or sedate it,

<!-- chunk -->

### against its normal inclination, that we will be practicing in a manner

<!-- chunk -->

### which is suppressive rather than curative. Likewise, if we use energetic

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies according to a lack of understanding of the energetic behavior

<!-- chunk -->

### of the organism, as is so often the case in modern homeopathy, we will

<!-- chunk -->

### lack a true comprehension of illness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs can be used in an artificial, suppressive manner. For instance,

<!-- chunk -->

### the killing of bacteria by berberine (found in barberry, Oregon grape,

<!-- chunk -->

### and goldenseal) is an artificial imposition. Bacteria are secondary

<!-- chunk -->

### scavengers, usually following upon a derangement in the tissue state.

<!-- chunk -->

### When the excess in hot or cold, damp or dry, constriction or relaxation is

<!-- chunk -->

### removed, the environment off which the bacteria feeds will be removed.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another method uses echinacea to enhance the body’s own method—

<!-- chunk -->

### white cell production—to kill bacteria. Does the practitioner know that

<!-- chunk -->

### the body really needs increased white cell production? Or is this just a

<!-- chunk -->

### convenient idea, one little fact torn out of the overall context of Nature,

<!-- chunk -->

### which the practitioner, having no idea how the organism really works,

<!-- chunk -->

### or what it really needs, applies according to his or her status as an

<!-- chunk -->

### outsider, a bumbler, an invader? These forcefully induced activities may

<!-- chunk -->

### temporarily, or perhaps permanently, remove acute conditions, but

<!-- chunk -->

### unless they are working with the body, stimulating or sedating it to its

<!-- chunk -->

### own needs, they are imposing an artificial condition which is never

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy. Killing bacteria is often unnatural and detrimental.

<!-- chunk -->

### The important point from the perspective of vitalism is that every herb

<!-- chunk -->

### and poison ultimately has a primal essence or identity pattern and this,

<!-- chunk -->

### whether delivered in a gentle or a toxic envelope, possesses curative

<!-- chunk -->

### power. The essence shines forth through the chemistry, qualities,

<!-- chunk -->

### toxicity, and signatures to show how the agent is to be used

<!-- chunk -->

### appropriately. It teaches the sick, disabled body how to rule supreme in

<!-- chunk -->

### its own environment, to add or expel, dry or lubricate, cool or warm, to

<!-- chunk -->

### maintain natural balance or homeostasis.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Law of Direction of Cure

<!-- chunk -->

### This principle was originally named and described by Constantine

<!-- chunk -->

### Hering, one of the chief students of Samuel Hahnemann. Therefore it is

<!-- chunk -->

### also called “Hering’s Law.” Hering observed that when the organism was

<!-- chunk -->

### healing itself, symptoms moved from the more vital to the less vital

<!-- chunk -->

### organs. They also moved from the top downwards (which is, in another

<!-- chunk -->

### sense, from vital to less vital), and from inside to outside. One of

<!-- chunk -->

### Hering’s successors, Dr. James Tyler Kent, called this “government from

<!-- chunk -->

### center to circumference.” We can easily see that the hypothalamus, as

<!-- chunk -->

### regulator of the balance between interior and exterior, follows this law.

<!-- chunk -->

### In fact, all vital activities in the organism conform to this principle.

<!-- chunk -->

### In disease, the organism protects the more vital organs, producing

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms towards the exterior and channels of elimination. In health it

<!-- chunk -->

### also acts in this direction as the disease is driven out. Only as the disease

<!-- chunk -->

### deepens do symptoms and locations become more vital and deeper. This

<!-- chunk -->

### law can therefore be used to monitor healing and disease. It also teaches

<!-- chunk -->

### that, as the organism gets better, the symptoms leave in a reverse order

<!-- chunk -->

### to that in which they arrived.

<!-- chunk -->

### The law of direction of cure was picked up by Dr. Lindlahr and

<!-- chunk -->

### recognized by some naturopaths and nature healers. It was known to Dr.

<!-- chunk -->

### Christopher and is sometimes mentioned in herbal literature. Dr. Jensen

<!-- chunk -->

### (1979, 31–33) called it the “reversal process.” Eva Graf and Lalitha

<!-- chunk -->

### Thomas, herbalists influenced by these writers, also use this phrase.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Law of the Healing Crisis

<!-- chunk -->

### When the correct remedy has been given, the symptoms leave from

<!-- chunk -->

### interior to exterior, from vital to less vital, and from top to bottom. As

<!-- chunk -->

### they come out they usually come to a “head.” There is a “healing crisis”

<!-- chunk -->

### during which the disease is re-experienced in its full nature—sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### psychological, sometimes physical, sometimes both. The homeopathic

<!-- chunk -->

### physicians called this the “aggravation.” They did not think of it as a law

<!-- chunk -->

### of healing, but it is. The idea of a “law of the healing crisis” goes all the

<!-- chunk -->

### way back to Hippocrates.

<!-- chunk -->

### The Hippocratic physicians used a single method of healing no matter

<!-- chunk -->

### what the nature of the illness or the constitution of the patient. This

<!-- chunk -->

### technique is today called the “detoxification fast.” A person is placed on

<!-- chunk -->

### a light diet that removes all foods causing an encumbrance on the self-

<!-- chunk -->

### healing ability of the organism. After a while, the organism begins to

<!-- chunk -->

### “detoxify.” That is to say, digestive, metabolic, and eliminative channels

<!-- chunk -->

### that were not working in the past begin to work again, causing an

<!-- chunk -->

### increase in tissue feeding and cleansing. The result is a healing crisis or

<!-- chunk -->

### detoxification. The body begins to send out all the stored up toxins that

<!-- chunk -->

### it has not been able to eliminate. The sudden elimination causes a

<!-- chunk -->

### “crisis,” or the appearance of a disease. Afterwards, the patient is

<!-- chunk -->

### cleansed and well, unless the discharges showed the Hippocratic

<!-- chunk -->

### physicians that the internal cleansing was incomplete.

<!-- chunk -->

### This principle mimics nature. Lindlahr observed that there was a

<!-- chunk -->

### “disease crisis” created when the disease took command of the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Instead of a detoxification, there is toxification as the body loses the

<!-- chunk -->

### ability to feed and cleanse itself appropriately. This is relived and

<!-- chunk -->

### released in the healing crisis.

<!-- chunk -->

### The law of the healing crisis is observed when herbal and

<!-- chunk -->

### homeopathic substances are used. In homeopathy it is often noted that

<!-- chunk -->

### the appropriate remedy causes an aggravation of the symptoms. In

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalism, there is a tendency to use herbs to detoxify in a heavy-handed

<!-- chunk -->

### manner. Laxatives and liver stimulants are given to fire up the channels

<!-- chunk -->

### of metabolism and elimination. In fact, what is more important is that

<!-- chunk -->

### the well-selected herb or formula causes the body to react by cleansing

<!-- chunk -->

### itself.

<!-- chunk -->

### Ultimately, the law of the healing crisis correlates with what today is

<!-- chunk -->

### called the “body memory.” There is no true healing until the trauma

<!-- chunk -->

### remembered by the bodily intelligence is eliminated. This is encoded

<!-- chunk -->

### into the body, resulting in the production of characteristic symptoms and

<!-- chunk -->

### discharges, until the body can relive the trauma fully through the

<!-- chunk -->

### healing crisis and be released from its sleep of enchantment.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes the healing crisis will come when a person is not doing

<!-- chunk -->

### anything in particular to seek a cure. Dr. Bernard Jensen (1979, 34)

<!-- chunk -->

### makes a very insightful comment. “A crisis comes usually after you feel

<!-- chunk -->

### your best. It is the will of nature. No doctor, no patient, no food, can

<!-- chunk -->

### bring a crisis on. It comes when your body is ready.” However, he says

<!-- chunk -->

### that we can “earn” the crisis by hard work.

<!-- chunk -->

### A similar event occurs just before a severe deterioration or death. A

<!-- chunk -->

### person suddenly feels better, as if himself again. Then, a few days later

<!-- chunk -->

### he is sent to meet his maker.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Practice of Herbal Medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### Once we have isolated the kind, seat, and context of the disease, we can

<!-- chunk -->

### apply the specific remedy, or several interrelated specifics, or a well-

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated compound. Let us consider an example of how this works. Say

<!-- chunk -->

### we have determined that we have a condition of heat/irritation of the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach. In addition, we note the context: the skin is dry and red, so

<!-- chunk -->

### there is heat blocked in the periphery of the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### We have a half dozen common sedatives for gastric heat and irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

### We also have a dozen more remedies which, though they do not as act

<!-- chunk -->

### primarily on the stomach or heat/irritation, might under certain

<!-- chunk -->

### circumstances. We also have several agents that might open the skin and

<!-- chunk -->

### release the internal heat. In order to arrive at a final prescription, we

<!-- chunk -->

### need understand which of these remedies is suited to this particular

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalance. In order to do this we need a knowledge of the specific

<!-- chunk -->

### indications associated with each medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Most of the remedies for gastric heat/irritation have the following

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms: elongated tongue with red tip and body; stomach burning

<!-- chunk -->

### and acidity; acid eructations and reflux; some amount of nervousness,

<!-- chunk -->

### insomnia, excess thinking. or worrying; and amelioration from water

<!-- chunk -->

### (since it dilutes acids). However, each remedy has differentiating

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms that “separate it from the crowd.” For instance, Rheum

<!-- chunk -->

### (rhubarb) is more precise where there are constrictive pains in the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach or abdomen (irritation combined with constriction), while

<!-- chunk -->

### Rumex crispus (yellow dock root) has a loose, weak feeling in the rectum

<!-- chunk -->

### after stool (irritation and relaxation). Both these remedies have more of

<!-- chunk -->

### a dark red color in the mucosa, tongue, and skin, whereas Amygdalus

<!-- chunk -->

### (peach) has a tongue that is more red/pink or carmine and a skin that

<!-- chunk -->

### looks more like it is dry, sensitive, or lightly sunburned. Melissa has

<!-- chunk -->

### more anxiety in the symptom picture but not the outright fear of its

<!-- chunk -->

### cousin, Lycopus. Hypericum, Lycopus, and Amygdalus are suited to more

<!-- chunk -->

### drying in association with heat than the others are. The skin of Rumex

<!-- chunk -->

### may even weep serum. Hypericum has more association with atrophy of

<!-- chunk -->

### the stomach, where there are wrongs of instinct and weaknesses in the

<!-- chunk -->

### solar plexus. Sambucus can have heat in the stomach and a dry, red skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### Althea would be indicated if there is drying out of the internal fluids

<!-- chunk -->

### (red, cracked tongue). By careful study we can determine which of these

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies is most likely to be the specific, curative medicine, or we can

<!-- chunk -->

### select a few compatible remedies to work together.

<!-- chunk -->

### Let’s illustrate specific medicine with some examples from practice.

<!-- chunk -->

### Recently I was called to see a young woman who was vomiting through

<!-- chunk -->

### the fifth to tenth weeks of her first pregnancy. She would retch hour

<!-- chunk -->

### after hour until she was dehydrated and exhausted. She had been to the

<!-- chunk -->

### hospital four times, once a week. They gave her intravenous fluids and

<!-- chunk -->

### nutrition. The drugs did not stop the retching. Every drop of fluid or

<!-- chunk -->

### food at home would set off another round of nausea. She was now

<!-- chunk -->

### getting dizzy, had a headache all the time and tightness in the shoulders.

<!-- chunk -->

### The tip of the tongue was bright red, extending to a light red along the

<!-- chunk -->

### sides. This indicated that the nausea was from heat. One remedy is

<!-- chunk -->

### specific for a red tipped tongue with nausea, worse from every drop of

<!-- chunk -->

### fluid or food. I put some peach twig tincture on her wrists (she did not

<!-- chunk -->

### want to take alcohol) and the symptoms subsided literally within

<!-- chunk -->

### seconds. Drinking water with peach twig tincture (boiled to take out the

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol) cured the condition in a short time. She and her husband were

<!-- chunk -->

### very grateful. They had no health insurance.

<!-- chunk -->

### I could not help but recall a similar case, about ten years previous,

<!-- chunk -->

### which had also been treated with a specific remedy. A thirty-one-year-

<!-- chunk -->

### old woman had been nauseated and retching from the moment of

<!-- chunk -->

### conception. She had an abortion after a month because of the misery,

<!-- chunk -->

### but this did not stop the painful vomiting, which continued another

<!-- chunk -->

### month. Her pulse felt sharply tense. She was kind of crouching or

<!-- chunk -->

### contorted from the tension. I gave Lobelia 6x. The herb would have been

<!-- chunk -->

### just as good, but the homeopathic was at hand. The cramp stopped

<!-- chunk -->

### literally immediately and she wet the chair with menstrual blood before

<!-- chunk -->

### she could get to the bathroom. Nothing more was needed.

<!-- chunk -->

### These two cases resemble each other but in one the symptoms indicate

<!-- chunk -->

### heat while in the other they indicate tension. Because the remedies were

<!-- chunk -->

### specific to nausea from heat, and nausea from tension respectively, they

<!-- chunk -->

### brought prompt and lasting cure.

<!-- chunk -->

### Prompt and radical cure is what we can expect from specific diagnosis

<!-- chunk -->

### and medication, but only some of the time. Unfortunately, we often do

<!-- chunk -->

### not always get a clear picture of the underlying cause or location of the

<!-- chunk -->

### disease, or enough information to find a specific. Sometimes we

<!-- chunk -->

### prescribe without really knowing what is going on inside the organism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes we see a specific indication pointing to a remedy and get a

<!-- chunk -->

### good cure without understanding what was going on. Our method is

<!-- chunk -->

### precise but the field is vast and not completely knowable.

<!-- chunk -->

### We must remember the first aphorism of Hippocrates: “The art so long

<!-- chunk -->

### to study, the life so short.” It is not possible for the young practitioner to

<!-- chunk -->

### master the vast field of herbal medicine. Generally, the beginner learns

<!-- chunk -->

### where his or her facility is greatest. Some people are better at

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding the body, while others are better with the herbs. These

<!-- chunk -->

### limitations are true in my case.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here is a case history illustrating successful prescription from a

<!-- chunk -->

### paucity of symptoms. The patient was a seven-year-old girl, thin, with

<!-- chunk -->

### fine hair and a somewhat refined, intelligent disposition. She had

<!-- chunk -->

### stomachaches that were increasing in intensity over the previous month

<!-- chunk -->

### to the point where she was now afraid to eat and was eating less. The

<!-- chunk -->

### doctor found nothing wrong. The pains come and go slowly, after eating,

<!-- chunk -->

### and are situated in the abdomen above the navel. There is no gas. She

<!-- chunk -->

### had a dairy allergy as a younger child, and was nursed for over a year.

<!-- chunk -->

### As an infant she used to spit up a lot. In addition, she has had

<!-- chunk -->

### incontinence since age three. Her tongue is slightly coated. The lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### gas indicated that dry/atrophy was not the problem. Damp/relaxation

<!-- chunk -->

### seemed more likely, with the easy spitting-up of infancy, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### incontinence. I gave a tincture of two parts Rumex crispus, Comptonia,

<!-- chunk -->

### and Alchemilla, one part Quercus, and three parts Osmunda. To my

<!-- chunk -->

### surprise, both the stomach ache and the incontinence were cured in two

<!-- chunk -->

### weeks. About a year later, the child was brought back for other

<!-- chunk -->

### problems. Generally, this is what we have to expect when we have only

<!-- chunk -->

### prescribed generally and have not gotten the specific cure.

<!-- chunk -->

### The beginning herbalist has my sympathy. At first, cure is a rumor. We

<!-- chunk -->

### hear a story about healing with herbs. The good teacher builds on this

<!-- chunk -->

### simple platform. He or she tells stories about healing that convey the

<!-- chunk -->

### authenticity of herbal medicine to the student. Later the student sees

<!-- chunk -->

### cure, and it becomes a personal experience. Subsequently, it becomes

<!-- chunk -->

### more and more common. Finally, practice becomes systematic. In the

<!-- chunk -->

### end, the herbalist will evolve a personal, individualized method. The

<!-- chunk -->

### teacher needs to stand back, let go, and let another herbalist prosper.

<!-- chunk -->

### It takes about fifteen years of continual practice to begin to see many

<!-- chunk -->

### connections and fill in the blanks. “The study is difficult,” concedes

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder (1874, iv). Eventually the time comes when one can stand on

<!-- chunk -->

### the mountaintop, overlooking a vast but sensible field of medicine plants

<!-- chunk -->

### and knowledge. Herbs and their uses, the body and its reactions, become

<!-- chunk -->

### more and more predictable. Even then, the final secrets belong to Dame

<!-- chunk -->

### Nature and the Creator.


<!-- chunk -->

### If you raise the stream level with the fountain, it stops the current

<!-- chunk -->

### and all motion will cease, and the same effects will follow by

<!-- chunk -->

### lowering the fountain to a level with the stream. When the outward

<!-- chunk -->

### heat becomes equal with the inward … cold assumes the power

<!-- chunk -->

### and death takes place.

<!-- chunk -->

### —S

AMUEL

<!-- chunk -->

### T

HOMSON

<!-- chunk -->

### he old granny doctors and traditional physicians looked to the

<!-- chunk -->

### “condition of the skin,” as they called it, to understand what was

<!-- chunk -->

### going on deeper in the body. Although they did not know modern

<!-- chunk -->

### physiology, their approach stood on solid scientific ground because the

<!-- chunk -->

### condition of the skin reflects the condition of the grand regulator of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, the hypothalamus. The latter regulates the periphery as a “shield”

<!-- chunk -->

### to protect the interior organs from sudden environmental changes. It

<!-- chunk -->

### opens and closes the “vents” of the periphery to increase or decrease

<!-- chunk -->

### blood volume and perspiration. It signals the sympathetic to induce

<!-- chunk -->

### shivering. Temperature-sensitive mechanisms in the center and

<!-- chunk -->

### periphery send information to the hypothalamus, so that it can regulate

<!-- chunk -->

### the overall body temperature. The center is slightly warmer (99.4°), so

<!-- chunk -->

### there is a flow of heat outwards to the periphery (98.6°). The

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothalamus also largely governs the interior by secreting hormones

<!-- chunk -->

### that oversee endocrine regulation of temperature, fluids, solids,

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism, growth, maturation, and reproduction. Through regulation

<!-- chunk -->

### of the thyroid it exerts a control on metabolism and circulation to the

<!-- chunk -->

### periphery.

<!-- chunk -->

### The old granny healers and traditional physicians manipulated these

<!-- chunk -->

### “vents” to cure fevers and correct weaknesses resulting from fever. The

<!-- chunk -->

### skin might remain too dried out or too prone to sweating, too full of

<!-- chunk -->

### blood and hot, or empty, cool, and pale. Shivering might continue long

<!-- chunk -->

### after the original malaria or influenza had disappeared. They observed

<!-- chunk -->

### that when a fever “broke,” sweat appeared on the skin. Thus, they used

<!-- chunk -->

### diaphoretics to induce sweating and clear fever. This was the primary

<!-- chunk -->

### technique of clearing acute disease in traditional medicine, not “germ-

<!-- chunk -->

### killing” or “immune-boosting.” By following out these processes they

<!-- chunk -->

### learned that the skin acted as an eliminative channel for waste material

<!-- chunk -->

### produced by the illness in the deeper tissues and organs. This led to the

<!-- chunk -->

### realization that there was an orderly flow within the organism, from

<!-- chunk -->

### interior to exterior.

<!-- chunk -->

### A particularly lucid summary of these ideas occurs in Samuel

<!-- chunk -->

### Thomson’s work. His mentor, the “widow Benton,” taught him the

<!-- chunk -->

### importance of diaphoresis. Later he drew a metaphorical picture of the

<!-- chunk -->

### way the body works in health and disease. He likened it to a fountain.

<!-- chunk -->

### The stomach is the center. If the fires of digestion are strong the body is

<!-- chunk -->

### nourished and the “inner fire” radiating towards the surface will be

<!-- chunk -->

### strong. Heat, life, and nourishment will flow outward to the periphery. If

<!-- chunk -->

### the pores of the skin and other channels of transportation and

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination are open, the organism will clean itself out. However, if the

<!-- chunk -->

### center is cold, phlegm coats the stomach, preventing digestion and

<!-- chunk -->

### keeping it “cold.” Or, if the periphery is blocked, there will be faulty

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination. As long as the fountain bubbles forth from interior to

<!-- chunk -->

### exterior there will be health, but if the heat in the center dies down or

<!-- chunk -->

### the heat at the periphery is raised by cold blocking an outlet, the flow

<!-- chunk -->

### stops. Thomson did not equate death with cold, but with the

<!-- chunk -->

### equalization of the inner temperature with the outer. It was the lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### flow that caused life to cease. Homeopathy elegantly expresses the same

<!-- chunk -->

### basic idea in the “law of direction of cure,” or “Hering’s law.”

<!-- chunk -->

### These concepts are not just the daydreams of alternative practitioners,

<!-- chunk -->

### but reflect physiological activities. The peripheral vents are manipulated

<!-- chunk -->

### by the hypothalamus to counteract environmental stress. If the surface is

<!-- chunk -->

### chilled or overheated, it will take the necessary measures to return the

<!-- chunk -->

### surface to a healthy balance. It really is true that we can “catch” a cold

<!-- chunk -->

### because external cold can overwhelm these peripheral, adaptive

<!-- chunk -->

### mechanisms of the body, causing a physiological trauma which remains

<!-- chunk -->

### with the organism after the external stimuli has passed on. Any such

<!-- chunk -->

### trauma invites in microorganisms, which live off the imbalanced

<!-- chunk -->

### environment. They secrete toxins which inhibit the normal bodily

<!-- chunk -->

### functions to insure their continuing propagation at the expense of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body. Of course, it is also true that the penetration of a bacteria or virus

<!-- chunk -->

### can be the initial causative agent, as well as “cold.” Even then, the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism creates a change in the internal environment analogous to the

<!-- chunk -->

### effect of heat or cold, so the end result is very much the same.

<!-- chunk -->

### The modern approach is to “kill the bacteria.” Thus, we have

<!-- chunk -->

### antibiotics, while alternative practitioners, aping the mainstream, use

<!-- chunk -->

### their various “natural” antibiotics. This, however, was not the approach

<!-- chunk -->

### of the traditional healers. They did not try to kill the little critters

<!-- chunk -->

### directly, but to change the environment that either led to the invasion in

<!-- chunk -->

### the first place or was caused by the invasion. This is the reason why

<!-- chunk -->

### diaphoretics play such an important part in the old literature.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sometimes the disease moves further into the body, causing acute

<!-- chunk -->

### respiratory disease or “stomach flu.” It is for this reason that traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### healers around the world used emetics as well as diaphoretics. The lungs

<!-- chunk -->

### and stomach are both innervated by the vagus nerve (called the

<!-- chunk -->

### pneumogastric in the old literature). Stimulation of the vagus tends to

<!-- chunk -->

### cause ejection through expectoration or emesis. It also raises the cold

<!-- chunk -->

### phlegm coating the stomach and dampening the internal fire, a matter of

<!-- chunk -->

### great concern to Thomson and to traditional Ayurvedic medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### If the peripheral barrier is pierced, the skin will assume a disturbed

<!-- chunk -->

### character—too hot, cold, damp, dry. The disease will then move to a

<!-- chunk -->

### deeper level. The lymphatic vessels under the skin will be engaged

<!-- chunk -->

### bringing in white cells to kill bacteria and carrying away dead white

<!-- chunk -->

### cells, bacteria, and toxins. There will be stress on the lymphatic system,

<!-- chunk -->

### which may cause tenderness, swelling, and congestion in the glands.

<!-- chunk -->

### Other peripheral tissues, such as the muscles, will be affected by the

<!-- chunk -->

### increase of fluids in the interstitial spaces around the engorged blood

<!-- chunk -->

### and lymph vessels, resulting in soreness and stiffness. It is only at this

<!-- chunk -->

### level that the body itself begins “critter killin’ ” with various white cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### If the disease process is not held in the periphery it will move to the

<!-- chunk -->

### deeper organs, at last causing problems at the level of function and

<!-- chunk -->

### regulation. The liver will be stressed to detoxify an additional load of

<!-- chunk -->

### dead bacteria, white cells, and toxins. The kidneys will be stressed to

<!-- chunk -->

### carry away protein waste products. Indeed, we often find that the urine

<!-- chunk -->

### becomes scanty and concentrated, and water is retained, before the fever

<!-- chunk -->

### reaches its releasing crisis. The pulse is increased to move the blood

<!-- chunk -->

### through the congestion in the periphery in a fever or chill, causing the

<!-- chunk -->

### heart to speed up.

<!-- chunk -->

### With increased pulse there is often a shift towards the sympathetic

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous system. Thus, the disease finally affects the centers regulating

<!-- chunk -->

### the metabolism, fluids, and solids of the body, as well as the nervous and

<!-- chunk -->

### endocrine systems. These are themselves largely regulated by the

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothalamus. The entire defensive mechanism, from beginning to end,

<!-- chunk -->

### is more or less under the dominion of this powerful “master gland.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Three Functional Levels

<!-- chunk -->

### There are three basic levels or spheres of activity in the organism. The

<!-- chunk -->

### most internal is the regulatory system that rules both the periphery

<!-- chunk -->

### where the defensive mechanisms are situated and the vital organs that

<!-- chunk -->

### undertake the basic functions of life—nutrition, metabolism, elimination,

<!-- chunk -->

### and so forth. From outer to inner we have:

<!-- chunk -->

## DefensivePeripheral “vents” (skin, mucosa)

<!-- chunk -->

## FunctionalVital organs

<!-- chunk -->

## RegulatoryNeuroendocrine system, hypothalamus

<!-- chunk -->

### Health problems tend to penetrate from the exterior, deranging the

<!-- chunk -->

### vital organs, and then the regulatory system. Problems can also move in

<!-- chunk -->

### the opposite direction—from interior to exterior. This is where the

<!-- chunk -->

### theory of toxemia enters the picture. The vital organs are not able to

<!-- chunk -->

### handle their work so there are metabolic leftovers that seek an outlet

<!-- chunk -->

### through the peripheral or defensive layer. The problem could even begin

<!-- chunk -->

### on the regulatory level with changes in temperature and water

<!-- chunk -->

### regulation percolating up through the organs to the surface.

<!-- chunk -->

### An important observation to be made is that the skin will always show

<!-- chunk -->

### the “dragon tracks” of disease. If the disease has penetrated from the

<!-- chunk -->

### exterior, the skin will remain too red and hot, or too cold and pale, or

<!-- chunk -->

### too dry, or sweat excessively, showing the “track” the disease took to get

<!-- chunk -->

### to the interior. If the disease originated in an internal weakness it causes

<!-- chunk -->

### a disturbance in the skin. Toxins, excessive heat, cold, dry, or damp will

<!-- chunk -->

### try to move from the interior to the exterior, in an effort to unburden the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism.

<!-- chunk -->

### I almost always find that when the appropriate remedy has been

<!-- chunk -->

### selected the skin will change to a more healthy expression. We do not

<!-- chunk -->

### always need to treat the deep internal organs and glands. Sometimes all

<!-- chunk -->

### we need to do is correct the condition of the skin, clear channels of

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination, remove toxins, or get the lymph moving. These are precisely

<!-- chunk -->

### the sorts of things that the old healers accompanied by changing such

<!-- chunk -->

### simple agents as food, water, exercise, and herbs.

<!-- chunk -->

### Due to limitations of space, in this volume we will only examine the

<!-- chunk -->

### defensive and regulatory levels. The neuroendocrine is the leading

<!-- chunk -->

### regulatory system from a modern perceptive, while fever and blood are

<!-- chunk -->

### the leading “systems” in nineteenth-century herbal medicine. Many of

<!-- chunk -->

### the most important remedies in the Western herbal materia medica act

<!-- chunk -->

### on the defensive level (fever), regulation, and blood. We count here the

<!-- chunk -->

### great febrifuges, diaphoretics, nervines, blood builders, antiseptics, and

<!-- chunk -->

### blood purifiers of Western herbalism.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Primal Tradition

<!-- chunk -->

### The importance of the skin, the self-governance of the organism, and

<!-- chunk -->

### regulation from center to circumference are features of traditional folk

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. Samuel Thomson was one of the few folk doctors to work

<!-- chunk -->

### them into a consistent medical philosophy. He learned his basic system

<!-- chunk -->

### by observing the practice of Mrs. Benton, an herbalist who treated his

<!-- chunk -->

### family when he was a child (Narrative, 1825, 15):

<!-- chunk -->

### There was an old lady by the name of Benton lived near us, who

<!-- chunk -->

### used to attend our family when there was any sickness. At that

<!-- chunk -->

### time there was no such thing as a Doctor known among us; there

<!-- chunk -->

### not being any within ten miles. The whole of her practice was

<!-- chunk -->

### with roots and herbs, applied to the patient, or given in hot

<!-- chunk -->

### drinks, to produce sweating; which always answered the purpose.

<!-- chunk -->

### When one thing did not produce the desired effect, she would try

<!-- chunk -->

### something else, till they were relieved. By her attention to the

<!-- chunk -->

### family, and the benefits they received from her skill, we became

<!-- chunk -->

### very much attached to her; and when she used to go out to collect

<!-- chunk -->

### roots and herbs, she would take me with her, and learn me their

<!-- chunk -->

### names, with what they were good for; and I used to be very

<!-- chunk -->

### curious in my inquiries, and in tasting every thing I found. The

<!-- chunk -->

### information I thus obtained at this early age, was afterwards of

<!-- chunk -->

### great use to me.

<!-- chunk -->

### “Mrs. Benton” has been tentatively identified as Mehitable Crane, wife of

<!-- chunk -->

### Elijah Benton, of Surrey, New Hampshire (Kingsbury, 1925, 904).

<!-- chunk -->

### Years later, when Thomson was working out his medical doctrines, he

<!-- chunk -->

### returned to Mrs. Benton’s basic practice, concluding that perspiration

<!-- chunk -->

### was the central element in successful treatment (Narrative, 1825, 43):

<!-- chunk -->

### I found that all disorders which the human family were afflicted

<!-- chunk -->

### with, however various the symptoms, and different the names by

<!-- chunk -->

### which they are called, arise directly from obstructed perspiration,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is always caused by cold, or want of heat; for if there is a

<!-- chunk -->

### natural heat, it is impossible but that there must be a natural

<!-- chunk -->

### perspiration.

<!-- chunk -->

### This same complex of ideas is found in Latin American folk medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### In 1985 anthropologist George M. Foster published an article defining

<!-- chunk -->

### basic treatment methods for the first time (“How to Get Well in

<!-- chunk -->

### Tzintzuntzan,” Sci. Soc. Med., v. 21, pp. 809–818). The four primary

<!-- chunk -->

### methods of healing are (1) sweating medicines (or the sweat lodge in

<!-- chunk -->

### Indian communities); (2) warming medicines; (3) the removal of

<!-- chunk -->

### empacho, a mucus-like substance lining the digestive tract; and (4)

<!-- chunk -->

### counteracting the evil eye. Except for the final method, Latin folk

<!-- chunk -->

### practice is similar to Thomson medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### This complex of ideas probably represents the bedrock of European

<!-- chunk -->

### peasant medicine meeting up with the vigorous indigenous practices of

<!-- chunk -->

### Indian America. Thomson’s basic theories bear the stamp of both

<!-- chunk -->

### traditions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Thomson’s folk-medical system also shows surprising analogies to the

<!-- chunk -->

### most famous and basic tenets of Ayurvedic medicine. In this system, the

<!-- chunk -->

### digestive/metabolic fire in the center of the organism is visualized as

<!-- chunk -->

### residing in the pyloric sphincter between the stomach and duodenum. At

<!-- chunk -->

### the same time, this primal fire is present throughout the body to allow

<!-- chunk -->

### feeding and waste removal. It is called the agni, the Sanskrit word for

<!-- chunk -->

### fire (related to the Latin root in the words igneous and ignition.) The

<!-- chunk -->

### agni is responsible for digestion, metabolism, gastric secretion, enzymes,

<!-- chunk -->

### immunity, and destruction of microorganisms, bacteria, viruses, and

<!-- chunk -->

### parasites. It also enhances perception, comprehension, and intelligence.

<!-- chunk -->

### When the agni is impaired, food particles are not entirely broken down

<!-- chunk -->

### or utilized, resulting in sluggish digestion, incomplete metabolism, dirty,

<!-- chunk -->

### clogged tissues, decreased immunity, increased disease processes, and

<!-- chunk -->

### obscuration of the mind.

<!-- chunk -->

### The offending materials, which escape the grasp of the agni and then

<!-- chunk -->

### work to oppose its influence in the organism, are called amma or toxins.

<!-- chunk -->

### The primary properties of amma are that it is heavy, cloudy, cold, slimy,

<!-- chunk -->

### thick, impure, and putrid. Putrefaction of amma causes a toxic, septic, or

<!-- chunk -->

### putrid heat that is disease producing. Characteristic symptoms of amma

<!-- chunk -->

### are loss of taste and appetite, coated tongue, indigestion, bad breath, bad

<!-- chunk -->

### odor of the body, urine and feces, loss of mental clarity, depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### irritability, lack of attention, heaviness, lethargy, fatigue, loss of

<!-- chunk -->

### strength, accumulation of waste materials, deep, heavy, or dull pulse.

<!-- chunk -->

### The Ayurvedic model emphasizes sweating, vomiting, and purging as

<!-- chunk -->

### means of eliminating amma and keeping the organism healthy.

<!-- chunk -->

### The basic tenets of Thomson’s system are three: opening the exterior,

<!-- chunk -->

### warming the periphery, and removing canker, humors, or toxins

<!-- chunk -->

### accumulating in the interior. Let us look at these three basic methods.

<!-- chunk -->

## Opening the Exterior

<!-- chunk -->

### Throughout Indian America the sweat lodge was, and still is, the primary

<!-- chunk -->

### center for physical and spiritual healing. The lodge is not only a place

<!-- chunk -->

### where spirits talk, but where people perspire. The fire is hot, the water

<!-- chunk -->

### splashes on the rocks, the vapor rises, and the pores open. The Indian

<!-- chunk -->

### healers who helped their pioneer neighbors did not usually speak good

<!-- chunk -->

### English or describe what they were doing, but the pioneers learned by

<!-- chunk -->

### observing their methods. They adopted a simplified, non-ceremonial

<!-- chunk -->

### form of the sweat lodge or used diaphoretic herbal draughts to open the

<!-- chunk -->

### skin and raise a sweat.

<!-- chunk -->

### Lewis and Clark tell us how a passing Indian hunter cured one of their

<!-- chunk -->

### men of a debilitating muscular stiffening by erecting a sweat lodge. This

<!-- chunk -->

### was not an exceptional experience on the frontier. Many settlers

<!-- chunk -->

### observed the Indians sweating themselves to cure. Not surprisingly,

<!-- chunk -->

### “steaming doctors” were common in pioneer society in the 1820s and

<!-- chunk -->

### ’30s. Professional doctors followed suit, inventing steaming devices for

<!-- chunk -->

### use in more sophisticated settings. These crossed the ocean and were

<!-- chunk -->

### widely used in Europe. There it was noted that the Finns used a sweat

<!-- chunk -->

### lodge for their health.

<!-- chunk -->

### One of Thomson’s students, A. I. Coffin, describes how he learned the

<!-- chunk -->

### value of sweating from an old Indian practitioner. As a small boy, he fell

<!-- chunk -->

### sick with a respiratory condition. The doctors failed to cure him and he

<!-- chunk -->

### was given up to die. About that time an old “gipsy-looking woman” of a

<!-- chunk -->

### neighboring remnant of the Seneca came by his house. She saw him and

<!-- chunk -->

### commented, “white man no cure you.” She spoke to his mother and

<!-- chunk -->

### offered to doctor him in exchange for a gallon of cider (a reasonable

<!-- chunk -->

### payment for medical services in those days). The old woman was

<!-- chunk -->

### employed. The stranger terrified young Albert Isaiah, but to please his

<!-- chunk -->

### mother he agreed to cooperate. The woman went out in the woods and

<!-- chunk -->

### returned with some medicines, one of which Coffin recognized as prickly

<!-- chunk -->

### ash. She made a strong decoction and the boy drank. In a short while he

<!-- chunk -->

### experienced, as he says, “a comfortable glow diffusing itself over my

<!-- chunk -->

### whole frame.” Soon, “a pleasant moisture bedewed my hands, and I felt

<!-- chunk -->

### as one reprieved from a sentence of death.” He exclaimed with joy, “The

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine will cure me” (original sources quoted by Barbara Griggs,

<!-- chunk -->

### 1981, 200). The full cure took several weeks. In his Botanic Guide, Coffin

<!-- chunk -->

### says simply, “We stand indebted to that poor unlettered Indian woman”

<!-- chunk -->

### (1890, 11).

<!-- chunk -->

### Coffin later went on to practice Thomson herbalism and brought it to

<!-- chunk -->

### Great Britain. The fact that his teachings met with enormous success

<!-- chunk -->

### indicates that, again, these teachings were recognized by the common

<!-- chunk -->

### people. Thomson herbalism was widely used by the English working

<!-- chunk -->

### class until the middle of the twentieth century.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another frontier doctor, Beal P. Downing, describes the Indian mode

<!-- chunk -->

### of sweating in Reformed Practice and Family Physician (1851, 161):

<!-- chunk -->

### I was among the Indians in the West [i.e., Midwest] four years,

<!-- chunk -->

### and did not find but one down with a fever; but saw many

<!-- chunk -->

### sweating themselves over a hot stone, by turning water on it, and

<!-- chunk -->

### covered with a blanket. Fever is the same in all countries, and

<!-- chunk -->

### with all classes of the human species, at the commencement; and

<!-- chunk -->

### the same thing will cure all, if taken in season. Some are attacked

<!-- chunk -->

### more violently than others, and then they are alarmed and send

<!-- chunk -->

### for the doctor, but he cannot cure it unless he can open the pores

<!-- chunk -->

### of the skin, and warm the stomach. Bleeding and giving cold

<!-- chunk -->

### minerals runs the patient down.

<!-- chunk -->

### This was also the essential method Downing had been taught in

<!-- chunk -->

### medical school by Nathan Smith, one of the leading lights of medical

<!-- chunk -->

### education in New England at the beginning of the nineteenth century.

<!-- chunk -->

### Downing writes: “I received a part of my knowledge of cure and disease

<!-- chunk -->

### of the great Dr. Smith, who lectured and dissected at Dartmouth College,

<!-- chunk -->

### in New Hampshire, forty years ago. He told me that a sweat at the

<!-- chunk -->

### commencement of a fever was better for a patient than all the medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### in the shops; for, says he, sweat opens the pores. Then give physic

<!-- chunk -->

### [purgatives] and strengthening bitters. It is all that is wanted in the early

<!-- chunk -->

### stages of a fever.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Smith’s method differs somewhat from that attributed by Downing to

<!-- chunk -->

### the Indian people. The former consists of sweating, purging, and

<!-- chunk -->

### strengthening, while the native approach is to “open the pores” and

<!-- chunk -->

### “warm the stomach.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The importance of keeping the skin open was known to some of the

<!-- chunk -->

### highest society doctors in America and Europe. Dr. Joseph Kidd,

<!-- chunk -->

### physician to Benjamin Disraeli, treated his eminent client by

<!-- chunk -->

### reestablishing long-absent perspiration through use of a “lamp bath.” In

<!-- chunk -->

### The Last Illness of Lord Beaconsfield (Trueherz, 195, 103), Dr. Kidd writes:

<!-- chunk -->

### The albuminuria (Bright’s disease of the kidney) was wasting his

<!-- chunk -->

### strength and was much aggravated by the dry state of the skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### Regarding it as the most important indication for treatment, I

<!-- chunk -->

### advised the regular use of a lamp bath at bedtime for fifteen

<!-- chunk -->

### minutes, and to sleep in a nightdress and sheets of soft Saxony

<!-- chunk -->

### flannel. At first he rebelled at this. “You say you want me to

<!-- chunk -->

### perspire. I never did since I was born. I have had a dry skin all my

<!-- chunk -->

### life. It is useless for you to try.” But he obeyed as I explained its

<!-- chunk -->

### importance for the relief of the kidneys.

<!-- chunk -->
