### About three months after this treatment was commenced at one

<!-- chunk -->

### of my morning visits, while I was feeling his skin carefully, he

<!-- chunk -->

### looked up with a most genial smile. “You have conquered,” he

<!-- chunk -->

### said; “I perspired freely last night.” Gradually his skin became soft

<!-- chunk -->

### like a child’s and a gentle moisture all night added to the comfort

<!-- chunk -->

### of his life; the morning depression vanished and the albuminuria

<!-- chunk -->

### lessened, his old freshness and vigor returned.

<!-- chunk -->

### This was in 1878, when Disraeli was expected to die. He lived until

<!-- chunk -->

### 1881, when he finally succumbed to Bright’s Disease.

<!-- chunk -->

## Warming the Interior

<!-- chunk -->

### Thomson saw the importance of maintaining the central fire, as well as

<!-- chunk -->

### opening the periphery. The stomach was the seat of this central fire and

<!-- chunk -->

### it must be kept warm and healthy (Botanic Guide, 1825, 8): “The

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach is the deposit from which the whole body is supported. The

<!-- chunk -->

### heat is maintained in the stomach by consuming food; and all the body

<!-- chunk -->

### and limbs receive their proportion of nourishment and heat from that

<!-- chunk -->

### source; as the whole room is warmed by the fire which is consumed in

<!-- chunk -->

### the fireplace.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The natural heat in the center of the body is continually radiating out

<!-- chunk -->

### to the periphery, dominating the economy of the body in all its activities

<!-- chunk -->

### and parts, maintaining it against the cold, toxins, and attacks from the

<!-- chunk -->

### outward environment. Thomson attributes death to the cessation in this

<!-- chunk -->

### outward movement. “It is immaterial whether we lose this power by

<!-- chunk -->

### losing the inward heat or raising the outward heat about it, as the effect

<!-- chunk -->

### is the same,” writes Thomson (1825, 17). “When the outward heat

<!-- chunk -->

### becomes equal with the inward … cold assumes the power and death

<!-- chunk -->

### takes place.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Working with this simple model, Thomson concluded that there were

<!-- chunk -->

### three basic kinds of disease. The first was hot: external heat invades the

<!-- chunk -->

### interior and dominates the organism. The second was cold: internal heat

<!-- chunk -->

### dies down in the organism or it is overwhelmed by external cold. The

<!-- chunk -->

### third is an alternation of heat and cold: cold is “stuck” in the periphery

<!-- chunk -->

### and the forces fight for ascendancy (Botanic Guide, 1825, 14, 16, 18).

<!-- chunk -->

### These three disease patterns anticipated the threefold classification of

<!-- chunk -->

### disease adopted by his student, Dr. Alvah Curtis.

<!-- chunk -->

### Since all disease had one basic cause, Thomson concluded that only

<!-- chunk -->

### one basic cure was needed. “All the art required to [cure] is to know

<!-- chunk -->

### what medicine will do it, and how to administer it, as a person knows

<!-- chunk -->

### how to clear a stove and the pipe when clogged with soot” (Narrative,

<!-- chunk -->

### 1825, 45).

<!-- chunk -->

## Removing Canker

<!-- chunk -->

### The old physicians taught that chronic disease was caused by thickened

<!-- chunk -->

### “humors” which had to be thinned by improving digestion, restoring the

<!-- chunk -->

### internal fire, and opening the channels of elimination. In the eighteenth

<!-- chunk -->

### and early nineteenth centuries these humors came to be known as

<!-- chunk -->

### canker in folk medicine. This idea was replaced by the concept of

<!-- chunk -->

### “impurities in the blood” and finally, in the twentieth century, by

<!-- chunk -->

### “toxins.” This subject has already been taken up in the previous chapter.

<!-- chunk -->

### The old authorities visualized canker as both a lining on the

<!-- chunk -->

### gastrointestinal mucosa and a substance burdening the internal organs.

<!-- chunk -->

### The idea has classical origins. Galen taught that the stomach separated

<!-- chunk -->

### food into pure and impure portions. The pure parts adhered to the wall

<!-- chunk -->

### of the stomach. From there they were absorbed into the interior, while

<!-- chunk -->

### the impure was moved down the alimentary tract for further digestion. If

<!-- chunk -->

### the digestate was particularly thick, adhesive, or heavy, it might adhere

<!-- chunk -->

### to the lining of the stomach, resulting in sluggish gastric digestion, slow

<!-- chunk -->

### transfer of food to the interior, lowered nutrition, and diminished heat

<!-- chunk -->

### radiating out from the stomach to the rest of the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### This idea continued to persist in professional medicine during the

<!-- chunk -->

### eighteenth century. The standard medical text for home use at that time

<!-- chunk -->

### was Dr. William Buchan’s Domestic Medicine. The sixth edition of 1779—

<!-- chunk -->

### the copy I have originally belonged to my ancestor, Thomas Pearsall of

<!-- chunk -->

### Westbury, Long Island—describes “cold viscid phlegm” adhering to the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach and bowels (1779, 160). He recommends both emetics and

<!-- chunk -->

### purgatives for its removal.

<!-- chunk -->

### In both Anglo and Latin America, the doctrine of thick, impure phlegm

<!-- chunk -->

### clinging to the wall of the stomach was elevated to a level of great

<!-- chunk -->

### importance when other traditional concepts passed away. Indeed, it had

<!-- chunk -->

### not completely disappeared even in conventional medicine in the early

<!-- chunk -->

### twentieth century. As late as 1924, Dr. Henry Harrower (1924, 139)

<!-- chunk -->

### graphically describes the “poultice” of mucus on the walls of the

<!-- chunk -->

### intestines: “the alimentary wall is covered with a more or less irritating,

<!-- chunk -->

### poison-filled blanket.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Thomson learned about canker from his mentor, Mrs. Benton. As a

<!-- chunk -->

### child he was afflicted with the “canker rash,” or scarlet fever. It was

<!-- chunk -->

### thought that the canker rises from the interior to the skin, blocks the

<!-- chunk -->

### pores, and creates a high fever. Mrs. Benton gave the boy a decoction of

<!-- chunk -->

### cocoash or red-stalked aster (Aster puniceus), a specific for “canker rash”

<!-- chunk -->

### (Thomson, Narrative, 1825, 18; 1842, 608).

<!-- chunk -->

### Although canker is a central feature in Thomson’s medical system, he

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves no good definition of it in his writings. Apparently he believed

<!-- chunk -->

### that his readers knew what he is talking about. This alone says

<!-- chunk -->

### something about the prevalence of the concept at that time in folk

<!-- chunk -->

### culture. For a good definition we turn to Dr. Downing (1851, 187):

<!-- chunk -->

### Cold affects the stomach, and makes it cold, then the pores of the

<!-- chunk -->

### skin close, and the imperceivable perspiration is thrown into the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach and becomes there an adhesive substance or matter,

<!-- chunk -->

### called phlegm.… If allowed to remain on the stomach, it becomes

<!-- chunk -->

### tough and stringy, and fills the velvety part of the coat of the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach, and that becomes smooth and glossed over, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### gastric juice is not able to remove it, or to digest the food. The

<!-- chunk -->

### food not being digested, does not nourish the system, and then

<!-- chunk -->

### the patient runs down and becomes weak and sick. The phlegm

<!-- chunk -->

### lying on the stomach becomes corroded after the juice of the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach becomes commingled with it, and then it becomes

<!-- chunk -->

### cankerous, and the cankerous fluid is thrown into the chyle, and

<!-- chunk -->

### thence into the blood; is circulated through the system, and is

<!-- chunk -->

### deposited in the weakest places, and forms disease. The blood is

<!-- chunk -->

### carried into the liver with the impurity, and the liver is not able

<!-- chunk -->

### to cleanse it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Downing goes on to describe how canker causes disease in the interior

<!-- chunk -->

### of the organism. If the liver cannot handle canker, then other organs are

<!-- chunk -->

### affected. The impure blood moves up to the lungs, which cannot purify

<!-- chunk -->

### the blood, becomes a burden and destroys them. Or it moves to the

<!-- chunk -->

### heart, “the valves of the heart become loaded and occasion the

<!-- chunk -->

### palpitation of the heart, and apoplexy and sudden death.” Or it runs

<!-- chunk -->

### down to the kidneys, forms an obstruction, and causes “the kidney

<!-- chunk -->

### complaint.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Canker, as mentioned above, went on in folk medicine to become

<!-- chunk -->

### “impure blood,” “bad blood,” and finally, “toxins in the blood” or

<!-- chunk -->

### “toxemia.”

# S

<!-- chunk -->

### The sick might be healed with substances that are natural, i.e.,

<!-- chunk -->

### physiological function remedies.

<!-- chunk -->

### —P

ROFESSOR

<!-- chunk -->

### M

OLESCHOTT

<!-- chunk -->

### o far we have studied the primary patterns and functions which

<!-- chunk -->

### are most evident within the body. These are often associated with

<!-- chunk -->

### different locations and organs. As medicine developed, it became

<!-- chunk -->

### more evident how the functions and structures were related, and this

<!-- chunk -->

### became an important tool for understanding disease and making an

<!-- chunk -->

### accurate diagnosis. This era began about the time William Harvey

<!-- chunk -->

### discovered the true circulation of the blood and has not yet completely

<!-- chunk -->

### closed. We are still learning how the more complex parts of the body

<!-- chunk -->

### operate.

<!-- chunk -->

### In Western medicine, as practiced in the nineteenth and early

<!-- chunk -->

### twentieth centuries, the organ or system was often cited as the location

<!-- chunk -->

### of the disease. This was customary in both lay and professional

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. Even today we say we are having “stomach problems” or

<!-- chunk -->

### visiting a “heart doctor.” The problem was not located in the humor, as

<!-- chunk -->

### it once had been, nor in the molecular lesion, as it is in modern

<!-- chunk -->

### biomedicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is something very natural about locating the problem in an

<!-- chunk -->

### organ or system. These are the natural units within the body about

<!-- chunk -->

### which people tend to think. One of the Hippocratic writers said that it

<!-- chunk -->

### was not appropriate to either learn or practice according to information

<!-- chunk -->

### that the patients themselves could not understand. More importantly,

<!-- chunk -->

### disease tends to derange the basic functions and structures of the body

<!-- chunk -->

### long before it comes to the surface in a local lesion. Long before the

<!-- chunk -->

### ulcer, there was a problem of the stomach. Long before the heart attack,

<!-- chunk -->

### there was high blood pressure. Before that there was excessive weight

<!-- chunk -->

### gain and before that there was an over-active appetite, digestion, and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism. Furthermore, it is upon these general functions and organs

<!-- chunk -->

### that herbs tend to expend their healing power. The great majority of

<!-- chunk -->

### them do not have affinities to specific lesions and tissue changes but to

<!-- chunk -->

### functional spheres. Of course, there are constitutional diseases, those

<!-- chunk -->

### that are found in the whole body, and there are local diseases, those

<!-- chunk -->

### which are found in a specific tissue or set of cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Modern biomedicine left behind the idea of general patterns of disease

<!-- chunk -->

### located in the general functions, organs, or systems of the body. That

<!-- chunk -->

### was the break that alienated biomedicine, not only from its roots in

<!-- chunk -->

### earlier Western medicine, but from all other systems of natural therapy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Primary Organizational Levels of the Body

<!-- chunk -->

### We cannot, however, go wrong with the understanding from

<!-- chunk -->

### biomedicine that there are four levels of organization in the living body:

<!-- chunk -->

### cell, tissue, organ, and system. The cell is the basic unit of life. Nutrition,

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism, and elimination constitute the basic functions of cellular

<!-- chunk -->

### life, determing the environment of cellular life.

<!-- chunk -->

### Cells in the body form into tissues, of which there are four basic kinds:

<!-- chunk -->

### muscular, nervous, epithelial, and connective. Epithelium forms the

<!-- chunk -->

### linings of the body, the skin, and mucosa. Those areas of the body

<!-- chunk -->

### exposed to the outside world are covered by epithelium. The unfolding

<!-- chunk -->

### epithelium forms secretory glands and, in combination with other

<!-- chunk -->

### tissues, these become the basis for the formation of the major visceral

<!-- chunk -->

### organs. The stomach, for example, represents a folding up of epithelium

<!-- chunk -->

### to become an enclosed mucous membrane with little secretory ducts

<!-- chunk -->

### releasing mucus and digestive juices. It is surrounded by muscular tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### that contracts the stomach so that it can churn and break up food. The

<!-- chunk -->

### whole organ is held together by connective tissue and innervated by

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous tissue to provide communication and instructions to or from the

<!-- chunk -->

### rest of the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tissues and organs are the second and third level of organization in

<!-- chunk -->

### the body. The fourth level is that of the system. A group of organs

<!-- chunk -->

### together will perform an important function such as digestion or

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination. The stomach joins with the mouth, salivary glands,

<!-- chunk -->

### pharynx, esophagus, intestines, etc., to form the gastrointestinal,

<!-- chunk -->

### alimentary, or digestive tract. The system is often a functional unit, not a

<!-- chunk -->

### singular anatomical structure.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many of the organs comprising the system are just barely linked

<!-- chunk -->

### anatomically, and sometimes not linked at all. However, there is often a

<!-- chunk -->

### dominant organ in the system. Since this is often the unit upon which

<!-- chunk -->

### folk medicine heaps the functions of the entire system, we will often

<!-- chunk -->

### denominate the entire system with the name of the organ. For instance,

<!-- chunk -->

### the stomach is the dominant organ in the upper gastrointestinal tract.

<!-- chunk -->

### Thus, we may speak of the stomach as an organ system representing the

<!-- chunk -->

### upper or entire GI. Since dominant organs are often associated with the

<!-- chunk -->

### systems they personify we will often speak of “organ systems.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Before the cells were understood to be the basic building blocks of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, scientists and physicians referred to the smallest unit of living

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue as “fibers.” We often see this term in nineteenth-century literature.

<!-- chunk -->

### I have retained it and used it when it seemed appropriate to refer to the

<!-- chunk -->

### basic tissue in general, indefinite terms.

<!-- chunk -->

## Primary Organ Systems

<!-- chunk -->

### Biomedicine recognizes about eleven basic systems: circulatory,

<!-- chunk -->

### digestive, respiratory, urinary, immune, nervous, endocrine,

<!-- chunk -->

### reproductive, skeletal, muscular, and integumentary (skin, hair, nails).

<!-- chunk -->

### To a large extent, these correspond to the major functions recognized in

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional folk medicine and holistic practice. In traditional Chinese

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine there are twelve organ systems which correlate roughly with

<!-- chunk -->

### the above systems. In Western astrological medicine, there are also

<!-- chunk -->

### twelve primary functional spheres with an almost exact correspondence

<!-- chunk -->

### to Chinese medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the most important differences we notice is between the organs

<!-- chunk -->

### or organ systems that serve important functions in the body (respiration,

<!-- chunk -->

### digestion, metabolism, circulation, etc.) and those that have a primarily

<!-- chunk -->

### regulatory purpose. These I have separated as the regulatory or

<!-- chunk -->

### “guidance systems” (nervous, endocrine, hypothalamus) and “functional

<!-- chunk -->

### organ systems” (gastrointestinal, cardiovascular, liver, and so forth).

<!-- chunk -->

### We must always keep in mind the very important hierarchy of organ

<!-- chunk -->

### systems that has already been alluded to in the last chapter: defensive,

<!-- chunk -->

### functional, regulatory. The body always seeks to move disease from the

<!-- chunk -->

### vital, regulatory level to the peripheral or defensive level.

<!-- chunk -->

## Guidance Systems

<!-- chunk -->

### The body possesses two systems for sending and receiving messages. The

<!-- chunk -->

### older one, found even in single celled life forms, uses chemical signals.

<!-- chunk -->

### These are called hormones. There is a core of glands which secrete

<!-- chunk -->

### hormones to control many bodily activities. These are called the

<!-- chunk -->

### “endocrine system.” The newer method of communication uses electrical

<!-- chunk -->

### signals, that is, electron exchange. These together form the “nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### system,” only found in higher (animal) forms of life. Although the

<!-- chunk -->

### endocrine and nervous systems function as separate entities with

<!-- chunk -->

### completely different structures (hormones and glands versus

<!-- chunk -->

### neurotransmitters, nerves and nerve plexi or gatherings), their work is

<!-- chunk -->

### largely integrated and overlapping. Both are directed in some measure

<!-- chunk -->

### by the hypothalamus, the “master gland” in the brain. It acts as a bridge

<!-- chunk -->

### between the two systems when there need to be signaling back and

<!-- chunk -->

### forth. It will be treated as a third guidance system.

<!-- chunk -->

## Nervous System. The nervous system is separated into two major

<!-- chunk -->

### divisions, the voluntary (under conscious control) and the involuntary

<!-- chunk -->

### (instinctive reactions). The voluntary is further subdivided into the

<!-- chunk -->

### central nervous system (CNS) and the peripheral nervous system (PNS).

<!-- chunk -->

### The former is associated with consciousness and decision making, the

<!-- chunk -->

### latter with sensation and movement. The autonomic nervous system,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is further subdivided into the sympathetic (awake, alert) and the

<!-- chunk -->

### parasympathetic (rest, digest) run involuntary functions. Danger ratchets

<!-- chunk -->

### up the sympathetic mode to the “fight or flight” level.

<!-- chunk -->

## Endocrine Core. Many organs of the body utilize hormones (chemical

<!-- chunk -->

### messengers) to send signals, but there is a core of glands which rely

<!-- chunk -->

### almost entirely on hormones to do their work: the pituitary, thyroid, and

<!-- chunk -->

### adrenals. This may be called the endocrine core. It is largely responsible

<!-- chunk -->

### for temperature, water levels, cellular and body growth, maturation, and

<!-- chunk -->

### (with the gonads) reproduction.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is misleading to speak of an “endocrine system” because many

<!-- chunk -->

### different organs use hormones to communicate.

<!-- chunk -->

## Hypothalamus. This small structure in the brain lies just above the

<!-- chunk -->

### pituitary, which it continually influences and controls. A portal system

<!-- chunk -->

### (vasculature spread out into a net) allows the hypothalamus to screen

<!-- chunk -->

### the blood for indicators from the endocrine system (hormone levels). It

<!-- chunk -->

### also receives signals from the nervous system. The hypothalamus then

<!-- chunk -->

### signals the appropriate neural and endocrine centers to maintain

<!-- chunk -->

### balance.

<!-- chunk -->

### The hypothalamus sends signals to the pituitary, which controls most

<!-- chunk -->

### of the endocrine core. In addition, the hypothalamus, through the

<!-- chunk -->

### autonomic nervous system, signals the peripheral “vents” of the body—

<!-- chunk -->

### sweat pores, sebaceous glands, capillaries, autonomic nerve reflexes

<!-- chunk -->

### (goose bumps, shivering) to open or close, in order to maintain thermic

<!-- chunk -->

### equilibrium. The hypothalamus maintains a “shield” to protect the

<!-- chunk -->

### deeper, internal organs from outward stress. When heat or cold assaults

<!-- chunk -->

### the body, the hypothalamus takes commensurate steps to reestablish

<!-- chunk -->

### equilibrium. If it fails, we came down with a “virus.” If it becomes

<!-- chunk -->

### permanently traumatized by environmental stress the skin will be hot,

<!-- chunk -->

### cold, closed or open, dry or damp, all very important signs of distress in

<!-- chunk -->

### the grand regulator of the body, the hypothalamus.

<!-- chunk -->

### The hypothalamus is also a part of the limbic centers regulating and

<!-- chunk -->

### controlling our emotional and subjective reactions. Thus, some of the

<!-- chunk -->

### same activities used to control peripheral defenses (blushing, flushing,

<!-- chunk -->

### pallor, shiver up the spine, etc.) are used to reinforce emotional

<!-- chunk -->

### reactions. The hypothalamus ratchets emotions up to the passionate

<!-- chunk -->

### level when there are strong emotional stimuli (life and death,

<!-- chunk -->

### procreation, love, creativity, etc.). At the same time, it attempts to

<!-- chunk -->

### maintain control over passion.

<!-- chunk -->

### The hypothalamus is a master gland that needs to be considered for

<!-- chunk -->

### comprehensive holistic care of the organism. It is the master conductor

<!-- chunk -->

### standing behind the nervous system, endocrine core, and the “febrile

<!-- chunk -->

### mechanism,” the three basic instruments of regulation in the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### The regulatory or guidance systems make a unit which, with the blood,

<!-- chunk -->

### will be discussed in a separate book on the practice of traditional

<!-- chunk -->

### Western herbal medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

## Blood, the Universal Medium

<!-- chunk -->

### In traditional medicine the blood is considered to be a functional unit by

<!-- chunk -->

### itself. It is looked upon as a universal medium carrying food and water

<!-- chunk -->

### to all parts of the body, while carrying waste products away. The blood

<!-- chunk -->

### is the seat of the disease in and of itself (anemia, coagulation,

<!-- chunk -->

### putrefaction), or it can pick up diseases and spread them throughout the

<!-- chunk -->

### body.

<!-- chunk -->

## Blood. The blood carries food, water, and oxygen to the capillaries (the

<!-- chunk -->

### smallest vessels) to be pushed out into the internal (interstitial) fluids to

<!-- chunk -->

### supply the cells. It picks up waste products and carbon dioxide (the

<!-- chunk -->

### waste product of cellular respiration using up the oxygen) in the venules

<!-- chunk -->

### just past the capillaries. It returns the carbon dioxide to capillaries in the

<!-- chunk -->

### lungs where it is exchanged for new oxygen. The waste materials are

<!-- chunk -->

### filtered out as the blood comes through the liver and kidneys. Some

<!-- chunk -->

### waste products are picked up by the lymphatic ducts and then conveyed

<!-- chunk -->

### to the blood.

<!-- chunk -->

### The blood serum is more or less identical to the body serum or lymph

<!-- chunk -->

### in the lymphatic vessels and the interstitial fluids roaming freely

<!-- chunk -->

### between the cells. Blood proteins manufactured in the liver keep the

<!-- chunk -->

### serum from seeping out of the porous vessels. To the serum is added red

<!-- chunk -->

### blood cells, white blood cells, and platelets manufactured in the bone

<!-- chunk -->

### marrow. The former carry oxygen to the tissues and carbon dioxide

<!-- chunk -->

### away from them. The blood appears to be blue when it is carrying

<!-- chunk -->

### carbon dioxide, and red when it is carrying oxygen. It actuality, venous

<!-- chunk -->

### blood is dark red, but diagnostically, we look for the blue color through

<!-- chunk -->

### the skin. The white blood cells (some of which are also made in the

<!-- chunk -->

### lymphatic glands) act as a police force to patrol the inner waters of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, removing impurities. The platelets coagulate the blood to create a

<!-- chunk -->

### patch when there is a rupture in the vessels carrying the blood.

<!-- chunk -->

## Functional Organ Systems

<!-- chunk -->

### Specific organ systems carry out the functions of the body under the

<!-- chunk -->

### guidance of the neuroendocrine system. They are responsible for the

<!-- chunk -->

### basic functions of life such as respiration, circulation, digestion, and

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination. They provide the pabulum that reaches the cells (food,

<!-- chunk -->

### water, oxygen), and process the waste materials which come away from

<!-- chunk -->

### them.

<!-- chunk -->

## Lungs/Respiration. The lungs open up to the outside world and are

<!-- chunk -->

### therefore coated with mucosa for protection. The structures of the lungs

<!-- chunk -->

### are cartilaginous and are encircled by muscles and tendons that expand

<!-- chunk -->

### and contract to allow the inhalation and exhalation of air. The fresh air

<!-- chunk -->

### fills the smallest pulmonary spaces (alveoli) so that oxygen can be taken

<!-- chunk -->

### up by red blood cells in the capillaries. They unload carbon dioxide,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is removed by exhalation. This exchange in the lungs provides for

<!-- chunk -->

### cellular respiration throughout the body. The bellows-like action of the

<!-- chunk -->

### lungs not only moves the air in and out, but also pumps the fluids of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body.

<!-- chunk -->

### The upper respiratory tract (nose, mouth, larynx, pharynx) is exposed

<!-- chunk -->

### to the outside world through several openings, and is therefore sensitive

<!-- chunk -->

### to infection. The lower respiratory tract (trachea, bronchial tubes, lungs)

<!-- chunk -->

### divides into smaller and smaller tubes, which allow the maximum

<!-- chunk -->

### amount of air to interface with the capillaries of the blood.

<!-- chunk -->

## Heart/Circulation. The heart pumps the blood throughout the body to

<!-- chunk -->

### distribute its contents (oxygen, foodstuffs) while taking away waste

<!-- chunk -->

### products (carbon dioxide, urates, etc.) Due to different tensions on the

<!-- chunk -->

### blood vessels leading to and from the heart, it must also act like a valve,

<!-- chunk -->

### adjusting the disparate tensions. Thus, the primary cause of heart disease

<!-- chunk -->

### is high blood pressure.

<!-- chunk -->

### The heart receives oxygenated blood from the lungs and sends it on to

<!-- chunk -->

### the tissues through the arterial system. It receives the spent, carbonized

<!-- chunk -->

### blood from the tissues through the veins and sends it back to the lungs.

<!-- chunk -->

### The heart beats in syncopation with the arteries; both are necessary for

<!-- chunk -->

### the movement of the blood. Part of the content of the blood (excluding

<!-- chunk -->

### the large white, red, and platelet cells) is squeezed out of the finest

<!-- chunk -->

### vessels, the capillaries, into the internal ocean of interstitial fluids, to

<!-- chunk -->

### provide food, air, and water to the cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### The circulatory system also maintains the even temperature of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body by moving cool blood out of the extremities and warm blood in, or

<!-- chunk -->

### hot blood out of the torso and head and cooler blood in. Incomplete or

<!-- chunk -->

### unbalanced circulation creates a burden on the heart.

<!-- chunk -->

## Stomach/Digestion. The upper gastrointestinal tract (lips, mouth,

<!-- chunk -->

### tongue, pharynx, esophagus, stomach, duodenum) is concerned with the

<!-- chunk -->

### breakdown of food into smaller and smaller particles, so that they can be

<!-- chunk -->

### absorbed or assimilated into the body. There are two mechanisms by

<!-- chunk -->

### which this occurs: mechanical and chemical. The lips, tongue, mouth,

<!-- chunk -->

### and teeth move and grind the food to break it up. The stomach churns it,

<!-- chunk -->

### and throughout the tract it is turning, mixing, and being leavened with

<!-- chunk -->

### the chemical means of digestion: enzymes, acids, and alkalis secreted

<!-- chunk -->

### from the mucosal lining of the tract which facilitate the breakdown of

<!-- chunk -->

### substances. Problems in the digestive sphere are usually due to

<!-- chunk -->

### neuromuscular weakness, wrongs of secretion, or improper diet.

<!-- chunk -->

## Small Intestine/Assimilation. The small intestine receives the jumble

<!-- chunk -->

### of broken down food particles and digestive secretions. The great length

<!-- chunk -->

### of the gut (twelve to twenty feet) allows the time for complete digestion

<!-- chunk -->

### to occur. The walls of the small intestine are studded with fixed enzymes

<!-- chunk -->

### that continue the digestive process and with brush-like hairs (villi),

<!-- chunk -->

### maximizing the spaces through which particles can be absorbed or

<!-- chunk -->

### assimilated into the organism. These spaces are in the walls of the cells

<!-- chunk -->

### lining the small intestine. Food/toxins move through several passive and

<!-- chunk -->

### active mechanisms. A large portion of immune tissue is situated in the

<!-- chunk -->

### small intestine, in the gut associated lymphoid tissue (GALT), which help

<!-- chunk -->

### protect the body from foreign substances and bacteria, and the Peyer’s

<!-- chunk -->

### Patches, which protect the small intestine from impurities coming up

<!-- chunk -->

### from the large intestine.

<!-- chunk -->

### The major problems with assimilation are usually due to

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation, depression, or malnourishment in the cells lining the

<!-- chunk -->

### small intestine, or stagnation in the portal vein and lymphatics receiving

<!-- chunk -->

### the food/toxins from the gut. This results in lack of nutrition for the

<!-- chunk -->

### body or poisoning from the entry of too much toxic material through the

<!-- chunk -->

### cells lining the small intestine. Food allergies may be related to excess or

<!-- chunk -->

### deficiency in the immune and lymph component of the tract.

<!-- chunk -->

## Large Intestine/Elimination. The rough and unusable part of the

<!-- chunk -->

### digestate passes on to the large intestine, where a few last tidbits of

<!-- chunk -->

### nutrition can be extracted using special means. The colon harbors

<!-- chunk -->

### bacteria that are able to break down material that the enzymes of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body cannot. Having finished this, the major function of the large

<!-- chunk -->

### intestine is to concentrate the remnant into a suitable solid mass for

<!-- chunk -->

### elimination. Water is removed until the right consistency for good stool

<!-- chunk -->

### is achieved, and the rectal sphincters, under voluntary and involuntary

<!-- chunk -->

### impulses, remove it.

<!-- chunk -->

## Lymph/Immune. The lymphatic system provides ductwork which, will

<!-- chunk -->

### the blood vessels, provides nutriment to the great internal ocean of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, the interstitial cells surrounding the cells, and takes away waste

<!-- chunk -->

### products. In biomedicine it is looked upon as a sort of minor assistant to

<!-- chunk -->

### the bloodstream, but in holistic medicine it is seen as an organ system of

<!-- chunk -->

### great importance.

<!-- chunk -->

### While fluids, food, and waste products are being transported through

<!-- chunk -->

### the blood and lymph, the immune cells (leukocytes and lymphocytes),

<!-- chunk -->

### which eat up and denature aberrant bacteria, viruses, foreign material,

<!-- chunk -->

### and cancerous cells, patrol the internal waters. The lymphatic system is

<!-- chunk -->

### the vehicle through which the immune system acts.

<!-- chunk -->

### The lymphatic system absorbs lipids and minerals from the small

<!-- chunk -->

### intestine, filtering them through ducts and glands containing immune

<!-- chunk -->

### cells (which police these materials), bringing them ultimately to the

<!-- chunk -->

### blood, then to the liver. A large amount of lymph/immune tissue is

<!-- chunk -->

### stationed in the small intestine to protect the body against infiltration of

<!-- chunk -->

### impurities and bacteria. The lymphatic system also drains and filters the

<!-- chunk -->

### water and toxins leading away from the cells, and back into the

<!-- chunk -->

### bloodstream. They also drain away from the surface of the body, where

<!-- chunk -->

### immune activity is needed to protect against environmental influences.

<!-- chunk -->

### The immune cells are produced in the stem cells of the red bone

<!-- chunk -->

### marrow and circulated through the blood and lymph to police the inner

<!-- chunk -->

### waters of the body. The lymph and interstitial fluids do not have their

<!-- chunk -->

### own pump, but rely on the movements of the diaphragm and the general

<!-- chunk -->

### “hum” of bodily movements.

<!-- chunk -->

### Problems in the lymphatic system are mainly due to immune over

<!-- chunk -->

### sensitivity or depression, and lymphatic stagnation or weakness. In

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional medicine, lymphatic and immune problems are often

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with the spleen, the largest structure in the system.

<!-- chunk -->

## Liver/Preparatory Metabolism. The rest of the nutriment in the small

<!-- chunk -->

### intestine (carbohydrates and proteins) cannot pass into the lymphatics

<!-- chunk -->

### but pass into the bloodstream. They are absorbed through cells lining the

<!-- chunk -->

### gut by more energy dependent mechanisms and are taken up by tiny

<!-- chunk -->

### capillaries that collect together into veins and are united in the portal

<!-- chunk -->

### vein, which drains all the digestive viscera. It is like a special trough for

<!-- chunk -->

### catching waste materials that flow off these hardworking, toxin-laden

<!-- chunk -->

### structures, while at the same time gathering the food/toxins from the

<!-- chunk -->

### small intestine for delivery to the liver. Indeed the toxins and foods

<!-- chunk -->

### gathered are, to some extent, indistinguishable. That is why they can be

<!-- chunk -->

### called “food/toxins.”

<!-- chunk -->

### After uniting all these veins, the portal vein begins subdividing into

<!-- chunk -->

### smaller and smaller veins which bring food/toxins into the liver for

<!-- chunk -->

### detoxification (catabolism) and rebuilding (anabolism) into foods fit for

<!-- chunk -->

### the uses of the body as blood and food proteins. The sugars are already

<!-- chunk -->

### pretty much rendered into glucose (blood sugar) when they arrive in the

<!-- chunk -->

### liver, which then decides (under direction from the pancreatic

<!-- chunk -->

### hormones) whether to store them or send them out. The lipids received

<!-- chunk -->

### from the lymphatic system are detoxified and remade into cholesterol

<!-- chunk -->

### and other lipids of use to the system.

<!-- chunk -->

### The “foodstuffs” prepared by the liver are collected into veins draining

<!-- chunk -->

### the liver, sent to the heart and pumped through the arteries to the

<!-- chunk -->

### capillaries, where they cross the barrier and enter the interstitial fluids,

<!-- chunk -->

### there to be picked up by the cells of the body. The cells finish the

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism of these substances, which have been prepared by the liver,

<!-- chunk -->

### hence the liver is in charge of “preparatory metabolism.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The active unit of the liver is the hepatic lobule. The portal veins feed

<!-- chunk -->

### into the lobules, along with an artery supplying red blood for nutrition

<!-- chunk -->

### of the liver cells themselves, a biliary duct to drain off bile (a byproduct

<!-- chunk -->

### of metabolism) and a vein to drain away carbonized blood and newly

<!-- chunk -->

### minted foodstuffs. The net of the portal vein provides the basic structure

<!-- chunk -->

### for the liver.

<!-- chunk -->

## Gall Bladder/Bile Regulation. The liver manufactures bile as a

<!-- chunk -->

### byproduct and a waste product. The gall ducts receives the bile, collects

<!-- chunk -->

### it together into larger ducts, saves some in the gall sac or bladder and

<!-- chunk -->

### secretes some, as needed, into the common bile duct and thence into the

<!-- chunk -->

### small intestine. The function of the bile is to emulsify or break down fats

<!-- chunk -->

### and oils in the gut. Much of it is reabsorbed through the walls of the

<!-- chunk -->

### intestines and brought back to the liver, but some of it is passed with the

<!-- chunk -->

### stool, which it lubricates and turns brown. Without bile, the stool would

<!-- chunk -->

### be a gray color.

<!-- chunk -->

## Pancreas/Digestive and Metabolic Assistant. A signal from the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach prompts the pancreas to dump digestive enzymes mixed in an

<!-- chunk -->

### alkaline solution into the common bile duct, where it joins the bile and

<!-- chunk -->

### exits into the duodenum, to act on the digestate. The carbohydrates are

<!-- chunk -->

### thus broken down into glucose, the proteins into amino acids, the lipids

<!-- chunk -->

### into short fatty acid chains, and the minerals freed for absorption.

<!-- chunk -->

### The pancreas also secretes insulin during feeding to help the cells pick

<!-- chunk -->

### up glucose (blood sugar) and proteins. Some of this is stored in the cells

<!-- chunk -->

### for later use as glycogen. During fasting (between meals) it secretes

<!-- chunk -->

### glucagon which helps the liver and cells mobilize stored blood sugar

<!-- chunk -->

### (glycogen) back into usable blood sugar (glucose). Thus, the pancreas

<!-- chunk -->

### keeps up a continuous level of blood sugar for the tissues, which use it

<!-- chunk -->

### continuously as a fuel. No internal organ is as sensitive to food as the

<!-- chunk -->

### pancreas.

<!-- chunk -->

## Kidneys/Water and Solid Regulation. The kidneys remove waste

<!-- chunk -->

### products from the interior of the body. The large intestine, by

<!-- chunk -->

### comparison, removes wastes from the alimentary tube; these have never

<!-- chunk -->

### truly been inside the body. However, the kidneys retain much more than

<!-- chunk -->

### they remove. While in one sense they are eliminative organs, in another

<!-- chunk -->

### they are filters that measure and balance the internal fluids and solids of

<!-- chunk -->

### the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Arteries feed red blood into the kidneys, where it is split up into little

<!-- chunk -->

### capillaries. A major part of the plasma and its contents are arbitrarily

<!-- chunk -->

### absorbed across the capillary wall into the tubules of the kidneys. The

<!-- chunk -->

### capillaries continue to nestle around the tubules and there is a

<!-- chunk -->

### continuous exchange, back and forth, until the waste products and some

<!-- chunk -->

### water necessary for their elimination are left in the tubules while the

<!-- chunk -->

### minerals and nutrients useful to the body are retained in the

<!-- chunk -->

### bloodstream. This shuffling back and forth is regulated primarily by

<!-- chunk -->

### aldosterone hormone from the adrenal cortex (excrete more) and

<!-- chunk -->

### vasopressin (antidiuretic hormone) from the posterior pituitary (retain

<!-- chunk -->

### more). Finally, a small amount of waste material (urates from protein

<!-- chunk -->

### breakdown, some extra minerals, and a few aberrant toxic chemicals),

<!-- chunk -->

### with enough water to remove them, are expelled into the pelvis of the

<!-- chunk -->

### kidney, a large collection area, and are excreted through the ureters as

<!-- chunk -->

### urine, and into the bladder.

<!-- chunk -->

### The kidneys also screen the blood to determine if there is enough

<!-- chunk -->

### oxygen; if not, they signal the stem cells in the bone marrow to produce

<!-- chunk -->

### more red blood cells. The stem cells can be stimulated by different

<!-- chunk -->

### signals to produce red blood cells, white blood cells and platelets, as well

<!-- chunk -->

### as “replacement parts” for many different organs of the body. The

<!-- chunk -->

### triggers for these mechanisms are unknown, but it seems likely that they

<!-- chunk -->

### are in the kidneys, or related to them through feedback loops. The

<!-- chunk -->

### kidneys also maintain blood pressure.

<!-- chunk -->

## Bladder/Water and Solid Elimination. The ureters bring the urine

<!-- chunk -->

### from the kidneys to the bladder, which acts like a holding tank,

<!-- chunk -->

### collecting it until there is enough of it to dispose. The bladder is a

<!-- chunk -->

### mucosa-lined sac that is emptied by powerful muscles under a

<!-- chunk -->

### combination of voluntary and involuntary neuromuscular impulses. The

<!-- chunk -->

### urine is pushed out through the urethra. Since the bladder, like the lungs

<!-- chunk -->

### and gastrointestinal tract, is open to the outside world, it is lined by

<!-- chunk -->

### mucosa and is susceptible to external bacterial infections.

<!-- chunk -->

## Special Systems

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to the above, there are several major systems which are

<!-- chunk -->

### complex because they are composed of many different tissues and

<!-- chunk -->

### organs, or because other major organ systems manifest through them.

<!-- chunk -->

## Reproductive System. The reproductive system of female and male

<!-- chunk -->

### contains specialized structures and hormone regulatory mechanisms. The

<!-- chunk -->

### female tract is particularly complicated and, as it is more readily open to

<!-- chunk -->

### the external world and to the rigors of childbearing and lactation, more

<!-- chunk -->

### easily disordered. Thus, great attention is paid to the female tract in

<!-- chunk -->

### biomedicine and herbal medicine. The male system tends to be less often

<!-- chunk -->

### disordered, though impotence and enlargement of the prostate is a

<!-- chunk -->

### common event of older age.

<!-- chunk -->

## Muscular and Skeletal System. This organ system operates as a unit,

<!-- chunk -->

### but is composed of many different structures, including muscles, nerves,

<!-- chunk -->

### cartilage, bones, tendons, complex joints, cerebrospinal fluid, circulatory

<!-- chunk -->

### vessels, and epithelial linings (bursa). In order to treat muscular and

<!-- chunk -->

### skeletal system problems effectively, it is usually necessary to attend to

<!-- chunk -->

### each component individually, as well as the whole.

<!-- chunk -->

## Skin. The hair, nails, and skin represent the “integumentary system,”

<!-- chunk -->

### which is composed particularly of connective tissue and functions at the

<!-- chunk -->

### exterior of the body. Often, if one of these structures is weak so are the

<!-- chunk -->

### others. The skin is particularly complex because it not only has its own

<!-- chunk -->

### problems (closed sweat pores, sebaceous glands, poor wound healing,

<!-- chunk -->

### etc.), it acts as an eliminative channel. In a healthy state, it secretes

<!-- chunk -->

### water and some salts. If the pores become clogged for some reason, a

<!-- chunk -->

### burden is thrown on the other channels of elimination —particularly, the

<!-- chunk -->

### kidneys, colon, and lungs. The reverse is also true. Closure of one of

<!-- chunk -->

### these three channels often causes problems in the skin as toxins try to

<!-- chunk -->

### make their way out.

<!-- chunk -->

### In traditional medicine the major organ systems are closely associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with certain emotions or psychological faculties. It is interesting to note

<!-- chunk -->

### that in both Eastern and Western medicine theory, the liver is associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with anger and the spleen with melancholy. These correspondences are

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes very useful and at other times not. Since we will not be going

<!-- chunk -->

### over the organ systems in this book, we will omit this information for

<!-- chunk -->

### now.

<!-- chunk -->

## Interactions Between Organ Systems

<!-- chunk -->

### It would be convenient if disease started in a specific location and stayed

<!-- chunk -->

### there, but in reality disease moves across the boundaries of the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Usually, an imbalance will spread from one functional unit into others as

<!-- chunk -->

### healthy functional units try to compensate for weaknesses. If the nose is

<!-- chunk -->

### plugged, the patient will breath through the mouth. If the respiratory

<!-- chunk -->

### tract is subject to chronic infection, the lymphatic system will

<!-- chunk -->

### compensate by carrying away waste materials. If the thyroid is secreting

<!-- chunk -->

### low levels of thyroxin, the pituitary will secrete higher levels of TSH

<!-- chunk -->

### (thyroid stimulating hormone). After a while, overtaxed organs and

<!-- chunk -->

### systems can no longer compensate for serious functional imbalance and

<!-- chunk -->

### the disturbance will move from a functional level to a condition of tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### breakdown. Here, at last, the material lesion may appear and the

<!-- chunk -->

### disturbance generally enters the radar screen of modern biomedicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### The usual biomedical approach is to compensate for the incapacity of

<!-- chunk -->

### the body by introducing a drug that makes the body do what it is

<!-- chunk -->

### supposed to do. The holistic approach is different. Instead of trying to

<!-- chunk -->

### force the body to operate as we want it to, we try to return it to a state

<!-- chunk -->

### of health where it can take care of itself. In reality, biomedicine does not

<!-- chunk -->

### believe in healing, but in pain and disease “management.” There are

<!-- chunk -->

### cures, however, and laws of cure. Of course, as the situation deteriorates,

<!-- chunk -->

### it reaches a level where the organism cannot heal itself, and drugs or

<!-- chunk -->

### surgical intervention are necessary.

<!-- chunk -->

### When we approach disease from the holistic angle we use medicines

<!-- chunk -->

### differently from how they are used in biomedicine. Instead of forcing the

<!-- chunk -->

### body to operate in a certain fashion, we want to stimulate or sedate, feed

<!-- chunk -->

### or cleanse, to allow organs and systems to return to healthy operation.

<!-- chunk -->

### This is the way medicine plants have always been used around the

<!-- chunk -->

### world. There are relatively few herbs that can force the body to do

<!-- chunk -->

### anything; most of them mildly increase or decrease functional activity.

<!-- chunk -->

### One modern fad attempts to use herbs like drugs to suppress an

<!-- chunk -->

### imbalance. It may be possible to practice herbalism by imitating modern

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine (though I have yet to see this proven as a comprehensive

<!-- chunk -->

### system), but this should not be mistaken for holistic treatment. It is not

<!-- chunk -->

### curative. It is more suppression and management.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another idea is that herbs are nutritional substances that feed the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism. This may be true, but it tends to confuse a real difference

<!-- chunk -->

### between herbs and foods. As Hippocrates observed, foods cure by being

<!-- chunk -->

### blander than herbs, which cure by virtue of their acid, acrid, bitter,

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent, or pungent taste.

<!-- chunk -->

### As far as herbs are concerned, they are neither drugs nor nutritional

<!-- chunk -->

### supplements, but something else which has not been recognized or

<!-- chunk -->

### defined by modern science. Medicinal plants are powers or forces which

<!-- chunk -->

### act on the body in a milder fashion than drugs, but in a stronger fashion

<!-- chunk -->

### than foods. They do not force, but “exercise” functions in the body by

<!-- chunk -->

### increasing or decreasing the level of activity, tension, and hydration.

<!-- chunk -->

## Medicinal Organ Affinities

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbs are often classified according to their affinity to act on a certain

<!-- chunk -->

### organ. As herbalist David Hoffman pointed out to me in conversation, if

<!-- chunk -->

### poisons can be classified according to their affinities for certain

<!-- chunk -->

### structures, than herbs can be classified in this manner just as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### Galenic medicine classified herbs according to their “appropriations,”

<!-- chunk -->

### i.e., the organ that picked up or appropriated them. Paracelsus, who

<!-- chunk -->

### rejected so much of the Greek system, saw fit to continue this concept.

<!-- chunk -->

### The appropriata were thus defined down to the era of Nicholas Culpeper

<!-- chunk -->

### and William Salmon.

<!-- chunk -->

### In the early nineteenth century, Dr. Johann Gottfried Rademacher

<!-- chunk -->

### (1772–1850), a Saxon physician influenced by Paracelsus, developed a

<!-- chunk -->

### system of practice based primarily on the affinities of different medicines

<!-- chunk -->

### to organs. Rademacher did not give this system a particular name but his

<!-- chunk -->

### followers called it “organopathy” and his medicines were called “organ

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies.”

<!-- chunk -->

### British herbalist Grahme Tobyn pointed out to me that Galen and

<!-- chunk -->

### Paracelsus conceived of the organ as “appropriating” the remedy, while

<!-- chunk -->

### Rademacher saw the remedy having an “affinity” to the organ. As I first

<!-- chunk -->

### studied in the school of Rademacher—via Compton Burnett (see below)

<!-- chunk -->

### —I use the terminology of affinity, without, however, rejecting the idea

<!-- chunk -->

### of appropriation. An attraction between an herb and its target is

<!-- chunk -->

### probably mutual, but the language often does not express this idea.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although he was not a homeopath, Rademacher adopted the doctrine

<!-- chunk -->

### of dilution of dosage. He found that the more specific his diagnosis and

<!-- chunk -->

### prescription, the less he needed of the remedy. He went as far as a

<!-- chunk -->

### dilution of one drop of the mother tincture in a half-cup of water. At

<!-- chunk -->

### other times he used the larger doses (ten to thirty drops), as experience

<!-- chunk -->

### taught him. Should anyone think he was copying Samuel Hahnemann,

<!-- chunk -->

### Rademacher pointed out that Paracelsus advocated dilution.

<!-- chunk -->

### An English homeopath, Dr. James Compton Burnett (1849–1900)

<!-- chunk -->

### rescued Rademacher’s organopathy from obscurity. Like Rademacher,

<!-- chunk -->

### Burnett used herbal remedies according to organ affinities, in small

<!-- chunk -->

### material doses. He refined the principles governing the selection of

<!-- chunk -->

### organ specific medicines. Burnett observed that they possessed three

<!-- chunk -->

### basic characteristics:

<!-- chunk -->

## Kind of action. The remedy has a specific kind of action on that tissue.

<!-- chunk -->

### Juniper berries are not just diuretic, but more specifically, an “irritating

<!-- chunk -->

### diuretic” (i.e., a stimulant). Therefore they are contraindicated in acute

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation of the kidneys but may be remedial in chronic depression

<!-- chunk -->

### of kidney function with edema.

<!-- chunk -->

## Seat of action. The remedy has a specific affinity to a particular tissue,

<!-- chunk -->

### organ, or function. Juniperus is a well-known diuretic with an affinity to

<!-- chunk -->

### the kidney, while Crataegus has a well known action on the heart.

<!-- chunk -->

## Range of action. The remedy may be curative to the seat and kind of

<!-- chunk -->

### action of the disease, but its range of action may be limited. For

<!-- chunk -->

### instance, Melissa may cure heat and irritation of the stomach due to

<!-- chunk -->

### nervousness, but it will not cure it when due to a general metabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### excessive stimulation of the stomach. Goldenseal may cure gastric

<!-- chunk -->

### ulceration but it will seldom cure cancer of the stomach. When a remedy

<!-- chunk -->

### has reached its limit of action, it comes to what Burnett called its “stop-

<!-- chunk -->

### spot.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Organ affinities are not so easily determined. They are very much the

<!-- chunk -->

### product of experience, and this is random and incomplete by nature.

<!-- chunk -->

### Burnett addressed this problem by adopting a technique from

<!-- chunk -->

### Rademacher that he called “organ-testing.” If the physician was certain

<!-- chunk -->

### of the seat of the disease, but uncertain of the exact remedy to be used,

<!-- chunk -->

### he gave a series of medicines with affinities to that organ, until the right

<!-- chunk -->

### one hit home. This procedure may seem rather crude, but it works, as I

<!-- chunk -->

### have learned from experience. It also requires a considerable knowledge

<!-- chunk -->

### of the organ affinities of remedies. “This organ-testing I have found of

<!-- chunk -->

### great importance in practice,” writes Burnett in Greater Diseases of the

<!-- chunk -->

### Liver (1891, 76). “But it cannot be utilized unless one is well acquainted

<!-- chunk -->

### with the organ-remedies, that is to say, with the local electivity of

<!-- chunk -->

### drugs.”

<!-- chunk -->

### I do not know exactly how Burnett tested his organ remedies; one is

<!-- chunk -->

### left to suppose that he gave one after another, week after week, as the

<!-- chunk -->

### patient returned to his office. This does not seem very effective. My own

<!-- chunk -->

### method of “organ-testing” is to give a dose of the herb on the wrist, one

<!-- chunk -->

### herb after another, until I feel the pulse change. If it is too fast, it slows

<!-- chunk -->

### down; if too tense, it relaxes.

<!-- chunk -->

### Burnett also recognized that some local organ diseases arise from

<!-- chunk -->

### general causes in the organism, not just local causes. These require

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment by constitutional medicines. To cure these conditions he used

<!-- chunk -->

### the homeopathic constitutional remedies and nosodes (remedies made

<!-- chunk -->

### from disease organisms).

<!-- chunk -->

### Burnett found that the organ-remedies worked better in small material

<!-- chunk -->

### doses (about one to twenty-five drops) repeated daily. In this, he was

<!-- chunk -->

### following both Paracelsus and Rademacher. The constitutional remedies

<!-- chunk -->

### he used in homeopathic doses, infrequently repeated.

<!-- chunk -->

### The early devotees of hormonal supplementation adopted the term

<!-- chunk -->

### “organopathy,” late in the nineteenth century. They were not aware of,

<!-- chunk -->

### or did not care, about the earlier usage. They used extracts of the

<!-- chunk -->

### endocrine glands of animals to treat disorder in the corresponding gland

<!-- chunk -->

### of the human. Some of this therapy must be seen as supplementation,

<!-- chunk -->

### while some of it seems to have stimulated self-healing in the organism.

<!-- chunk -->

### An example of this literature is Dr. Henry Harrower’s Pluriglandular

<!-- chunk -->

### Therapy (1928). I used this little book to help me understand patterns in

<!-- chunk -->

### hormonal excesses and deficiencies.

<!-- chunk -->

### In my own practice I adhere rather closely to the teachings of

<!-- chunk -->

### Rademacher and Burnett, using small doses of the herbal tincture. I also

<!-- chunk -->

### follow the teachings of Dr. John M. Scudder who, like Rademacher and

<!-- chunk -->

### Paracelsus, used small doses of the herb. The more specific the medicine,

<!-- chunk -->

### the less needed, because a specific medicine is so well adapted to the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism or the imbalance it is treating. For a further discussion of

<!-- chunk -->

### specific medicine and organ specific medicine, please refer to my book

<!-- chunk -->

### Vitalism: The History of Herbalism, Homeopathy, and Flower Essences

<!-- chunk -->

### (1992).


<!-- chunk -->

### In any attempt to study living forms some system of classification is

<!-- chunk -->

### necessary.

<!-- chunk -->

### Heretofore most students of Man have used an arbitrary division

<!-- chunk -->

### based on two basic types—the long thins and short thicks.

<!-- chunk -->

### —G

EORGE

<!-- chunk -->

### D

RAPER

<!-- chunk -->

### , M.D.

<!-- chunk -->

### he six tissue states constitute a system for describing pathology.

<!-- chunk -->

### By comparison, when we speak of a person’s constitution we are

<!-- chunk -->

### referring to the predisposing strengths and weaknesses of the

<!-- chunk -->

### entire organism. Often the two are related, i.e., a hot person will have

<!-- chunk -->

### hot diseases, but we also see manifestations of disease which are out of

<!-- chunk -->

### character with the constitution. It is often a good idea to understand the

<!-- chunk -->

### disease pattern, the tissue state, and the constitution, in order to grasp

<!-- chunk -->

### the entire picture.

<!-- chunk -->

## Three Primary Constitutions

<!-- chunk -->

### The word constitution is derived from a Latin root referring to the basic

<!-- chunk -->

### “constituents” in a system. An older term would be temperament. This is

<!-- chunk -->

### derived from the root tempero, referring to the temper or mixture of

<!-- chunk -->

### different constituent elements into a whole organism. Well-proportioned

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds are well-tempered.

<!-- chunk -->

### Closely related to constitution and temperament is the study of

<!-- chunk -->

### physiognomy, or the shape of the face and body. Various structures and

<!-- chunk -->

### shapes are associated with different psychological, or physical

<!-- chunk -->

### predispositions, or constitutional elements in a person. Physiognomic

<!-- chunk -->

### study is found in all traditional cultures. Several doctors (William

<!-- chunk -->

### Sheldon, George Draper) undertook extensive statistical studies on

<!-- chunk -->

### physiognomy in the early twentieth century which have never been

<!-- chunk -->

### impeached, though they are ignored by modern biomedicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### The ancient Greeks introduced a basic system of four humors or

<!-- chunk -->

### primal substances in the body (phlegm, blood, yellow bile, and black

<!-- chunk -->

### bile). There were thus four temperaments, in which one of these

<!-- chunk -->

### substances predominated: the phlegmatic, sanguine, choleric, or

<!-- chunk -->

### melancholic. This approach remained frozen in tradition until the

<!-- chunk -->

### seventeenth century, when Greek medicine began to break down.

<!-- chunk -->

### Various temperaments were added or subtracted over the next two

<!-- chunk -->

### centuries. Eventually, the old approach was dropped in favor of a more

<!-- chunk -->

### practical approach (thin, medium, thick). Interestingly, this change

<!-- chunk -->

### occurred in both alternative and conventional medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Alternative practitioners in nineteenth-century America settled upon a

<!-- chunk -->

### system of three basic constitutions corresponding to the three general

<!-- chunk -->

### classes of organs. They called these the mental, motive, and vital types,

<!-- chunk -->

### corresponding to the nervous system, the muscular/skeletal system, and

<!-- chunk -->

### the visceral organs. An early proponent of this system was Dr. D. H.

<!-- chunk -->

### Jacques (1878).

<!-- chunk -->

### In the early twentieth century Western doctors adopted a

<!-- chunk -->

### constitutional system based on thin and thick. The former was called the

<!-- chunk -->

### asthenic type, the latter the esthenic, plethoric, or pyknic type. (Evidently

<!-- chunk -->

### they did not want their terms to become part of everyday language). The

<!-- chunk -->

### leader of this innovation was Dr. Kretchmer, in Germany, and these

<!-- chunk -->

### terms are still used in German medicine. Dr. George Draper was a major

<!-- chunk -->

### American proponent of this system. Dr. William Sheldon developed a

<!-- chunk -->

### system based on three types, thin, medium, and thick, or ectomorph,

<!-- chunk -->

### mesomorph, and endomorph. These terms are still used in non-medical

<!-- chunk -->

### conversation. Michio Kushi uses a modified form of this system in

<!-- chunk -->

### macrobiotic diagnosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### Today, many of these ideas and concepts have been forgotten.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, Western students of constitution have gravitated to the same

<!-- chunk -->

### tripartite system, adopting the Ayurvedic doshas: vata, pitta, and kapha.

<!-- chunk -->

### These correspond respectively to thin, medium, and thick. It should

<!-- chunk -->

### perhaps not be surprising that the same ideas again prevail. As Dr.

<!-- chunk -->

### George Draper (1928, 9) observed about the constitutions thick and thin,

<!-- chunk -->

### “having started to study the personality differences between members of

<!-- chunk -->

### various disease groups, we have come out at precisely the same point in

<!-- chunk -->

### classification of types as have all other observers of mankind.” There is

<!-- chunk -->

### also the medium, which has its own problems. All these systems lie in

<!-- chunk -->

### beautiful and almost perfect alignment. Thus, we get to learn from

<!-- chunk -->

### several different approaches, incorporating insights from ancient and

<!-- chunk -->

### modern studies.

<!-- chunk -->

### The threefold system is based upon the “three classes of organs,” to

<!-- chunk -->

### use the nineteenth-century expression, or the “three layers of the

<!-- chunk -->

### embryo,” to use Sheldon’s. This ties constitution to the organs, helping

<!-- chunk -->

### us to see otherwise hidden connections.

<!-- chunk -->

## Western Physiognomy

<!-- chunk -->

### The Fowler brothers were phrenologists interested in the nature and

<!-- chunk -->

### interpretation of character. Dr. D. H. Jacques brought their ideas into the

<!-- chunk -->

### medical arena. His posthumous work, The Temperaments (1878), gives us

<!-- chunk -->

### basic descriptions of the three primal types which are quoted below. For

<!-- chunk -->

### the sake of clarity, I have used the term “visceral” instead of “vital.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The three types, according to both alternative and conventional

<!-- chunk -->

### doctors were defined as follow:

<!-- chunk -->

## Nervous. The nineteenth-century Western doctors and phrenologists

<!-- chunk -->

### called this the mental type. Nervous seems better suited to the modern

<!-- chunk -->

### ear. This is the vata type of Ayurveda.

<!-- chunk -->

### According to Jacques, “the Mental Temperament is characterized

<!-- chunk -->

### physically by a frame relatively slight, and a head relatively large; an

<!-- chunk -->

### oval face; a high, pale forehead, broadest at the top; delicately cut if not

<!-- chunk -->

### sharp features; an expressive countenance; a delicate, transparent skin;

<!-- chunk -->

### fine, soft hair, generally light in color and not abundant; brilliant,

<!-- chunk -->

### speaking eyes, generally gray or hazel, and quick in their movements;

<!-- chunk -->

### and a high-keyed, flexible voice. The figure is often elegant and graceful,

<!-- chunk -->

### but seldom striking or commanding. The muscles are small, but well

<!-- chunk -->

### formed, and adapted to rapid action, rather than to strength. Fineness

<!-- chunk -->

### and delicacy characterize the whole structure; but there is not

<!-- chunk -->

### necessarily any lack of real stamina, as the healthiness and longevity of

<!-- chunk -->

### persons of this constitution (when it be not subjected to great abuse)

<!-- chunk -->

### abundantly show.

<!-- chunk -->

### “Mentally this temperament indicates activity of the brain, acuteness

<!-- chunk -->

### of the senses, and intensity of emotion.” In the positive expression, these

<!-- chunk -->

### people are intelligent, literary, and sensitive. In the negative, the

<!-- chunk -->

### constitution “is characterized by the smallness and emaciation of the

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles, the quickness and intensity of the sensations, the suddenness

<!-- chunk -->

### and fickleness of the determinations, and a morbid impressibility.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Motive. This is the pitta type of Ayurveda. “In the person of this

<!-- chunk -->

### temperament, firmness of texture characterizes all the organs, imparting

<!-- chunk -->

### great strength and endurance, with an almost unlimited capacity for

<!-- chunk -->

### both mental and bodily labor. There is never much superfluous flesh

<!-- chunk -->

### where the Motive temperament is strongly predominant, and there is

<!-- chunk -->

### often more or less hollowness of cheeks, giving additional ruggedness to

<!-- chunk -->

### the features.”

<!-- chunk -->

### In these people, the bones, muscles, and tendons are emphasized.

<!-- chunk -->

### “Bones proportionally large, and long rather than broad; strong, hard

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles, and prominent articulations, give to the outlines of the form in

<!-- chunk -->

### the Motive temperament a tendency to angularity and sharpness. The

<!-- chunk -->

### figure is commonly tall and striking, if not elegant; the chest moderate in

<!-- chunk -->

### size and fullness; the shoulders broad and definite; the abdomen

<!-- chunk -->

### proportional; the limbs long and only moderately tapering. The face is

<!-- chunk -->

### oblong; the check bones rather high …; the front teeth large; and the

<!-- chunk -->

### features generally prominent and sharply defined. The expression of the

<!-- chunk -->

### countenance is striking, grave, earnest, determined, and sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### severe and stern.

<!-- chunk -->

### “Political agitations, revolutionary movements, heated religious

<!-- chunk -->

### controversies, opposition in all forms, constant resistance to authority or

<!-- chunk -->

### the will of others, and the necessity for being always watchful and on

<!-- chunk -->

### the defensive, are amongst the strongest of these influences” which build

<!-- chunk -->

### this constitution, as well as “the exercise of authority, especially in

<!-- chunk -->

### military forms, and the weight of great responsibilities.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Visceral. The emphasis is on the “vital” or visceral organs associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with digestion and nutrition. This produces a large type. According to

<!-- chunk -->

### Jacques, the “Vital temperament is characterized, physically, by a

<!-- chunk -->

### stature above the medium, and marked by a breadth and thickness of

<!-- chunk -->

### body proportionally greater, and a size of limbs proportionally less than

<!-- chunk -->

### in the Motive temperament. The chest is full, the abdomen well

<!-- chunk -->

### developed, the limbs plump, but tapering, and terminating in hands and

<!-- chunk -->

### feet relatively small. The neck is short and thick, the shoulders broad

<!-- chunk -->

### and well founded, and the head and face, corresponding with the other

<!-- chunk -->

### parts of the system, incline to roundness. The expression of the

<!-- chunk -->

### countenance is generally lively, pleasing, frank, benevolent, and often

<!-- chunk -->

### mirthful. The pose of the body and its movements are natural, easy,

<!-- chunk -->

### graceful, and dignified. The pulse is quicker than in the Motive

<!-- chunk -->

### temperament, and the organization throughout betokens vigor, warmth,

<!-- chunk -->

### and activity. The cheeks flush readily with exercise or emotion, and all

<!-- chunk -->

### the senses are active, acute, and refined.”

<!-- chunk -->

### “An undue preponderance of the absorbent system [the mesenteric

<!-- chunk -->

### lymphatics], conjoined with a sluggish action of the heart and lungs,

<!-- chunk -->

### give rise to an abnormal deposit of watery fluids under the skin,

<!-- chunk -->

### producing additional fullness, and presenting softer and more rounded

<!-- chunk -->

### contours than even those properly belonging to this constitution, but

<!-- chunk -->

### lacking their well-defined and graceful outlines.” This condition, “when

<!-- chunk -->

### fully developed, constitutes the Lymphatic temperament” or phlegmatic

<!-- chunk -->

### constitution of Greek medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

## Ayurvedic Tridosha

<!-- chunk -->

### Vedic philosophy recognizes five basic elements, the same earth, air,

<!-- chunk -->

### fire, and water of the Western/Greek system, plus the additional element

<!-- chunk -->

### of ether, which changes and directs the others (the so-called “fifth

<!-- chunk -->

### element” of Western philosophy). However, it is the three doshas that are

<!-- chunk -->

### primarily used in medical typology: vata, pitta, and kapha. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### considered to be derivatives of the five elements.

<!-- chunk -->

### The word dosha means “that which darkens, or decays.” Thus, the

<!-- chunk -->

### three doshas are looked upon as the substances that cause disease. The

<!-- chunk -->

### tridosha system, therefore, is essentially pathological, rather than

<!-- chunk -->

### “anatomical.” It describes three pathological tendencies. This contrasts

<!-- chunk -->

### with the approach of Fowler, the nineteenth-century physiognomists and

<!-- chunk -->

### the twentieth-century physicians. Their approach was “anatomical”

<!-- chunk -->

### rather than pathological. Thus, a comparison of systems will enrich our

<!-- chunk -->

### understanding of the three basic constitutional types. It is well to

<!-- chunk -->

### understand the constitution in both health and disease. For a

<!-- chunk -->

### comprehensive treatment of the three doshas in relationship to modern

<!-- chunk -->

### health see Rudolph Ballentine’s Radical Healing (1999).

<!-- chunk -->

## Vata. The tendency of this dosha is to dry out. People of this constitution

<!-- chunk -->

### therefore tend to be lean, dry, and concentrated in color, or dark. Their

<!-- chunk -->

### musculature is lean or under developed. The joints, muscles, tendons,

<!-- chunk -->

### and veins are visible through the skin. The eyes, hair, nails, and skin

<!-- chunk -->

### tend to be dry and lusterless. Perspiration and urine are scanty, feces dry

<!-- chunk -->

### and hard. The personality is active, restless, alert, sometimes to the point

<!-- chunk -->

### of sleeplessness, nervousness, fearfulness, and anxiety. They are mentally

<!-- chunk -->

### searching but changeable and lacking in confidence. They like the fields

<!-- chunk -->

### of scholarship, discovery, and communication.

<!-- chunk -->

## Pitta. The tendency of this dosha is to heat up and burn. The

<!-- chunk -->

### constitution is medium in build, sitting midway between the leanness of

<!-- chunk -->

### vata and the heaviness of kapha. Pitta people are emotionally fiery and

<!-- chunk -->

### physically hot. They tend to have a strong appetite, digestion, and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism. They like to eat large quantities of food and beverage,

<!-- chunk -->

### which their system handles well. The body temperature tends to be a

<!-- chunk -->

### little warm and the extremities are usually warm. Their system throws

<!-- chunk -->

### off a moderate to large amount of perspiration and urine. The skin tends

<!-- chunk -->

### to be nicely warm and moist. The complexion tends to be fair, red, or

<!-- chunk -->

### yellow. Thirst may be excessive. They are intelligent and quick-witted,

<!-- chunk -->

### with good comprehension. They can be ardent, aggressive, irritable,

<!-- chunk -->

### jealous. Their speech may be sharp and cutting. They are sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### good orators and, with their natural ambition, sometimes assume roles

<!-- chunk -->

### of leadership.

<!-- chunk -->

## Kapha. This dosha tends to get waterlogged, heavy, and cold. The

<!-- chunk -->

### features are more rounded than in the previous constitutions, because of

<!-- chunk -->

### the thickness of the tissues. The complexion tends to be light, the hair

<!-- chunk -->

### thick, dark, lustrous, and well nourished, the skin soft and oily, but

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes pale, moist, and cold. The eyes may be glowing and

<!-- chunk -->

### magnetic. Internal functions tend to be slow and ponderous, so that

<!-- chunk -->

### digestion is sluggish, the stool is sometimes soft and poorly rid of, the

<!-- chunk -->

### perspiration and urine of moderate amount and sometimes retained.

<!-- chunk -->

### Thinking processes are slow, but more profound, and conclusions more

<!-- chunk -->

### enduring. Once they comprehend a fact they retain it well. They are

<!-- chunk -->

### emotionally more sensitive and subtle. Physically, they like ease,

<!-- chunk -->

### pleasure, and luxury, and tend to collect goods around them.

<!-- chunk -->

## Sheldon’s Three Types

<!-- chunk -->

### In the 1940s, psychologist and physician William Sheldon began to

<!-- chunk -->

### develop a system of body types based on scientific research. The model

<!-- chunk -->

### he came up with also has three types that are thin, medium, and thick in

<!-- chunk -->

### build. Each somatotype had its own peculiar psychological, physical, and

<!-- chunk -->

### medical tendencies, which he determined through statistical studies.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sheldon went a step further and speculated that the three types were

<!-- chunk -->

### derived from a preponderance, during embryological development, of

<!-- chunk -->

### one of the three layers of tissue in the embryo: the ectoderm, mesoderm,

<!-- chunk -->

### or endoderm (meaning the outer, middle, and inner developmental

<!-- chunk -->

### layers). Thus, he named his three types the ectomorph, mesomorph, and

<!-- chunk -->

### endomorph.

<!-- chunk -->

### It turns out that there is a striking correlation between Sheldon’s work

<!-- chunk -->

### and the earlier, physiognomic model, not only with regard to the three

<!-- chunk -->

### constitutional types, but also in regard to the three classes of organs

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with each type. The groupings developed by the earlier,

<!-- chunk -->

### intuitive researchers, correspond exactly with the organs and systems

<!-- chunk -->

### which develop out of the three embryological layers. Remarkably, the

<!-- chunk -->

### earlier researchers had no knowledge of embryological science. Thus, the

<!-- chunk -->

### endoderm develops into the digestive tract, including pancreas, liver,

<!-- chunk -->

### and gall bladder; the respiratory system; and the bladder. The mesoderm

<!-- chunk -->

### evolves into the muscular and skeletal system; the circulatory system

<!-- chunk -->

### (heart and vasculature); and the kidneys and adrenals. The ectoderm

<!-- chunk -->

### gives rise to the nervous system, the sensorium, and the skin through

<!-- chunk -->

### which the nerves and senses operate so strongly.

<!-- chunk -->

## Ectomorph. The ectoderm develops into the outer layers of the body,

<!-- chunk -->

### including the skin and the sensory nervous system. Thus, ectomorphs are

<!-- chunk -->

### directly exposed to the world, making them oversensitive, nervous, and

<!-- chunk -->

### overexposed. They tend to set up ideals that protect them from direct

<!-- chunk -->

### emotional interaction, because they are too sensitive and are constantly

<!-- chunk -->

### being pushed out of balance. They have erratic habits in eating, sleeping,

<!-- chunk -->

### and living. They tend to be easily disturbed and are more vulnerable to

<!-- chunk -->

### sickness than the other types. They tend to strong reactions, emotions,

<!-- chunk -->

### thoughts, and opinions, which can be expressed suddenly and

<!-- chunk -->

### surprisingly, for they keep to themselves for self-protection and

<!-- chunk -->

### contemplation. They have a hard time dealing with these strong

<!-- chunk -->

### reactions, with feelings, sexuality, and other people.

<!-- chunk -->

## Mesomorph. This constitution develops out of the mesoderm, which

<!-- chunk -->

### creates the muscular and skeletal, cardiovascular, and renal systems.

<!-- chunk -->

### They are thus powerful, agile, well built, athletic, strong, and

<!-- chunk -->

### charismatic. They have a medium build between the other two types.

<!-- chunk -->
