### Mesomorphs thrive on action, taking an active role in the world and

<!-- chunk -->

### attempting to have more impact than others do. They tend to exude a

<!-- chunk -->

### feeling of confidence, energy, and optimism, so that others are drawn to

<!-- chunk -->

### them as leaders. They are naturally healthy, but so driven that they can

<!-- chunk -->

### create problems for themselves through injuries and tension. They have

<!-- chunk -->

### a hot arterial system and tendencies to coronary problems. Because of

<!-- chunk -->

### their external orientation, they tend to become isolated from their own

<!-- chunk -->

### internal life, and to be less sensitive to those with whom they have

<!-- chunk -->

### friendships and relationships. This gets them into situations where they

<!-- chunk -->

### are stressed and tense, increasing their health problems. Unlike the

<!-- chunk -->

### endomorphs, the mesomorphs tend not to accept what life hands them,

<!-- chunk -->

### the process of aging, for instance. They belong to the cult of youth and

<!-- chunk -->

### do not necessarily collect the wisdom that comes with the years.

<!-- chunk -->

## Endomorph. This type develops out of a preponderance on the

<!-- chunk -->

### endoderm, the fetal layer that creates the digestive organs and intestines.

<!-- chunk -->

### As a consequence they eat more, gain weight, are thicker in build and

<!-- chunk -->

### rounder in feature. The nerves are less exposed, and the endomorph is

<!-- chunk -->

### more likely to come across as calm, good-natured, easy-going,

<!-- chunk -->

### sympathetic, humorous, friendly, jovial, and affectionate. Some,

<!-- chunk -->

### however, are of a more lethargic, dense, heavy disposition, with less

<!-- chunk -->

### humor and interest in the outside world. In attitude, the endomorph

<!-- chunk -->

### tends to be practical, accepts the reality of situations, and is more rooted

<!-- chunk -->

### in an earthy, sensuous world. Because of the preponderant emphasis on

<!-- chunk -->

### the digestive tract, the endomorphs tend to enjoy and focus on eating,

<!-- chunk -->

### becoming thereby overweight, if not checked by discipline and exercise,

<!-- chunk -->

### both of which they tend to dislike. Endomorphs like all sensuous

<!-- chunk -->

### experiences: eating, drinking, and making merry. Because of the

<!-- chunk -->

### emphasis on intake, psychologically the endomorphs tend to be

<!-- chunk -->

### accepting of people and changes, regarding life experiences as material

<!-- chunk -->

### to be absorbed and assimilated. However, this can degenerate into lack

<!-- chunk -->

### of motivation, apathy, and complacency. They gain weight with age, and

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes become slothful, but they tend to remain functionally strong.

<!-- chunk -->

## The Three Classes of Organs

<!-- chunk -->

### The threefold system provides a basis for understanding which organs

<!-- chunk -->

### will be dominant, overactive, stressed, or underactive. In order to keep

<!-- chunk -->

### this important information in mind, we will draw up a table of

<!-- chunk -->

### correlations between the three embryological layers, the three primary

<!-- chunk -->

### constitutions, and the organ systems.

<!-- chunk -->

### We often see the correlation between, say, the nervous system and the

<!-- chunk -->

### skin. Many diaphoretics that open the pores of the skin also relax the

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous system and help maintain body temperature, hence acting on

<!-- chunk -->

### the nerves and circulation. Work on the muscular and skeletal tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### often contributes to the health of the kidneys and cardiovascular system,

<!-- chunk -->

### since all these tissues and organs rely heavily on the same

<!-- chunk -->

### electrolyte/serum balances. Almost all the organs listed under the

<!-- chunk -->

### visceral/kapha heading are subject to becoming waterlogged, while

<!-- chunk -->

### those in the nervous/vata profile tend to atrophy. At the same time,

<!-- chunk -->

### there are interactions between these columns. Skin, kidneys, lungs, and

<!-- chunk -->

### large intestine are all organs of elimination. We need to see the ebb and

<!-- chunk -->

### flow of disease across the complex field of the body.

<!-- chunk -->

## Neuroendocrine System and Constitution

<!-- chunk -->

### The adrenals are responsible for setting up the body’s basic strategy for

<!-- chunk -->

### coping with stress, the originating factor for disease. They have three

<!-- chunk -->

### basic mechanisms for dealing with stress—the adrenaline, glucocorticoid

<!-- chunk -->

### (GC) and mineralocorticoid (MC) pathways. The former activates the

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous system but deactivates the digestion, so it tends to produce a

<!-- chunk -->

### hyper-alert, nervous, anxious, thin type—the vata/nervous constitution.

<!-- chunk -->

### The GC pathway promotes a non-inflammatory response to stress, which

<!-- chunk -->

### increases appetite and digestion, blood sugar levels, weight gain, and

<!-- chunk -->

### decreases immune response to cause weight gain, lymphatic stagnation,

<!-- chunk -->

### water and toxin retention, more or less the kapha type. When the MC

<!-- chunk -->

### pathway is prominent, the organism responds with increased

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammatory response, higher immune activity and liver catabolism,

<!-- chunk -->

### increased sodium levels, decreased water retention but inflammatory

<!-- chunk -->

### tissues, increased periphery circulation (red, hot, swollen, tender), and a

<!-- chunk -->

### lean or medium figure—the pitta constitution. The GC people tend to be

<!-- chunk -->

### thinking, problem-solvers, while the MC people are fighters who jump in

<!-- chunk -->

### and oppose the enemy before thinking of a solution. Additional

<!-- chunk -->

### endocrine factors (pituitary, thyroid, pancreas) also help develop

<!-- chunk -->

### constitutional tendencies.

<!-- chunk -->

### The cardiovascular system in particular, is susceptible to changes from

<!-- chunk -->

### the dominance of these three coping mechanisms. This is true of both

<!-- chunk -->

### the heart and the circulation. The gastrointestinal system,

<!-- chunk -->

### lymph/immune system, liver, and kidneys are also affected. The

<!-- chunk -->

### respiratory tract and bladder, open as they are to the outside world, are

<!-- chunk -->

### more affected by environmental influences and less by constitutional or

<!-- chunk -->

### endocrine programming.

<!-- chunk -->

## Vata/PittaPitta/KaphaKapha/Vata

### Bob MarleyJesse VenturaQueen Victoria


<!-- chunk -->

### he main symptoms of heat are classic: heat, swelling, redness, and

<!-- chunk -->

### tenderness. However, heat can arise from a number of causes. The

<!-- chunk -->

### heat we are looking for here is that which arises from an inherent

<!-- chunk -->

### over-activity in the tissues themselves. There is an excitement of

<!-- chunk -->

### function, so that the tissues are too easily irritated and incited to action.

<!-- chunk -->

### We find this type of over-activity in auto-immune excess conditions. One

<!-- chunk -->

### of the most characteristic symptoms of excitation is an elongated,

<!-- chunk -->

### pointed, flame-shaped, carmine/red, or pink/red tongue. This is often

<!-- chunk -->

### described in the old literature and is found in all of the following

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies, to my knowledge, except Melissa.

<!-- chunk -->

### When heat arises from the breakdown of waste products in the body,

<!-- chunk -->

### or bacterial activity, or from a lack of water to lubricate and cool the

<!-- chunk -->

### tissues, we are looking at heat as a secondary symptom, not as a primary

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state. Thus, heat from sepsis and deterioration requires stimulants,

<!-- chunk -->

### not sedatives, while heat from a lack of fluids requires moistening

<!-- chunk -->

### agents.

<!-- chunk -->

### Excitation, irritation, or heat especially affects the mucosa, creating

<!-- chunk -->

### allergies of the respiratory and gastrointestinal tracts. It also irritates and

<!-- chunk -->

### congests the capillaries, causing renal congestion and cardiovascular

<!-- chunk -->

### complications.

<!-- chunk -->

### The principal remedies that reduce heat or excitation are, in my

<!-- chunk -->

### experience, sour fruity plants. The sour taste is said to be pitta or fire-

<!-- chunk -->

### generating, but this refers primarily to the “post digestive effects.” Large

<!-- chunk -->

### doses of acids are burning. However, in practice the sour fruit acids

<!-- chunk -->

### almost invariably reduce heat, excitation, or overstimulation of the cells.

<!-- chunk -->

### Think of fruit on a hot summer day. Most fruits are in fact defined as

<!-- chunk -->

### refrigerants, even in Ayurveda.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many members of the Rosaceae family have long been classified as

<!-- chunk -->

### refrigerants or coolants: Rosa (rosehip, rose petal), Fragaria (strawberry),

<!-- chunk -->

### Prunus persica (peach), Amygdalus amara (bitter almond), Crataegus

<!-- chunk -->

### (hawthorn), and Prunus serotina (wild cherry). Other sour plants are also

<!-- chunk -->

### sedative: Rhus typhina (sumac berry), Melissa (lemon balm), Sambucus

<!-- chunk -->

### (elder flower or berry), Passiflora (passionflower), Rheum (rhubarb root),

<!-- chunk -->

### Rumex crispus (yellow dock root), Rumex acetosella (sheep sorrel), Oxalis

<!-- chunk -->

### (wood sorrel), and Hibiscus.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are some anomalous remedies. A very important remedy, which

<!-- chunk -->

### is sedative and yet stimulating, is Achillea (yarrow). It reduces capillary

<!-- chunk -->

### congestion and heat by stimulating venous circulation. As a group, the

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulants treat the opposite tissue state, depression. An elongated,

<!-- chunk -->

### flame-shaped, pink-red tongue with a blue undertone in the center

<!-- chunk -->

### showing the combination of excitation and stagnant blood or depression

<!-- chunk -->

### indicates Achillea.

<!-- chunk -->

### There is also a group of bitter mints (Scutellaria, Leonurus, Lycopus)

<!-- chunk -->

### which decrease heat/excitation and dry/atrophy, especially in

<!-- chunk -->

### conjunction with hyperthyroid tendencies.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are several different kinds of heat. Only that associated with an

<!-- chunk -->

### acceleration in the rate of tissue function—an increase in the innate heat

<!-- chunk -->

### of life—is associated with the irritated tissue state. Heat can also arise

<!-- chunk -->

### from the depressed tissue state because inactive cells (low oxidation)

<!-- chunk -->

### deteriorate and produce heat due to chemical decomposition and

<!-- chunk -->

### bacterial activity. This type of heat is secondary, not primary. It was

<!-- chunk -->

### traditionally called “putrefaction.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Heat can may rise from a lack of fluids. This is associated with the

<!-- chunk -->

### dry/atrophic tissue state, and is treated by moistening herbs such as

<!-- chunk -->

### Althea (marshmallow), Tilia (linden flower), Stellaria (chickweed),

<!-- chunk -->

### Cucurbita (watermelon), and Plantago (plantain). The archetypal sedative

<!-- chunk -->

### for the nineteenth-century doctors was Asclepias tuberosa (pleurisy root),

<!-- chunk -->

### a sweet, moistening herb.

<!-- chunk -->

### A note on the following descriptions of plants and their uses: The section

<!-- chunk -->

### on “Literature” at the end of each plant portrait attempts to give credit

<!-- chunk -->

### to sources for especially unique and original contributions to our

<!-- chunk -->

### knowledge of the medicinal uses of the plant. When a number is

<!-- chunk -->

### included after a name, this shows that the source contributed a

<!-- chunk -->

### prominent symptom. The number refers to the symptom under the

<!-- chunk -->

### heading “Specific Indications,” counting down from the top.

<!-- chunk -->

### Many symptoms are not credited to a single author because they are

<!-- chunk -->

### well known or the original source is unknown. Sometimes, authors are

<!-- chunk -->

### listed who contributed to knowledge of an herb in a significant fashion,

<!-- chunk -->

### but this information is not easily reduced to a single symptom. Giving

<!-- chunk -->

### the actual source by date and page seemed inappropriately tedious, so I

<!-- chunk -->

### developed the above-described method. The idea was not to fulfill

<!-- chunk -->

### scientific standards (which don’t exist for this sort of thing), but to

<!-- chunk -->

### provide practical guidance. Generally, the information can be found in

<!-- chunk -->

### the prominent writings of the person cited under the name of the herb. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is usually not hard to find the account of the herb in most herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### literature since the name is used as a heading.

<!-- chunk -->

### “Specific Indications” are uniquely based on a combination of personal

<!-- chunk -->

### experience and the ability to express, in a succinct manner, an

<!-- chunk -->

### experience of disease as “wrong of life.” They are therefore an

<!-- chunk -->

### expression of the “energetic” experience of the practitioner. The

<!-- chunk -->

### discovery of new information reflects personal experience and deserves

<!-- chunk -->

### to be credited. It is also a matter of responsibility. If a symptom given by

<!-- chunk -->

### one practitioner is not borne out in the experience of others, then the

<!-- chunk -->

### source should be held responsible.

<!-- chunk -->

### Unfortunately, experience is not considered the basis of credible fact

<!-- chunk -->

### in modern biomedicine. It is dismissed as “anecdotal evidence.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Practitioners of an alternative form of health care must therefore

<!-- chunk -->

### develop our own standards and give credit where it is due. Perhaps it

<!-- chunk -->

### would be better to consider this credit as an artistic, rather than a

<!-- chunk -->

### scientific, responsibility.

<!-- chunk -->

## Prunus persica, Amygdalus persica (Peach)

<!-- chunk -->

### Listen to the following language; it is a perfect example of nineteenth-

<!-- chunk -->

### century botanical energetics, localizing both the tissue state (irritation or

<!-- chunk -->

### excitation) and the organ (stomach). “Of all remedies in the materia

<!-- chunk -->

### medica, there are few, if any, so valuable in the treatment of irritation of

<!-- chunk -->

### the stomach and its complications as this one. Its effect is both sedative

<!-- chunk -->

### and tonic to this organ, according to the dose given and the frequency of

<!-- chunk -->

### its repetition. When there is great irritation of the stomach, shown by an

<!-- chunk -->

### elongated, pointed tongue, with reddened tip and edges, and the patient

<!-- chunk -->

### is nervous, restless, and inclined to vomit even from a teaspoonful of

<!-- chunk -->

### water, or the smell or sight of anything to eat, with acute tenderness

<!-- chunk -->

### over the stomach, peach tree will act as a sedative, and should be given

<!-- chunk -->

### in frequently repeated doses.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder, quoted by Dr. John William Fyfe (1909, 368), continues:

<!-- chunk -->

### “Peach tree is the remedy for vomiting when due to irritation, and the

<!-- chunk -->

### above symptoms present; for vomiting of nervous origin; and many times

<!-- chunk -->

### it is an excellent medicament in the vomiting of pregnancy, and will

<!-- chunk -->

### relieve when everything else fails. In gastritis, either acute or chronic,

<!-- chunk -->

### with other indicated remedies, amygdalus is always the treatment. With

<!-- chunk -->

### the symptoms here enumerated pronounced, it is the remedy never to be

<!-- chunk -->

### overlooked or forgotten in cholera infantum, in cholera morbus, and in

<!-- chunk -->

### the debilitating diarrheas and dysenteries so common in children in the

<!-- chunk -->

### summer months.” See the case history on this page.

<!-- chunk -->

### The slight mucilaginous quality of the peach is what makes it tonic in

<!-- chunk -->

### addition to sedative. It acts somewhat more precisely when heat is

<!-- chunk -->

### complicated with dryness. Samuel Thomson (1825, 63) used peach “to

<!-- chunk -->

### recover the natural tone of the stomach after long sickness; and to

<!-- chunk -->

### restore weak patients, particularly in dysentery. A tea made of the leaves

<!-- chunk -->

### of the peach-tree is very good for bowel complaints in children and

<!-- chunk -->

### young people, and will remove cholic.” It is indicated when there is

<!-- chunk -->

### wasting and emaciation with diarrhea.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. O. Phelps Brown (1863, 119) writes that peach leaves are useful

<!-- chunk -->

### “in all inflammations of the stomach and abdomen. They act as a

<!-- chunk -->

### sedative in doses of a tablespoonful every hour or two of the cold

<!-- chunk -->

### infusion. In larger doses, the infusion slightly acts upon the bowels, and

<!-- chunk -->

### has been found useful in removing worms. The kernels [unopened pits]

<!-- chunk -->

### are similarly employed in the form of tincture, infusion, or syrup.”

<!-- chunk -->

### William Cook (1869, 243) concurs in the use of large doses of peach

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves as a laxative to remove worms. “In large quantities, they act

<!-- chunk -->

### mildly upon the bowels, securing mucous discharges without pain; and

<!-- chunk -->

### in this act many times leading to the expulsion of worms in their nests.

<!-- chunk -->

### They are best given in infusion … fresh.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Prunus persica is also beneficial in food allergies. Eva Graf (1978, 76–

<!-- chunk -->

### 9) used peach pit tea as a long-term digestive tonic. She recommends

<!-- chunk -->

### several tablespoons of peach pit or twig tea before and after a meal as a

<!-- chunk -->

### tonic in weak digestion. She used it this way for six, nine, or even twelve

<!-- chunk -->

### months. She said it is most effective for people having trouble digesting

<!-- chunk -->

### protein—usually a stomach ailment—and where there is a lot of gas. Boil

<!-- chunk -->

### the pits and discard those that break open.

<!-- chunk -->

### When my good friend and comrade in herbalism Julia Graves was

<!-- chunk -->

### visiting in Minnesota, I recommended peach tincture for food allergies.

<!-- chunk -->

### “That’s interesting,” she said. “Peach is my first and worst allergy.” We

<!-- chunk -->

### had to make it very dilute, like a homepathic

IX

<!-- chunk -->

### potency (one part in nine

<!-- chunk -->

### parts neutral spirits or water). “One drop gave me a sore throat. Now, I

<!-- chunk -->

### can take three drops, three times a day. I can now eat wheat better than

<!-- chunk -->

### before, my allergic reactions are less acute, and I have no more hay fever

<!-- chunk -->

### (that might be due to change of location, also), and I can eat one peach,

<!-- chunk -->

### kind-of OK! I still react, but only a little.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Peach is a superlative remedy for the irritative tissue state, especially

<!-- chunk -->

### for heat and irritation in people with sensitive skin and tissues. It is also

<!-- chunk -->

### used for nervous agitation with gastric irritation and inflammation. The

<!-- chunk -->

### leaves, twigs, bark, and unopened kernels are used but the seed is

<!-- chunk -->

### poisonous due to the high content of hydrocyanic acid. The classic

<!-- chunk -->

### indication for heat and irritation, an elongated, pink/red tongue, is

<!-- chunk -->

### always present.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here is a case history demonstrating the utility of peach in allergies

<!-- chunk -->

### and asthma. A school teacher in her early thirties came to me for

<!-- chunk -->

### allergies that started after she went to work in an unventilated,

<!-- chunk -->

### windowless, carpeted classroom. It had been getting progressively worse

<!-- chunk -->

### over five years; now it was even bad during summer vacation and she

<!-- chunk -->

### reacted to a lot of other things: the down comforter, wine, corn, milk,

<!-- chunk -->

### etc. She was on an antihistamine and an inhalant as the condition went

<!-- chunk -->

### into asthma. Fair-skinned and blond (a typical Minnesotan), red, dry skin

<!-- chunk -->

### on cheeks and arms, red and scaly spots on the lower legs, slightly

<!-- chunk -->

### sunburned from a few hours of sunshine in early May, a picture of

<!-- chunk -->

### sensitivity. Dark under the eyes, characteristic of a good sinus or

<!-- chunk -->

### bronchial infection with lymphatic stagnation in the adjacent drainage

<!-- chunk -->

### and stress on the adrenals from all the allergy reactions or medicines.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tired, worn out, coughing, wheezing, asthmatic episodes, slight post-

<!-- chunk -->

### nasal drip. Expectorates brown globs of mucus in the morning. Tip of

<!-- chunk -->

### tongue red, sides scalloped, center slightly coated yellow and dry, pulse

<!-- chunk -->

### slow, slightly choppy, tense, and elevated. Peach tincture (leaf, twig),

<!-- chunk -->

### three drops as needed, lessened the cough and irritation, cleared out the

<!-- chunk -->

### mucus, and reduced the need for drugs completely within two weeks.

<!-- chunk -->

### She kept taking the peach, as needed, for two years, with slow, steady

<!-- chunk -->

### improvement in her immunity and no need for the drugs.

<!-- chunk -->

### I always wondered if peach fruit had the same properties as peach

<!-- chunk -->

### leaf. John Heinerman indicates that it does. He recommends the juice for

<!-- chunk -->

### fevers, indigestion, and morning sickness, the standard uses for peach

<!-- chunk -->

### leaf. He also recommends pear juice as a substitute and even gives a case

<!-- chunk -->

### of tuberculosis ameliorated by pear juice.

<!-- chunk -->

### The bark is stronger than the leaves (Tommie Bass). Heat destroys the

<!-- chunk -->

### virtues. Cold infusion of the leaves, small twigs, and bark of the small

<!-- chunk -->

### limbs or maceration in brandy or alcohol. Dose, 1–10 drops of the

<!-- chunk -->

### tincture or extract (Fyfe). The kernels are cleaned and dried for making a

<!-- chunk -->

### cold decoction (Kate Gilday).

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: sour, bitter, sweet, cool, moist, slightly mucilaginous.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: irritation with tendencies to atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: nervous system, skin and mucosa, respiratory,

<!-- chunk -->

### gastrointestinal, sexual-urinary.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervousness, restlessness, insomnia; jangled nerves from bad news;

<!-- chunk -->

### over-excited children.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Overstimulation, hyperactivity, insomnia, nervousness, restlessness,

<!-- chunk -->

### irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fair-skinned persons with tendencies to sunburn, irritation from mild

<!-- chunk -->

### heat, allergies, auto-immune over-activity; heat and irritation of the

<!-- chunk -->

### skin and mucosa.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tongue elongated, pointed, red/pink, dry.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Upper respiratory allergies and irritation; eyes, nose, throat.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cough due to irritation of the throat and bronchial tubes; whooping

<!-- chunk -->

### cough.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Irritation of the stomach and upper GI tract.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Acute tenderness over the stomach; vomiting of nervous origin;

<!-- chunk -->

### especially during pregnancy; cannot bear the sight or smell of food,

<!-- chunk -->

### even water.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous, restless babies with inclination to vomit from the least taste

<!-- chunk -->

### or smell of food; even a teaspoonful of water may be vomited up.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Debilitating diarrhea and dysentery; cholera infantum; cholera

<!-- chunk -->

### morbus.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Worms, with symptoms of heat and irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Extreme tenderness over the irritable bladder; retention of urine,

<!-- chunk -->

### blood in the urine, irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Leucorrhea.

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Maurice Messegue, Samuel Thomson,

<!-- chunk -->

### Constantine Rafinesque, Elisha Smith, Horton Howard, Leonard Tresher,

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

## Rosa canina, Rosa spp.

<!-- chunk -->

## (Wild Rose, Domestic Rose)

<!-- chunk -->

### Rose hips are sour and astringent. In Greek medicine, they are classified

<!-- chunk -->

### as cooling and drying. Apropos of this, Maude Grieve (1931, 690) writes

<!-- chunk -->

### that they “were long official in the British Pharmacopoeia for refrigerant

<!-- chunk -->

### and astringent properties.” She says they are “useful in diarrhea and

<!-- chunk -->

### dysentery, allaying thirst.” They are also beneficial for “coughs and

<!-- chunk -->

### spitting of blood” due to heat and irritation. Avicenna, the great

<!-- chunk -->

### medieval authority, cured bloody expectoration with rose jelly alone and

<!-- chunk -->

### in subsequent centuries the use of rose became established for the

<!-- chunk -->

### treatment of tuberculosis. This is certainly a condition where we would

<!-- chunk -->

### have heat/irritation and bleeding, i.e., the perfect indications for a

<!-- chunk -->

### cooling astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

### Numerous members of the rose clan are extensively used in the

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine of the world. The Greeks, Romans, and Arabic physicians used

<!-- chunk -->

### them. The American wild rose was a favorite with native people. In

<!-- chunk -->

### recent times, rose hips have been used on the premise that they are high

<!-- chunk -->

### in vitamin C. They are also considered to be anti-oxidant. The petals,

<!-- chunk -->

### sepals, hips, leaves, stalks, roots, root bark, and thorns have all been

<!-- chunk -->

### used in traditional medicine. Salmon (1710, 956), lists fifty different

<!-- chunk -->

### preparations.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rose is the personification of passion yet we find that rose and many

<!-- chunk -->

### of its relatives (peach, wild cherry, hawthorn) reduce heat. My poetic

<!-- chunk -->

### muse tells me that these plants turn down excitement in the limbic

<!-- chunk -->

### centers, which regulate both heat and passion. The rosaceae are primary

<!-- chunk -->

### remedies for reducing auto-immune heat and irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Wild or dog rose was the standard remedy in the Roman period for

<!-- chunk -->

### rabies. According to Plinius, the mother of a centurion who dreamed

<!-- chunk -->

### that her son had been bitten by a dog introduced this usage during the

<!-- chunk -->

### Augustan period. She also dreamed of the remedy for him. After his

<!-- chunk -->

### recovery, the emperor publicized this usage.

<!-- chunk -->

### The hips and petals are used for acute inflammatory conditions of the

<!-- chunk -->

### respiratory tract, including sore throat, free nasal secretion, and

<!-- chunk -->

### bronchial secretion or obstruction. It is also a remedy for chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation. It is indicated in the weakness of convalescence, old age,

<!-- chunk -->

### and delicate children. Rose also acts on heat in the digestive tract,

<!-- chunk -->

### including diarrhea, dysentery, and stubborn inflammatory conditions.

<!-- chunk -->

### Maurice Messague (1979, 248) recommends it to counteract the ill

<!-- chunk -->

### effects of antibiotics on the intestinal flora. It is also used for vaginal

<!-- chunk -->

### discharge, menstrual irregularity, and yeast infection (local and

<!-- chunk -->

### systemic). Rose petal waters, lotions, or baths are used to cure arthritis

<!-- chunk -->

### from heat, probably an auto-immune condition.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalist Phyllis Light gives a more specific description of rose. “I tend

<!-- chunk -->

### to use rose hips when the mucous membranes needs to be dried up and

<!-- chunk -->

### cooled down. It helps the body eliminate more through the colon than

<!-- chunk -->

### the kidneys. I ask people if they’re constipated. If they are constipated

<!-- chunk -->

### there is usually a little bit of mucus when they do have a movement. The

<!-- chunk -->

### stool is more ball-like, less formed, with mucus wrapped around or in it.

<!-- chunk -->

### The constipation is due to the fact that the stool is too damp and

<!-- chunk -->

### unformed for the colon to push it out.” Rumex crispus has this symptom;

<!-- chunk -->

### the muscles are too weak to move out the stool.

<!-- chunk -->

### “People might think this is a remedy for mucus colitis, but the

<!-- chunk -->

### problem may arise further up in the system and is just emptying out

<!-- chunk -->

### through the colon. There may be a lot of allergies and lymphatic

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnation further up in the system. To confirm this, I have the person

<!-- chunk -->

### lie down and I press up under the left rib cage to feel if the diaphragm is

<!-- chunk -->

### pressed down by the congestion of the thoracic duct. When that valve is

<!-- chunk -->

### closed, there are going to be allergies, lymphatic congestion, and

<!-- chunk -->

### constipation. Everything stagnates back up the lymphatic system, all the

<!-- chunk -->

### way up to the head, sinuses, and glands in back of the head. Sometimes,

<!-- chunk -->

### when you release the thoracic duct, you can hear the gurgling and

<!-- chunk -->

### everything starts to release. With the lymphatic congestion there will

<!-- chunk -->

### usually be an irritative, nonproductive cough.”

<!-- chunk -->

### I find rose to be slightly less useful than its cousin peach, because the

<!-- chunk -->

### latter combines heat with dryness, a very common condition. The look of

<!-- chunk -->

### the tongue is similar, but peach has an elongated, red/carmine, dry

<!-- chunk -->

### tongue, while rose usually has a red, elongated, and pointed, with

<!-- chunk -->

### streamers of dampness down the sides.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rosehips are the usual part used most frequently today, in capsule,

<!-- chunk -->

### tablet, or seldom, tincture or glycerite. The flowers, leaves, bark, root

<!-- chunk -->

### bark, and even the thorns have all been used in traditional medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (hips) sour, sweet, cool, astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: excitation, relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: mucosa, respiratory tract, gastrointestinal, lymphatics.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Delicate, sensitive; children.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Red, irritated skin and mucosa, with watery deposits or stagnation of

<!-- chunk -->

### the lymphatic drainage from the upper body, head, and chest.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Elongated, pointed, red/pink tongue with moist streamers down the

<!-- chunk -->

### sides.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sore throat, irritative cough.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Vascular anomalies (especially in children); passive venous

<!-- chunk -->

### congestion; circulatory leakage, clotting, sluggishness, easy bruising,

<!-- chunk -->

### thrombosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Heat and irritation in the intestines, constipation, damp, mucus-

<!-- chunk -->

### containing stool, mucus colitis, chronic yeast infection.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Vaginitis, vaginal irritation, and discharge.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Arthritic inflammation from auto-immune hyperactivity.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Easily bruised.

## Melissa officinalis (Lemon Balm)

<!-- chunk -->

### The indications for lemon balm are short and simple but it is still one of

<!-- chunk -->

### the most useful remedies. It is a most important sedative for the nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### system when there is sympathetic excess, hyperadrenalinism, or

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperthyroidism. Like many sour plants, it is cooling. It is often

<!-- chunk -->

### combined with Lycopus in hyperthyroidism or is specific by itself.

<!-- chunk -->

### A seventy-six-year-old man came to see me because he had an

<!-- chunk -->

### aneurysm. I gave yarrow (it had worked once before), plus a bunch of

<!-- chunk -->

### basic heart tonics, like hawthorn and tulip poplar. A year went by, and

<!-- chunk -->

### while I helped him with some minor skin and eye annoyances, the

<!-- chunk -->

### aneurysm only enlarged. Finally, we had to do something about it or

<!-- chunk -->

### surgery would be necessary. I looked at the case afresh. Over the past

<!-- chunk -->

### few months his face had gotten red, he had gained ten pounds, and he

<!-- chunk -->

### was having night sweats. His pulse was sharp-edged and irregular; his

<!-- chunk -->

### tongue was strongly red/pink. This suggested sympathetic nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### excess with heat/irritation. I finally decided on that great sympathetic

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxant and sedative for the heart, Melissa. Six months later the

<!-- chunk -->

### aneurysm was smaller, the night sweats were long gone, the weight was

<!-- chunk -->

### back to normal, pulse less irregular and relaxed, tongue less red. We

<!-- chunk -->

### were both very happy.

<!-- chunk -->

### A fifty-six-year-old woman came to me for severe short term memory

<!-- chunk -->

### loss which occurred after she received various potencies of homeopathic

<!-- chunk -->

### Cannabis from a young homeopathic enthusiast. She had never had much

<!-- chunk -->

### marijuana as a youth, so I am not sure why this remedy was chosen, but

<!-- chunk -->

### it caused severe psychological hardship. She felt out of her body, had

<!-- chunk -->

### memory lapses severe enough to send her to biomedical doctors; and a

<!-- chunk -->

### continual mental tension that made it hard to think. The pulse was

<!-- chunk -->

### slightly tense and sharp, shifting slowly from faster to slower. Melissa

<!-- chunk -->

### tincture, five drops as needed, cleared up these problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### The fresh plant is so much superior to the dried that it should be

<!-- chunk -->

### picked from the garden for tea or tinctured fresh in a good, sweet

<!-- chunk -->

### brandy. Dosage can be small (one to three drops) to large (ten to thirty

<!-- chunk -->

### drops).

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: sour, cool.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: excitation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: nervous system (sympathetic excess),

<!-- chunk -->

### hyperthyroidism, heart.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervousness, depression, anxiety, insomnia.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Debility and weakness of nervous origin.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Recent colds and fevers.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Acute febrile conditions in children.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dyspepsia, upset stomach, and flatulence.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Anxiety and palpitations; hyperthyroidism.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Heart palpitations, atrial fibrillation, high blood pressure, associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with nervousness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hot flashes.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fever.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Burns, blisters, herpetic sores, and stings.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Humberto Santillo, Rudolf Weiss, Matthew

<!-- chunk -->

### Wood.

<!-- chunk -->

## Rumex crispus (Yellow Dock Root)

<!-- chunk -->

### Yellow dock root is sour, astringent, and faintly bitter, therefore suited

<!-- chunk -->

### to conditions where there is heat/irritation combined with relaxation

<!-- chunk -->

### and a little congestion. It contains small amounts of anthroquinones,

<!-- chunk -->

### which are purgative, but it is better to think of it as a heat-reducer

<!-- chunk -->

### rather than as a laxative. Like its cousin rhubarb, it would be badly and

<!-- chunk -->

### incompletely defined in this way. It is always indicated by an elongated

<!-- chunk -->

### tongue with red on the sides or middle, so it is a remedy for

<!-- chunk -->

### heat/irritation. Often there is a thrushlike coating and it is indeed a

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy for thrush, a symptom often associated with relaxation. The

<!-- chunk -->

### facial skin is usually red and dry on the checks, showing heat and

<!-- chunk -->

### dryness, with yellow around the eyes, nose, and mouth, showing heat in

<!-- chunk -->

### the digestive tract. This last indication comes from my friend Lise Wolff.

<!-- chunk -->

### Yellow dock root is a primary remedy in the treatment of heat in the

<!-- chunk -->

### GI tract. It is indicated when there is an over abundance of saliva,

<!-- chunk -->

### showing that there is digestive over activity and excessive appetite,

<!-- chunk -->

### when there is excessive hydrochloric acid, with esophageal reflux and

<!-- chunk -->

### burning, and when there is irritable heat in the small intestine or colon

<!-- chunk -->

### causing Crohn’s disease or colitis. The stool is frequently moist, pappy,

<!-- chunk -->

### and difficult to expel, with an unfinished feeling and, sometimes

<!-- chunk -->

### hemorrhoids. In addition, yellow dock root acts on the skin and may

<!-- chunk -->

### have an alterative or sanative effect through the liver and gall bladder.

<!-- chunk -->

### Drugs for esophageal reflux and acid stomach are the biggest sellers in

<!-- chunk -->

### America at the present time. In the past, this has been an area with few

<!-- chunk -->

### options in the herbal pharmacopoeia. If people only knew about yellow

<!-- chunk -->

### dock root. It comes close to being a specific in this condition. Wooster

<!-- chunk -->

### Beach (1842, 420) reported the use of yellow dock root tea to cure an

<!-- chunk -->

### acid stomach.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here is a case history from my own records. A forty-five-year-old man

<!-- chunk -->

### had been suffering from loose stools, difficult to move for about six or

<!-- chunk -->

### seven years. He also had a hemorrhoid that bled from time to time,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially when the stool was difficult to pass. He had excess salivation

<!-- chunk -->

### and appetite, occasional esophageal reflux, and stomach acidity. The

<!-- chunk -->

### tongue was elongated, flame-like, with reddish coloration, and a thick,

<!-- chunk -->

### moist, yellow coating. All these symptoms are classic for yellow dock

<!-- chunk -->

### root. Doses of the tincture, when needed, over the next two years

<!-- chunk -->

### removed all the symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

### What yellow dock does for the stomach it will do further down the

<!-- chunk -->

### digestive canal. Forbush (quoted by Fyfe, 1909) gives the following

<!-- chunk -->

### description: “In diarrhea and dysentery Rumex is a remedy of very great

<!-- chunk -->

### value. It has cured exceedingly persistent cases of exhaustive morning

<!-- chunk -->

### diarrhea with the following symptoms, viz.: Evacuations scanty, bloody,

<!-- chunk -->

### oftentimes offensive, colicky pains before and after stool, sinking at the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach, fetid breath, sweat, and urine; the discharges being very

<!-- chunk -->

### frequent between early morning and midday. In the asthenic forms of

<!-- chunk -->

### intestinal wrongs, and in the diarrhea of phthisis, it affects the bowels,

<!-- chunk -->

### sensibly reacting as a mild astringent not unlike rhubarb. It is especially

<!-- chunk -->

### serviceable in the dysentery of aged persons, and has been used

<!-- chunk -->

### successfully in apparently hopeless cases.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Rumex is a superlative remedy for enteritis and colitis. Here is a case

<!-- chunk -->

### where the small intestine was involved. A forty-two-year-old woman

<!-- chunk -->

### came to see me for Crohn’s disease. It had been quiet for about ten years,

<!-- chunk -->

### but recently her home life flared up, she threatened to leave her husband

<!-- chunk -->

### (“he’s so boring”), and she suffered a serious recurrence. The tongue was

<!-- chunk -->

### elongated, with red sides and tip, with a little bit of thrushlike coating

<!-- chunk -->

### one sometimes sees on the back of the tongue when this remedy is

<!-- chunk -->

### needed. (A thrushlike coating looks like the coat on brie cheese). She

<!-- chunk -->

### had the usual pains in the abdomen, and diarrhea. Three drops of Rumex

<!-- chunk -->

### crispus, three times a day proved curative. At her next doctor’s

<!-- chunk -->

### appointment, two weeks later, she was informed that the intestine had

<!-- chunk -->

### not looked this good in two years. She continues to do well eight months

<!-- chunk -->

### later, but as people with Crohn’s are prone to setbacks, we will wait and

<!-- chunk -->

### see how the situation resolves itself.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here is a case where the problem was in the colon. I was called to the

<!-- chunk -->

### hospital to see a thirty-four-year-old woman suffering from severe colitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### It had started five weeks after the birth of her third child. She was on

<!-- chunk -->

### sixty milligrams of prednisone daily; it provided no help whatsoever. She

<!-- chunk -->

### was having fifteen to twenty dysenteric movements a day and the

<!-- chunk -->

### doctors were saying that if it didn’t stop they would remove her colon in

<!-- chunk -->

### three days. I noticed that her face was irritated, and red with yellow

<!-- chunk -->

### patches; it looked like serum was coming up to the surface. This

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated yellow dock root (see below). I gave a half dropper in water.

<!-- chunk -->

### She was constipated for two days and recovered completely. Three years

<!-- chunk -->

### later there has been no major relapse. Every time some little diarrhea

<!-- chunk -->

### starts, she takes a few doses of yellow dock root. She has been on a drug

<!-- chunk -->

### for enteritis all these years and was afraid to stop taking it. However, it

<!-- chunk -->

### is the yellow dock root that, again and again, controls the symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### when they arise.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here’s a case history relating to acute diarrhea. My friend Julia Graves

<!-- chunk -->

### helped a friend in India with constant morning diarrhea lasting for days.

<!-- chunk -->

### He had a red, dry tongue with a white coat. One dose cured this

<!-- chunk -->

### condition.

<!-- chunk -->

### In addition to acting on the gastrointestinal tract, yellow dock acts on

<!-- chunk -->

### the skin. It is indicated in weeping eczema, when there is a rusty-red

<!-- chunk -->

### appearance to the skin (like the color of ripened yellow dock seed) with

<!-- chunk -->

### some yellowness, or actual weeping of yellow serum.

<!-- chunk -->

### The root is collected after going to seed in mid-summer and used by

<!-- chunk -->

### decoction or tincture. Usual dose of the tincture, five to thirty drops

<!-- chunk -->

### (Fyfe).

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (root) slightly sour, bitter, cool, damp, astringent, “moldy.”

<!-- chunk -->

### (leaves) sour, astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue States: irritation, relaxation, stagnation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: gastrointestinal, skin.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Yellow about the eyes, nose, and mouth with reddish, often dry

<!-- chunk -->

### checks.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fractious, irritable, sleep does not give needed recuperation;

<!-- chunk -->

### especially in young girls about puberty.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Throat swollen, sensitive and dark red, with little pain or absence of

<!-- chunk -->

### pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nursing sore mouth and ulceration of the mucosa of the mouth

<!-- chunk -->

### (thrush).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sides of the tongue hurt, especially from consuming acids; patches of

<!-- chunk -->

### white thrush on tongue or back in mouth.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Irritable cough, worse in cold air.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Slow digestion; food sits in the stomach or the colon, like a brick.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea, dysentery, colitis; of aged persons, infants, nursing mothers.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea, especially in the morning; exhaustive; scanty; bloody;

<!-- chunk -->

### offensive; colicky pains before and after passage; sinking at the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pain in the lower back caused by constipation; the patient is not

<!-- chunk -->

### necessarily aware of the lessened elimination, but of the pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lessened activity of the intestinal canal; food sits or is poorly expelled

<!-- chunk -->

### by the rectum.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hemorrhoids, with bleeding, itching, smarting; poor expulsion of stool

<!-- chunk -->

### (cf. Polygonum punctatum, a cousin).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menstrual irregularities with anemia; amenorrhea; vicarious

<!-- chunk -->

### menstruation; particularly in young girls and women, at puberty,

<!-- chunk -->

### when nervous symptoms predominate.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Red, damp, corrosive skin conditions.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fetid breath, sweat, urine.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Lise Wolff (1), Frobush, quoted by Fyfe (2, 3, 4,

<!-- chunk -->

### 8, 9, 15), homeopathic provings (5, 6), J. I. Lighthall (7), David Milgrom

## Achillea millefolium (Yarrow)

<!-- chunk -->

### Yarrow is one of the primal remedies of traditional Western herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. I have discussed it elsewhere in detail and will undoubtedly

<!-- chunk -->

### enlarge upon it in the future, so I will restrain my remarks for the

<!-- chunk -->

### present. The taste is pungent, bitter, and mildly astringent. There is a bit

<!-- chunk -->

### of acridity hidden under the pungency. This should make it a stimulant

<!-- chunk -->

### with slightly relaxant and toning properties, but yarrow is an anomalous

<!-- chunk -->

### plant that does not fit the usual analysis. It is one of the few stimulants

<!-- chunk -->

### that are quite cooling, although there are others such as echinacea and

<!-- chunk -->

### lavender. The main action of yarrow is exerted on the veins, to tone,

<!-- chunk -->

### clarify, and stimulate them, so that it cools by opening up the veins and

<!-- chunk -->

### allowing congestion out of the capillaries. This is exactly how

<!-- chunk -->

### Christopher Hedley, one of the prominent British herbalists describes it,

<!-- chunk -->

### and it accords with my experience as well. Thus it is indicated when

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue is hot, red, tender, and swollen. At the same time, as a bitter/acrid

<!-- chunk -->

### herb it settles fever and chills that affect the circulation, opening pores

<!-- chunk -->

### to release sweat. Yarrow is especially used for (1) lacerations, bruises,

<!-- chunk -->

### and bleeding; (2) fever and chills; (3) heat and congestion in the

<!-- chunk -->

### digestive tract, portal vein, liver, and abdominal viscera; and (4) uterine

<!-- chunk -->

### congestion and excessive (or lack of) bleeding. It is excellent for uterine

<!-- chunk -->

### fibroids with bright red bleeding. In several months it will remove large

<!-- chunk -->

### fibroids.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are several specific indications leading especially to yarrow. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### almost always indicated by an elongated, pointed, reddish tongue (the

<!-- chunk -->

### classic indication for heat/irritation), but there is usually a blue

<!-- chunk -->

### undertone indicating venous stagnation. The tongue is usually dry in the

<!-- chunk -->

### center, indicating that heat is driving off fluids. These indications come

<!-- chunk -->

### from my own practice. The pulse is usually rapid, full, and non-resistant,

<!-- chunk -->

### showing that heat is having its way with the tissues. William LeSassier

<!-- chunk -->

### taught that there may be a crack down the center of the tongue, in

<!-- chunk -->

### severe cases opening up to reveal a deeper layer of tissue which is dark

<!-- chunk -->

### and crisscrossed by lines (chained or feathered). This is an indication

<!-- chunk -->

### that heat is burning deep into the tissues, especially of the digestive

<!-- chunk -->

### tract.

<!-- chunk -->

### The flower tops are harvested at their peak and dried for use as a tea or

<!-- chunk -->

### tinctured fresh in alcohol. The young leaves and roots may also be used.

<!-- chunk -->

### The best quality yarrow grows on sandy, gravely soil that warms up in

<!-- chunk -->

### the sun and is exposed in winter. Moist soil dilutes the bitter property.

<!-- chunk -->

### Usual dose is five to twenty drops (Fyfe). It should not be taken in large

<!-- chunk -->

### doses during pregnancy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: bitter, pungent, cold, dry, stimulating, diffusive, slightly

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: irritation, depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: blood, veins, capillaries, temperature regulation, GI

<!-- chunk -->

### tract, female reproductive tract.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Robust, sanguine persons; with red, full-blooded complexion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • The “wounded warrior, wounded healer” remedy; people that jump

<!-- chunk -->

### in, put out the fires, get cut to the bone, emotionally and physically.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Complexion of face and skin red with blue veins showing through on

<!-- chunk -->

### the arms and legs; bruises are red and blue.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Congestion of blood to the head; headache, nosebleed; in red, robust,

<!-- chunk -->

### sanguine persons, or during fever.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tongue elongated, pointed, red, dry towards the center, not heavily

<!-- chunk -->

### coated, wet towards the edges.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tongue cracked down the middle and opening up to reveal

<!-- chunk -->

### crisscrossed, red tissues (“chaining” or “feathering–looks like a

<!-- chunk -->

### feather or a yarrow leaf); this indicating “heat attacking the blood

<!-- chunk -->

### level.”

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tongue elongated, pointed, red, with blue in the center.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nosebleed.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bronchitis, pleurisy, pneumonia; inflammation and pain in the chest,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially before mucus sets in.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bleeding from the lungs with night sweats; palliative in tuberculosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dyspepsia, colitis, diverticulitis, bleeding hemorrhoids; tongue red

<!-- chunk -->

### and cracked down the middle; with rapid, full, non-resistant pulse.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea in infants.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bleeding from the stomach or intestines.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bleeding hemorrhoids (cf. Bidens, Rumex crispus).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lack of menstrual flow; or, heavy, hemorrhagic flow.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menstrual cramping, amenorrhea, excessive bleeding.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Specific and highly effective for uterine fibroids; with bright red

<!-- chunk -->

### hemorrhage (cf. Capsella, fibroids with clotted, dark flow).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Vaginal discharge.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menopausal restlessness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nephritis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • High blood pressure where there is venous congestion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • In acute fever and some chronic conditions, pulse rapid, full, and non-

<!-- chunk -->

### resistant.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fever; sudden onset, in robust persons, with or without chills; brings

<!-- chunk -->

### out rash.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Arthritis, gout, rheumatism.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lacerations; injuries to the arteries of violent origin; hemorrhage, of

<!-- chunk -->

### bright red arterial blood.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bruises of violent origin; with cuts (cf. Arnica, bruises without cuts or

<!-- chunk -->

### bleeding); blood blisters.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Old bruises that have become hard; especially on the head.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stroke, head injury, hematoma.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Antidote to radiation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Maria Treben (16, 17, 18), Barbara St. Dennis

<!-- chunk -->

### (2), William LeSassier (5, 6, 7), Matthew Wood (1, 5, 6, 7), E. B. Nash

<!-- chunk -->

### (10), Jack Ritchason (12), Nicholas Culpeper (14), Richard Hool (14),

<!-- chunk -->

### Brent Davis, Flower Essence Society (30).


<!-- chunk -->

### he constrictive tissue state is one of the easiest to recognize

<!-- chunk -->

### because we have all experienced tension—psychological or

<!-- chunk -->

### physical—and suffered from muscle spasms. However, there is

<!-- chunk -->

### some subtlety to this tissue state that might be missed. Repeated chills,

<!-- chunk -->

### as were once common in malaria and still occur in influenza and deep

<!-- chunk -->

### infections, will unstring the autonomic nervous system, causing chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### chills. This was a serious problem in old-time medicine. It is less so

<!-- chunk -->

### today, but there are still many people who have never been better since

<!-- chunk -->

### they suffered from a chill or influenza. In addition to tension and chills,

<!-- chunk -->

### we should also mention as characteristic symptoms that are sudden

<!-- chunk -->

### (coming or going), intermittent, or alternating (like diarrhea and

<!-- chunk -->

### constipation). The mind and nerves are greatly affected by this tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### state. The gastrointestinal tract, which is so intimately associated with

<!-- chunk -->

### the autonomic nervous system, is the physical area most affected.

<!-- chunk -->

### Plants with an acrid flavor are used in Chinese herbalism to treat wind

<!-- chunk -->

### and chill, conditions equivalent to constriction. Almost all of the great

<!-- chunk -->

### antispasmodics traditionally used in the Western pharmacopoeia are

<!-- chunk -->

### acrid. (Acridity tastes like bile in the throat). Since this is an unfamiliar

<!-- chunk -->

### taste, I have listed the flavor of each plant as best I could: Lobelia (purely

<!-- chunk -->

### acrid); Symplocarpus (skunk cabbage; extremely acrid); Piper methysticum

<!-- chunk -->

### (kava kava; acrid and pungent); Liriodendron (tulip poplar; acrid and

<!-- chunk -->

### sweet); Cimicifuga (black cohosh; acrid and sweet); Chamomilla

<!-- chunk -->

### (chamomile; acrid, sweet, bitter and pungent); Valeriana (acrid,

<!-- chunk -->

### pungent); Humulus (hops; acrid and bitter); and Nepeta (catnip; acrid and

<!-- chunk -->

### pungent). Valerian, hops, catnip, and viburnum (or crampbark) all

<!-- chunk -->

### contain substances that break down into valerianic acid.

<!-- chunk -->

### Lobelia was the archetypal relaxant for the old physiomedicalist

<!-- chunk -->

### doctors who came up with the tissue state model. I have not given an

<!-- chunk -->

### account of it here because I covered it thoroughly in The Book of Herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### Wisdom. Valerian and its pharmacological kin would be fairly archetypal

<!-- chunk -->

### too; valerian has the acrid taste moderated by some pungency. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### my favorite relaxant is agrimony. Another superlative relaxant, which is

<!-- chunk -->

### also a building, nutritive tonic, is the sweet/acrid Liriodendron, but that

<!-- chunk -->

### is too obscure a medicine for most of our readers.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another taste which acts on wind, chills, and tension is the “bitter

<!-- chunk -->

### nauseant” flavor of some of the extremely bitter plants like Eupatorium

<!-- chunk -->

### perf. (boneset), Lactuca virosa (wild lettuce), Verbena (blue vervain), and

<!-- chunk -->

### Artemisia absinthium (wormwood). These plants are so bitter they send a

<!-- chunk -->

### chill through the body, hence they are for chills. It is likely that this taste

<!-- chunk -->

### is due to a conjunction of acridity and bitterness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tension can be caused by some of the other tissue states. Dryness or

<!-- chunk -->

### atrophy will cause constriction because water sedates the nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### system. The opposite is also true; constriction of the muscles causes the

<!-- chunk -->

### circulation to be thwarted, resulting in under nutrition of the part,

<!-- chunk -->

### drying, and atrophy. Heat and excitation cause tension by drying out

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids. Relaxation can cause it by piling up blood, which blocks

<!-- chunk -->

### circulation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Agrimonia eupatoria, A. grysopetela (Agrimony)

<!-- chunk -->

### In my experience, this is the superlative remedy for tension. I have

<!-- chunk -->

### covered it elsewhere extensively (The Book of Herbal Wisdom), so the

<!-- chunk -->

### following account will be brief.

<!-- chunk -->

### I am not sure whether agrimony relieves tension because it is an

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent—many of these agents reduce tension even though one would

<!-- chunk -->

### think otherwise—or whether it works because there is a little bit of acrid

<!-- chunk -->

### resin on the leaves before flowering. Agrimony is fairly bland, but if we

<!-- chunk -->

### study the taste carefully we detect some sweetness, astringency, and

<!-- chunk -->

### acridity. After flowering, the astringency is more pronounced. Those

<!-- chunk -->

### who believe in the doctrine of signatures will note that it is a tense

<!-- chunk -->

### looking plant. I use agrimony collected before flowering and prefer the

<!-- chunk -->

### native American species, A. grysopetela. I use small doses (one to three

<!-- chunk -->

### drops), but any size dose will work. An especially relaxing combination

<!-- chunk -->

### is agrimony (seven parts) and true Solomon’s seal (four parts).

<!-- chunk -->

### The indication for tension originated with Dr. Bach. It holds true on

<!-- chunk -->

### both the mental and physical levels, for both the flower essence and the

<!-- chunk -->

### herbal preparation. The patient tries to hide the tension behind a false

<!-- chunk -->

### facade of suave sophistication or cheerfulness, denying the pain with a

<!-- chunk -->

### smile. I like to say she is “torturedly cheerful.” Others are so cool and

<!-- chunk -->

### suave you think, “How come I’m not going to them for advice? What do

<!-- chunk -->

### they need to see me for?” Or, according to Bach, they like speed and

<!-- chunk -->

### danger, drugs, alcohol, and stimulants. The problem often revolves

<!-- chunk -->

### around business and hierarchical relations (employee/employer).

<!-- chunk -->

### Agrimony is a specific in any condition where the person holds the

<!-- chunk -->

### breath to stop the pain. Patients report all sorts of digestive problems

<!-- chunk -->

### due to tension and incorrect timing of functions. Innervation to the liver,

<!-- chunk -->

### gall bladder, and GI tract is off. Agrimony is a remedy for liver problems,

<!-- chunk -->

### allergies, skin rashes, and wasting (due to poor liver anabolism and

<!-- chunk -->

### catabolism of proteins), diarrhea, ulcerations of the bowels and lower

<!-- chunk -->

### extremities, kidney and bladder pain, menstrual pain and bleeding.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: mildly sweet, sour, acrid, neutral/cool, resinous, slightly

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: constriction, relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: nervous system, gastrointestinal, liver, kidneys.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Mental tension; feels caught in a bind, “torturedly cheerful,” hides

<!-- chunk -->

### anger and frustration behind an artificial facade; suave but inwardly

<!-- chunk -->

### tortured.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Work-related tension; employer-employee tension.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Holds the breath to suppress the pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Resorts to drugs, alcohol, speed, and danger to suppress emotions.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tension headaches.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Slight yellow/gray complexion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Allergic welts associated with tension.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Ulcerations of the tongue, sores in the mouth, aphtha.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Respiratory problems; whooping cough, bronchitis, asthma; “tortured

<!-- chunk -->

### to capture the breath;” holds the breath.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Ulceration of the gastrointestinal tract, tongue, mouth, stomach,

<!-- chunk -->

### intestines.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Difficulty with fat digestion and metabolism.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nausea, vomiting, diarrhea with griping and spasms.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Constipation suddenly followed by diarrhea.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Loose, fatty, floating stools; undigested food, large lumps, nuts,

<!-- chunk -->

### proteins in the stool.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Food poisoning, amoebic dysentery, gastroenteritis; doesn’t kill the

<!-- chunk -->

### micro-organisms but tones the bowels (cf. Rubus).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hepatitis, cirrhosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mucus colitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tension and pain during the passage of a gall-or kidney stone; blue,

<!-- chunk -->

### purple tongue, wiry pulse, and holding of the breath to stop the pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Appendicitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Exhaustion of kidneys.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pointing pains in the kidneys; with or without the passage of stones,

<!-- chunk -->

### in various kinds of urinary problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Urinary tract infections with severe pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bed-wetting and diarrhea; in children, suffering anxiety about toilet

<!-- chunk -->

### training.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Incontinence in the elderly; after passing stones.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Edema with welts, coming and going suddenly.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menstrual pain; hides the pain, pretends to be fine, composed.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Vaginal ulceration.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tense pulse.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Articular, spinal pain, acute and chronic; torturous; sprains, strains.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Ulceration on the labia, buttocks, legs.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Skin rash, allergies, ulcers, psoriasis; with mental tension.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cancer, stomach.

<!-- chunk -->

### Literature: Traditional (19, 24), Edward Bach (1, 2, 4, 9, 27), Matthew
