## Chamomilla matricaria,

<!-- chunk -->

## Matricaria chamomilla (Chamomile)

<!-- chunk -->

### Half a dozen plants go by the name chamomile, all belonging to the

<!-- chunk -->

### Asteraceae or Composite family. Two of them are used in herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine, Chamomilla matricaria and Anthemis nobile, sometimes called

<!-- chunk -->

### “German” and “Roman chamomile.” Both contain a blue volatile oil

<!-- chunk -->

### (azulene) which is the active ingredient responsible for the medicinal

<!-- chunk -->

### properties. They have been used interchangeably since antiquity and

<!-- chunk -->

### their uses are not well differentiated in the literature, even in

<!-- chunk -->

### homeopathy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Chamomile is an old popular medicine in Europe. It has a long history

<!-- chunk -->

### of use in folk medicine as a sedative for cross, whining, irritated babies,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the problems incident to babyhood, such as teething, earaches, and

<!-- chunk -->

### intolerance to pain. The child is peevish, whining, complaining, angry,

<!-- chunk -->

### demanding attention and petting. He demands a toy, then throws it on

<!-- chunk -->

### the floor. Beyond this, however, Chamomilla is suited to “babies of any

<!-- chunk -->

### age,” as I like to say. Whatever the age, the behavior is unmistakable.

<!-- chunk -->

### Petulant, self-centered, intolerant of pain or not having their way,

<!-- chunk -->

### inclined to pick quarrels, or averse to being touched, soothed, or spoken

<!-- chunk -->

### to. It is not suited to infants (Sambucus) or children (chicory), but to

<!-- chunk -->

### babies. It has been proven and is used in homeopathy on the same

<!-- chunk -->

### indications as in herbalism.

<!-- chunk -->

### Certain structures of the body are more susceptible to maddening

<!-- chunk -->

### irritation and pain than others. The ears, eyes, teeth, and sexual organs

<!-- chunk -->

### seem to be the principal organs. Many cases of earache and toothache

<!-- chunk -->

### have been cured by homeopaths.

<!-- chunk -->

### The material herb, the tincture, and the homeopathic potencies meet

<!-- chunk -->

### the same conditions. The fresh herb has considerably more virtue than

<!-- chunk -->

### the dried (Rudolf Weiss). The homeopathic preparations are made from

<!-- chunk -->

### the fresh juice of the herb. A few people are allergic to chamomile.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: sweet, acrid, bitter, cooling.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: irritation, constriction.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: babies, GI tract, fever, pain.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Children, during teething, “terrible twos;” fretful, must be carried all

<!-- chunk -->

### the time; wants different things, throws them away when getting

<!-- chunk -->

### them; nightmares. Adults who act in a similar manner; “babies of any

<!-- chunk -->

### age.”

<!-- chunk -->

### • Petulant, quarrelsome, vitiated humor; inclines to be angry and out of

<!-- chunk -->

### sorts with everything and everybody; very impatient, can hardly

<!-- chunk -->

### answer with civility; contrary to normal condition, out of humor,

<!-- chunk -->

### particularly at menses or when sick.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Whining, peevish, complaining, wants attention but rejects it; low

<!-- chunk -->

### resistance to pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dandruff.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Eye irritation (wash), earache.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tongue tends to be irritated along the sides and coated white-yellow

<!-- chunk -->

### in the middle.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Acute colds, fevers, flu, coughs, earache; bronchitis, asthma.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Acute digestive upset, coming on after vexation and anger; with heat

<!-- chunk -->

### of the face, thirst, taste of bile, nausea, anxiety, restlessness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lack of appetite; cramps, colic, diverticulitis, colitis, diarrhea,

<!-- chunk -->

### constipation, irritable bowel syndrome.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Liver and gall bladder upset; jaundice, gallstones, gallstone colic.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menstrual pain and cramping associated with great tenderness; cannot

<!-- chunk -->

### be borne with any patience; pain in the abdomen and breasts, cysts.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Edema.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tightness and tension in the tendons, ligaments, muscles, and

<!-- chunk -->

### extremities; paralytic pains, with numbness and tearing, in the

<!-- chunk -->

### extremities; convulsion, spasm, tightness, rheumatic pain, and

<!-- chunk -->

### neuralgia.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Incomplete fever; excitement, increased sensibility, prostration; heat

<!-- chunk -->

### partial, confined to one cheek; profuse sweat of the head. The fever

<!-- chunk -->

### does not last long, but often recurs.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Abscess, pus formation, especially in the face, jaws, with tenderness

<!-- chunk -->

### and pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pulse small, tense, and rapid.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional. The most specific information comes from

<!-- chunk -->

### homeopathic provings and clinical experience, including the following

<!-- chunk -->

### indications and authors collected by Temple Hoyne: William Bayes (6),

<!-- chunk -->

### Samuel Hahnemann (8), Carrol Dunham (14), Adolphe Lippe (16).

<!-- chunk -->

## Nepeta cataria (Catnip)

<!-- chunk -->

### Catnip contrasts well with chamomile, since they are both remedies for

<!-- chunk -->

### tension in children. This is not one of my commonly used remedies, but

<!-- chunk -->

### it was one of the favorites of my late friend, William LeSassier, and I

<!-- chunk -->

### thought I would transmit his teachings on the subject, as a small

<!-- chunk -->

### monument. He used it himself when he had a hiatal hernia due to some

<!-- chunk -->

### accident in a martial arts class.

<!-- chunk -->

### Catnip is the mint nervine to give “when in doubt,” i.e., when there is

<!-- chunk -->

### a nervous problem that can’t be differentiated enough to indicate a more

<!-- chunk -->

### specific medicine. The action of catnip centers on the stomach, with

<!-- chunk -->

### anxiety, energy, and pain rising upwards, rather than downwards, as is

<!-- chunk -->

### the case with chamomile. Unlike the chamomile person or child, catnip

<!-- chunk -->

### tends to internalize the stress and goes to the stomach. Think of it as

<!-- chunk -->

### “visceral over-stimulation from the mind.” The stomach is bothered,

<!-- chunk -->

### with a pain in the organ like a heart attack. There is congestion in

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach, and pain from the stomach up to the vagus nerve. Painful

<!-- chunk -->

### nerve involvement. Rising anxiety. Hiatal hernia. Sensation of extreme

<!-- chunk -->

### pressure up against the diaphragm. Colicky gas pains. Anxiety, attacks in

<!-- chunk -->

### the stomach, rising upwards into the chest instead of downwards. Not

<!-- chunk -->

### for sinking, diarrheal anxiety (see chamomile). “Burpitis.” Antidote to

<!-- chunk -->

### bad reactions to onions and garlic. It is suited to mild, not serious, cases

<!-- chunk -->

### of motion sickness, and is good for people who are nervous to be on an

<!-- chunk -->

### airplane.

<!-- chunk -->

### Catnip has a long reputation in the treatment of children’s diseases. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is an old specific for colic in babies at the breast. According to LeSassier,

<!-- chunk -->

### it sweetens the milk. It is suited to mild infantile fever, which is often

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with constipation or colic, whether recognized or not, in the

<!-- chunk -->

### colon. Catnip is a general first aide remedy for children, for the “crisis of

<!-- chunk -->

### childhood.” The old doctors note that the legs of the child are flexed

<!-- chunk -->

### forward, an indication of tension in the colon.

<!-- chunk -->

### William LeSassier’s directions for making the best catnip are precise.

<!-- chunk -->

### Make a nice, strong, green tincture from the fresh plant in flower in a

<!-- chunk -->

### high proof alcohol. Dilute a few drops in water, enough to tint it light

<!-- chunk -->

### green and give a teaspoonful or modest amount. It will take two to three

<!-- chunk -->

### doses to work, so persist. There will be a big belch and the symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### will be relieved. (This is a characteristic symptom of wind or constriction

<!-- chunk -->

### in the stomach.) The herb is gathered before flowering and prepared

<!-- chunk -->

### fresh as a tea, or tincture. It loses properties on drying. “Do not use for

<!-- chunk -->

### extended periods of time with small children. It has an almost narcotic

<!-- chunk -->

### effect when overused” (Daryl Patton, Tommie Bass).

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: acrid, pungent, moist, resinous.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: constriction, depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: skin (diaphoretic), GI, uterus Specific Indications:

<!-- chunk -->

### • Swollen under the eyes.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Infants; nervous, fretful; intestinal colic, constipation, and fever.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Children; over-active, nervous, restless, mean-spirited.

<!-- chunk -->

### • “Tends to internalize stress in the stomach.”

<!-- chunk -->

### • Congestion, pain, and anxiety in the stomach; pain up to the vagus

<!-- chunk -->

### nerve.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pain in the stomach, mimicking heart attack.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Anxiety attacks felt in the stomach, rising upwards (not downwards to

<!-- chunk -->

### produce diarrhea).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hiatal hernia; sensation of extreme pressure upwards against the

<!-- chunk -->

### diaphragm; hiccup.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Intestinal colic; draws the legs up for relief.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Colicky gas pains, relieved by burping, belching.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Spasm, pain ascending from the stomach or uterus.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Motion sickness (mild palliative); nervous to be on airplane.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Skin irritation; hives, measles, fever, acne.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sweetens the milk.

<!-- chunk -->

### • General first aid for infants and children.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lessens fatigue from muscular exertion.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Jack Ritchason (1), William LeSassier (4–14),

<!-- chunk -->

### Elisabeth Janos (3), John William Fyfe (11, 13), Tommie Bass (13).

<!-- chunk -->

## Valeriana officinalis (Valerian)

<!-- chunk -->

### Valerian is acrid and pungent (pungency increases the smell of a plant).

<!-- chunk -->

### Therefore it is relaxant and warming. This is not a plant I use much, so I

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t have a lot of stories to tell.

<!-- chunk -->

### Valerian is principally used as a remedy for insomnia or to relax

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles and ease pain. Ellingwood (1919, 125) writes, “its action upon

<!-- chunk -->

### the nervous system is best obtained when the circulation of those centers

<!-- chunk -->

### is inactive and feeble, especially when there is a paleness of the face and

<!-- chunk -->

### the skin is cool. It is directly indicated in hysterical conditions of

<!-- chunk -->

### whatever character with feebleness; with nervous excitement, and

<!-- chunk -->

### morbid vigilance; in hysterical epilepsy [or seizures, really]; and nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### disturbances incident to menopause. Its general soothing effect in all

<!-- chunk -->

### these cases is desirable. It controls distress and imaginary pain and

<!-- chunk -->

### produces quiet, permitting sleep and rest.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Fyfe (1909, 753) writes, “Valerian is an excellent cerebral stimulant,

<!-- chunk -->

### and is employed with advantage in diseases resulting from enfeebled

<!-- chunk -->

### cerebral circulation. Its administration often affords great relief in the

<!-- chunk -->

### high tension common to many nervous states, and in wakefulness it

<!-- chunk -->

### exerts a sleep-producing power of much value.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Valerianic acid is a powerful relaxant to the nerves and muscles. In

<!-- chunk -->

### large doses or susceptible persons (and cats) it has the opposite property,

<!-- chunk -->

### causing nervousness, restlessness, wakefulness, and twitching followed

<!-- chunk -->

### by drowsiness, sleepiness, lessened sensibility, and relaxation. It has

<!-- chunk -->

### been classified as a stimulant and a depressant, but it is ultimately a

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxant, reducing neuromuscular tension.

<!-- chunk -->

### The fresh root provides the best tincture, preserving the volatile oils. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is more “tonic and less reactive” than preparations from the dried root

<!-- chunk -->

### (Matthew Becker). Small doses are generally relaxing, while large doses

<!-- chunk -->

### are stimulating. However, reaction is idiosyncratic. In some people,

<!-- chunk -->

### valerian produces the symptoms it is supposed to cure: nervousness,

<!-- chunk -->

### irritation, wakefulness, and restlessness. Dosage of the tincture,

<!-- chunk -->

### according to John William Fyfe, is one to sixty drops.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: acrid.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: constriction.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: nerves, smooth muscles, large intestine, lower spine.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Pale complexion, cool extremities.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous, irritated, tense, despondent.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous breakdown, nervous fatigue, atrophy from constriction.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Insomnia, wakefulness, nervousness, restlessness, and tension,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially when associated with spinal pain, intestinal spasm, and

<!-- chunk -->

### flatulence.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lack of concentration, attentiveness, in tense people (one to three

<!-- chunk -->

### drops).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Temporal and frontal headache.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bronchial spasm; colds, coughs.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Flatulence, colic; spasms and convulsions associated with nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### tension.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stomach ulcers.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous heart palpitations; high blood pressure; slows and

<!-- chunk -->

### strengthens the heart beat; increases peripheral circulation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Spasmodic cough, asthma, and dyspnea.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bladder spasm.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menstrual spasm, amenorrhea.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Labor; after birth pains.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lower back spasm.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Convulsions, epilepsy, lack of coordination.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fever with spasm, measles, scarlet fever.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Arthritis, gout, lack of coordination, paralysis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Counters the effects of alcohol.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Finley Ellingwood, John William Fyfe, Matthew

<!-- chunk -->

### Becker (5).

<!-- chunk -->

## Verbena hastata (Blue Vervain)

<!-- chunk -->

### Vervain (Verbena officinalis) has a long history of use in European folk

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine and was even a plant of magical repute among the Druids. It

<!-- chunk -->

### has properties similar to the American blue vervain, and the following

<!-- chunk -->

### symptomology obtains for either plant. The vervains are acrid bitters,

<!-- chunk -->

### which in large doses send a chill through the body. Verbena hastata is

<!-- chunk -->

### used to open the skin to bring on sweating when the skin was

<!-- chunk -->

### particularly closed shut, according to LeSassier. Vervain causes intense

<!-- chunk -->

### reactions: intensity of sweat, lack of sweat, intensity of hormonal drives

<!-- chunk -->

### and emotions and ideals.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Bach introduced European vervain as a remedy for extremists who

<!-- chunk -->

### suffer from strong ideals or opinions they wish to thrust upon others,

<!-- chunk -->

### and people who feel driven. (It is perhaps not surprising that it was used

<!-- chunk -->

### in antiquity for protection against psychic attack, a form of over-intense

<!-- chunk -->

### projection upon others). He gives a case history where it solved tension

<!-- chunk -->

### in the neck in a farmer who was highly ambitious. This same portrait

<!-- chunk -->

### applies to the American species.

<!-- chunk -->

### Verbena hastata is indicated in cases where people are intensely driven

<!-- chunk -->

### but do not have the energy to sustain their drive. They often are stiff-

<!-- chunk -->

### necked, metaphorically and literally. They suffer from extreme tension in

<!-- chunk -->

### the neck, yet the neck sometimes weakens and the head drops slightly.

<!-- chunk -->

### These people are often thin and lack the physical sustenance necessary

<!-- chunk -->

### to carry them through the intensity of their work habits and drive. They

<!-- chunk -->

### are constantly exhausted, yet constantly striving. They often try to lay

<!-- chunk -->

### their ideas on other people, as Dr. Bach comments, but just as often they

<!-- chunk -->

### are hard on themselves and don’t accept the faults in themselves or

<!-- chunk -->

### others. The mind is often crowded out with a host of thoughts, which

<!-- chunk -->

### drive them (Karyn Sanders). Dorothy Hall notes that vervain people like

<!-- chunk -->

### to make lists.

<!-- chunk -->

### I remember one thin, tall, idealistic young high school girl suffering

<!-- chunk -->

### from tension and heart palpitations. I asked her if she make lists and she

<!-- chunk -->

### replied that she made lists of other people’s faults! “Yikes, blue vervain

<!-- chunk -->

### run amok,” I thought to myself. After receiving blue vervain, the heart

<!-- chunk -->

### palpitations went away and she evidently relaxed her ideals because

<!-- chunk -->

### within three months, she started her first relationship.

<!-- chunk -->

### The drive found in the vervain or blue vervain person can often be

<!-- chunk -->

### hormonal. It is often a specific for hormonal food craving during

<!-- chunk -->

### menstruation. This was discovered by LeSassier, who also notes that it is

<!-- chunk -->

### a specific for many of the problems occurring in the last half of the

<!-- chunk -->

### cycle, the progesterone half. This is a remedy for what Michael Moore

<!-- chunk -->

### calls “progesterone stress.” Women cannot handle extra progesterone.

<!-- chunk -->

### LeSassier successfully helped one woman who said she felt “like a school

<!-- chunk -->

### of piranhas on a feeding frenzy.” It is suited to PMS women who say

<!-- chunk -->

### they “want to kill somebody.” Blue vervain likes intensity. In my

<!-- chunk -->

### experience, it is the single most important remedy for menopausal hot

<!-- chunk -->

### flashes; blue vervain is suited to intense women with severe sweats. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### a remedy for impotence in men; it also relaxes high ideals and neurotic

<!-- chunk -->

### self-concepts about sex.

<!-- chunk -->

### It terms of Chinese herbalism, blue vervain is a remedy for “yin not

<!-- chunk -->

### holding down the yang,” or a lack of substance and nutrition to support

<!-- chunk -->

### an intense level of activity. These people overdo themselves. It was

<!-- chunk -->

### likewise used for fevers where the profuse sweating was followed by

<!-- chunk -->

### seizures from lack of sustenance. Vervain is an intense, nauseant bitter,

<!-- chunk -->

### easily provoking a shiver from the depths of the body. Thus it reduces

<!-- chunk -->

### tension, shivering, and convulsions. However, it also provokes secretion.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is suited to fevers that simply will not break, where an extremely

<!-- chunk -->

### intense diaphoretic is needed. It is also suited to intense fevers with

<!-- chunk -->

### profuse perspiration followed by seizure, chills, and shivering. It has a

<!-- chunk -->

### reputation for provoking perspiration where other plants cannot open

<!-- chunk -->

### the skin, but it is so bitter it is hard to get down.

<!-- chunk -->

### The roots, leaves, and mature seeds have been used; alternatively, the

<!-- chunk -->

### flower tops are gathered are the height of flowering and extracted fresh

<!-- chunk -->

### in alcohol or dried for use as an infusion. My dose is one to three drops,

<!-- chunk -->

### one to three times per day. More can be used, but this is an extremely

<!-- chunk -->

### bitter plant.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: bitter, acrid.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: constriction, atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: febrifuge; keeps the fluids from drying out; nervine

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxant, antispasmodic, anticonvulsant; hormonal regulator.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Thin, tall, strong-willed, but lacking stamina to sustain effort;

<!-- chunk -->

### overexertion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • High-minded, idealistic, driven, intolerant of shortcomings in self and

<!-- chunk -->

### others; sexual neurosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mental over-activity, excessive thinking, excessive talking, loud voice.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Headache; earache.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Eyes; weak, sore, inflamed.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mouth ulcers; soft, spongy gums (use as mouth wash); adenoids.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stiffness of the nape of the neck.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Colds, catarrh, irritable, spasmodic cough, whooping cough, croup,

<!-- chunk -->

### asthma, pneumonia.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Wheezing, irritation, congestion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dyspepsia; harassing pain, sluggish digestion; constipation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Malaria ague, hepatitis; liver congested, swollen, cirrhotic; bilious,

<!-- chunk -->

### indigestion, jaundice, gallstone colic.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Atrial fibrillation; poor peripheral circulation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Kidney stone colic.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Incontinence; bladder spasm.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menstruation; painful and irregular.

<!-- chunk -->

### • PMS; anger before the period; tension and cramping.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Food craving before the period; driven to eat.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menopause; hot flashes, hormonal drives, nymphomania.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Labor.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mastitis; lack of lactation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Impotence.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Epileptic seizures and spasms beginning in the neck.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sprains and strains, especially hyperextension.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fevers with profuse sweating, loss of fluids, stiffness in the neck,

<!-- chunk -->

### headache.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stubborn fevers with complete lack of sweating; difficult to get a

<!-- chunk -->

### sweat going.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Convulsions following fevers which have depleted the fluids.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Eczema, dry skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cystic fibrosis.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, O. Phelps Brown (7, 8, 10, 11, 21, 22), Edward

<!-- chunk -->

### Bach (2), Matthew Wood (1, 2, 3, 7, 12, 14, 18, 23), Jack Ritchason (5,

<!-- chunk -->

### 6, 15, 28), F. Wheeler (13), William LeSassier (7, 16, 17, 25).


<!-- chunk -->

### he terms “tonic” and “dry” are not really adequate for the job

<!-- chunk -->

### here, but we need some simple vocabulary to describe tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### states and their treatment. Atrophy is much better, since it

<!-- chunk -->

### includes dryness (from lack of water or oil), weakness, and wasting or

<!-- chunk -->

### loss of flesh. These are the major symptoms associated with the atrophic

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state. The tongue is usually dry, and in severe cases withered; skin

<!-- chunk -->

### dry; constipation common.

<!-- chunk -->

### The agents needed to treat this condition are diverse. The term “tonic”

<!-- chunk -->

### or even “nutritive tonic” is not very accurate, since there are so many

<!-- chunk -->

### different ways to approach atrophy. Included here are remedies that are

<!-- chunk -->

### moist, mucilaginous, oily, salty, bitter (in some instances), sweet, and

<!-- chunk -->

### “meaty.” The tastes of all the major food groups (sweet, oily, and meaty

<!-- chunk -->

### or proteinaceous) have to be part of the picture. Anything that

<!-- chunk -->

### contributes moisture helps with the feeding of tissues.

<!-- chunk -->

### Tissues especially affected by dryness or atrophy include external and

<!-- chunk -->

### internal coverings, i.e., the skin, mucosa, and synovial membranes. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### thus an important influence in muscular and skeletal disease, where the

<!-- chunk -->

### synovial membranes of the joints dry out, in respiratory conditions, and

<!-- chunk -->

### most importantly in gastrointestinal conditions where there is a lack of

<!-- chunk -->

### digestive fluids, poor digestion (hence atrophy), and constipation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mucilage is a thick, moist carbohydrate substance that coats and

<!-- chunk -->

### moistens the tissues. It also tends to penetrate the tissues and cells to

<!-- chunk -->

### bring out toxins. The most important mucilages in Western herbalism

<!-- chunk -->

### include Chondrus (Irish moss), Symphytum (comfrey leaf and root), Althea

<!-- chunk -->

### (marshmallow), Plantago (psyllium seed, plantain), and Ulmus fulva

<!-- chunk -->

### (slippery elm). Some of these are also sweet, bitter, or salty, increasing

<!-- chunk -->

### the action on dry, hard, or atrophic tissues.

<!-- chunk -->

### The most commonly used mucilages in Western herbalism are

<!-- chunk -->

### marshmallow, comfrey, and slippery elm. Unfortunately, I tend to use

<!-- chunk -->

### “off brand” mucilages like true and false Solomon’s seal, so I have not

<!-- chunk -->

### chosen to write about comfrey and slippery elm. These really should be

<!-- chunk -->

### studied for inclusion under this tissue state.

<!-- chunk -->

### Water without salt has no intelligence or activity. Salt attracts water

<!-- chunk -->

### and causes it to become active in the tissues. Salty plants such as Althea

<!-- chunk -->

### (marshmallow), Chondrus (Irish moss), and Eryngium (rattlesnake master,

<!-- chunk -->

### sea holly, eryngo) are traditionally used to break up hard swellings; they

<!-- chunk -->

### allow water to penetrate into the tissues. Herbalist David Winston has

<!-- chunk -->

### noted that there is also a “salty/minerally” taste (like the “crispness” of

<!-- chunk -->

### spinach or nettle). This can be applied to Verbascum (mullein),

<!-- chunk -->

### Taraxacum (dandelion leaf), Arctium (burdock root), Urtica (nettles), and

<!-- chunk -->

### Medicago (alfalfa). Herbs high in silicon are also nutritive: Avena sativa

<!-- chunk -->

### (milky oat seed), Equisetum (horsetail), and Agropyron or Eletrigia (couch

<!-- chunk -->

### grass).

<!-- chunk -->

### The third group of medicinal herbs suited to atrophy contain fixed

<!-- chunk -->

### oils. This group is especially prominent in the modern health food store.

<!-- chunk -->

### The most obvious members of the group are Linum (flaxseed oil),

<!-- chunk -->

### Oenotheris (evening primrose oil), Borago (borage), and Sesamum (sesame

<!-- chunk -->

### seed oil). Less widely used oily plants are Juglans cinerea (butternut

<!-- chunk -->

### bark), Angelica, Arctium (burdock), Salvia (garden sage), and Cannabis

<!-- chunk -->

### (marijuana seed). These herbs increase digestion and the metabolism of

<!-- chunk -->

### fats and oils. They provoke the gall bladder, which releases the bile to

<!-- chunk -->

### digest fats and oils, and lubricate the colon. They may provide lipids,

<!-- chunk -->

### which exercise liver metabolism, cellular membrane nutrition, or

<!-- chunk -->

### lipophilic hormone travel. Oils are readily absorbed through the skin and

<!-- chunk -->

### a traditional form of treatment is “fatty inunction” (application of oils to

<!-- chunk -->

### the surface). Fixed oils have a “nutty” taste.

<!-- chunk -->

### The fourth nutritive category is the “sweet tonic.” This class was not

<!-- chunk -->

### developed in Western herbalism; we owe this useful concept to Chinese

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalism. Sweet herbs contain sugars and starches that increase

<!-- chunk -->

### carbohydrate digestion and metabolism. The cells and tissues are thus

<!-- chunk -->

### nourished and build up. Nourished tissues are able to retain more water,

<!-- chunk -->

### so sweet herbs reduce dryness (and sometimes even heat or wind). Sweet

<!-- chunk -->

### native or imported herbs used in Western herbalism include Myrrhis

<!-- chunk -->

### odorata (European sweet cicely), Osmorrhiza longistylus (American sweet

<!-- chunk -->

### cicely), Panax quinquifolius (American ginseng), Arctium (burdock root),

<!-- chunk -->

### Taraxacum (dandelion root), Avena sativa (milky oat seed), Agropyron

<!-- chunk -->

### (couch grass), Galium (cleavers), Trifolium (red clover), Glycyrrhiza

<!-- chunk -->

### (licorice), Rehmannia, Codonopsis, and Astragalus.

<!-- chunk -->

### Moist, mucilaginous, salty, oily, and sweet herbs can all reduce heat

<!-- chunk -->

### by increasing the fluids. Some of them, like marshmallow, are seldom

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated unless there is heat conjoined with dryness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another “flavor” which may be included here is “meaty.” This flavor is

<!-- chunk -->

### recognized in Japanese cooking. Foods high in protein taste “meaty.” In

<!-- chunk -->

### the vegetable world, this includes Urtica (nettle) and the mushrooms.

<!-- chunk -->

### The latter are more closely akin to animals than plants, which

<!-- chunk -->

### incidentally, they feed off. Most mushrooms are nutritive and tonic in

<!-- chunk -->

### effect, for example, Ganoderma (reishi).

<!-- chunk -->

### Atrophy bears an interesting relationship to the opposite category,

<!-- chunk -->

### torpor or stagnation. Since water is the means of transportation in the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, both a lack of water (atrophy) and a stagnation (torpor) can stop

<!-- chunk -->

### the transportation of foodstuffs, waste products, and water. Therefore,

<!-- chunk -->

### both tissue states are associated with weak, undernourished tissue,

<!-- chunk -->

### accumulation of toxic material, and pockets of water. Both respond to

<!-- chunk -->

### bitters and alteratives. In atrophy, these symptoms will appear against

<!-- chunk -->

### an emaciated, withered background, while in torpor they appear in

<!-- chunk -->

### association with a swollen, boggy, expressionless tissue state. Both

<!-- chunk -->

### conditions trap toxins in the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Both the atrophic and the torpid tissue states respond to bitter herbs

<!-- chunk -->

### and alteratives. Since digestion and metabolism is dependent on

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion, bitters can improve digestion and tissue nutrition to relieve

<!-- chunk -->

### atrophy. Some of the bitters which act especially on atrophy are Alnus

<!-- chunk -->

### (alder), Arctium (burdock), Lycopodium (wolf’s paw club moss), Panax

<!-- chunk -->

### quinquifolius (American ginseng), and Berberis aquifolium (Oregon grape

<!-- chunk -->

### root). Some of these are also cited as alteratives that remove torpor.

<!-- chunk -->

### Thurston defined remedies for the atrophic condition as

<!-- chunk -->

### “trophorestoratives.” He applied this term to many plants with a strong

<!-- chunk -->

### organ affinity because they rebuilt the “trophism” of the organ. I do not

<!-- chunk -->

### believe this is the correct way to define remedies for atrophy. I consider

<!-- chunk -->

### the appropriate agents to be those that build fluids (water and oil),

<!-- chunk -->

### increase nutrition, hold water, and soften hardness.

<!-- chunk -->

## Althea officinalis (Marshmallow)

<!-- chunk -->

### Marshmallow is one of the central mucilages in the Western

<!-- chunk -->

### pharmacopoeia. It is also somewhat salty, so it not only restores

<!-- chunk -->

### lubrication, it also leads water into hardened, dried out tissue. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated when dryness is combined with heat. The tongue is usually

<!-- chunk -->

### dried and red, with hardening and sometimes cracking of the surface. A

<!-- chunk -->

### slightly reddish complexion seems to be typical. Sometimes there is

<!-- chunk -->

### noticeable drying and exfoliation of the cuticle.

<!-- chunk -->

### Marshmallow has been used since the most ancient times. The name

<!-- chunk -->

### althea derives from the Greek for “heal all.” Hippocrates used a

<!-- chunk -->

### decoction of the roots externally (with honey and rosin) to stop bleeding

<!-- chunk -->

### and internally to moisten and nourish when there was severe blood loss.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is native to salt marshes in Europe, but naturalized in low ground in

<!-- chunk -->

### coastal and inland North America. Of all mucilages, it is considered the

<!-- chunk -->

### most softening (Palaiseul, 1974, 178), and diuretic (Ellingwood, 1919,

<!-- chunk -->

### 431). The roots, leaves, flowers, and seeds are used somewhat

<!-- chunk -->

### interchangeably, both internally and externally. They soothe

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation of the skin and mucosa.

<!-- chunk -->

### Marshmallow root syrup is a major remedy for irritable coughs. It

<!-- chunk -->

### soothes the passageways, and reduces inflammation and dryness. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### also beneficial in continuous runny colds. (Perhaps, like red clover, it

<!-- chunk -->

### breaks up cysts in the sinuses that often cause this condition). It is “of

<!-- chunk -->

### value in inflammation of the fauces and tonsils, and in coughs and

<!-- chunk -->

### hoarseness its soothing influence has been found useful” (Fyfe, 1909,

<!-- chunk -->

### 362).

<!-- chunk -->

### Marshmallow is used for canker sores, dental abscesses, gingivitis,

<!-- chunk -->

### laryngitis, tracheitis, bronchitis, gastritis, enteritis, colitis, inflammation

<!-- chunk -->

### of the kidneys, and cystitis. In my experience, it is indispensable in

<!-- chunk -->

### colitis and Crohn’s disease, but it usually only palliates and patients need

<!-- chunk -->

### more specific treatment. It is used as a softening, moistening lubricant to

<!-- chunk -->

### open the constipated intestine (when due to dryness). Like slippery elm,

<!-- chunk -->

### it has been used to lubricate the passages to procure an easier birth. As a

<!-- chunk -->

### nutritive, it is used to increase milk production.

<!-- chunk -->

### Externally, marshmallow is used on dry, scruffy, skin, dandruff, dry

<!-- chunk -->

### scabs on the scalp, unhealthy scalp with hair loss, and insect bites. A

<!-- chunk -->

### woman came to me with a red, dry patch on the face that was not

<!-- chunk -->

### cancerous. She had a reddish complexion generally, lower back pain,

<!-- chunk -->

### and a broad, red, dry, cracked tongue with some carmine red, showing

<!-- chunk -->

### damaged fluids and heat. Two marshmallow capsules, three times a day,

<!-- chunk -->

### cured in a few weeks.

<!-- chunk -->

### Marshmallow breaks up swollen breast glands, ducts, and cysts. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### laid on the eyes, to take away redness and swelling. It is used externally

<!-- chunk -->

### to dissipate hard swellings, inflammations, boils, abscesses, and cysts. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is placed over a swollen, hard liver or spleen. A poultice draws out

<!-- chunk -->

### thorns and splinter. Marshmallow is used externally on bruises, bones

<!-- chunk -->

### out of joint, swollen joints, muscles, tendons, and arteries.

<!-- chunk -->

### The root in capsule form is a good medium for use in intestinal

<!-- chunk -->

### problems, since the swallowed capsule gets down to the stomach. It

<!-- chunk -->

### lubricates the stool and eases constipation. Although commonly used for

<!-- chunk -->

### constipation, it can also be used for diarrhea and dysentery where

<!-- chunk -->

### soothing and cooling is needed. Culpeper (1652, 180) treats us to a case

<!-- chunk -->

### history. “You may remember, that not long since there was a raging

<!-- chunk -->

### disease called the bloody-flux [dysentery]; the college of physicians not

<!-- chunk -->

### knowing what to make of it, called it the inside plague, for their wits

<!-- chunk -->

### were at Ne plus nultra about it. My son was taken with the same disease,

<!-- chunk -->

### and the excoriation of the bowels was exceeding great; myself being in

<!-- chunk -->

### the country, was sent for up; the only thing I gave him, was Mallows

<!-- chunk -->

### bruised and boiled both in milk and drink.” He was cured in two days.

<!-- chunk -->

### And here the grateful father writes, “And here I show my thankfulness to

<!-- chunk -->

### God in communicating it to his creatures, leave it to posterity.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Marshmallow root is useful to soothe the parts in the passage of both

<!-- chunk -->

### kidney and gall stones. It is especially indicated when the parts are dry,

<!-- chunk -->

### inflamed, and sensitive. Phyllis Light recommends marshmallow root, or

<!-- chunk -->

### its cousin okra root, to soothe the gall bladder, increase fluids, loosen up

<!-- chunk -->

### the stones hard and dried in the gall sac, soothe the ducts, and help with

<!-- chunk -->

### their passage.

<!-- chunk -->

### Marshmallow is extracted best in water rather than alcohol. Cold

<!-- chunk -->

### water picks up the mucilage but not as much of the starch, while hot

<!-- chunk -->

### water picks up the starch but not the mucilage. Capsules are convenient.

<!-- chunk -->

### The flowers, leaves, and roots are all used interchangeably. A number of

<!-- chunk -->

### cousins are used as a substitute. Extracts poorly in water and alcohol, so

<!-- chunk -->

### it is often used in capsules or tablets.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (root) salty, sweet, cool, mucilaginous.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: atrophy and irritation dependent on lack of moisture.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: skin and mucosa, upper gastrointestinal tract, kidneys.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Lips red, dry, sometimes with a slight white powder around them.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tongue red, dry, sometimes cracked; sometimes with dry coating.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Irritated, inflamed, hot, dry mucosa and skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Swollen, hard, indurated glands.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dry cough in the throat or upper bronchus; in children; worse at night

<!-- chunk -->

### (syrup).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Soothing and anti-inflammatory to the digestive tract; acid stomach,

<!-- chunk -->

### burning (capsules).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Over-acidity, ulcers, gastric, duodenal, mucus colitis (roots).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Low enzyme production (leaves).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea, dysentery, Crohn’s disease, colitis (capsules).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hemorrhoids (external).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Painful, scanty urination; soothes the passage of stones.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cystitis, strangury, hematuria.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sores, ulcers, and inflamed skin (external).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Skin slightly reddish, dry, exfoliating.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Nicholas Culpeper (9), Margi Flint (1), Dorothy

<!-- chunk -->

### Hall (7, 8).

## Asclepias tuberosa (Pleurisy Root, Butterfly Weed)

<!-- chunk -->

### Pleurisy root is a cousin of milkweed. It was used by the earliest colonial

<!-- chunk -->

### root doctors of whom we have record. It was popular in the nineteenth

<!-- chunk -->

### century as a diaphoretic and moistener. The physiomedicalists—Curtis,

<!-- chunk -->

### Cook, Thurston—considered it to be the archetypal remedy for the

<!-- chunk -->

### excitation tissue state. After a long period of study, I decided that it

<!-- chunk -->

### worked on fevers because it was lubricating, moistening, and opened the

<!-- chunk -->

### pores, not because it was a sedative that reduced heat directly.

<!-- chunk -->

### Therefore, I placed it under the tonics. It is not really that widely useful

<!-- chunk -->

### today, but it is an interesting remedy and I thought a short account

<!-- chunk -->

### would be appropriate.

<!-- chunk -->

### Pleurisy root is a sweet, nutritive herb that increases fluids in the

<!-- chunk -->

### internal membranes and passages, so that diaphoresis to the surface will

<!-- chunk -->

### be complete and healthy. Although it was one of the most important

<!-- chunk -->

### agents in the nineteenth-century materia medica of professional and lay

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine, pleurisy root is less often called for or used at the present

<!-- chunk -->

### time. The roots have little round structures that look astonishingly like

<!-- chunk -->

### alveoli. It is a specific for old pleuritic stitches, rather than recent cases

<!-- chunk -->

### of acute pleurisy (for that, use homeopathic Bryonia). It also lubricates

<!-- chunk -->

### the intestines.

<!-- chunk -->

### Asclepias is indicated when the skin and synovial membranes (bursa,

<!-- chunk -->

### pleura) are dry or the pores are closed, trapping fluids in the

<!-- chunk -->

### membranes. By increasing fluids, it reduces heat and constriction, or a

<!-- chunk -->

### sense of stuffiness in the chest. It can prevent inflammation of the pleura

<!-- chunk -->

### from going into pneumonia, which is a frequent event, and also cures the

<!-- chunk -->

### pleuritic stitches left behind after an old case of bronchitis or pneumonia

<!-- chunk -->

### has damaged the pleura.

<!-- chunk -->

### My friend Jennifer Tucker, an experienced herbalist in State College,

<!-- chunk -->

### Pennsylvania, put this information to work when she had a frozen

<!-- chunk -->

### shoulder. Little motion was left in the left shoulder. “It looks like an old,

<!-- chunk -->

### advanced scoliosis,” said the physical therapist. She was in the waiting

<!-- chunk -->

### room reading my book when she got to the part about Asclepias tuberosa

<!-- chunk -->

### and joints. She knew it was the right remedy. “I’m afraid we may have

<!-- chunk -->

### to do surgery,” said the physical therapist. “We’re not making any

<!-- chunk -->

### progress.” “Oh no,” replied Jennifer. “You wait. I know what plant to

<!-- chunk -->

### cure it with now.” She went home, dug up a plant in her field (which

<!-- chunk -->

### has some of the best pleurisy root I’ve ever seen), and started chewing it.

<!-- chunk -->

### Even on the first day, motion was increased twenty per cent. Now the

<!-- chunk -->

### physical therapy exercises worked. In sixty days, to the pleasant surprise

<!-- chunk -->

### of the physical therapist, she had normal range of motion.

<!-- chunk -->

### Now, one would never know she’d had a problem.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: slightly sweet, salty, and minerally/salty.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue States: atrophy, constriction, irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: skin and mucosal synovial membranes.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Dry skin, with stuffiness in the chest.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cough, dry in the throat and upper lungs, but wet in the lower

<!-- chunk -->

### reaches of the lungs.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Chronic pleurisy following old cases of bronchitis and pneumonia,

<!-- chunk -->

### rather than acute pleurisy (for which homeopathic Bryonia is usually

<!-- chunk -->

### sufficient); sharp, stitching pains in the chest; pain in the chest from

<!-- chunk -->

### coughing.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pneumonia with water in the bottom of the lungs.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Asthma with oppressed breathing and pulse.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Acute bursitis and arthritis with redness, swelling, tenderness, pain,

<!-- chunk -->

### and lack of perspiration.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Old cases of bursitis and arthritis with adhesions in the joints that

<!-- chunk -->

### crackle on movement (will sometimes lubricate the joint

<!-- chunk -->

### instantaneously). • Frozen shoulder; with atrophy, weakness,

<!-- chunk -->

### crackling, and limitation of movement.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional (1–5), John Scudder (6), Matthew Wood (6),

<!-- chunk -->

### Jennifer Tucker (7).

## Avena sativa (Milky Oat Seed)

<!-- chunk -->

### Oat seed picked in the milky stage is a beautiful, moistening, sweet

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy. It is a very important tonic remedy for the nervous system

<!-- chunk -->

### depleted by stress, prolonged illness, sexual excesses, drug use, or

<!-- chunk -->

### protracted illness. It is high in silicon, like all the grasses, and thus

<!-- chunk -->

### strengthens the connective tissue, skin, mucosa, and nerves. Oat grass is

<!-- chunk -->

### higher in silica, which makes it strengthening to the connective tissue.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oats are widely used as a tonic for nervous debility and nervousness

<!-- chunk -->

### by contemporary practitioners. This is not a remedy I use a great deal,

<!-- chunk -->

### and I cannot offer personal insights, but it should be included under any

<!-- chunk -->

### good survey of sweet tonics for dryness and atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

### The seed is collected in the milky stage, before maturity, and dried or

<!-- chunk -->

### tinctured fresh. Standard dose of the tincture with the old physicians is

<!-- chunk -->

### five to sixty drops, three times per day. Oats are strengthening to thin

<!-- chunk -->

### people (sympathetic dominance), but fattening to those who are thick

<!-- chunk -->

### (parasympathetic dominance).

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (milky seed) sweet, moist.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue States: atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: nerves (sympathetic excess), skin, nails, hair,

<!-- chunk -->

### connective tissue.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous prostration, debility, weakness, paralysis (neurasthenia).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous system exhausted, all played out, drained; from stress,

<!-- chunk -->

### excessive duties, drug use, prolonged illness, sexual excess.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Inability to keep the mind fixed on any one subject; lack of focus,

<!-- chunk -->

### concentration, and memory; vagary of thought and manner.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous prostration following mental strain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Insomnia associated with nervous exhaustion, despondency.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Occipital headache extending down the spine and into the lower

<!-- chunk -->

### extremities, associated with neurasthenia; with burning sensation on

<!-- chunk -->

### top of the head.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Paralysis of the throat in diphtheria.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous palpitations.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bedwetting, nocturnal emissions; with weakness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Emissions of phosphates and other white substances in the urine; with

<!-- chunk -->

### exhaustion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Impotence in men under middle age; from nerve exhaustion, sexual

<!-- chunk -->

### excesses, nocturnal emissions.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Swollen prostate with neurasthenia.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Amenorrhea with nervous debility; mental depression; pain in the

<!-- chunk -->

### pelvis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervous headache at menstruation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Extremities weak; numbness; lack of strength; nervous tremors;

<!-- chunk -->

### spasm; epilepsy; paralysis and wasting diseases of the elderly.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Weakness and convalescence from protracted disease, fever, typhoid,

<!-- chunk -->

### drug addiction.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Weak, broken hair, nails, skin (cf. Equisetum, homeopathic Silicea).

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, H. E. Russell (3), John William Fyfe (3), Yadubir

<!-- chunk -->

### Sinha (8).

<!-- chunk -->

## Verbascum thapsus (Mullein)

<!-- chunk -->

### Mullein is a member of the scrophularia family, long associated with

<!-- chunk -->

### scrofula (swollen glands). However, it is best known as a respiratory

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy. It is particularly used for irritable, dry coughs, but it is also for

<!-- chunk -->

### dry/atrophy of the lymphatic system with poor absorption through the

<!-- chunk -->

### small intestine and weakness in the lymphatics. Although generally

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated when the mucosa are dry, mullein will also remove deposits of

<!-- chunk -->

### water dependent on weak tissue states. It promotes absorption in cellular

<!-- chunk -->

### dropsy, chronic abscesses, pleuritic effusions, and similar accumulations

<!-- chunk -->

### of fluid (Cook). It lubricates around bones, and helps set bones and

<!-- chunk -->

### straighten vertebra. Sedative to inflamed and irritated nerves, sharp

<!-- chunk -->

### nerve pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mullein is a very important remedy for muscular and skeletal

<!-- chunk -->

### problems, though it is not apt to appear on the top of anyone’s list. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated in dry/atrophic conditions of the mucosa. It has a moistening,

<!-- chunk -->

### lubricating effect on the synovial membranes as well, so that it is

<!-- chunk -->

### hydrating to the spine and joints. It is often indicated in back injuries.

<!-- chunk -->

### People think back injuries are untreatable and incurable, but increase in

<!-- chunk -->

### the synovial fluids will make the spine more pliable and comfortable.

<!-- chunk -->

### The vertebra will slip back into place more readily, pain and

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation will decrease, and the condition will get better. Mullein

<!-- chunk -->

### also has a rather unbelievable ability to set bones in the right place

<!-- chunk -->

### when they have been broken or are out of place. I have seen this several

<!-- chunk -->

### times. It will set a broken rib or digit, getting it back into place quickly. I

<!-- chunk -->

### remember watching it visibly dampen around the coffin bone of a horse

<!-- chunk -->

### as I placed it on the manure covered hoof. The leaves are picked during

<!-- chunk -->

### the first season; the flowers at the end of the second.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (leaf) salty/minerally.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: respiratory tract, lymphatics, synovial fluids, bones.


<!-- chunk -->

### • General dryness and atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervousness, insomnia.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Earache with pain, wax in ear.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Voice unnaturally high or low, due to inflammation of the pharynx.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sore throat, tonsillitis, mumps.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Allergies, sub-acute; relapsing bronchitis; asthma; pleurisy;

<!-- chunk -->

### pneumonia; croup; emphysema.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cough deep, “basso profoundo,” from inflammation of the velvety

<!-- chunk -->

### linings of the respiratory tract.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cough—dry, harsh, racking, shakes the frame.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea, dysentery, enteritis, and colitis with weakening from loss of

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bleeding from the bowels.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hemorrhoids, painful bleeding.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nephritis, painful urination.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Scrofula (chronic swollen lymph glands associated with poor nutrition

<!-- chunk -->

### and dry tissues).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Painful injuries to fingers, toes, spine, and areas rich in nerves.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Joints swollen with weakness and pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Spinal injuries, pain, misalignment, lack of synovial lubrication.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Chronic abscesses, with pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Broken bones that are hard to set are quickly returned to their correct

<!-- chunk -->

### position under the influence of Verbascum.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, William LeSassier (4), William Boericke (4, 8),
