<!-- chunk -->

## Polygonatum spp. (True Solomon’s seal)

<!-- chunk -->

### True Solomon’s seal is an extraordinary medicine for the muscular and

<!-- chunk -->

### skeletal system, probably the most important remedy for this system I

<!-- chunk -->

### have ever encountered. It is suited to a wide range of problems affecting

<!-- chunk -->

### the joints, ligaments, tendons, and muscles. It tightens or loosens

<!-- chunk -->

### tendons and attachments (depending on which is needed), strengthens

<!-- chunk -->

### and repairs joint damage, soothes or even cures many kinds of arthritis

<!-- chunk -->

### in the joints, and works nicely with repetitive-injury problems such as

<!-- chunk -->

### tennis elbow, jogger’s knee, and carpal-tunnel syndrome. True Solomon’s

<!-- chunk -->

### seal is moist, sweet, and slightly acrid, so it is suited to dry/atrophic

<!-- chunk -->

### conditions affecting the tension-bearing structures.

<!-- chunk -->

### John Gerard originally introduced true Solomon’s seal into English

<!-- chunk -->

### language herbal medicine in 1597. The old Greek and Roman authors

<!-- chunk -->

### dismissed it as a poisonous plant. However, only the blue-black berries

<!-- chunk -->

### are toxic; the roots are sweet, edible, and medicinal.

<!-- chunk -->

### “Galen saith, that neither herbe nor root hereof is to be given

<!-- chunk -->

### inwardly,” Gerard comments (1635, 905). “But note what experience

<!-- chunk -->

### hath found out, and of late dayes, especially among the vulgar sort of

<!-- chunk -->

### people in Hampshire, which Galen, Dioscorides, or any other that have

<!-- chunk -->

### written of plants have not so much as dreamed of.” These clever folk-

<!-- chunk -->

### healers used Solomon’s seal extensively. “If any of what sex or age

<!-- chunk -->

### soever chance to have any bones broken, in what part of their bodies

<!-- chunk -->

### soever; their refuge is to stampe the roots hereof, and give it unto the

<!-- chunk -->

### patient in ale to drinke: which sodoreth and glues together the bones in

<!-- chunk -->

### very short space, and very strangely, yea although the bones be but

<!-- chunk -->

### slenderly and unhandsomely placed and wrapped up. Moreover, the said

<!-- chunk -->

### people do give it in like manner unto their cattell, if they chance to have

<!-- chunk -->

### any bones broken.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Gerard reports several other valuable uses. “The root stamped and

<!-- chunk -->

### applied in manner of a pultesse, and layd upon members that have been

<!-- chunk -->

### out of ioynt, and newly restored to their places, driveth away the paine,

<!-- chunk -->

### and knitteth the ioynt very firmely, and taketh away the inflammation, if

<!-- chunk -->

### there chance to be any.” It is also beneficial for bruises. “The root of

<!-- chunk -->

### Solomon’s seale stamped while it is fresh and greene, and applied taketh

<!-- chunk -->

### away in one night, or two at the most, any bruise, black or blew spots

<!-- chunk -->

### gotten by falls.” It is also good for internal bruises. The root stamped and

<!-- chunk -->

### the juice drunk, “helps any inward bruise, disperseth the congealed and

<!-- chunk -->

### clotted bloud in very short space.” This will be beneficial during healing

<!-- chunk -->

### from a broken bone, which is usually surrounded by congealed blood. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is beneficial for many of the complications associated with broken bones.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is used “against inflammation, tumors, or swellings that happen unto

<!-- chunk -->

### members whose bones are broken, or members out of ioynt, after

<!-- chunk -->

### restaruration.”

<!-- chunk -->

### “That which might be written of this herbe … would seeme unto some

<!-- chunk -->

### incredible,” he continues. “But common experience teacheth, that in the

<!-- chunk -->

### world is not to be found another herbe comparable to it.” My own

<!-- chunk -->

### experience validates these comments.

<!-- chunk -->

### Solomon’s seal is one of those plants which demonstrates the truth of

<!-- chunk -->

### that oft-ridiculed idea, the doctrine of signatures. The white roots look

<!-- chunk -->

### like bones, joints, knuckles, and vertebra. When dry, they look even

<!-- chunk -->

### more like a bone, because the outside covering and the inside begin to

<!-- chunk -->

### look different, like the periosteum covering the bone. Then there are

<!-- chunk -->

### little spurs. These suggest an affinity to bone spurs. Solomon’s seal

<!-- chunk -->

### usually cures spurs and bunions by correcting the tension on the muscles

<!-- chunk -->

### and tendons, which cause malformation of the bones. It decalcifies, as

<!-- chunk -->

### needed by the body, breaking down spurs and deposits, yet it also

<!-- chunk -->

### recalcifies, building up and repairing bones. Most white roots contain or

<!-- chunk -->

### act on calcium. One of my students, a polarity therapist, noticed the way

<!-- chunk -->

### the leaves fold around the stems. They resemble the way muscles fold

<!-- chunk -->

### around and attach to bones.

<!-- chunk -->

### Solomon’s seal is the superlative remedy for tightening or loosening

<!-- chunk -->

### tendons, ligaments, and muscles. It adjusts to the right tension. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### useful for ligamentous looseness, where the ligaments are stretched and

<!-- chunk -->

### don’t hold after an adjustment. I helped one fellow who had a ligament

<!-- chunk -->

### in his foot that was stretched an inch too long. The doctors wanted to

<!-- chunk -->

### cut out a piece and sew it back together, but Solomon’s seal tincture

<!-- chunk -->

### rubbed into the area corrected the problem in about a week. A

<!-- chunk -->

### veterinarian was stiff and arthritic because of old football injuries when

<!-- chunk -->

### he was a cornerback at Ohio State. He had suffered from broken bones, a

<!-- chunk -->

### torn shoulder joint, calcification, stiffness, and arthritic pain all over the

<!-- chunk -->

### place. Solomon’s seal cleared out the majority of the problems in a

<!-- chunk -->

### month. It is great for young athletes (and old athletes), not only because

<!-- chunk -->

### it corrects so many muscular and skeletal problems, but because it

<!-- chunk -->

### adjusts to the right tension, thus avoiding injuries and off periods. As

<!-- chunk -->

### Gerard points out, it is good for spinal problems because it helps get the

<!-- chunk -->

### bones in the right places. Solomon’s seal is the great medicine for

<!-- chunk -->

### arthritis in the joints, while black cohosh and teasel are for rheumatic

<!-- chunk -->

### pains in the muscles. Although it will not al ways cure, it will almost

<!-- chunk -->

### always palliate and remove pain, even in rheumatoid arthritis. It is a

<!-- chunk -->

### good remedy for hip dysplasia in dogs, as I have seen from experience.

<!-- chunk -->

### Solomon’s seal is nearly foolproof for repetitive use tendonitis (and if

<!-- chunk -->

### it fails, we still have other remedies such as homeopathic Rhus

<!-- chunk -->

### toxicodendron or comfrey poultices). One of my students worked in a

<!-- chunk -->

### jewelry-making factory. One Christmas she made Solomon’s seal salve

<!-- chunk -->

### and brought jars for everyone as presents for her coworkers because they

<!-- chunk -->

### suffered from repetitive-use injuries. It cured all twenty-plus people and

<!-- chunk -->

### they started using it for arthritis on themselves and their relatives. That’s

<!-- chunk -->

### how we learned about its properties as an arthritic medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### William Salmon (1710, 1045) gives some additional information.

<!-- chunk -->

### “Taken at a time morning and evening … is said to cure ruptures when

<!-- chunk -->

### the guts fall into the cods, a cataplasm of the root being applyed

<!-- chunk -->

### outwardly.” The macerated roots in wheat or rye meal are applied

<!-- chunk -->

### outwardly to seal green wounds and “strengthen a weak back.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The rhizomes are the useful part. They are so sweet they turn a sweet

<!-- chunk -->

### alcohol sticky and should be made up in grain alcohol or oil. The berries

<!-- chunk -->

### are toxic.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: sweet, bitter, cool, moist.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: tendons, mucosa.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Characteristic symptoms: stretched or loose tendons and ligaments.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Muscular and skeletal tensions, bones spurs resulting from such

<!-- chunk -->

### tensions.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Repetitive use injury; carpal tunnel syndrome; arthritis associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with old injuries, calcifications, muscular, and skeletal tensions.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Irritation of the mucosa of the intestines, lungs, vagina.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Profuse menstrual flow, vaginal irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nutritive food for weak and prostrated persons; tuberculosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Premature ejaculation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Restores hormonal glow to the face, tones the ovaries, and strengthens

<!-- chunk -->

### the estrogen side of the cycle.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hemorrhoids.

<!-- chunk -->

### • External on poison ivy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Gerard (1, 2), William Cook (4), Matthew Wood

<!-- chunk -->

### (1, 2, 3, 4), Christopher Reid (7), Julia Graves (8), William Cullen (9).

<!-- chunk -->

## Galium aparine (Cleavers, Lady’s Bedstraw)

<!-- chunk -->

### Cleavers is native to the Old World, but widely naturalized in the New. It

<!-- chunk -->

### has long been used in folk medicine. I wrote about it in The Book of

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbal Wisdom, so my remarks will be brief, but I will add some

<!-- chunk -->

### additional information here as I have learned new indications with the

<!-- chunk -->

### passage of time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Picked before going to seed, it is moist, sweet, and salty. The sweet

<!-- chunk -->

### flavor is from coumarins, so it thins the blood and aerates the tissues.

<!-- chunk -->

### When there is atrophy with fibrous deposits in the tissues, the moisture

<!-- chunk -->

### and saltiness indicate this remedy. It has strong affinities to the

<!-- chunk -->

### lymphatic system, kidneys, and nervous system. It is suited to lymphatic

<!-- chunk -->

### congestion and stagnation, swollen glands, under and about the ears,

<!-- chunk -->

### and after infections. It dissolves calcareous deposits in the tissues,

<!-- chunk -->

### sweeps fluids and solids down to the kidneys, and helps remove them. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is beneficial in “gatherings of the nerves” and inflammation of the nerve

<!-- chunk -->

### endings (neurofibrositis). It is one of the few lymphatic remedies with an

<!-- chunk -->

### affinity for the vata constitution, but it helps people large and small. It

<!-- chunk -->

### should be considered in neurological diseases, over-sensitivity of the

<!-- chunk -->

### nerve endings, tickling, and itchy skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### Based on the doctrine of signatures (swollen bulbs at the end of the

<!-- chunk -->

### long stalk), I once gave this plant to a young girl suffering from

<!-- chunk -->

### neurofibromatosis (Elephant man’s disease). The bulbs of the nerves

<!-- chunk -->

### were inflamed, producing an outbreak of round, reddened spots on the

<!-- chunk -->

### forearms and elsewhere. The spots lessened almost immediately as we

<!-- chunk -->

### put cleavers on the skin. Unfortunately, I never heard back from this

<!-- chunk -->

### case, as I was on traveling at the time. However, I was reminded of it

<!-- chunk -->

### when I was consulted to treat a case of Morton’s neuroma, a painful

<!-- chunk -->

### ganglion-like nerve swelling on the underside of the foot near the second

<!-- chunk -->

### toe. It was not the neuroma that joggled my memory, but the “eczema”

<!-- chunk -->

### on the forearm. It looked like scattered red bumps resembling the

<!-- chunk -->

### neurofibroma on the arm of the young girl. “I control that with

<!-- chunk -->

### cortisone, so my patients don’t see it,” said the woman, a very good

<!-- chunk -->

### acupuncturist. She was not looking for a cure for that, but when we tried

<!-- chunk -->

### some cleavers, the neuroma on her foot started feeling better. “It made a

<!-- chunk -->

### huge difference, even the next day,” said my patient. We had tried Ruta

<!-- chunk -->

### and Smilacina with slight pain relief for several months. The eczema also

<!-- chunk -->

### improved.

<!-- chunk -->

### My acupuncturist friend was very precise about the areas where the

<!-- chunk -->

### eczema broke out, and her observations have a bearing on the utility of

<!-- chunk -->

### cleavers. “It broke out wherever there is a lot of muscular and ligament

<!-- chunk -->

### stress. On the forearm, the shoulder and the scapula, around GB 20. Also

<!-- chunk -->

### on the hips, down the gall bladder channel on the thigh. The neuroma

<!-- chunk -->

### was near the junction of the gall bladder and stomach channels.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Later I found that cleavers works on the analogous tendons in the

<!-- chunk -->

### hand, under the middle finger on the palm. There can be a thinning of

<!-- chunk -->

### flesh, a gathering together of tendons, and lack of mobility. This is

<!-- chunk -->

### usually diagnosed as Duputreyan’s contractures. Another practitioner

<!-- chunk -->

### called this “flexor tendon nodules.” The most holistic explanation came

<!-- chunk -->

### from a polarity therapist, who noted that the gathered tendons were

<!-- chunk -->

### under the middle or fire finger or toe, indicating a “fire problem.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Cleavers should be collected in the spring and early summer before

<!-- chunk -->

### the seeds appear, and when it still tastes like vanilla. It must be prepared

<!-- chunk -->

### or used fresh. My dose is small—one to three drops, one to three times

<!-- chunk -->

### per day—but it certainly can be used in moderate and large doses. In

<!-- chunk -->

### long-term build up of fibrous tissue, this remedy may need to be

<!-- chunk -->

### persisted in for a year or longer, though immediate results will be

<!-- chunk -->

### evident.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: sweet, salty/minerally, cool, moist.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: atrophy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: nerves, lymphatic system, kidneys.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Nervousness, sympathetic excess.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fussy, irritated, unsatisfied, “not myself.”

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dry eyes.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sensitive nervous system; skin tickles and itches easily.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stagnation of water, in the lymphatic system, kidneys, bladder (any or

<!-- chunk -->

### all); poor elimination of effete material; calcifications and fibrous

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fibrocystic breasts with many cysts, fibrous tissue.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lymphatic swellings under and behind the ears; after acute infections;

<!-- chunk -->

### in newborn babies.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Edema, swelling of the hands and feet.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bladder infections with fever, heat, and straining; nonspecific cystitis,

<!-- chunk -->

### urethritis extending to kidneys, testicles.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pregnancy.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Male sexual system; prostate inflamed, enlarged, difficulty passing

<!-- chunk -->

### water; hydrocele, orchitis; after venereal infections.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Irritability of the vas deferens and seminal vesicles; soreness of the

<!-- chunk -->

### testicles on rising.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Neurological disease; Morton’s neuroma (gathering of nerves under

<!-- chunk -->

### middle toes, swollen and hot, cramps up legs, walks with a limp);

<!-- chunk -->

### neurofibrositis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Skin eruptions; febrile exanthema, scarlet fever; eczema resembling

<!-- chunk -->

### neurofibrositis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Epilepsy.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bleeding.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cancer. “It is of great service in cancer. Give tincture thirty to sixty

<!-- chunk -->

### drops mixed with milk, four times a day, and see the miracle”

<!-- chunk -->

### (Sinha).

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Maude Grieve (1), Dorothy Hall (2, 11), Edwin

<!-- chunk -->

### Hale (homeopathic uses), Gideon Lincecum (Indian uses), Maria Treben,

<!-- chunk -->

### Matthew Wood (1–5, 13), David Winston (12), Yadubir Sinha (16).


<!-- chunk -->

### he term “relaxation” was applied in old-time medicine as far back

<!-- chunk -->

### as Hippocrates to any condition where the tissues could not hold

<!-- chunk -->

### on to fluids and thus flowed away. This would include excessive

<!-- chunk -->

### sweating, salivation, digestive secretion, diarrhea, menstrual bleeding,

<!-- chunk -->

### and urination. Another term we sometimes find in the old literature is

<!-- chunk -->

### “free secretion.” In addition, the tissues are usually flaccid, atonic, or

<!-- chunk -->

### prolapsed. One variation is “passive venous congestion,” where the

<!-- chunk -->

### vasculature is relaxed, the blood collects in the veins, and there are

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of high blood pressure, hemorrhoids, varicose veins, dilatation

<!-- chunk -->

### of the right side of the heart, congestion of the liver and lungs. Chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes mellitus often creates a relaxed condition with loss of fluids via

<!-- chunk -->

### the kidneys and the skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### In traditional Western herbalism, astringents have always treated this

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state. Dr. Edward Shook (1978, 87, 115) explains, “nearly all

<!-- chunk -->

### astringents coagulate albumin, tone up relaxed or debilitated condition

<!-- chunk -->

### of muscular fiber, contract both arterioles and capillaries, restrain

<!-- chunk -->

### peristalsis, contract gland ducts, and repress excessive secretions.” Even

<!-- chunk -->

### diabetes will respond to astringents, if the case is characterized by free

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion of fluids.

<!-- chunk -->

### One of the ironies of the relaxed tissue state is that it tends to create a

<!-- chunk -->

### deep tension, which can be either psychological or physical, or both. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with high blood pressure in some cases. This probably occurs

<!-- chunk -->

### because fluid loss is associated with potassium depletion and sodium

<!-- chunk -->

### buildup.

<!-- chunk -->

### The most prominent astringent herbs are Rubus spp. (raspberry,

<!-- chunk -->

### blackberry), Rhus spp. (sumach bark), Hamamelis (witch hazel),

<!-- chunk -->

### Geranium spp. (wild geranium, herb robert), Quercus spp. (white oak

<!-- chunk -->

### bark), Osmunda regalis (royal fern), Salvia spp. (sage), Tsuga canadensis

<!-- chunk -->

### (Canadian hemlock), Aesculus hippocastanum (horse chestnut), Collinsonia

<!-- chunk -->

### (stone root), and Myrica cerifera (bayberry bark). The last five are

<!-- chunk -->

### warming, stimulating astringents.

<!-- chunk -->

### Excessive use of astringents can impair mineral absorption. Astringents

<!-- chunk -->

### bind with alkalis. However, as a remedy, they help retain minerals.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are a number of anomalous remedies that stop fluid loss by

<!-- chunk -->

### increasing nutrition or stimulating the tissues. This includes Monarda

<!-- chunk -->

### fistulosa (sweet leaf), Astragalus, and Foeniculum (fennel). They do not fit

<!-- chunk -->

### this tissue state.

<!-- chunk -->

## Rubus villosa (Blackberry)

<!-- chunk -->

### Blackberry is the standard old-time remedy for simple, uncomplicated

<!-- chunk -->

### diarrhea due to “relaxation of the bowels.” The leaf, fruit, or root may be

<!-- chunk -->

### used. It is sweet, sour, cool, and astringent, so it sedates heat and tones

<!-- chunk -->

### the mucosa of the gastrointestinal tract, improving digestion, absorption,

<!-- chunk -->

### and nutrition. If it is not available, use raspberry leaf in its place.

<!-- chunk -->

### Blackberry “is especially indicated when there is evidence of

<!-- chunk -->

### relaxation and enfeeblement of the mucous coats of the stomach and

<!-- chunk -->

### bowels, and the discharges are large, watery, and clay-colored.” It is also

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated in “passive hemorrhage from the stomach, bowels, or uterus”

<!-- chunk -->

### (Fyfe, 1909). Blackberry leaf tea was Dr. V. G. Rocine’s favorite medicine

<!-- chunk -->

### for “prolapse” of the stomach.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Wooster Beach (1842, 670) preserved an interesting anecdote from

<!-- chunk -->

### upstate New York about blackberry. “It is a medicine much used by

<!-- chunk -->

### Indians in dysentery; and it is said that, in the Oneida tribe, five hundred

<!-- chunk -->

### were attacked with this diseases in one season, and, by the use of the

<!-- chunk -->

### blackberry root, all recovered, while their neighbors, the whites, fell

<!-- chunk -->

### before the disease; no doubt in consequence of taking mercury, or some

<!-- chunk -->

### of the common agents made use of.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Rhus typhina, R. aromatica, R. coriara (Sumach)

<!-- chunk -->

### Sumach (R. coriara) has a long history of use in Greek and Arabic

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine. It was largely forgotten at the time of American settlement

<!-- chunk -->

### and the use of the native species were picked up from the Indians.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fragrant sumach (R. aromatica) and smooth sumach (R. glabra) were

<!-- chunk -->

### officinal in the nineteenth century, but staghorn sumach (R. typhina) has

<!-- chunk -->

### been used in folk medicine. The symptoms are relatively interchangeable

<!-- chunk -->

### but R. glabra seems to have more putrefaction. I use R. typhina because it

<!-- chunk -->

### grows in my neighborhood and has the beautiful antler-like branches of

<!-- chunk -->

### an “elk medicine.” The bark is astringent while the berries are sour and

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent. Herbalist Glenda Croft, of Wagga Wagga, Australia, uses the

<!-- chunk -->

### ground berries and bark of the Old World sumach, which is widely

<!-- chunk -->

### available as a condiment.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalist Phyllis Light has developed a constitutional picture of the

<!-- chunk -->

### sumach person, based on traditional Appalachian usage in her region,

<!-- chunk -->

### and through personal experience. The person is usually thin, weak,

<!-- chunk -->

### lethargic, lacking in will, codependent, with weak kidneys, dark circles

<!-- chunk -->

### under the eyes, anemia (poor blood) from renal insufficiency (kidneys

<!-- chunk -->

### not signaling the marrow), low immunity (also from bone marrow

<!-- chunk -->

### weakness), with fluid loss. Kidneys are relaxed, exhausted, not able to

<!-- chunk -->

### remove fluids (edema), or not able to retain filtered urine. A

<!-- chunk -->

### characteristic symptom is frequent urination associated with weakness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fluid loss after a period of fever. Also indicated when other fluids are

<!-- chunk -->

### not being retained, hence in impotence, nocturnal emissions, clear

<!-- chunk -->

### vaginal discharge, dribbling of urine, excessive blood loss from the

<!-- chunk -->

### kidneys, uterus, bowels, stomach, or lungs, with excessive expectoration.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. J. T. McClanahan, of Booneville, Missouri, introduced fragrant

<!-- chunk -->

### sumach (Rhus aromatica) in 1879 as a remedy for urinary incontinence

<!-- chunk -->

### and excessive urination. It became an officinal remedy in nineteenth-

<!-- chunk -->

### century medicine. The leaves and bark of the fragrant sumach are

<!-- chunk -->

### beneficial for both diabetes insipidus and mellitus. I have several times

<!-- chunk -->

### used staghorn sumach with the same results associated with its aromatic

<!-- chunk -->

### cousin—diminished perspiration and urination and return of blood sugar

<!-- chunk -->

### to a stable level.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. I. J. M. Goss (1885, 124) learned about Rhus aromatica for diabetes

<!-- chunk -->

### from Dr. McClanahan. An old man who had what we would now call

<!-- chunk -->

### type II diabetes approached him. He was very weak, and was passing

<!-- chunk -->

### large quantities of urine filled with sugar. “I put this old gentleman upon

<!-- chunk -->

### the lycopus and nitrate of uranium, which treatment he continued for

<!-- chunk -->

### one month, and which reduced the quantity of urine, but not the

<!-- chunk -->

### quantity of sugar.” Goss then put him on Rhus aromatica, thirty drops,

<!-- chunk -->

### three times a day. This decreased both urine and sugar. After two

<!-- chunk -->

### months, “he reported himself perfectly well, and so continues up to last

<!-- chunk -->

### accounts.” The old man had visited both his brothers, who were “old and

<!-- chunk -->

### reputable physicians;” both had pronounced the case incurable.

<!-- chunk -->

### “By thus quoting an extreme case of diabetes mellitus, in an old man

<!-- chunk -->

### 74 or 75, I desire to show that the Rhus aromatica is the most reliable

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy that has hitherto been used.” From my own experience I would

<!-- chunk -->

### agree, but twentieth-century practitioners have used blueberry or

<!-- chunk -->

### bilberry leaf as their standard remedy in type II diabetes and it may be

<!-- chunk -->

### that it is also of great effectiveness.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here is a case where I used Rhus typhina. A forty-three-year-old

<!-- chunk -->

### woman in Harrisonburg, Virginia, had contracted type I diabetes twenty-

<!-- chunk -->

### two years previously and was on the insulin pump. In most such cases,

<!-- chunk -->

### after so many years, the cells become somewhat insulin-resistant and

<!-- chunk -->

### there are increasing symptoms of what is really type II diabetes. Blood

<!-- chunk -->

### sugar levels gyrated between 400 and as low as twenty-six,

<!-- chunk -->

### unpredictably. She had extreme mood-swings, nervousness, poor

<!-- chunk -->

### eyesight, numbness, and soreness in the legs, cold feet with low sugar

<!-- chunk -->

### levels, and telltale thirst with excessive urination. When her blood sugar

<!-- chunk -->

### levels plunged, she would “cuss like a dirty sailor” and not remember

<!-- chunk -->

### afterwards. After starting on Rhus typhina, three drops, three times per

<!-- chunk -->

### day, blood sugar levels became stable for the first time in ten years and

<!-- chunk -->

### all the above symptoms of type II diabetes disappeared. Of course she

<!-- chunk -->

### still had an insulin pump but now she could tell when her blood sugars

<!-- chunk -->

### were down. She literally threw away her glasses and regained normal

<!-- chunk -->

### eyesight.

<!-- chunk -->

### The bark is collected in the spring, when the sap is running and used

<!-- chunk -->

### per decoction or preserved in alcohol, glycerin, or sugar. The berries are

<!-- chunk -->

### collected as soon as they are ripe and cleaned to remove worms. Usual

<!-- chunk -->

### dose of the tincture, five to thirty drops (Fyfe).

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (bark) astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

### (berries) sour, cooling, astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: (bark) relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

### (berries) relaxation, irritation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: kidneys, skin, fluids.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Fragile persons; pale, anemic, poor immunity, weak bone marrow and

<!-- chunk -->

### kidneys, with fluid loss from the skin, lungs, bowels, kidneys.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Weak, fragile, lethargic, lacking in will, whiny, and codependent.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dark under the eyes, pale, flushing during flu and fever alternating

<!-- chunk -->

### with gray pallor.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Spongy gums, canker sores, ulcerations in the mouth (rinse, Rhus

<!-- chunk -->

### glabra).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Colds and flues with copious secretion, postnasal drip, sore throat

<!-- chunk -->

### with inflamed uvula and tonsils (but no pitting or pus), cough from

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion into lungs.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bronchitis with copious, free secretion weakness; lethargy; fragile

<!-- chunk -->

### capillaries; bleeding.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Traditional for consumption; bleeding, weakness and copious

<!-- chunk -->

### perspiration.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea with fetid flatus and stool (Rhus glabra).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Urine profuse, frequent, pale; without active inflammation, with

<!-- chunk -->

### exhaustion, weakness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • “Profuse and painful discharges, mucous and hemorrhagic, from the

<!-- chunk -->

### mucous surfaces of the kidneys, bladder, gastrointestinal canal,

<!-- chunk -->

### uterus, lungs, and bronchi” (Fyfe).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Swollen prostate, with great pain on urination.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Impotence; with frequent urination at night.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diabetes insipidus; large quantities of urine passed, no sugar, great

<!-- chunk -->

### thirst.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diabetes mellitus with copious urine and perspiration.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Incipient albuminuria.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Edema with weakness, fragility; not indicated when stones are

<!-- chunk -->

### present.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bedwetting in the young and the old; with weakness; frequent

<!-- chunk -->

### urination at night; unable to fill the bladder before needing relief;

<!-- chunk -->

### dribbling, lack of retention of urine.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fever with debility, fluid loss, night-sweats, thirst; preceded by

<!-- chunk -->

### frequent urination.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stiffness, inflammation, and weakness of the lower back and knees

<!-- chunk -->

### (cf. homeopathic Rhus toxicodendron).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Skin fungus associated with dampness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Antidotal to mercury salivation and putrefaction (Rhus glabra).

## Literature: Traditional, Phyllis Light (1–7, 10, 17, 20), William Boericke

## Juglans nigra (Black Walnut Leaf)

<!-- chunk -->

### Black walnut is a fragrant bitter with astringent properties. The hulls are

<!-- chunk -->

### described under the section on torpor. The leaves are more astringent

<!-- chunk -->

### than the hulls. This is not a remedy I am familiar with through personal

<!-- chunk -->

### experience, but the indications from Dr. Edward Shook (1978, 92)

<!-- chunk -->

### interest us here because they bring out the properties of astringency

<!-- chunk -->

### which are inherent in the leaves, a part of the plant which has long been

<!-- chunk -->

### ignored.

<!-- chunk -->

### Shook used a decoction of walnut leaves to stop diarrhea and tone the

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles of the bowel. “In relaxed and atonic conditions of the muscular

<!-- chunk -->

### structure of any organ, the astringing, contracting, and toning properties

<!-- chunk -->

### of walnut leaves or bark are most remarkable. Let us take, for instance,

<!-- chunk -->

### the ballooned condition of the intestines so frequently met with in our

<!-- chunk -->

### practice.” The terminology illustrates the use of the terms “relaxation”

<!-- chunk -->

### and “astringent” by the old doctors as a description of an energetic state.

<!-- chunk -->

### Shook preferred to combine it with Chondrus crispus, which would be a

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue building mucilage.

<!-- chunk -->

### W. Burns Lingard (1958, 18) recommended fluid extract of black

<!-- chunk -->

### walnut hulls in ten to fifteen minimum doses for “bilious diarrhea,” cases

<!-- chunk -->

### where diarrhea is associated with a free flux of bile from the gall

<!-- chunk -->

### bladder. There may be a bacterial element here, which black walnut is

<!-- chunk -->

### suited to as a remedy.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (leaves) bitter, astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue state: relaxation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ-affinities: mucosa, intestines, skin.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Canker sores, tonsillitis, sore throat, relaxed uvula.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Epistaxis, nasal catarrh.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hoarseness of the voice.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Internal ulcerations, inflammations, mucous and hemorrhagic

<!-- chunk -->

### discharges.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea, dysentery; relaxed and ballooned intestines; bleeding

<!-- chunk -->

### hemorrhoids.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Leucorrhea.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Externally for ringworm, falling hair, eczema, itch, shingles, tumors,

<!-- chunk -->

### cancers, abscesses, boils, acne.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Edward Shook.

<!-- chunk -->

## Quercus alba, Quercus robur (White Oak Bark)

<!-- chunk -->

### Quercus alba is the officinal plant in North America. Quercus robur is used

<!-- chunk -->

### in Europe. Many other oaks have been used on the same indications.

<!-- chunk -->

### They seem fairly interchangeable. I discussed Quercus rather extensively

<!-- chunk -->

### in The Book of Herbal Wisdom and will give only a brief account here.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oak bark is a very powerful astringent. It tightens, strengthens, and

<!-- chunk -->

### rebuilds varicose veins and hemorrhoids. It is indicated by the nastiest

<!-- chunk -->

### looking, dilapidated veins resembling blue-black clumps of grapes with

<!-- chunk -->

### yellow serum discoloring the tissue. It increases the venous return

<!-- chunk -->

### circulation, tones the veins, reduces the swelling and color, reestablishes

<!-- chunk -->

### the action of the valves in veins, and returns the tissue almost to a new

<!-- chunk -->

### appearance. It also acts on relaxed, rotten gums and, strange to say, on

<!-- chunk -->

### the teeth themselves. Dr. Christopher taught that it could cure cavities

<!-- chunk -->

### and I have seen this so many times (sometimes with x-ray

<!-- chunk -->

### documentation) that I consider it one of the most reliable remedies I

<!-- chunk -->

### know. It is specific for lymphedema following the removal of glands

<!-- chunk -->

### during cancer therapy, but here it cures only about half the time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oak is the great remedy when long, arduous suffering or usage has

<!-- chunk -->

### broken down the integrity of mind or body. Dr. Bach introduced Quercus

<!-- chunk -->

### robur for those who fight on against adversity, never giving in, but never

<!-- chunk -->

### succeeding. It helps a person choose the appropriate, winnable battles,

<!-- chunk -->

### instead of trying to “out-integrity” every obstacle. J. G. Rademacher,

<!-- chunk -->

### Compton Burnett, and Dorothy Shepherd developed the use of Q. robur

<!-- chunk -->

### for the treatment of alcoholism (which I have seen confirmed).

<!-- chunk -->

### In the winter of 2000–01, there was a particularly harsh influenza

<!-- chunk -->

### with tendencies towards pneumonia. The hospital beds were full. Ginger

<!-- chunk -->

### root tea helped some people who caught the disease early, but those

<!-- chunk -->

### who were down with it for a long time became exhausted. I saw several

<!-- chunk -->

### people who were yellow and swollen beneath the eyes, with recurrent

<!-- chunk -->

### coughing fits. Oak bark steered them back to health.

<!-- chunk -->

### Oak is such a strong astringent that it can only be used in small doses:

<!-- chunk -->

### one to three drops is my favorite dosage. A gargle is good in gum and

<!-- chunk -->

### teeth problems.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: relaxation, depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: mucosa, veins, tooth, and bone.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Persons who struggle against adversity; they never give up but they

<!-- chunk -->

### never succeed; helps a person choose the battles they can win.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Alcoholism.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Goiter.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sinus congestion and postnasal drip.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Strep throat, tonsillitis, swollen glands in throat.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Exhausted after bronchitis; blue, yellow, swollen, puffy under and

<!-- chunk -->

### around the eyes; spitting of blood.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Gum disease, dental caries, loose teeth, bad breath, canker sores.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dyspepsia; diarrhea; hemorrhoids.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Intestinal prolapse, lack of tone, candida.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Saggy, boggy, prolapsed mucosa; especially of the alimentary tract.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Ulceration of the bladder, bloody urine, cystitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Blue/black, knobby varicose veins on the legs, surrounded by yellow

<!-- chunk -->

### infiltration.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Vaginitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Osteoporosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bleeding, bruises.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lymphedema after surgical removal of glands.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Edward Bach (1), Johann Gottfried Rademacher

<!-- chunk -->

### (2), J. Compton Burnett (2, 6), Dorothy Shepherd (2), Finley Ellingwood,

<!-- chunk -->

### Matthew Wood (1, 2, 6, 7, 12, 16), John Christopher (7).

<!-- chunk -->

## Salvia officinalis (Sage)

<!-- chunk -->

### Sage is a plant of remarkable range and depth. It is oily, astringent, and

<!-- chunk -->

### warming, so it builds fluids, stops or restores their loss, and mildly

<!-- chunk -->

### warms and restores functions associated with fluids. Anthony Godfrey,

<!-- chunk -->

### N.D., of Toronto, put it as simply as can be: “Sage balances fluids of all

<!-- chunk -->

### kinds.” On the one hand, it is used to check excessive flux of fluids from

<!-- chunk -->

### the body, as in the drenching sweats of tuberculosis and menopause,

<!-- chunk -->

### excessive urination, diarrhea, and menstruation. On the other hand, it is

<!-- chunk -->

### used to nourish tissue that is dried out, withered, and atrophic. It has a

<!-- chunk -->

### complex hormonal effect, which makes it drying for young women (a

<!-- chunk -->

### famous antigalactagogue), and moistening for older women. It is a

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulant for tissue depression and blood stagnation. It also has a strong

<!-- chunk -->

### action on the nervous system and is traditionally used for nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### delirium and spasm, drowsiness, lethargy, and weak nerve reflexes

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with low fevers.

<!-- chunk -->

### On the simplest level, we may think of sage as it is used in cooking: to

<!-- chunk -->

### help digest the fats. It stimulates the gall bladder—I used it once to cure

<!-- chunk -->

### gall bladder colic—improves digestion, and lubricates the colon. Like

<!-- chunk -->

### burdock, it improves the use of fats and oils in the interior, especially

<!-- chunk -->

### helping them build tissue. It moistens and rebuilds dried skin, and

<!-- chunk -->

### especially, dried tendons. When I go to take the pulse and I cannot find

<!-- chunk -->

### the tendon that should run alongside the artery, I think of sage and I am

<!-- chunk -->

### never disappointed.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Dietrich Gümble, in Principles of Holistic Therapy with Herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### Essences (1993), states that the principle activity of sage oil is to help

<!-- chunk -->

### with the absorption of fluids into the tissues and cells, enhancing

<!-- chunk -->

### “swellability” of the connective tissue, increasing pliability, and

<!-- chunk -->

### retention of fluids. This gives it a powerful influence on the kidneys.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sage helps the re-absorption of blood from the kidneys back into the

<!-- chunk -->

### bloodstream and cuts down on fluid loss by excessive urination. The skin

<!-- chunk -->

### and the kidneys communicate to hold in water.

<!-- chunk -->

### By preserving internal fluids, sage prevents nervous irritability and

<!-- chunk -->

### debility (from lack of fluids to sedate and calm the nervous system).

<!-- chunk -->

### Because lipids are the medium of transportation for lipophilic hormones

<!-- chunk -->

### (thyroid, adrenals, gonads) it has a profound influence on the endocrine

<!-- chunk -->

### system and menopause.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is especially called for when the ovaries stop producing estrogen

<!-- chunk -->

### during menopause and the fluids and skin start to dry out. It is suited to

<!-- chunk -->

### the hot flushes, profuse perspiration and emotional imbalance of

<!-- chunk -->

### menopause. It has long been used to increase fertility, but also to stop

<!-- chunk -->

### lactation. Gümbel says sage gives the skin “a soft, feminine look and a

<!-- chunk -->

### good circulation.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Maude Grieve (1931, 703) emphasizes the action of sage on stopping

<!-- chunk -->

### flux from relaxed tissues. “The principle and most valued application of

<!-- chunk -->

### [sage] is as a wash for the cure of affections of the mouth and as a gargle

<!-- chunk -->

### in inflamed sore throat, being excellent for relaxed throat and tonsils,

<!-- chunk -->

### and also for ulcerated throat. The gargle is useful for bleeding gums and

<!-- chunk -->

### to prevent an excessive flow of saliva.” Ben Charles Harris (1961, 161)

<!-- chunk -->

### gives the following formula for relaxed and bleeding gums: sage (one

<!-- chunk -->

### part), sumach (one part), and goldthread (optional; one part).

<!-- chunk -->

### Sage has a long history of use as a folk remedy for sore throat. Sage

<!-- chunk -->

### tea, with some honey in it, is a delicious beverage soothing to the throat,

<!-- chunk -->

### strengthening to the peripheral circulation when there is relaxation of

<!-- chunk -->

### the uvula, tonsils, and mucous membranes. It strengthens resistance to

<!-- chunk -->

### external environmental influences, much like Astragalus. Both

<!-- chunk -->

### consolidate a weak periphery with excessive sweating. Sage is therefore

<!-- chunk -->

### used for those who suffer from coughs, colds, and flu. I used it myself for

<!-- chunk -->

### recurrent sore throats dating back to childhood. A problem I have now

<!-- chunk -->

### largely outgrown, thanks to this remedy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rudolf Steiner suggested its use in asthma; it is suited to cases where

<!-- chunk -->

### there is excessive expectoration and weakness with a history of “low

<!-- chunk -->

### immunity.” Here is a case history, if we can call it that, illustrating the

<!-- chunk -->

### use of sage in asthma. I was invited to dinner at some friends, where I

<!-- chunk -->

### was treated to a story of my own healing prowess, recounted by the host

<!-- chunk -->

### in front of a millionaire. It was one of those times you cannot help but

<!-- chunk -->

### feel a happy glow on your checks. I had completely forgotten the entire

<!-- chunk -->

### event, though both the husband and the wife vouchsafed all the details.

<!-- chunk -->

### Apparently I brought some sage tincture to a dinner years ago to give to

<!-- chunk -->

### the wife and opened it up part way through the dinner. Her husband,

<!-- chunk -->

### sitting several seats away, had been suffering from asthma for many

<!-- chunk -->

### years. He suddenly took several deep breaths and turned red in the face

<!-- chunk -->

### as oxygen poured into his head. I put the lid back on the bottle with a

<!-- chunk -->

### comment like, “See, even small amounts of an herb will have an effect if

<!-- chunk -->

### it’s the right one.” He started taking sage and has been completely free

<!-- chunk -->

### of asthma since.

<!-- chunk -->

### Hildegard von Bingen recommended it when there were solidified,

<!-- chunk -->

### putrefying humors, or phlegm making a person cough blood, i.e., in

<!-- chunk -->

### tuberculosis. She recommended waiting for the bleeding to stop, then

<!-- chunk -->

### giving sage cooked in mild, sweet wine mixed with water. She also

<!-- chunk -->

### recommends supplementing the diet with butter or olive oil (Physica,

<!-- chunk -->

### I:LXIII), which would indicate that she was thinking of people who do

<!-- chunk -->

### not have enough oil.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sage is also indicated when there is profuse, free perspiration, or night

<!-- chunk -->

### sweats with respiratory diseases (cf. Astragalus). It was an important

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy used to check the colliquative sweating of tuberculosis in the

<!-- chunk -->

### nineteenth century. Sage can be used as a diaphoretic to promote

<!-- chunk -->

### sweating, or as an astringent to contain it. A warm sage tea may provoke

<!-- chunk -->

### a cleansing sweat, which clears out the chill and strengthens the

<!-- chunk -->

### periphery. LeSassier notes that sage especially diffuses sweat from the

<!-- chunk -->

### lung area, whereas ginger brings it more out of the stomach. Such

<!-- chunk -->

### observations form the basis of our knowledge of “organ affinities.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Amy Minel, an herbalist in Ulster County, New York, related a nice

<!-- chunk -->

### case history. A man in his early thirties, thin to begin with, started losing

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids by urination and drying out. His skin was dry and his joints,

<!-- chunk -->

### formerly bendable, were becoming painful. “I feel like I’m becoming an

<!-- chunk -->

### old man,” he said ruefully. She gave sage and licorice. The symptoms

<!-- chunk -->

### quickly and efficiently reversed themselves.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here is another case from a student in Pennsylvania. A friend suffered

<!-- chunk -->

### from profuse sweating and urination, aggravated or caused by

<!-- chunk -->

### hypertensive remedies. She was suffering from labile hypertension. She

<!-- chunk -->

### recommended a quart a day of fresh sumach berry and sage leaf tea. The

<!-- chunk -->

### profuse fluid losses were quickly checked.

<!-- chunk -->

### Sage acts on veins that are relaxed and depressed. Sage bath oil is

<!-- chunk -->

### used in anthroposophical medicine as a remedy for the veins. Sage also

<!-- chunk -->

### dissolves clotted blood. Salvia miltiorrhiza is a major remedy for

<!-- chunk -->

### “congealed blood” in traditional Chinese medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### I was first made aware of the use of our Western or garden sage as a

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy for blood stagnation in a manuscript relating the teachings of

<!-- chunk -->

### herbalist Eva Graf (1978, 14–6), of Great Barrington, Massachusetts. She

<!-- chunk -->

### used it to cleanse the blood vessels, to remove plaque or hardening, for

<!-- chunk -->

### varicose veins, to restrain immoderate bleeding of dark, coagulated

<!-- chunk -->

### blood, diabetic ulcerations of the veins, especially of the calves. She

<!-- chunk -->

### assures her students that it dissolves clots without releasing them into

<!-- chunk -->

### the circulation to cause emboli and so forth. Hot sage tea, she says, will

<!-- chunk -->

### banish depression quickly.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mrs. Graf tells a typical folk-medical story about “a little girl who had

<!-- chunk -->

### a disease where her blood vessels had gone through and all of her legs

<!-- chunk -->

### and arms were kind of black looking. It was quite terrible to look at. The

<!-- chunk -->

### doctors said, ‘There’s nothing we can do for it and it will eventually kill

<!-- chunk -->

### her.’ It took two sage baths where they completely immersed the child in

<!-- chunk -->

### sage tea, and played with her for a period of twenty minutes, to

<!-- chunk -->

### completely take care of the whole condition.” Let the doctors complain

<!-- chunk -->

### of a lack of detail and the anecdotal nature of such stories; in herbalism

<!-- chunk -->

### we appreciate the graphic picture in the story more than the medical

<!-- chunk -->

### diagnosis. Also, we appreciate the success, rather than the theory and

<!-- chunk -->

### consensus opinion of experts.

<!-- chunk -->

### In holistic medicine we follow the American Indian method, where the

<!-- chunk -->

### story is held to convey “medicine power.” Fortified with this story, I

<!-- chunk -->

### applied sage to the foot of a woman who had been nearly paralyzed in a

<!-- chunk -->

### car accident, and whose cerebellum had been damaged. She was able to

<!-- chunk -->

### walk with great difficulty and needed an antispasmodic drug to prevent

<!-- chunk -->

### muscular spasms. An operation on her right foot, or perhaps just the

<!-- chunk -->

### problems incipient to her condition, caused the foot to become swollen

<!-- chunk -->

### and blue/purple. Tests showed no obvious reason for the problem. When

<!-- chunk -->

### we applied sage, internally and externally, the condition promptly

<!-- chunk -->

### lessened and went away in several weeks. (In this instance I had to use

<!-- chunk -->

### California sage as well, since I was running out of garden sage).

<!-- chunk -->

### Here is another case history. A forty-five-year-old man smashed his

<!-- chunk -->

### calf into a wood stove. He suffered internal contusions with swelling,

<!-- chunk -->

### tenderness, and lumpiness along the veins. When he took a flight the

<!-- chunk -->

### high altitude caused troubled breathing, due to blood clots breaking

<!-- chunk -->

### loose and blocking capillaries in the lungs. Thereafter, he had to avoid

<!-- chunk -->

### flying. Yarrow, St. John’s Wort, and other remedies helped, but nothing

<!-- chunk -->

### really moved the condition for the following year and a half. Finally I

<!-- chunk -->

### recommended sage, internally and externally. He used what he had on

<!-- chunk -->

### hand—California white sage (Saliva apiana). The lumps dissipated within

<!-- chunk -->

### two weeks, the veins tingled with new life, and the circulation to the

<!-- chunk -->

### extremities improved. Even some lumps on his arm that he got as a boy

<!-- chunk -->

### disappeared.

<!-- chunk -->

### The great white sage (Salvia apiana), native to California, can be used

<!-- chunk -->

### in place of garden sage (S. officinalis). It is more stimulating and

<!-- chunk -->

### warming, but less astringent. Here it might be well to quote William

<!-- chunk -->

### Turner (1562, 275): “Dioscorides maketh but one kind of Sage,

<!-- chunk -->

### but … now are there found more kinds, the which, though they differ

<!-- chunk -->

### one from another much in roughness and smoothness, in greatness and

<!-- chunk -->

### smallness, and in diversity of colours, yet in my judgment do agree in

<!-- chunk -->

### one virtue and property, and although some be stronger than other some

<!-- chunk -->

### be.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Sage tea is prepared by steeping a closed pot, to prevent the loss of the

<!-- chunk -->

### volatile oils. It is taken hot to induce sweating and cold to prevent

<!-- chunk -->

### sweating and discharges. Some honey can be added for flavor, making it

<!-- chunk -->

### a delicious drink. A tincture can be prepared from the leaves. For a more

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulating, less astringent tincture, use California or great white sage

<!-- chunk -->

### (Salvia apiana). I use the tincture in small doses.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: pungent, warm, moist, oily, astringent.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue States: relaxation, atrophy, depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: skin, mucosa, kidneys, endocrine.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Older persons with dryness of the skin, hair, mucosa, tendons; drying

<!-- chunk -->

### up of secretions.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Weak, debilitated persons, sensitive persons, children, old people;

<!-- chunk -->

### prone to colds, flu, coughs, which begin with dryness, soreness of the

<!-- chunk -->

### throat; with weak digestive powers, bloating, and flatulence.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mental lethargy, depression, poor concentration, “dull and heavy

<!-- chunk -->

### spirit.”

<!-- chunk -->

### • Typhoid and brain fevers with nervous tension, restlessness, insomnia

<!-- chunk -->

### or drowsiness, lethargy, unconsciousness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Loss of hair, fading hair color, dandruff.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Running sinuses, postnasal drip, colds.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Gums, sore, bleeding, ulcerated, inflamed, receding; canker sores;

<!-- chunk -->

### inflammation of tongue.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Throat dry, tickles, sore; sometimes descending into the lungs,

<!-- chunk -->

### resulting in dry, irritable cough, followed by secretion of mucus,

<!-- chunk -->

### which dries out, resulting in mucus difficult to raise; voice loss; weak

<!-- chunk -->

### and spasmodic cough reflexes.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sore throat, swollen glands, tonsillitis, pain on swallowing.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Palliative for tuberculosis; with night sweats, emaciation, swollen

<!-- chunk -->

### glands.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Atonic digestive mucosa with increased secretion, or lack of secretion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Indigestion with weakness and debility; difficulty digesting fats and

<!-- chunk -->

### oils; gas.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stomach dry, with bloating and flatulence. Pain in the side.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Chronic diarrhea, with debility.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Increased secretion of urine with low specific gravity; with debility;

<!-- chunk -->

### cystitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Excessive or deficient sexual desire; infertility; dryness, lack of vaginal

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion; lack of bleeding, amenorrhea; menopausal hot flashes,

<!-- chunk -->

### night sweats.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Morning sickness during pregnancy.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Contraindicated during lactation, dries up milk.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menopause: estrogen changes after menopause.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Excessive, debilitating perspiration, skin soft and relaxed, with

<!-- chunk -->

### enfeebled circulation to the extremities, which are cold.

<!-- chunk -->

### Contraindicated when the night sweat is preceded by hectic fever

<!-- chunk -->

### and dry, harsh skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Skin and mucosa dry, withered, like a sage leaf; lichenification.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tendons dry, weak, malnourished, tight, flaccid.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Deterioration of the veins; bleeding, varicosities; in diabetes and other

<!-- chunk -->

### diseases.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Blood stagnation; removes clots and coagulation without causing

<!-- chunk -->

### strokes, etc.; improves circulation and cardiovascular function.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pains in limbs and joints; arthritis; worse from cold and damp.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Epilepsy, palsy.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Insect bites; wounds, bleeding.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Matthiolus (3), William Coles (21), John

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder (20), John William Fyfe, Rose Elliot and Carlo di Paoli (2, 3),

<!-- chunk -->

### Matthew Wood (4, 6, 21, 22), Tommie Bass, Eva Graf (22–3), Phyllis

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

## Aesculus hippocastanum (Horse Chestnut)

<!-- chunk -->

### Horse Chestnut is an interesting remedy because it unites a strong

<!-- chunk -->

### warming stimulation to the astringency. Thus it increases circulation as

<!-- chunk -->

### well as improving the tone of the veins. It acts strongly on passive

<!-- chunk -->

### venous congestion and hence is an astringent with a strong activity on

<!-- chunk -->

### neuromuscular tension, which is often caused by venous backup. It was

<!-- chunk -->

### widely used as a remedy to relax horses after a workout. At one time a

<!-- chunk -->

### horse liniment was sold in farm supply stores. Herbalist Evelyn Snook

<!-- chunk -->

### made a liniment by peeling horse chestnuts and macerating them for two

<!-- chunk -->

### weeks in witch hazel (Jennifer Tucker). It can also be made by chopping

<!-- chunk -->

### the nuts and hulls without peeling them.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Edward Bach used this plant under the name “white chestnut” as

<!-- chunk -->

### one of his flower essences. It is suited to people who feel attacked by

<!-- chunk -->

### their own thoughts, and unable to find peace and rest. This has been

<!-- chunk -->

### borne out in practitioner experience, and indeed, it is a keynote around

<!-- chunk -->

### which the physical symptoms associated with the remedy may be

<!-- chunk -->

### organized for greater clarity. Aesculus hippocastanum (whether the herb,

<!-- chunk -->

### the flower essence, or the homeopathic remedy) is suited to people who

<!-- chunk -->

### are suffering from obsessive/compulsive thought disorders. Thoughts are

<!-- chunk -->

### continual and give no rest. Insomnia is common.

<!-- chunk -->

### The pulse corresponds to the psychological profile. In a fully

<!-- chunk -->

### developed case it feels as if there is not enough space between the beats,

<!-- chunk -->

### which are bounding and tensed up. It is as if there is not enough space

<!-- chunk -->

### for the thoughts, which fill the mind and cause tension; also for the

<!-- chunk -->

### blood, which makes the pulse bounding, as if looking for a place to

<!-- chunk -->

### escape, as with a hemorrhoid, for instance.

<!-- chunk -->

### These pulse indications are based on my experience. The following is

<!-- chunk -->

### one of the more dramatic of many cases in which I have used Aesculus

<!-- chunk -->

### hippocastanum. A woman came to see me for glaucoma. She was legally

<!-- chunk -->

### blind; her eyesight was damaged by forceps delivery forty-five years

<!-- chunk -->

### previous. She had hemorrhoids, mental restlessness, and sleeplessness. “I

<!-- chunk -->

### have not slept for over four months,” she said. I didn’t need anymore

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms. Aesculus hip. 12x brought a flow of tears to her dry eyes and

<!-- chunk -->

### completely cured the insomnia, hemorrhoids, and glaucoma.

<!-- chunk -->

### In another case, an overweight, middle-aged, but rather jolly, woman

<!-- chunk -->

### came to see me for phlebitis, for which she was taking antibiotics. She

<!-- chunk -->

### had moderately high blood pressure and was on a beta-blocker. The

<!-- chunk -->

### veins were distended but not corrugated, like oak bark, nor dark like

<!-- chunk -->

### sumach or echinacea. There was poor circulation to the periphery with

<!-- chunk -->

### cool hands and feet so I thought a stimulating astringent like Aesculus or

<!-- chunk -->

### Collinsonia would be appropriate. Did she have insomnia? All the time.

<!-- chunk -->

### Obsessive thinking? She was on antidepressants and suffered from

<!-- chunk -->

### inability to focus the mind. No glaucoma, fortunately. Aesculus hip.

<!-- chunk -->

### capsules internally and capsules crushed up in witch hazel extract

<!-- chunk -->

### externally. The swelling went down, the phlebitis improved

<!-- chunk -->

### considerably, circulation improved, the skin became smooth and

<!-- chunk -->

### beautiful, and the thinking processes improved.

<!-- chunk -->

### The nineteenth-century doctors classified Aesculus as a remedy for

<!-- chunk -->

### “passive venous congestion” (see, for instance, William Boericke).

<!-- chunk -->

### Hemorrhoids are common as an outlet from the pressure exerted through

<!-- chunk -->

### the veins. This tension backs up through the portal vein to the liver, or

<!-- chunk -->

### perhaps it goes from the liver back upon the portal system. At any rate,

<!-- chunk -->

### it is a remedy for liver congestion, and also a remedy for high blood

<!-- chunk -->

### pressure. Uterine congestion, engorgement, and malposition come under

<!-- chunk -->

### its dominion.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. J. Compton Burnett wrote a fine little treatise on hemorrhoids and

<!-- chunk -->

### varicosities entitled Diseases of the Veins. What he says about the internal

<!-- chunk -->

### pathology associated with hemorrhoidal conditions helps us to

<!-- chunk -->

### understand the organ-affinities of Aesculus. “Heart affections; imperfect

<!-- chunk -->

### aeration of the blood; liver affections; congestion in the portal system of

<!-- chunk -->

### veins; enlarged spleen; abdominal tumors; great accumulation of fat in

<!-- chunk -->

### the omentum, or of feces in the intestines; in fact, anything that disturbs

<!-- chunk -->

### the reflex of blood to the right heart, vena cava interior, vena portae,

<!-- chunk -->

### tends to hypo-static hyperemia of the hemorrhoidal veins.” This

<!-- chunk -->

### perfectly describes the structures to which Aesculus has an affinity,

<!-- chunk -->

### though Burnett had no acquaintance with this valuable remedy at the

<!-- chunk -->

### time he wrote.

<!-- chunk -->

### Julia Graves remarks, “The venous tonic in Germany is horse chestnut.

<!-- chunk -->

### Every supermarket has Aesculus capsules for ‘tired legs and veins.’ They

<!-- chunk -->

### even advertise it on television. The tincture or salve on the legs gives a

<!-- chunk -->

### nice feeling of lightness. I believe it makes the venous blood rise, giving

<!-- chunk -->

### it levity to defy gravity. Also, it is a big rheumatic remedy for the left

<!-- chunk -->

### knee specifically, and the creme helps me with pain in the left knee and

<!-- chunk -->

### pain in the hips.” Also, “I feel the tincture sucks the venous blood

<!-- chunk -->

### upwards, thus helping the veins not to get too bogged down. I do not get

<!-- chunk -->

### that sensation from using any of the other varicose remedies, such as

<!-- chunk -->

### sage, witch hazel, or oak. It even seems to lift up the piles. I have asked

<!-- chunk -->

### others and they have confirmed this sensation.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Aesculus is moderately toxic and should not be used internally, except

<!-- chunk -->

### in homeopathic form or small doses (one to three drops). It is similar in

<!-- chunk -->

### action to Collinsonia, with which it is often combined as a hemorrhoid

<!-- chunk -->

### salve.

# A

<!-- chunk -->

### s we have mentioned previously, in Greek medicine there are two

<!-- chunk -->

### kinds of dampness, that which flows and that which remains

<!-- chunk -->

### stuck in the tissues. The latter is the genesis of the of torpid

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state. This condition is not only associated with a buildup of

<!-- chunk -->

### unneeded fluids, but with their precipitation into thickened phlegm—

<!-- chunk -->

### mucopolysaccharides, actually—which were called “humors” in the old

<!-- chunk -->

### literature. In addition to the thickening of fluids, waste products build

<!-- chunk -->

### up because of the stuck state of transportation in the body. Indeed, even

<!-- chunk -->

### unused food becomes a waste product. Meanwhile, the tissues are not

<!-- chunk -->

### getting enough nutrition and they tend to become flabby and weak. The

<!-- chunk -->

### thickening fluids cause pain in the muscles. It would be called

<!-- chunk -->

### fibromyalgia today, or rheumatism (dampness) as per the old books. The

<!-- chunk -->

### single most characteristic symptom is said to be skin lesions. Another

<!-- chunk -->

### typical symptom is hangover. The digestion and liver functions are

<!-- chunk -->

### backed up. Lymphatic stagnation and sluggishness in also typical. The

<!-- chunk -->

### channels of elimination may be taxed. Some cases of hypothyroidism

<!-- chunk -->

### would fit here.

<!-- chunk -->

### All of these are easy enough to describe, but torpor is a hard folk

<!-- chunk -->

### medical diagnosis to make. In my experience, the best indication is a

<!-- chunk -->

### pulse where the beat is obscured, as if by phlegm or water. Dr. John

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder pointed out this pulse as a major indicator for “bad blood,” i.e.,

<!-- chunk -->

### torpor. He also noted the lack of expression in the tissues.

<!-- chunk -->

### The term alterative was introduced to indicate that the body was

<!-- chunk -->

### altered in some unknown fashion. It is connected perhaps with Galen’s

<!-- chunk -->

### use of the term “alteration” to describe what changes food into

<!-- chunk -->

### nutriment for the tissues, i.e., what we would call metabolism. Faults of

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism and elimination of waste products are indeed the stuff from

<!-- chunk -->

### which torpor, toxemia, or damp stagnation is made. And its names are

<!-- chunk -->

### legion—bad blood, impure blood, humors in the blood, humor, and

<!-- chunk -->

### toxemia.

<!-- chunk -->

### I rather like the term “blood purifier” more than alterative, as it is

<!-- chunk -->

### more expressive and meaningful. In addition to blood purifiers or

<!-- chunk -->

### alteratives, the main remedies for this tissue state include laxatives. Most

<!-- chunk -->

### of these agents are bitter: Arctium (burdock root), Taraxacum (dandelion

<!-- chunk -->

### root), Hydrastis (goldenseal), Xanthorrhiza (yellow root), Medicago

<!-- chunk -->

### (alfalfa), Polymnia (bearsfoot), Berberis vulgaris (barberry), and Berberis

<!-- chunk -->

### aquifolium (mahonia). The bitter laxatives are: Aloe, Juglans nigra (black

<!-- chunk -->

### walnut), Rhamnus (buckthorn, cascara sagrada), Cassia (senna), and

<!-- chunk -->

### Phytolacca (poke root). Alterative and laxative Rumex crispus (yellow

<!-- chunk -->

### dock root) is sour, astringent, and bitter. Its cousin Rheum (rhubarb) is

<!-- chunk -->

### bitter, acrid, and sour. In excess, bitters can dry out tissue.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are also some non-bitter alteratives such as Smilax ornata

<!-- chunk -->

### (sarsaparilla), Fumaria officinalis (fumitory), Trifolium pratense (red

<!-- chunk -->

### clover), and Urtica (nettle). Most of these plants increase digestion and

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism because they are nourishing. As mentioned above, there

<!-- chunk -->

### tends to be an interplay between stagnation and atrophy, even though

<!-- chunk -->

### one condition is too damp and the other too dry. Both limit organ

<!-- chunk -->

### nutrition, elimination, and function.

<!-- chunk -->

## Arctium lappa (Burdock)

<!-- chunk -->

### I have placed burdock here under the alteratives since it is usually

<!-- chunk -->

### classified as one of the front rank blood purifiers. However, I tend to

<!-- chunk -->

### think of it as a nutritive tonic as well because it is so oily. It nourishes

<!-- chunk -->

### and cleanses the lipid pathways of the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Burdock is bitter, sweet, and oily. It is therefore well suited to

<!-- chunk -->

### dry/atrophic conditions when an alterative would be useful. The oil

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulates increased secretion of bile, promoting better absorption of fats

<!-- chunk -->

### and oils through the small intestine and better activity in the gall

<!-- chunk -->

### bladder. It is called for when there is poor secretion of bile, indicated by

<!-- chunk -->

### dry stool, constipation, and poor emulsification of fats and oils. With low

<!-- chunk -->

### absorption of lipids there is a shortage of these substances in the body.

<!-- chunk -->

### Burdock is thus associated with dry, scaly skin conditions and dry skin in

<!-- chunk -->

### general. Sometimes the sebaceous glands get blocked, due to a lack of oil

<!-- chunk -->

### moving through, resulting in inflammation (hence the association of

<!-- chunk -->

### burdock with acne and boils). The sinuses and lungs are also dry and the

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion through the kidneys is limited. This results in dry tissues, or

<!-- chunk -->

### water retention conjoined with the dryness. The liver is also affected.

<!-- chunk -->

### With a lack of fats and oils, there is reduced anabolism of foodstuffs for

<!-- chunk -->

### the body, with wasting and dryness (dry/atrophy). In order to get

<!-- chunk -->

### nutrition, there may be a dependence on “quick fixes” of sugar. Thus, it

<!-- chunk -->

### is a remedy for sugar imbalances. Since steroid and sex hormones are

<!-- chunk -->

### made from oil and require oil for transmission through the body, and all

<!-- chunk -->

### hormones of any kind require adequate fluids for movement, burdock

<!-- chunk -->

### comes up as something of an endocrine and reproductive remedy. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes beneficial in swollen prostate. It may be necessary when

<!-- chunk -->

### there is not enough oil to maintain healthy adrenals and steroid-

<!-- chunk -->

### building. By moistening the system, burdock also has an influence on the

<!-- chunk -->

### kidneys. It is an old remedy for kidney stones.

<!-- chunk -->

### Generally, in my experience, people who need burdock are on the thin

<!-- chunk -->

### side, nervous, with dry skin and scalp. It may be necessary to give small

<!-- chunk -->

### to large doses over a long time as a building rejuvenative.

<!-- chunk -->

### The root is used for chronic conditions, the seed in acute. A biennial,

<!-- chunk -->

### the roots are dug at the end of the first year or the beginning of the

<!-- chunk -->

### second; the seed are harvested just after the end of the second year,

<!-- chunk -->

### before the plant is completely dead; the leaves are sometimes used

<!-- chunk -->

### externally as a poultice. The root, high in oils, easily goes rancid and

<!-- chunk -->

### needs to be dried with artificial heat and left in the open air. It extracts

<!-- chunk -->

### well in alcohol (brandy is a very good medium). The dried root and

<!-- chunk -->

### seeds may be used by decoction as a tea.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (root) sweet, bitter, pungent, warm, oily.

<!-- chunk -->

### (seed) bitter, pungent, sweet, warm, diffusive.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: atrophy, torpor.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: liver, gall bladder, kidneys, skin, endocrine system.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Heat, dryness, lack of oil.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Thin, withered persons with dry skin and scalp.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Emotionally stoic; phlegmatic about pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tired, worn-out, worried.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dry scalp with red, scaly patches and hair loss.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Headache, frontal, with dry sinus.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Respiratory conditions with dry mucosa; allergies, sinusitis,

<!-- chunk -->

### bronchitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lack of secretion in the upper gastrointestinal (seed).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lack of lubrication in the lower gastrointestinal; constipation (root).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Poor glandular function; lymphatics, pancreas, endocrine, prostate,

<!-- chunk -->

### liver, spleen.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Liver and gall bladder congestion; gallstones.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Blood sugar lability.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Uterine prolapse; with tired feet.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pregnancy: balancing and nutritive during; helps prevent water

<!-- chunk -->

### retention and jaundice in babies.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Specific for swollen prostate in men who lift weights incorrectly.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Leeches out concretions, kidney stones, arthritis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Arthritis, bursitis; better from movement; worse when immobilized.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lower part of spine stiff.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sciatica; unbearable at night.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Gouty pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Better from movement, exercise, bending, open air; worse from

<!-- chunk -->

### inactivity, closed, warm room.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Carpal tunnel syndrome.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fever with excessive oily sweating and worry.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Profuse underarm sweat.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dry or oily skin, acne, eczema, dandruff; boils, abscesses, poison ivy,

<!-- chunk -->

### rashes, itching, measles (leaf externally).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Retention of water in persons who are generally dry; swelling in the

<!-- chunk -->

### upper thighs and across the belly and the upper eyelids, with bags

<!-- chunk -->

### under the eyes, absence of perspiration, the skin is trying to conserve

<!-- chunk -->

### water.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pulse thin.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Boils, abscesses, carbuncles.
