## Berberis aquifolium, Mahonia spp.

<!-- chunk -->

## (Oregon Grape Root)

<!-- chunk -->

### The older literature usually calls Oregon grape root Berberis aquifolium.

<!-- chunk -->

### For the sake of distinction it is convenient to use the name Mahonia.

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many different species that are used interchangeably.

<!-- chunk -->

### However, it cannot be used in place of its cousin Berberis vulgaris. The

<!-- chunk -->

### two have different properties. Both are bitter, but the Mahonias are

<!-- chunk -->

### suited to dry, thin persons, while Berberis, like dandelion, is better for

<!-- chunk -->

### heat and stagnation of fluids.

<!-- chunk -->

### Mahonia is suited to dry, atrophic conditions where the anabolic

<!-- chunk -->

### function of the liver and the secretory aspect of digestion are

<!-- chunk -->

### compromised. It is also suited to heat, chills, intermittent fever, and

<!-- chunk -->

### muscular trembling which arise because the fluids are too low to sedate

<!-- chunk -->

### the heat. The muscles cannot resist tensions and chills (see Ellingwood,

<!-- chunk -->

### 1919, 369–70). Like other intense bitter tonics, it is suited to conditions

<!-- chunk -->

### of stagnation. The tissues dry out and the fluids are caught and build up.

<!-- chunk -->

### Scudder, (quoted by Fyfe, 1909, 417) gives a good overview of

<!-- chunk -->

### Mahonia, which he calls “berberis.” It “is a blood maker. It does this by

<!-- chunk -->

### promoting secretion and excretion. It stimulates the glands of the body,

<!-- chunk -->

### and especially the lymphatics and the liver. It is said to have a diuretic

<!-- chunk -->

### action, also. It certainly aids digestion and assimilation.” From Scudder’s

<!-- chunk -->

### other writings, we know that lack of secretion and excretion is his

<!-- chunk -->

### definition of “bad blood.”

<!-- chunk -->

### From its affinity for glandular structures it acts quickly and

<!-- chunk -->

### pleasingly in hepatic torpor, and is said to prove its efficiency as a

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy when given for cirrhosis of the liver. In stomatitis

<!-- chunk -->

### [canker-sores] and in dyspepsia, especially when in its incipiency

<!-- chunk -->

### and when coupled with hepatic affections, berberis is given with

<!-- chunk -->

### confidence.

<!-- chunk -->

### In chronic disease of the mucous membranes, as catarrh,

<!-- chunk -->

### leucorrhea, bronchorrhea, etc., it is just as efficient. In chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### pulmonary affections in persons of syphilitic or broken down,

<!-- chunk -->

### depraved constitutions, berberis is a miracle worker. It rights the

<!-- chunk -->

### wrongs and cleans the Augean stables, sharpens the appetite,

<!-- chunk -->

### gives new tone and new blood to the body, and comes as near to

<!-- chunk -->

### curing consumption as any remedy known to us at the time. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### both a blood maker and a blood cleanser, and as there is no

<!-- chunk -->

### known remedy so virulent to microorganisms of nearly all

<!-- chunk -->

### varieties, as healthy blood serum, berberis becomes, indirectly if

<!-- chunk -->

### not directly a microbicide.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is an excellent tonic to the weak and debilitated—to the

<!-- chunk -->

### convalescent. It may be safely added to cascara sagrada for the

<!-- chunk -->

### cure of constipation. As we have faith in medicine, we have faith

<!-- chunk -->

### in the so-called alterative and tonic effects of berberis aquifolium

<!-- chunk -->

### when given in appreciable doses. When effective, we like the

<!-- chunk -->

### small dose; when necessary we give the large one with equal

<!-- chunk -->

### delight.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalist Terry Willard (1988, 112) uses Mahonia as a tonic for

<!-- chunk -->

### delicate and weak children. “It alleviates anemia and general

<!-- chunk -->

### malnutrition in just a few weeks.” The typical Mahonia patient is thin,

<!-- chunk -->

### dry, and constipated, with rough, scaly, dry skin.

<!-- chunk -->

### I am sure I am not the only herbalist who has discovered that Oregon

<!-- chunk -->

### grape root combines nicely with burdock root, but just in case this fact

<!-- chunk -->

### goes unnoticed, I will say that the two are particularly compatible. Both

<!-- chunk -->

### are bitter alteratives suited to thin, dry, atrophic patients.

<!-- chunk -->

### “When effective, we like the small dose; when necessary we give the

<!-- chunk -->

### large one with equal delight” (Scudder). Usual dose of the tincture or

<!-- chunk -->

### fluid extract, five to thirty drops (Fyfe).

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: bitter.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue States: atrophy, stagnation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: gastrointestinal mucosa, liver, skin.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Poor anabolism, with withering, wasting, dry skin and mucosa,

<!-- chunk -->

### constipation from lack of secretion; at the same time a buildup of

<!-- chunk -->

### catabolic waste products with lack of secretion from skin and

<!-- chunk -->

### mucosa.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Anemia and malnutrition in children.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Chronic catarrhal conditions of the upper respiratory tract, nose, and

<!-- chunk -->

### throat; thick, gummy, tenacious mucus; with wasting; with soreness

<!-- chunk -->

### in the muscles, cartilage, bones.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Incipient or chronic indigestion, loss of appetite, wasting,

<!-- chunk -->

### constipation.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Catarrh of the mucosa of the gastrointestinal canal.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Constipation, dry intestine.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Ulceration of the intestines, Crohn’s disease.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lymphatic congestion; chronic swelling and induration of glands, with

<!-- chunk -->

### dry, atrophic condition; mononucleosis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diabetes.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hepatitis, jaundice.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Female: amenorrhea, vaginitis, uterine weakness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Dry, scaly, itching patches; facial eruptions; psoriasis, eczema,

<!-- chunk -->

### dandruff, acne.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Waxy, yellow, dry, parchment-like appearance of the skin, similar to

<!-- chunk -->

### what is sometimes seen in the beginning of jaundice or diseases of

<!-- chunk -->

### the spleen, where there is poor assimilation and wasting of flesh.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Arthritis; pain as from a blow, with lameness and stiffness;

<!-- chunk -->

### rheumatoid arthritis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Muscular, bone, and periosteal pains, worse at night.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Intermittent chills and fever.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Michael Moore (1), John M. Scudder, John

<!-- chunk -->

### William Fyfe.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taraxacum officinale (Dandelion)

<!-- chunk -->

### Dandelion has a general reputation as a “spring tonic” and “blood

<!-- chunk -->

### purifier.” However, it has more specific indications that are little known.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is particularly suited to eliminating “thickened humors.” It is indicated

<!-- chunk -->

### when the bile is thick and the liver and gall bladder are congested, or

<!-- chunk -->

### when the kidneys are sluggish. With these thickened fluids there is

<!-- chunk -->

### usually a low-grade heat. Dandelion is especially indicated where there

<!-- chunk -->

### is a “mapped or geographical tongue.” This specific indication comes to

<!-- chunk -->

### us from the homeopathic provings of Samuel Hahnemann. I have

<!-- chunk -->

### verified it scores of times. “Tongue covered with a white film; feels raw;

<!-- chunk -->

### comes off in patches, leaving red, sensitive spots” (Boericke, 1927, 633).

<!-- chunk -->

### If we understand the significance of the mapped tongue, we

<!-- chunk -->

### understand the process to which dandelion is remedial. This indication

<!-- chunk -->

### would be associated with “phlegm fire” in traditional Chinese medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### The basic idea is that heat is baking down fluids while thickened fluids

<!-- chunk -->

### are blocking the outward escape of the heat. In practice, I find that

<!-- chunk -->

### dandelion is indicated when heat has settled in the deeper tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### (muscles, bones), and is simmering away. It is well indicated when there

<!-- chunk -->

### is decalcification from infection in the bone.

<!-- chunk -->

### I was not expecting dandelion to be a remedy for manic/depression,

<!-- chunk -->

### but following the mapped tongue indication I have actually cured two

<!-- chunk -->

### cases. In both, the tongue cleared and the terrible mentality was

<!-- chunk -->

### apparently removed permanently. Usually, when one clears off the

<!-- chunk -->

### mapped tongue it does not come back.

<!-- chunk -->

### Here is a beautiful case history, using both the flower essence and

<!-- chunk -->

### herbal tongue indications, passed on by herbalist Julia Graves in

<!-- chunk -->

### conversation. The allergies and rings around the eyes suggest the

<!-- chunk -->

### kidneys and liver. “An eight-year-old girl, lanky, tall, skinny, pale, with

<!-- chunk -->

### black rings around the eyes, severe pollen allergies. Her mom brings her

<!-- chunk -->

### in because of her episodes of breathtaking meanness. Her tongue is

<!-- chunk -->

### coated white, with angry red patches. The mother says she has had this

<!-- chunk -->

### since at least age three. One dose of dandelion flower essence brought

<!-- chunk -->

### about a complete change. Her walk became graceful, her complexion

<!-- chunk -->

### golden and radiant and she features a big smile. The tongue is less red.

<!-- chunk -->

### Taking the essence and herb for a week stops her meanness. She shares

<!-- chunk -->

### more willingly with her younger brother and plays with him sweetly.

<!-- chunk -->

### She even allows role inversion in the play, where he is allowed to be the

<!-- chunk -->

### dad and her the child.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The root is used more for the alterative effect, to thin the fluids and

<!-- chunk -->

### reduce heat. It is indicated in stagnant bile, where heat is baking down

<!-- chunk -->

### the bile and we get gallstones. It has long been used to slowly cleanse

<!-- chunk -->

### the liver and gall bladder. The digestion is sometimes disordered.

<!-- chunk -->

### Salivation, bitter taste, eructations, loss of appetite, fermentation and

<!-- chunk -->

### swelling in the bowels, chilliness after eating.

<!-- chunk -->

### The leaf is salty/minerally, so it increases water removal and is

<!-- chunk -->

### diuretic. It is indicated when the patient feels better from urination;

<!-- chunk -->

### tired, achy, feverish, chronic infections, with relief from urination.

<!-- chunk -->

### Because the leaves contain both sodium and potassium, they remove

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids while increasing potassium levels. Dandelion is effective in small

<!-- chunk -->

### to large doses, fresh or dry.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (root) bitter, sweet, salty/minerally, moist, oily. (leaf) bitter,

<!-- chunk -->

### salty/minerally, moist, cool.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: torpor.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: liver, kidneys, muscles, and bones.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Dullness of the mind, lethargy, with congestion of fluids.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mania, with heat burning away the fluids; insomnia;

<!-- chunk -->

### manic/depressive.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Swollen membranes around the sinuses, from chronic allergy and

<!-- chunk -->

### infection.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mapped tongue. Patches of opaque white coating torn off in places to

<!-- chunk -->

### expose a red, raw tongue body. Also suitable when all the coating

<!-- chunk -->

### has been exfoliated by heat, or when the coating is yellow, or there

<!-- chunk -->

### are only traces of the coat, or for a dark, red tongue.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mastoiditis; pain, swelling, or redness in the sternoclydomastoid

<!-- chunk -->

### muscle (side of neck).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bronchitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stomach, indigestion, gas.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Liver congested, swollen, gall bladder congested, bile thick, adhesive,

<!-- chunk -->

### gall stone formation, lack of bile secretion, jaundice from backed up

<!-- chunk -->

### bile, constipation from lack of bile in the intestines; anemia from

<!-- chunk -->

### lack of bile production and recycling.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diabetes, type II, hypoglycemia.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Edema, chronic kidney infection, cardiovascular insufficiency.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Swollen, achy feeling in kidney region, feels better after urination.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Breast cancer.

<!-- chunk -->

### • PMS.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fever with high temperature, dark red tongue, or patch on tongue.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Muscular pain, swelling, heat; especially in the sternoclydomastoid

<!-- chunk -->

### muscle and neck.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menopausal problems from poor liver function; liver not breaking

<!-- chunk -->

### down extra hormones.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Infections in bones.

<!-- chunk -->

### • High cholesterol, high blood pressure.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Uric acid.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Acne, abscesses, boils, age spots.

<!-- chunk -->

## Juglans nigra (Black Walnut)

<!-- chunk -->

### Traditionally, the blackened, ripened hulls of the black walnut were

<!-- chunk -->

### used, but recently Huldah Clark has insisted upon the use of the green

<!-- chunk -->

### hulls. The old herbalists had no problem with the blackened hulls and

<!-- chunk -->

### those who were taught by their elders, rather than out of books, should

<!-- chunk -->

### not feel bad in adhering to this practice. The leaves are even more

<!-- chunk -->

### astringent, and these are the component lauded by Dr. Edward Shook

<!-- chunk -->

### (Astringents, Damp Flowing, or the Relaxed Tissue State, this page).

<!-- chunk -->

### Black walnut is both an alterative and a laxative. Its oils stimulate the

<!-- chunk -->

### gall bladder and liver and lubricate the intestines to remove

<!-- chunk -->

### constipation. Butternut bark is somewhat oilier and may be substituted

<!-- chunk -->

### when this effect is needed. It will stimulate the removal of gallstones

<!-- chunk -->

### from the gall bladder quite effectively. Black walnut hulls are also

<!-- chunk -->

### antibacterial and antiparasitical so they can remove diarrhea due to

<!-- chunk -->

### infection and the green hulls, to follow Huldah Clark, are particularly

<!-- chunk -->

### effective against parasites. Black walnut undoubtedly goes deeper, like

<!-- chunk -->

### burdock, into the lipid economy, improving skin conditions such as

<!-- chunk -->

### impetigo that involve microorganisms.

<!-- chunk -->

### The alterative effect of black walnut is increased by the fact that it

<!-- chunk -->

### acts on the thyroid, the keeper of the metabolism. Black walnut is high

<!-- chunk -->

### in iodine. Phyllis Light notes that the black hulls (not green) are a

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional remedy in the South for goiter. The high iodine content

<!-- chunk -->

### causes the characteristic black dye of walnut hulls. The hulls are bitter,

<!-- chunk -->

### oily, astringent, and slightly sweet (the reverse of the nuts, which are

<!-- chunk -->

### sweet, oily, slightly astringent, and bitter). Because it is a remedy for

<!-- chunk -->

### hypothyroidism, black walnut also works on fibromyalgia, which is often

<!-- chunk -->

### associated with this problem, this again from Phyllis Light.

<!-- chunk -->

### Another characteristic of black walnut (and several of its cousins) is

<!-- chunk -->

### that it produces herbicides in its roots that kill off plants trying to grow

<!-- chunk -->

### underneath it, including its own young. This points to its use in killing

<!-- chunk -->

### parasites, bacteria, fungus, and yeast in the intestines. The reason it is

<!-- chunk -->

### able to survive from generation to generation is because of the hull of

<!-- chunk -->

### the nut, which causes it to bounce away from the tree. This signature

<!-- chunk -->

### points to the teaching of Dr. Bach: walnut is for those who are too much

<!-- chunk -->

### under the influence of another. It corrects a poor parental/child

<!-- chunk -->

### relationship, where there is too much domination and closeness. From

<!-- chunk -->

### experience I can say it also acts the opposite way: to heal children who

<!-- chunk -->

### have lost a parent or are experiencing problems with parental

<!-- chunk -->

### separation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Black walnut not only cleanses the colon, but according to Phyllis it

<!-- chunk -->

### treats leaky gut syndrome, where toxins are getting into the bloodstream

<!-- chunk -->

### through the small intestine. It also treats the opposite, or malabsorption

<!-- chunk -->

### from a closed gut. She says it helps the assimilation of nutrients,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially fats and proteins, and reduces unhealthy fatty acids in the

<!-- chunk -->

### bloodstream.

<!-- chunk -->

### In February 2003, a woman in her early sixties consulted me for

<!-- chunk -->

### various problems. She had low thyroid for many years, her mother had

<!-- chunk -->

### low thyroid, and she had something of the hysterical voice one often

<!-- chunk -->

### finds in high and low thyroid cases. She refused to take thyroid

<!-- chunk -->

### supplementation from the doctors. I put her on black walnut hull

<!-- chunk -->

### tincture, three drops, three times per day. In August, tests showed

<!-- chunk -->

### normal thyroid levels for the first time in her life. She maintained a

<!-- chunk -->

### normal volume and pitch of voice for the first time since I had known

<!-- chunk -->

### her, some twenty years. I only wished I had been able to give her black

<!-- chunk -->

### walnut hulls back when I first met her.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (hulls) pungent, bitter, astringent, aromatic.

## Tissue State: torpor, depression.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Conditions where there is a combination of intestinal and skin

<!-- chunk -->

### problems suggesting the participation of low forms of animal life.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Too much under the influence of another person, thought, or scheme.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Goiter, low thyroid, low metabolism, “bad blood.”

<!-- chunk -->

### • Irritation of the mucosa.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Scrofulous enlargement of glands (ulcerating, chronic, suppurative).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Jaundice; pain about liver and right scapula.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Duodenal catarrh, with torpid liver and jaundice.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Congestion of the gall bladder; removes gallstones.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea, constipation, parasites.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Irritability and inflammation of the intestines; worms.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Skin conditions; impetigo, eczema, herpes, shingles, cold sores, acne,

<!-- chunk -->

### pemphigus, rupia, athletes’ foot, prurigomoluscum (itching skin

<!-- chunk -->

### eruption), lichen, ringworm, rashes, chronic scaly skin conditions,

<!-- chunk -->

### especially when associated with intestinal disorder (external).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Skin eruptions red, like the flush of scarlatina or erysipelas (cf.

<!-- chunk -->

### Stellaria).

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Edward Shook (indications for the leaves),

<!-- chunk -->

### Phyllis Light (11, 24), Brent Davis, Edward Bach (mental indications),

<!-- chunk -->

### Jack Ritchason (11, 22, 23).


<!-- chunk -->

### he depressed tissue state is an interesting one. It is well described

<!-- chunk -->

### by the term “cold,” but the cold here is one of the tissues and

<!-- chunk -->

### functions, a deep cold within the organism from the dying down

<!-- chunk -->

### of the innate heat of life, and not from mere exposure to cold. Yet that

<!-- chunk -->

### latter and more superficial type of cold would also respond to the

<!-- chunk -->

### characteristic remedies used here—stimulants.

<!-- chunk -->

### Depression is characterized by cold skin and extremities, pus-

<!-- chunk -->

### generating diseases, sepsis, putrefaction, dark colors in the complexion

<!-- chunk -->

### and tongue, low oxidation of the tissues, the appearance of bacteria or

<!-- chunk -->

### parasites which are living off tissue waste and secreting toxins that

<!-- chunk -->

### depress the tissue functions of the host. This is not a condition of

<!-- chunk -->

### toxicity, as in the previous tissue state, so much as poisoning, or tissue

<!-- chunk -->

### death. Yet the progression starts slowly, is not too evident at first, and

<!-- chunk -->

### thus, the depressed tissue state presents a subtle expression in the earlier

<!-- chunk -->

### stages.

<!-- chunk -->

### Treatment of depression is usually by aromatics containing volatile

<!-- chunk -->

### oils. These are oils that quickly vaporize into the atmosphere. The oils

<!-- chunk -->

### used to treat atrophy are either fixed, nonvolatile food oils or such large

<!-- chunk -->

### amounts of volatile oil that they function like fixed oils. Throughout the

<!-- chunk -->

### world, fragrant plants are used to banish depressive, putrefactive, dead

<!-- chunk -->

### energy, or bring in life-giving, spiritualizing influences. They quickly

<!-- chunk -->

### energize the lungs and open them up to life-giving oxygen, which this

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue state needs. The aromatic property is akin to the pungent taste,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is not really a taste but a smell.

<!-- chunk -->

### More broadly, remedies for the depressed tissue state may be called

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulants. There are several kinds, including aromatics, pungent and

<!-- chunk -->

### spicy herbs, fragrant bitters, and pine oils. Most stimulants are warming,

<!-- chunk -->

### but some so influence the organism that they end up being cooling, like

<!-- chunk -->

### Echinacea, Lavendula, and Achillea. Stimulants that act on the stomach to

<!-- chunk -->

### increase function and decrease indigestion and gas are known as

<!-- chunk -->

### carminatives.

<!-- chunk -->

### The most common stimulants are the pungent and spicy herbs. The

<!-- chunk -->

### former contain sulfur compounds, the latter volatile oils. Typical

<!-- chunk -->

### warming stimulants include Capsicum (cayenne pepper), Curcuma longa

<!-- chunk -->

### (turmeric), Zingiberis (ginger), Armoracia or Cochlearia (horseradish),

<!-- chunk -->

### Allium spp. (garlic, onion), Brassica spp. (cabbage, mustard), Ocimum

<!-- chunk -->

### (basil), Salvia (sage), Oreganum (oregano), Thymus (thyme), Xanthoxylum

<!-- chunk -->

### (prickly ash), Commiphora (myrrh), and Sambucus (fresh elder flowers).

<!-- chunk -->

### Urtica (nettle) is an anomalous stimulant that is neither pungent nor

<!-- chunk -->

### aromatic.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalist David Winston has established an important category called

<!-- chunk -->

### the “fragrant bitters.” These plants, in addition to their uses as bitters,

<!-- chunk -->

### have vermifuge properties. They generally act on depression. This group

<!-- chunk -->

### includes Artemisia absinthium (wormwood), Artemisia annua (sweet

<!-- chunk -->

### annie), Juglans cinerea (butternut), and Juglans nigra (black walnut).

<!-- chunk -->

### Another group acting on depression is the plants containing aromatic

<!-- chunk -->

### pine oils: Pinus strobus (white pine), Thuja occidentalis (cedar), Juniperus

<!-- chunk -->

### communis (juniper), and Tsuga canadensis (hemlock spruce). Members of

<!-- chunk -->

### the heath family (Ericaceae) could also be included here. These are

<!-- chunk -->

### usually designated as “antiseptics.” They remove bacteria by cleansing

<!-- chunk -->

### the environment of bacterial toxins. Another important antiseptic is

<!-- chunk -->

### Baptisia tinctoria (wild indigo).

<!-- chunk -->

## Zingiberis officinalis (Ginger)

<!-- chunk -->

### Ginger rhizome is probably the most simple, widely used stimulating and

<!-- chunk -->

### warming agent. It is used in folk medicine throughout the world. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### suited to cases where the system is cold, the mucosa are inactive, with

<!-- chunk -->

### free secretion and irritation, or the mucus is settled and stagnant so that

<!-- chunk -->

### it needs to be raised by sneezing, coughing, or vomiting. Ginger has an

<!-- chunk -->

### affinity to superficial, acute disease processes, rather than deep, chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### ones. It is used as a local stimulant when the stomach is inactive and

<!-- chunk -->

### congested and the patient feels chilly or when there are chills associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with tension. Ginger pack is used externally on the lungs, to help break

<!-- chunk -->

### up mucus congestion, and on the uterus, to dispel blood congestion.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is especially useful for its effect on the stomach. Julia Graves gives

<!-- chunk -->

### her own testimonial. “I had a cramping stomach for years in medical

<!-- chunk -->

### school from the stress. Sometimes I would have to eat oats in water with

<!-- chunk -->

### a bit of salt as the only diet for weeks, because I could tolerate nothing

<!-- chunk -->

### else. One day I just had the thought to buy some candied ginger. It’s very

<!-- chunk -->

### expensive in Germany, so I had not had any for a long time. My stomach

<!-- chunk -->

### had been in severe cutting pain for days. The minute I ate the first piece

<!-- chunk -->

### I was cured.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Julia notes that the candied ginger is better for the stomach. Dried

<!-- chunk -->

### ginger is more irritating. It is sold in Germany for seasickness. “Great for

<!-- chunk -->

### nausea and car sickness, especially in the cold winter.” It is

<!-- chunk -->

### contraindicated in heat. “Ginger foot baths are so good, for any kind of

<!-- chunk -->

### warming up or belly cramps. Makes you sweat, like a sauna.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Ginger oil is an excellent warming stimulant for spasmodic muscles, as

<!-- chunk -->

### I can attest from personal experience. Here it is rivaled by the American

<!-- chunk -->

### wild ginger (Asarum canadensis), a superlative remedy for muscle spasm

<!-- chunk -->

### better from warmth (Lise Wolff).

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: pungent, sweet, warm, diffusive, stimulating.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: mucosa, circulation, and blood.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Inactive gastrointestinal mucosa; with indigestion, fullness, catarrh,

<!-- chunk -->

### flatulence, colic, spasm, diarrhea, dysentery; sometimes associated

<!-- chunk -->

### with dietary indiscretion, tainted food, or chills.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Flatulence caused by ingesta in a state of decomposition.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hot decoction of the rhizome as a diaphoretic to break up incipient

<!-- chunk -->

### colds.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Poultices on the chest to break up mucus; on the uterus to break up

<!-- chunk -->

### stagnant blood.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Poor peripheral circulation; coldness, stagnant blood.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pregnancy: morning sickness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Muscle spasm (external).

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, Jack Ritchason (6).

## Calendula officinalis (Calendula)

<!-- chunk -->

### Calendula compares in an interesting way with the preceding remedy.

<!-- chunk -->

### Ginger is a strong heating stimulant, while calendula has a very subtle

<!-- chunk -->

### warmth. Yet, I think that it is more penetrating, into the interior of the

<!-- chunk -->

### organism than ginger, which has more effect on the exterior—skin,

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles, stomach. It was classified as “warm in the first degree” in

<!-- chunk -->

### traditional Greek medicine, which means that it drives fluids out

<!-- chunk -->

### through the skin. However, the warmth is quite penetrating and I myself,

<!-- chunk -->

### with a hot digestive tract, find it very irritating on the stomach. I think

<!-- chunk -->

### of it as penetrating as far as the third degree of heat, which was the

<!-- chunk -->

### category of medicines that have an antiseptic, antipurulent virtue, but

<!-- chunk -->

### who am I to argue with the old Greek physicians who invented the

<!-- chunk -->

### degrees?

<!-- chunk -->

### Calendula is perhaps the best general remedy when swollen glands

<!-- chunk -->

### take on an existence of their own, lingering after an acute illness or

<!-- chunk -->

### coming and going with “low immunity.” It increases the dispersal of

<!-- chunk -->

### fluids through the lymphatic system and quiets inflammation in the

<!-- chunk -->

### glands and ducts. A capital indication is a deep ache in the left chest,

<!-- chunk -->

### above the thoracic outlet of the lymphatic system. This indicates general

<!-- chunk -->

### lymphatic congestion and inflammation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Calendula was an old German folk remedy adopted by Samuel

<!-- chunk -->

### Hahnemann into the homeopathic materia medica as a specific topical

<!-- chunk -->

### dressing for wounds, sores, burns, and infections. The internal

<!-- chunk -->

### indications have not been developed in homeopathy. Regarding these we

<!-- chunk -->

### may say calendula possesses five main uses.

<!-- chunk -->

### 1. It is applied externally to improve lymphatic drainage from wounds

<!-- chunk -->

### and is bacteriostatic, i.e., it does not kill bacteria but prevents the

<!-- chunk -->

### extension of the infection.

<!-- chunk -->

### 2. Used internally it is therapeutic for swollen glands and lingering,

<!-- chunk -->

### unresolved infections.

<!-- chunk -->

### 3. Used for high enzyme counts from damaged liver.

<!-- chunk -->

### 4. Soothing to the digestive mucosa, and other mucous membranes

<!-- chunk -->

### 5. A traditional European peasant “immune tonic.”

<!-- chunk -->

### The flowers are gathered through the year and thrown in soups in the

<!-- chunk -->

### fall and winter to warm and protect against wind and chill. It cleans

<!-- chunk -->

### lymphatic stagnation predisposing to infection. Case histories,

<!-- chunk -->

### illustrating some of these virtues, are given in The Book of Herbal

<!-- chunk -->

### Wisdom.

<!-- chunk -->

### Herbalist Matthew Becker says that calendula is “one of the best

<!-- chunk -->

### diaphoretics for deep fever,” when the bones hurt. He compares it to

<!-- chunk -->

### boneset. I have noticed that it works with people who are “bone weary.”

<!-- chunk -->

### These associations led me to wonder if it does not have some kind of

<!-- chunk -->

### immune stimulating effect on the bone marrow where the immune cells

<!-- chunk -->

### are largely produced.

<!-- chunk -->

### The flowers are collected through the calends of the year and dried.

<!-- chunk -->

### Fresh flowers produce too watery an extract so they are better dried. Can

<!-- chunk -->

### be used as an infusion, tincture, salve, or ointment, in small to large

<!-- chunk -->

### doses.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: bitter, astrigent, sweet, warm, resinous.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue state: cold/depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: skin (vulnerary), lymph/immune, liver.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Yellow look about the eyes; tired eyes, feels and looks “bone weary;”

<!-- chunk -->

### sometimes the facial skin is red and dry; generalized yellow

<!-- chunk -->

### complexion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tongue; slightly puffy, pink or red papillae scattered around the

<!-- chunk -->

### periphery, indicating unresolved infections.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Swollen glands; residual after infections.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Inflammation of the deeper lymphatic ductwork, resulting in pain in

<!-- chunk -->

### the upper left side of the left chest (rarely, right) due to congestion of

<!-- chunk -->

### the thoracic duct.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Asthma, bronchitis; associated with old, lingering infections.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tongue slightly puffy and atonic, with indentations; heat in the

<!-- chunk -->

### digestive mucosa.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Gum disease, stomach irritation, ulcers, and hemorrhoids.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sluggish liver; high enzyme counts; viral hepatitis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Neuralgia, cramp.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lacerations, cuts, abrasions, burns, stings, bruises, blisters, chicken

<!-- chunk -->

### pox, measles, frostbite, acne; with redness, tenderness, swelling

<!-- chunk -->

### (external).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Vaginal wall, irritated or dry, bleeding, tearing at birth.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Vaginal warts.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Fibroids at menopause or after partus.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Leucorrhea.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Last stage of smallpox, sepsis, where life is threatened.

## Angelica archangelica (Angelica)

<!-- chunk -->

### Angelica is native to cutaneous and northern Europe, as far west as

<!-- chunk -->

### Iceland and Greenland. The American angelica (A. atropupurea) is native

<!-- chunk -->

### through the eastern Woodland region, up into the Canadian shield and

<!-- chunk -->

### west into the Rockies. I use them interchangeably, but only the European

<!-- chunk -->

### archangelica is acceptable in commerce.

<!-- chunk -->

### Throughout the world, various members of this clan are worn on the

<!-- chunk -->

### person as protection against bad vibrations and contagious diseases.

<!-- chunk -->

### They are also considered to have a mild effect on the mind; they relax

<!-- chunk -->

### the thinking apparatus and open the imagination. If one is sensitive, one

<!-- chunk -->

### can fairly easily feel this effectby burning some of the root and inhaling

<!-- chunk -->

### the smoke. The “sphincter of the mind” is relaxed and opened. Thus,

<!-- chunk -->

### angelica was considered a “journeying medicine” in ancient times.

<!-- chunk -->

### Angelica is a sacred herb in Saami or Lapp culture.

<!-- chunk -->

### The root, stalks, leaves, and seeds of angelica are oily, aromatic,

<!-- chunk -->

### warming, and stimulating. This makes them suited to the dry/atrophic

<!-- chunk -->

### and cold/depressive tissue states. They warm up the system, increase the

<!-- chunk -->

### circulation of blood, remove bruises, and increase digestive secretion.

<!-- chunk -->

### Although moistening by virtue of its oily content, angelica often has a

<!-- chunk -->

### warming and drying effect, removing water and mucus in the lungs and

<!-- chunk -->

### gastrointestinal tract. It is suited to dry, undernourished, thin,

<!-- chunk -->

### neurasthenic, often older people with rigid thinking who are subject to

<!-- chunk -->

### respiratory infections, flatulence, bloating, poor digestion, and stiff

<!-- chunk -->

### articulations (Ellen Hopman, David Winston). It is also suited to thick,

<!-- chunk -->

### chunky people with yellow/gray/blue complexion around the veins,

<!-- chunk -->

### showing stagnation of blood and fluids (Matthew Wood).

<!-- chunk -->

### A tall, thin, sixty-two-year-old man came to see me about a number of

<!-- chunk -->

### problems. He walked stiffly and joked about telling his kids (they were

<!-- chunk -->

### quite young) that he needed “oil” to lubricate his hips. His complexion

<!-- chunk -->

### was gray/yellow, the skin was dry, and the hands were somewhat

<!-- chunk -->

### red/purple, showing poor circulation. When we gave him Angelica

<!-- chunk -->

### atropurpurea (I use the local, wild species) his faced reddened, the skin

<!-- chunk -->

### became moister, the hands less purple, and he got up and started

<!-- chunk -->

### dancing around the room like a hula or belly dancer! Old hippies never

<!-- chunk -->

### die. They know enough to trust herbs. We “put the hip back in the

<!-- chunk -->

### hippie.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Angelica is perhaps most important as a carminative or

<!-- chunk -->

### aromatic/bitter stimulant to the stomach and intestines. It improves

<!-- chunk -->

### secretion and removes severe spasm and bloating associated with great

<!-- chunk -->

### pain. It is one of the most important carminatives used in European

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine.

<!-- chunk -->

### Angelica was not originally used as a female medicine in our tradition,

<!-- chunk -->

### like its Chinese cousin Angelica sinensis. It does not possess the estrogenic

<!-- chunk -->

### compounds of the latter. However, by increasing digestion, assimilation,

<!-- chunk -->

### and fat metabolism, while decreasing blood stagnation, it would have

<!-- chunk -->

### pretty much the same effects. Herbalist Stephen Buhner has introduced it

<!-- chunk -->

### for women who are having troubles establishing their own identity or

<!-- chunk -->

### separating from socially accepted role models. They feel hollow and

<!-- chunk -->

### without internal purpose. He especially finds it useful for women in their

<!-- chunk -->

### late twenties.

<!-- chunk -->

### Chinese angelica has been shown to have estrogenic effects,

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulating uterine contractions. It will do this when given

<!-- chunk -->

### inappropriately to a high estrogen woman. It is better suited to thin, dry

<!-- chunk -->

### women with lack of fat and estrogen, who are having muscular cramps

<!-- chunk -->

### with the period.

<!-- chunk -->

### The seeds and roots are used, extracted in high proof alcohol. Small

<!-- chunk -->

### doses are sufficient, in my experience.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (seed, root): pungent, sweet, bitter, warm, moist, oily,

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulating, diffusive.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: atrophy, depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: lipid digestion and metabolism, respiratory tract,

<!-- chunk -->

### gastrointestinal tract, blood, menses.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Emotionally empty, hollow; stimulates the imagination and relaxes

<!-- chunk -->

### the mind.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Innately lyrical, imaginative nature.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mental dullness, exhaustion, and obtuseness, sometimes with phlegm

<!-- chunk -->

### congestion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Thin, dry, pallid persons with poor digestion, gas, bloating, but

<!-- chunk -->

### tendencies to accumulation of fluids; poor peripheral circulation,

<!-- chunk -->

### cold hands and feet.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Complexion yellow/gray; yellow/gray/green/blue;

<!-- chunk -->

### yellow/gray/purple/red.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Headache.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Catarrhal deafness (ear-drops).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Influenza, chills, lung congestion, asthma, dullness of the mind, pallid

<!-- chunk -->

### complexion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lack of appetite.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Stomach troubles; bloating, gas, heartburn, sour stomach, nausea,

<!-- chunk -->

### vomiting.

<!-- chunk -->

<!-- chunk -->

## Rosmarinus officinalis (Rosemary)

<!-- chunk -->

### This warm, dry, stimulating mint is one of the most important herbs in

<!-- chunk -->

### Mediterranean medicine. It is one of the great stimulants and has some

<!-- chunk -->

### antiseptic power. Dioscorides, Galen, and Plinius described its

<!-- chunk -->

### properties. The Arabic physicians considered it one of the most valuable

<!-- chunk -->

### items in the materia medica. Rosemary is mentioned in the herbals of

<!-- chunk -->

### northern Europe from the Renaissance down to the present. It was

<!-- chunk -->

### popularized by Father Sebastian Kneipp and is still used in conventional

<!-- chunk -->

### medicine in Germany.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Dietrich Gümbel (1993) describes the general physical affinities of

<!-- chunk -->

### rosemary. It acts on the metabolism, enhancing the burning and

<!-- chunk -->

### consumption of blood sugars and fats. It thus cleans and oxidizes

<!-- chunk -->

### throughout the body. Through nerve stimulation, it activates the smooth

<!-- chunk -->

### muscles, thus influencing the arteries, stomach, intestines, gall passages,

<!-- chunk -->

### and heart, while relaxing the voluntary muscles. It acts strongly on the

<!-- chunk -->

### blood organs, especially on the liver, also on the heart, lungs, spleen,

<!-- chunk -->

### and kidneys. The catabolic processes of the liver are increased and more

<!-- chunk -->

### gall is manufactured.

<!-- chunk -->

### By increasing the oxygenation and burning processes throughout the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, excessive blood sugar is used up. This strengthens all the functions

<!-- chunk -->

### of the body, but especially those of the heart and brain, which subsist on

<!-- chunk -->

### huge quantities of blood sugar (Gümbel). Rudolf Steiner taught that

<!-- chunk -->

### rosemary was a diabetes remedy. He associated it with the fire element

<!-- chunk -->

### or warmth organism or “I-consciousness.” It strengthens the sense of self,

<!-- chunk -->

### which is so often lacking in diabetes, and provokes a deeper incarnation.

<!-- chunk -->

### It is good for those who can’t wake up in the morning and need to drink

<!-- chunk -->

### coffee, but, as Julia Graves remarks, do not use too much because it can

<!-- chunk -->

### cause the heart to race.

<!-- chunk -->

### At the same time, rosemary is indicated in people who have too strong

<!-- chunk -->

### an incarnation, so to speak, or who grasp the material world with such

<!-- chunk -->

### vigor that they get headaches and nervous tension. I do not mean that

<!-- chunk -->

### they are materialistic; it is for people who grasp life with great intensity,

<!-- chunk -->

### or not with enough intensity.

<!-- chunk -->

### One of my friends in Vermont had a severe migraine headache that

<!-- chunk -->

### was keeping her in bed. An inspiration came to her to put rosemary

<!-- chunk -->

### under her upper lip and poultice the back of her head. For good measure

<!-- chunk -->

### she wrapped a piece around her temples like a victory wreath. The

<!-- chunk -->

### headache slowly but completely disappeared. Several months latter some

<!-- chunk -->

### friends called her in virtual anguish. The wife had a terrible migraine

<!-- chunk -->

### that had defied all medical help. The husband was supposed to be

<!-- chunk -->

### bringing the daughter back to boarding school the next morning, but

<!-- chunk -->

### now they didn’t dare leave the house. “Do you have any rosemary in the

<!-- chunk -->

### house?” my friend asked. The husband later admitted that his thought

<!-- chunk -->

### was, “How can you talk about cooking at a time like this!” but he

<!-- chunk -->

### answered with a short affirmative. The directions were given and the

<!-- chunk -->

### next day my friend got a call from the husband and daughter on their

<!-- chunk -->

### way to school. The cure was rapid and fairly complete. “I came out

<!-- chunk -->

### looking like some kind of shaman or something,” commented my friend.

<!-- chunk -->

### Those are the best cures.

<!-- chunk -->

### In a somewhat similar vein, Eva Graf (1978, 25) recommends

<!-- chunk -->

### rosemary for tension in the occipital region. She applies a cotton pad

<!-- chunk -->

### saturated with the tincture to the occiput and the sacral/lumbar joint,

<!-- chunk -->

### with the indication to lay down and relax for twenty minutes before

<!-- chunk -->

### taking off the pads. This usage is not only similar to that inaugurated by

<!-- chunk -->

### my friend, but bears some slight resemblance to the use of rosemary by

<!-- chunk -->

### Carlos Castaneda (below).

<!-- chunk -->

### Lest we missed anything to do with the head or brain, Culpeper

<!-- chunk -->

### enlightens us: “The decoction thereof in wine, helps the cold distillations

<!-- chunk -->

### of rheums into the eyes, and all other cold diseases of the head and

<!-- chunk -->

### brain, as the giddiness or swimmings therein, drowsiness or dullness of

<!-- chunk -->

### the mind and senses like a stupidness, the dumb palsy, or loss of speech,

<!-- chunk -->

### the lethargy and falling-sickness, to be drunk, and the temples bathed

<!-- chunk -->

### therewith. It helps the pains in the gums and teeth … causing an evil

<!-- chunk -->

### smell from them, or a stinking breath. It helps a weak memory, and

<!-- chunk -->

### quickens the senses.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Rosemary has an extremely ancient reputation in folklore as an herb

<!-- chunk -->

### for remembrance. It was used from ancient times down to the

<!-- chunk -->

### Renaissance as an emblem for fidelity between lovers. Ann of Cleves is

<!-- chunk -->

### supposed to have worn a wreath of rosemary around her head at her

<!-- chunk -->

### wedding to Henry VIII. Hopefully she was able to forget that marriage

<!-- chunk -->

### quickly. Shakespeare mentions it several times. And our dear friend,

<!-- chunk -->

### whom we hope not to forget, the late great herbalist William LeSassier,

<!-- chunk -->

### described rosemary as the remedy to help get rid of bad memories.

<!-- chunk -->

### Apropos of this, Amy Wallace, in Sorcerer’s Apprentice (2003, 111), her

<!-- chunk -->

### fascinating account of life with Carlos Castaneda, says that the latter

<!-- chunk -->

### recommended putting a poultice of rosemary on the genitals to remove

<!-- chunk -->

### the influence of past sexual experiences, an herbal technique he claimed

<!-- chunk -->

### to have gotten from don Juan.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dr. Rudolf Weiss gives a general picture of the person who responds to

<!-- chunk -->

### rosemary. Where there is chronic circulatory weakness, including low

<!-- chunk -->

### blood pressure, and especially in young people who are weak, thin, pale,

<!-- chunk -->

### and easily run-down, rosemary is indicated. It is also appropriate for

<!-- chunk -->

### older people with weak circulation, sometimes due to constitutional

<!-- chunk -->

### weakness but in many cases from overwork or following weakening

<!-- chunk -->

### diseases such as influenza or pneumonia. It stimulates the appetite and is

<!-- chunk -->

### especially indicated when atonic conditions of the stomach accompany

<!-- chunk -->

### poor circulation.

<!-- chunk -->

### Moving to the physiological level, Rose Elliot and Carlo di Paoli say

<!-- chunk -->

### that rosemary is indicated in a cold, sluggish liver and gall bladder

<!-- chunk -->

### condition, where there is a pale, yellowish complexion, slow digestion,

<!-- chunk -->

### bitter taste in the mouth, and lack of energy. It stimulates the arterial

<!-- chunk -->

### blood supply to the liver, promoting cleansing and burning, and

<!-- chunk -->

### generates more gall, which is more readily secreted. Australian herbalist

<!-- chunk -->

### Dorothy Hall recommends rosemary as a general tonic at the beginning

<!-- chunk -->

### of a case where there is need for increased detoxification, metabolism,

<!-- chunk -->

### and elimination.

<!-- chunk -->

### The stimulating volatile oils of rosemary act upon the stomach to

<!-- chunk -->

### increase secretion, remove gas, improve appetite, and renew function.

<!-- chunk -->

### They make their impression upon the autonomic nervous system, and

<!-- chunk -->

### hence upon the innervation and circulation of the abdominal viscera. “It

<!-- chunk -->

### is very comfortable to the stomach in all the cold maladies thereof: helps

<!-- chunk -->

### both retention of meat, and digestion,” says Culpeper (1652, 1994, 155).

<!-- chunk -->

### “It is a remedy for the windiness in the stomach, bowels, and spleen, and

<!-- chunk -->

### expels [wind] powerfully.” By “warming and comforting” the heat of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, “it helps all cold diseases, both of the head, stomach, liver, and

<!-- chunk -->

### belly.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Father Sebastian Kneipp gives similar testimony. “It cleanses the

<!-- chunk -->

### stomach from phlegm,” he says. “The stomach will soon become

<!-- chunk -->

### sensible, i.e., will not stick fast much longer in phlegm.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Rosemary has been used since the time of Dioscorides and Galen to

<!-- chunk -->

### decongest the liver and gall bladder and remove phlegm. It stimulates

<!-- chunk -->

### the arterial blood supply to the liver, promoting cleansing and burning

<!-- chunk -->

### processes. By increasing oxidation and burning processes throughout the

<!-- chunk -->

### body, excessive blood sugar is taken up. “Through this, all organs and

<!-- chunk -->

### organ systems are strengthened in their function, especially the

<!-- chunk -->

### constantly beating heart, which uses blood sugar all the time in vast

<!-- chunk -->

### amounts” (Gümbel).

<!-- chunk -->

### Through the circulation and nervous system, rosemary acts strongly on

<!-- chunk -->

### the heart. One of the most remarkable properties of rosemary is its

<!-- chunk -->

### effectiveness in treating serious cardiopulmonary edema. Father Kneipp

<!-- chunk -->

### speaks highly of it. “Rosemary-wine, taken in small doses, has also

<!-- chunk -->

### proved an excellent remedy against heart-infirmities. It operates in a

<!-- chunk -->

### sedative manner, and in cases of heart-dropsy it works strongly on

<!-- chunk -->

### removal through the urine. Such wine renders the same service in dropsy

<!-- chunk -->

### in general.”

<!-- chunk -->

### Father Sebastian Kneipp (1896, 201) gives several impressive accounts

<!-- chunk -->

### of Rosemary. “The whole body of a peasant woman about forty-eight

<!-- chunk -->

### years of age begins to swell; she is scarcely able to walk. The enervation

<!-- chunk -->

### is already great, breathing a great burden. I advised her immediately to

<!-- chunk -->

### put rosemary in wine and daily to drink two wine-glassfuls.” Externally,

<!-- chunk -->

### she applied warm water and vinegar poultices and half baths for the

<!-- chunk -->

### upper part of the body (evidently a heart-dropsy). “The peasant woman

<!-- chunk -->

### got well and was able to attend to her work entirely and unhindered.”

<!-- chunk -->

### This usage has continued down to the present time in Germany.

<!-- chunk -->

### Rosemary ointment is listed in the German pharmacopoeia for use in

<!-- chunk -->

### functional heart complaints, when it is rubbed into the chest. The spirit

<!-- chunk -->

### of Rosemary is used externally for arthritic complaints, neuralgia, and

<!-- chunk -->

### the heart. Dr. Rudolf Weiss recommends making an elixir from rosemary

<!-- chunk -->

### in white wine, preferably of southern European origin; Californian

<!-- chunk -->

### would work as well.

<!-- chunk -->

### I have seen rosemary work very nicely in several cases. One woman

<!-- chunk -->

### described the action to me succinctly. “I feel it tingling all over my body,

<!-- chunk -->

### in every cell, down to my toes. About fifteen minutes later I have to go

<!-- chunk -->

### to the bathroom. The difference between rosemary and lasix is that it is

<!-- chunk -->

### more selective. Lasix pumps out all the fluids it can get its hands on,

<!-- chunk -->

### whereas rosemary pulls out fluids that I can feel are stagnant and need

<!-- chunk -->

### to go.” She was suffering from chronic pulmonary edema caused by scar

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue and suffered an acute aggravation from burns caused by chlorine

<!-- chunk -->

### fumes.

<!-- chunk -->

### The influence of rosemary extends to the extremities. Nerves are

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulated, congealed blood is removed, and sensation is improved. This

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy is used to remove cold conditions from the limbs, including the

<!-- chunk -->

### stiffness and soreness resulting from undue muscular activity as well as

<!-- chunk -->

### that from arthritic conditions, bruises, and strains. The diluted oil, or an

<!-- chunk -->

### ointment, is “singularly good to help cold benumbed joints, sinews, or

<!-- chunk -->

### members” (Culpeper, 1994, 347). It is also used externally on the skin to

<!-- chunk -->

### increase activity and circulation. It improves the water-retentive

<!-- chunk -->

### qualities of the skin, stimulates heat-discharge through a better

<!-- chunk -->

### metabolism of fats and sugars, and nourishes the skin glands. It is

<!-- chunk -->

### indicated for a pale, cool skin with weak peripheral circulation

<!-- chunk -->

### (Gümbel).

<!-- chunk -->

### There are many ways to prepare and use rosemary. The diluted oil is

<!-- chunk -->

### used externally on the skin to increase activity and circulation. It

<!-- chunk -->

### improves the water-retentive qualities of the skin, stimulates heat-

<!-- chunk -->

### discharge through a better metabolism of fats and sugars, and nourishes

<!-- chunk -->

### the skin glands. It is indicated for a pale, poorly circulated, cool skin (Dr.

<!-- chunk -->

### Dietrich Gümbel, 1993). Keep the oil out of the eyes. Pouring a cup of

<!-- chunk -->

### boiling water over a tablespoon of the leaves and flowers makes the

<!-- chunk -->

### infusion. Keep the pot covered, so that the volatile oils do not escape

<!-- chunk -->

### from the vessel. Father Kneipp gives directions for making a simple,

<!-- chunk -->

### elegant tincture. “A handful of rosemary is cut up as small as possible,

<!-- chunk -->

### put into a bottle, and good, well-kept wine poured upon it; white wine is

<!-- chunk -->

### preferable. Even after half a day’s standing, it may be used” (Kniepp,

<!-- chunk -->

### 1896, 201). Rudolf Weiss (1988) adds that wines from southern

<!-- chunk -->

### countries, with a relatively high alcohol content, extract the active

<!-- chunk -->

### constituents best. Brandy, likewise a product of the vine, makes a fine

<!-- chunk -->

### tincture.

<!-- chunk -->

### And to close our account with our gallant astrological savant … “the

<!-- chunk -->

### Sun claims privilege in it, and it is under the celestial Ram,” proclaims

<!-- chunk -->

### Nicholas Culpeper (1994, 347). This is his metaphorical way of

<!-- chunk -->

### describing a strong, fixed, radiant warming and drying medicine. “The

<!-- chunk -->

### whole plant has a fragrant and aromatic smell: it is lighter and more

<!-- chunk -->

### delicate in the flower, and stronger in the leaves. The taste also is warm

<!-- chunk -->

### and aromatic, and not disagreeable.”

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: spicy, warm, dry, oily, diffusive, stimulating.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue States: atrophy, depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: respiratory, digestion, liver, gall bladder and pancreas,

<!-- chunk -->

### heart and circulation, nervous system.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Pale, thin, weak persons with poor circulation to the surface, cool

<!-- chunk -->

### skin; pale, sallow persons with weak digestion; older persons with

<!-- chunk -->

### cardiopulmonary edema.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lack of self-identity and strength of personality.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Depression, nervousness, anxiety.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Young children with head colds, ear and throat problems; due to weak

<!-- chunk -->

### liver.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hair loss, thinning (oil externally).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Weak cerebral circulation; vertigo, loss of memory, headache (oil

<!-- chunk -->

### externally).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Headaches associated with weak liver function.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Eyestrain, headache; from overuse, after long driving (oil externally).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sinusitis, bronchitis, asthma.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diminished appetite, bitter taste in the mouth.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Poor gastric reflexes and secretions, weak autonomic and digestive

<!-- chunk -->

### nervous system.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Bloating, vomiting.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Incipient intestinal fever (very hot tea).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Spleen congestion; chronic hypertension resulting in spleen

<!-- chunk -->

### engorgement and hypertrophy; spleen enlargement from malaria.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Liver function depressed; poor anabolism, low blood sugar, weakness,

<!-- chunk -->

### pallor; fetid breath, pale, yellow, gray complexion.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Gall bladder reflexes depressed; poor expulsion of bile, sluggish

<!-- chunk -->

### digestion, bitter taste, indigestion in the stomach, pale, yellow

<!-- chunk -->

### complexion, lack of energy.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Cardiovascular depression; weak peripheral circulation, cold

<!-- chunk -->

### extremities; low blood pressure; cardiopulmonary edema.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Arteriosclerosis, high cholesterol, high blood pressure.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Older people with heart palpitations, pain and cramp in the region of

<!-- chunk -->

### the heart, but actually in the stomach, immediately after eating.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Amenorrhea in pale, weak, cold women.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Leucorrhea; infection, foul-smelling discharge.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Postpartum: depression.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Extremities weak, unsteady, paralytic, cold, pale.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Externally, the oil as a liniment on bruises and strains; paralytic

<!-- chunk -->

### affections; muscle tension; popular use for stimulating hair growth.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Rheumatoid arthritis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Occipital tension (apply a saturated cotton pad to the occiput and the

<!-- chunk -->

### sacrum).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Tired muscles; after unaccustomed exercise (external).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Contraindicated in high blood pressure and headaches with hot,

<!-- chunk -->

### bursting symptoms.

<!-- chunk -->

## Xanthoxylum americanum (Northern Prickly Ash)

<!-- chunk -->

### The bark or berry causes an intense tingling on the tongue and throat,

<!-- chunk -->

### which can quickly become torturous. This stimulating impression moves

<!-- chunk -->

### down the throat and through the body, awakening the nervous system,

<!-- chunk -->

### left and right. The physiomedicalists included it among their handful of

<!-- chunk -->

### diffusives, remedies that cause a diffusing sensation through the nerves. It

<!-- chunk -->

### is a powerful stimulant to the nervous system; it “equalizes the charge

<!-- chunk -->

### on the nerves,” so to speak. It is the superlative remedy for torture.

<!-- chunk -->

### I remember the first time I ever used prickly ash. The woman was

<!-- chunk -->

### literally writhing in agony as she tried to sit on the chair next to me. She

<!-- chunk -->

### had fallen down a stair and injured the sciatic. She begged the doctors to

<!-- chunk -->

### “cut it” (not knowing what this would have meant, evidently) but they

<!-- chunk -->

### rightly refused. Prickly ash bark tincture, three drops, as needed,

<!-- chunk -->

### brought the pain to a level where it was easily tolerable, though not

<!-- chunk -->

### completely eliminated. Another woman came over with a torn disc in

<!-- chunk -->

### the neck. She was literally writhing in agony. Her sixteen-year-old

<!-- chunk -->

### daughter had to drive her over. I felt sorry for both of them. It was a

<!-- chunk -->

### little frightening. I gave her a dose of prickly ash, a few drops, and she

<!-- chunk -->

### was relatively pain-free for five minutes. She had to take the tincture at

<!-- chunk -->

### five minute intervals for the first day or two, then less and less. The disc

<!-- chunk -->

### has never healed, she says, but the pain has never returned.

<!-- chunk -->

### Xanthoxylum was given a homeopathic proving and produced such

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms as a sensation as if the ovaries were being ripped out of the

<!-- chunk -->

### body by wires. I had a case where exactly this symptom appeared. The

<!-- chunk -->

### woman was thirty-nine, lived on welfare, and had just about been an

<!-- chunk -->

### invalid since age thirteen, when her menses started. Those were the days

<!-- chunk -->

### when one could still live on welfare. She had been going to a classical

<!-- chunk -->

### homeopath for several years, with no result. I gave her a bottle of prickly

<!-- chunk -->

### ash tincture and she brought it back six months later with a smile on her

<!-- chunk -->

### face. “Thanks for giving me my life back,” she said. And she did get it

<!-- chunk -->

### back—moved to Hollywood, became a make-up artist and had a new

<!-- chunk -->

### boyfriend. Before leaving she gave me a gift I still treasure, an old

<!-- chunk -->

### homeopathic kit from the nineteenth century.

<!-- chunk -->

### Prickly ash also produced symptoms of numbness, tingling, and pain

<!-- chunk -->

### in the nerves. It is an excellent remedy for nerve damage resulting in

<!-- chunk -->

### numbness, tingling, and pain. One of my friends gave it to a woman who

<!-- chunk -->

### had a numb spot on the side of the face for decades following a car

<!-- chunk -->

### accident. In one hour she could feel it again. One of the conditions for

<!-- chunk -->

### which prickly ash is considered remedial in homeopathic literature

<!-- chunk -->

### (Boericke, 1927) is hemiplegia, or one-sided paralysis (Bell’s palsy). I

<!-- chunk -->

### have seen it cure this condition. It “equalizes the charge on the nerves.”

<!-- chunk -->

### One side of the body becomes intensely painful while the other side is

<!-- chunk -->

### semi-paralyzed. This condition perfectly expresses the essence of prickly

<!-- chunk -->

### ash, which is a remedy for imbalance in the charge on the nerves. It will

<!-- chunk -->

### rectify conditions where the nerves have been injured, torn,

<!-- chunk -->

### overstimulated, are numb, tingling, torturously painful, and it will do it

<!-- chunk -->

### with a power that is hard to believe.

<!-- chunk -->

### A woman came to me who had been diagnosed with polymyositis, or

<!-- chunk -->

### muscular inflammation in various places. She had fallen down, folding

<!-- chunk -->

### her knee back and injuring it severely. After the swelling and bruising

<!-- chunk -->

### went down, she had routine orthoscopic surgery on the knee. The next

<!-- chunk -->

### day she felt pain all over her body. For the next two years she suffered in

<!-- chunk -->

### agony. To fight the pain she was on prednisone and other

<!-- chunk -->

### pharmaceutical anodynes. These weakened her immune system, causing

<!-- chunk -->

### an ulcerating skin eruption, which made her feel grotesque and unclean.

<!-- chunk -->

### “I used to love my life; now it is hell.” I asked her for the actual

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms of the so-called polymyositis, since I thought we would never

<!-- chunk -->

### cure the skin problem until she was off the drugs. The first three

<!-- chunk -->

### symptoms she listed were muscular weakness, tingling in different places

<!-- chunk -->

### around the body, and paralysis. It was especially hard to get up out of a

<!-- chunk -->

### chair or bathtub, or climb stairs, due to weakness in the knees. A more

<!-- chunk -->

### perfect description of symptoms calling for prickly ash could hardly be

<!-- chunk -->

### offered. These immediately reduced the pain—she was able to walk up a

<!-- chunk -->

### flight of stairs right then and there to prove it. As prickly ash continued

<!-- chunk -->

### to remove the pain I could not help but think that the diagnosis was

<!-- chunk -->

### wrong. Something like “neuritis” would have been more appropriate.

<!-- chunk -->

### Prickly ash bark is plenty strong in small doses: a few drops, several

<!-- chunk -->

### times a day.

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (bark) pungent, slightly warm, highly stimulating and diffusive.

<!-- chunk -->

### (fruit) pungent, warm, moderately stimulating and diffusive.

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: depression.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: nerves.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Spare, withered women in late middle age, afflicted with chronic

<!-- chunk -->

### wasting diseases or robust, full persons with nerve injuries causing

<!-- chunk -->

### intense torment.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Writhing in agony, can’t sit still from the pain; torment of the most

<!-- chunk -->

### intense kind, bringing the patient to their knees.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Sore throat and respiratory tension and discomfort.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nerve damage; numb, tingling, painful.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hemiplegia (Bell’s palsy); one-sided pain, numbness, paralysis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Nerve debility, withered, weakness.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Deep respiratory infection, with debility and chronic illness.

<!-- chunk -->

## Literature: Traditional, homeopathic provings and clinical experience,

<!-- chunk -->

## Urtica dioeca, U. urens (Nettle, Nettles)

<!-- chunk -->

### This widespread weed is one of the fundamental remedies of Western

<!-- chunk -->

### folk herbalism. It is high in protein, vitamins, and minerals, so that it is

<!-- chunk -->

### an excellent all-around nutritive tonic. It is the most proteinaceous plant

<!-- chunk -->

### common in North America. It usually grows on old manure piles, septic

<!-- chunk -->

### fields, and in places where it is exposed to protein byproducts. As a

<!-- chunk -->

### stinging plant, nettle possesses stimulating properties.

<!-- chunk -->

### Nettle is one of the most generally useful plants in the pharmacopoeia.

<!-- chunk -->

### It acts on all constitutions. It feeds and builds up the thin and

<!-- chunk -->

### undernourished; counteracts the protein-sensitivity (allergies) of the

<!-- chunk -->

### medium pitta constitution; and helps remove excess stagnation of water

<!-- chunk -->

### and mucus in the thick kapha types.

<!-- chunk -->

### Yet, nettle also possesses a specific affinity and properties as well. It

<!-- chunk -->

### acts on the protein cycle throughout the body. It mildly desensitizes

<!-- chunk -->

### mucosa that are overly reactive to protein, i.e., allergic. It thus removes

<!-- chunk -->

### phlegm in the gastrointestinal tract generated for self-protection. It helps

<!-- chunk -->

### the liver build blood proteins, which keep the plasma inside the vessels.

<!-- chunk -->

### Without blood proteins, the fluids run out of the vasculature, the tissues

<!-- chunk -->

### become edematous, and there is low blood pressure. These are typical

<!-- chunk -->

### nettle symptoms. Australian herbalist Dorothy Hall and American

<!-- chunk -->

### nutritionist Bernard Jensen both considered nettles to be a specific

<!-- chunk -->

### remedy for low blood pressure. Nettles probably also helps the liver

<!-- chunk -->

### break down protein waste products. It certainly helps the system remove

<!-- chunk -->

### them, hence it is an old remedy for gout, arthritis, muscular soreness,

<!-- chunk -->

### tissue acidity, and kidney inactivity. The final goal of protein is to

<!-- chunk -->

### increase the “intelligence of the cells,” which depend on proteins—DNA,

<!-- chunk -->

### RNA. Nettles are thus also a specific restorative and stimulant, bringing

<!-- chunk -->

### back function to parts that have become paralyzed, atrophied, or

<!-- chunk -->

### functionally inactive. In the old days, the part paralyzed or arthritic or

<!-- chunk -->

### gouty was whipped with the fresh picked nettle. It is especially active

<!-- chunk -->

### when the part has been paralyzed since the use of anesthesia. It also

<!-- chunk -->

### works internally to get organs working when they have stopped

<!-- chunk -->

### functioning, such as inactive kidneys, thyroid, menstruation, hormones,

<!-- chunk -->

### nerves, muscles. Urtica is used homeopathically for burns, since it causes

<!-- chunk -->

### burning pain.

<!-- chunk -->

### The stimulating effects of nettle are reduced immediately after

<!-- chunk -->

### picking, so the best form of use is the freeze-dried capsule. However,

<!-- chunk -->

### some of the stimulating properties remain even in more slowly dried

<!-- chunk -->

### nettles. The nutritive effects are well represented by the slowly dried

<!-- chunk -->

### method of picking. The young shoots in the spring are especially good

<!-- chunk -->

### for medicine or food. The root is used for prostate problems. David

<!-- chunk -->

### Winston introduced the use of the seeds for kidney problems.

<!-- chunk -->

### I’ve had several amazing experiences with nettles. In one case a

<!-- chunk -->

### woman had atrophy of a muscle in the inner thigh which was making

<!-- chunk -->

### her lame and interfering with recovery from an injury. Nettles

<!-- chunk -->

### immediately improved her walk. I was actually flummoxed and nervous

<!-- chunk -->

### from the rapidity with which it worked. In another case, a student used

<!-- chunk -->

### nettle tea from her barnyard to restore her father’s kidneys and get him

<!-- chunk -->

### off dialysis. David Winston has collected other stories of the efficacy of

<!-- chunk -->

### nettle in kidney failure; he uses the seed. A man who had been paralyzed

<!-- chunk -->

### from the waist down, due to an accident, got so he could walk again

<!-- chunk -->

### through the use of herbs and acupuncture. Then he started walking in

<!-- chunk -->

### the old nettle patch on his farm until the sensation came back!

<!-- chunk -->

## Taste: (young shoots and leaves) sweet, bitter, salty/minerally,

<!-- chunk -->

### stimulating

<!-- chunk -->

## Tissue State: depression, atrophy, stagnation.

<!-- chunk -->

## Organ Affinities: liver, kidneys, muscles.


<!-- chunk -->

### • Loss of hair, hair color (external wash).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mouth sores.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Mucus, bronchitis, asthma.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Diarrhea, dysentery, mucus in stools.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Distention and swelling of kidneys; edema and eczema of the fingers

<!-- chunk -->

### or elsewhere.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Inactive kidneys (requiring dialysis); glomerulonephritis.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Impotence.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Menstruation: excessive.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Premature cessation of menses.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Pregnancy: anemia.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Lactation: deficient.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Benign prostatic hypertrophy (root).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Atrophy and paralysis of muscles (voluntary and involuntary).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Arthritis, gout (especially in those eating a rich, proteinaceous diet).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Low blood pressure; high blood pressure.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hypothyroid.

<!-- chunk -->

### • Burns with excess protein burdening the kidneys (external or

<!-- chunk -->

### internal).

<!-- chunk -->

### • Hives.
